Discussion Title: Should There be a Universal Basic Income (UBI)?

1. Wealthy countries should provide citizens with a universal basic income \(UBI\).
1.1. Pro: A UBI reduces crime.
1.1.1. Pro: Systems that already exist, [like child allowances payments](http://www.businessinsider.com/study-makes-strong-case-for-basic-income-for-kids-2016-12), are a first step towards a UBI, and they show that children who receive this allowance are less likely to grow up to commit crimes.
1.1.1.1. Con: Child allowances are not "a first step" towards UBI. Despite similarities, children’s labour is not needed for the economy so child allowances \(unless too high\) do not cause the vicious circle of economic output decline and increasing real poverty, like UBI.
1.1.1.2. Pro: In high crime areas [Cash for Criminals](https://www.cnn.com/2016/05/19/health/cash-for-criminals-richmond-california/index.html) programs have actually reduced crime by youth through using money as an incentive for not committing crime.
1.1.2. Con: A UBI attracts more illegal immigration.
1.1.2.1. Pro: Politically, a UBI might add to anti-immigration sentiments, as citizens will likely be concerned with immigrants taking advantage \(economic dependency\) of this system. This makes the lives of immigrants and perceived immigrants worse.
1.1.2.1.1. Pro: Immigration is a force for reducing global inequality. Reduced immigration is therefore a net harm because it harms the welfare of humanity as a whole and makes borders more rigid in general.
1.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: Migration and remittances increases the welfare of families who remain in their countries of origin. In [Guatemala](http://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0152089), having a migrant relative is associated with improved child nutrition.
1.1.2.1.1.2. Pro: Migration increases job opportunities and access to productive inputs to improve the effectiveness of labour. Both of these factors increase income for migrants in destination countries. Therefore, reduced migration increases global labour market inequalities.
1.1.2.1.1.3. Con: This is dependent on the actual reduction of migration.
1.1.2.1.1.3.1. Con: The United States would need 4 trillion a year to pay every citizen 15,000 a year. Adding 4 trillion a year to the national deficit would create inevitable desires to reduce immigration.
1.1.2.1.2. Pro: UBI guaranteed to any citizen creates greater economical inequality between citizens and non-citizens, be it foreign students, refuges or other migrants
1.1.2.1.3. Pro: The cost of financing incomes for migrants is difficult to justify politically. This is likely to cause migrants to be treated like second class citizens who will be further neglected by the lack of social institutions.
1.1.2.1.3.1. Pro: Even if immigrants do not immediately receive a UBI, people are likely to be concerned about immigrants coming to access it eventually. This may cause countries to not accept immigrants when they are needed to provide labor.
1.1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: If someone comes to a new country to work, they will inevitably pay tax, therefore supporting the overall system, including a UBI. If that work finishes and they choose to remain, they should be  entitled to receive UBI themselves.
1.1.2.1.3.1.2. Pro: UBI on a national level would create a very exclusive nationhood, and could cause strong differences between citizens and non-citizens living in a country. Freedom of movement could suffer as xenophobia for social envy would arise.
1.1.2.1.3.2. Pro: In many countries, plenty of political parties trade on the popular perception that governments favour immigrants over citizens. If immigrants receive the UBI, the resulting backlash would empower these parties to find new ways to reduce immigration or reduce the rights of immigrants in other areas.
1.1.2.1.3.2.1. Con: Some of the largest causes for xenophobia and anti immigrant sentiment are related to economic anxiety or fearing the possibility of losing something. This is less true in a world where everyone is guaranteed some level of income regardless of immigration trends.
1.1.2.1.3.2.1.1. Con: Inequality is also supported by the [demonization](https://www.theguardian.com/books/2011/jun/08/chavs-demonization-owen-jones-review) and mischaracterisation of the poor by the middle and upper-classes. Those who do not lack opportunity and resources employ that tactic to maintain their superiority.
1.1.2.1.3.3. Pro: People in rich countries receive a citizenship rent which they are unwilling to share. Voters thus seek to create citizen-only benefits \(tax discrimination of migrants or immigration crackdowns\) to reduce the benefits of migration.
1.1.2.1.3.4. Con: A large percentage of immigrants would not be economically dependent on the UBI due to the realities of most immigration systems, which give preferences to migrants with wealth, high levels of education, etc.
1.1.2.1.3.4.1. Con: Economically dependent or not - once accepted as citizens those immigrants would receive a UBI as well.
1.1.2.1.3.5. Con: Even where some immigrants are dependent on the UBI \(refugees, asylum seekers\) the overwhelming majority of all UBI payments are directed to local citizens rather than incoming immigrants.
1.1.2.1.3.5.1. Con: Public perception will often overrule a careful consideration of the facts about the total number of immigrants receiving UBI payments. Even if only a few hundred immigrants receive the UBI, that could be enough to create a public outcry.
1.1.2.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.1.
1.1.2.1.5. Con: A UBI is not likely to lead to anti-immigrant sentiment because it ensures that opportunities exist for all individuals, not just immigrants.
1.1.2.1.5.1. Pro: Xenophobia [is mainly fed by](http://digitalcommons.unl.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1089&context=edpsychpapers) a lack of economic resources and opportunity; thus a UBI will increase social cohesion.
1.1.2.1.5.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.3.2.1.1.
1.1.2.1.6. Con: Anti-immigrant sentiment will not occur because in order to gain the status of having a UBI, one needs to be registered either as a permanent resident or citizen of that government. Immigrants who do not pay taxes would not receive government benefits such as the UBI.
1.1.2.1.6.1. Con: Even if this is true, most immigrants strive to eventually achieve legal status, and subsequent citizenship benefits. This backlash still hurts them a lot.
1.1.2.1.6.1.1. Pro: For example, it likely harms efforts to grant visas and/or amnesty to illegal immigrants.
1.1.2.1.6.1.1.1. Pro: A UBI replacing current forms of welfare would make it harder for immigrants to become naturalized citizens.
1.1.2.1.7. Con: The economic benefits that migrants give by spending money in the system lead to far more benefits than their consumption of government expenditure costs.
1.1.2.1.7.1. Pro: Many undocumented workers pay tax despite not being eligible for many government assistance programs. On net they [contributed $12 billion to the US economy in 2010](https://www.ssa.gov/oact/NOTES/pdf_notes/note151.pdf).
1.1.2.1.7.2. Pro: Immigrants "[grease the wheels of the labor market by flowing into industries and areas where there is a relative need for workers](http://www.bushcenter.org/catalyst/north-american-century/benefits-of-immigration-outweigh-costs.html)" and generate rises in income for both migrants and citizens.
1.1.2.1.8. Con: State dependency is contingent on the fact that these immigrants contribute nothing to the economy, which is false.
1.1.2.1.8.1. Pro: Once migrants have the chance to work, they would pay taxes as everybody else. In this sense, nobody could claim that they don't deserve a UBI.
1.1.2.1.8.1.1. Con: It does not matter whether or not immigrants contribute to the economy, it matters whether they are perceived as contributing to the economy. Many people hold anti-immigrant sentiments because they perceive immigrants as economic parasites.
1.1.2.1.8.2. Pro: The majority of [immigrants](http://www.migrationpolicy.org/article/what-immigrants-say-about-life-united-states) have and value a 'strong work ethic', which often means they work long hours and many jobs. Even with a UBI, this work ethic is likely to continue.
1.1.2.1.9. Con: Most anti-immigrant sentiment comes from people who feel that their own economic security is threatened. If they did not have to worry about their own livelihood anymore, they would likely perceive other people living a decent life in their proximity as less of a threat.
1.1.2.2. Pro: In many countries being born in that country grants the child automatic citizenship. If that child then receives the UBI, it provides an incentive to immigrate illegally.
1.1.2.2.1. Con: UBI payments for children are typically set at approximately half of that of an adult. Even if a child receives a UBI payment, that is unlikely to be enough money to support the parents.
1.1.2.2.2. Con: Historically, Europe has granted [citizenship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jus_soli) by right of blood \(i.e. inherited by parents\) and America by birth, thus this is only applicable to North and South American countries as most countries allow citizenship by birth with restrictions, and several have increasingly changed policies due to immigration.
1.1.2.2.3. Con: This seems to be more an issue with how citizenship works.
1.1.2.3. Pro: Even if UBI payments are only available to citizens, this still provides an increased incentive to immigrate to countries with a UBI and in doing so start the pathway towards citizenship.
1.1.2.3.1. Con: This would be a bizarrely long gamble in most countries, where receiving citizenship post-illegal-immigration is exceptionally difficult and requires a substantial period of residence in the country if it is possible at all.
1.1.2.3.2. Con: These immigrants, if accepted, would provide valuable skills to our society.
1.1.2.4. Con: Eventually, UBI may be in place in every country, so there would not be such movement across borders due to economic migration.
1.1.2.4.1. Con: While UBI could work in the end game, getting there would be difficult as, at some point, only a few wealthy countries would have it, increasing the pressure on their immigration system to levels not seen in the modern era.
1.1.2.5. Con: Arguing this as a con presupposes that immigration automatically has a negative effect on society. Many people would disagree with this - for example, in the UK, migrants since 2000 have been found to contribute £1.34 in taxes for every £1 that migrants recieved in benefits, in addition to the labour they provide. Source: [fullfact.org](https://fullfact.org/immigration/do-eu-immigrants-contribute-134-every-1-they-receive/)
1.1.2.5.1. Con: That doesn't distinguish between legal and illegal immigrants. Furthermore, the situation in the UK, with wealthy Europe next door and no other near neighbours, is a "best case scenario" for immigration. The USA is next to poorer countries such as Mexico; Australia is next to poor Asian and Pacific states, etc.
1.1.2.5.1.1. Con: Poor immigrants do not necessarily have a negative effect on the society they migrate to. Before this is asserted, it needs to be backed up with data.
1.1.2.6. Con: When there is more economic stability among less wealthy people there will be less anger and anxiety directed towards immigrants and those with different cultures — the fear is often that those groups will take "their" houses and jobs. Reducing the cause of this antagonism will then reduce the stigma — and enhance the dignity — of immigrants.
1.1.2.6.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.1.
1.1.2.6.2. Con: On the contrary, people might just as well  become more resentful of the hand that feeds them, as they are completely dependant on it.
1.1.2.7. Con: Only citizens would receive a UBI, so illegal immigrants do not have an additional incentive to immigrate.
1.1.2.7.1. Con: With a UBI, low-wage jobs will become less attractive to most citizens, attracting unprotected [immigrants](http://inequality.org/argument-universal-basic-income/) to do the hard labor, as in [Dubai](http://www.news.com.au/travel/world-travel/middle-east/dubai-migrant-workers-the-hidden-slaves-behind-glamour-city/news-story/b3997ed5b013870424e84d78a561946c).
1.1.2.7.1.1. Con: Dubai is not a liberal [democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Dubai) nor does it have high levels of workers' [protections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights_in_Dubai#Foreign_workers_and_labour_rights). The fact that there is an increase in working immigrants does not necessarily mean they have to be unprotected or exploited.
1.1.2.7.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.3.
1.1.2.7.3. Con: Even illegal immigrants have children who would qualify and would dilute the available UBI resources.
1.1.2.7.3.1. Con: Non-citizen children would not qualify.
1.1.2.7.4. Con: Even if a UBI is only available to citizens, potential immigrants might have family members who are citizens and who could use their UBI payments to support them.
1.1.2.7.4.1. Con: Given most illegal immigration is from poorer to richer countries, it usually makes more sense for relatives to instead support their family by way of remittances to a poor country where their money has much higher purchasing power.
1.1.2.7.4.2. Con: Since a UBI would only be sufficient to meet basic needs, it is unlikely to be able to support additional people.
1.1.2.7.4.3. Con: It is unclear why anyone would view this as any different from other discretionary spending that people may put their UBI payments towards. These family members would be collecting no more UBI payments than anyone else.
1.1.3. Pro: For the years of [2008-2012](https://www.bjs.gov/index.cfm?ty=pbdetail&iid=5137) the US Bureau of Justice Statistics reported that persons in poor households at or below the Federal Poverty Level had more than double the rate of violent victimization as persons in high-income households.
1.1.3.1. Con: This shows a correlation, but not necessarily a causative link. It is also possible that more violent people are less likely to succeed in a civilized society, or potentially that less educated people – who would as such be less successful as well – might be more violent.
1.1.3.1.1. Con: Stress makes it harder for one to control their emotions, and one more liable to feel desperate and lash out. It seems likely that this is causatively related to violence, and that reducing stress via providing a UBI to fund basic necessities would thus reduce violence.
1.1.4. Con: This is not always the case. [Crime exists at all strata of income.](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2016/12/pyschology-white-collar-criminal/503408/)
1.1.4.1. Con: Yet this does not deny that a UBI would reduce the total rate of crimes.
1.1.5. Pro: Crime stems from a lack of economic opportunities. A UBI provides a means to ameliorate this by enabling people to pursue an education or start a business; or at least live on a stable income.
1.1.5.1. Pro: With their basic needs covered by a UBI, many people are able to afford to continue with their higher education.
1.1.5.1.1. Pro: When citizens can better afford to spend time studying, [overall levels of education grow](http://pas.sagepub.com/content/32/1/7.short) and the workforce becomes more skilled. This would increase the quality and pay of jobs in general.
1.1.5.1.1.1. Con: A UBI could also lead to decreasing levels of education, as universal financial security would lessen incentives for citizens to pursue higher education.
1.1.5.1.1.1.1. Pro: The prospect of securing a higher-paying job \(an incentive undermined by a UBI\) is an important factor for many people when considering [higher education](http://study.com/articles/Why_Should_I_Seek_a_Higher_Education.html).
1.1.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Many people study motivated by higher ideals or out of curiosity.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3. Con: A UBI only covers basic needs. If an individual wants to achieve a high-paying job, higher education is normally required.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1. Pro: Even if people merely end up changing jobs, that requires a degree of flexibility, a higher education and continuous training. A guaranteed income makes people more confident embracing these changes in the job market.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.1. Pro: People aren't able to magically change their skill set overnight, even if one job was made available before the old job was gone, that's useless to someone who can't yet fill the new role.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.2. Con: Companies are less likely to offer re-training programs when they think that workers can subsist on the UBI. Workers would have to actively seek re-training, which is a significantly higher barrier to entry to maintain a position in the workforce. In this sense, people would be more likely to exit the job force, resulting in decreased innovation as a society.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.2.1. Con: Workers should actively be putting in effort anyway, both to improve their current standing within their jobs and to keep whatever industry they're in moving forward.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.2.2. Con: In the status quo, without a UBI, employers don't offer re-training to employees out of a sense of charity; they do so because it allows them to continue to retain a valued asset. The introduction of a UBI doesn't change this calculus, it only makes the worker better of when this training isn't on offer.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.3. Con: Even if retraining were desirable, a direct solution to this problem would be to introduce a welfare benefit tied conditionally to taking up skills retraining courses. This would avoid the possibility of workers leaving the workforce altogether because welfare looks too comfortably attractive.
1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.3.1. Con: Introducing such conditions would only force people to register for training that they had no interest in. There are already many such programs that tie in conditions to receiving welfare, and all they do is create an incredibly burdensome bureaucracy.
1.1.5.1.1.1.4. Pro: A UBI would disqualify most people from Student Aid Finance, Grants, and scholarships presently based on low income level. Many would no longer feel obliged to educate themselves minimally to qualify for jobs that they don't want to do anyway and those desiring higher education would no longer qualify for government aid to do so. Feed a nominal UBI amount into your FAFSA and watch the bottom line Expected Family Contribution figure go up!
1.1.5.1.1.2. Con: If an entire population is highly educated then it is no longer a defining feature for any individual worker. As a result it would only increase the baseline level of skills required for all jobs; leading to people doing the same work for the same money regardless of the fact that they have pursued higher education.
1.1.5.1.1.2.1. Con: Even if everybody has access to higher education, different capabilities and interests will still result in workers with different skills.
1.1.5.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: In Scotland, where higher education is entirely [subsidised](https://beta.gov.scot/news/higher-education-student-support-for-scotland-2015-16/) by the government, and therefore everybody has access to it, not all [students](http://www.gov.scot/Publications/2003/03/16786/20354) decide to pursue a university degree.
1.1.5.1.1.2.2. Pro: [Credentialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Credentialism_and_educational_inflation) and degree creep have been identified as problems in a number of professions.
1.1.5.1.1.2.3. Con: Degree creep is already caused by requirements and [regulations](http://www.kevinmd.com/blog/2011/11/stop-degree-creep-health-care.html) \(e.g. asking nurses or other health professionals to have extra degrees to exercise their professions\). The additional capacity to access education would not increase degree creep significantly.
1.1.5.1.1.2.4. Con: If a population in general has a higher level of education, there is greater opportunity for the diversification of interests alongside intellectual exchanges between citizens. This encourages  theoretical advancements and the creation of new jobs at levels of experience not yet achieved.
1.1.5.1.1.3. Pro: Increased levels of education are good for the economy, as educated workers are more likely to innovate.
1.1.5.1.1.3.1. Con: A UBI would take away the drive to innovate.
1.1.5.1.1.3.1.1. Con: A UBI would provide the freedom to innovate for those who already have the drive to do so.
1.1.5.1.1.3.1.2. Con: Some people have a genuine drive to improve things for society/other people/other forms of life, which provides a drive to innovate irrespective of financial incentives.
1.1.5.1.1.4. Pro: A UBI garanteed to any citizen of a said country would allow students to focus way more on their studies than on the need of earning money, thus allowing them to optimize the intellectual benefit of their learning years, to become more educated individuals, and also more capable and productive workers once they are done with their studies, increasing in return their chance to contribute to a higher production of wealth which would ultimately benefit the public finances of the said country.
1.1.5.1.1.4.1. Pro: Studying and working at the same time requires a student to split their energy, effort, time, and headspace between their study and their work, likely making them less effective at both. Exhaustion and limited personal time from the combination also creates stress and makes studies less effective by increasing the difficulty of absorbing information.
1.1.5.1.1.5. Pro: An UBI offers an individual the possibility to improve their education extensively throughout their lives which is very hard at the moment when everyone needs to work all the time for basic needs. A society with more well educated people is a much more succesfull one.
1.1.5.1.1.6. Con: Growing education levels do not necessarily increase social mobility, especially for people belonging to traditionally [oppressed](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/education/education-news/free-university-tuition-in-scotland-has-not-benefited-the-poor-say-academics-a6757546.html) groups. For example, many university graduates with bachelor degrees are unable to find work in their field, and [end up stuck in minimum-wage or part time jobs](http://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2014-03-06/college-grads-taking-low-wage-jobs-displace-less-educated).
1.1.5.1.1.6.1. Con: This is more of a problem with the economy as a whole. It is still definitely [true](https://www.forbes.com/sites/jeffreydorfman/2017/01/23/dispelling-the-myth-of-underemployed-college-graduates/#43527cfb502c) that people with college degrees have an easier time finding high-paying jobs than those without.
1.1.5.1.1.6.2. Pro: This is especially pernicious given the amount of debt many students accumulate when undertaking higher education.
1.1.5.1.1.6.2.1. Pro: Sixty-eight percent of graduates from public and nonprofit colleges in the United States had an average student loan debt of [$30,100](http://ticas.org/posd/map-state-data) per borrower. This is difficult to pay back on a [minimum-wage](http://www.epi.org/publication/minimum-wage-workers-poverty-anymore-raising/) salary.
1.1.5.1.1.6.2.1.1. Con: [College graduates' average income](http://time.com/money/collection-post/3829776/heres-what-the-average-grad-makes-right-out-of-college/) is higher than the minimum-wage salary.
1.1.5.1.2. Pro: After the [Iranian government introduced an UBI](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income), identified that the lessened working hours of young adults in the program would likely lead them to enroll at schools and universities to focus on their education.
1.1.5.1.2.1. Con: [The study](http://erf.org.eg/wp-content/uploads/2017/05/1090.pdf) does not appear to conclude that the UBI increased education rates; at best, it speculates \(which anyone can do, including outside of a study\). In fact, in the conclusion, the authors write that "youth... can stay in school longer, or enjoy more leisure, though we do know which of these options they choose." \(p. 13\). Thus, we cannot use the study to conclude that the UBI improved educational enrollment, let alone by a significant amount.
1.1.5.1.2.2. Con: Even if this were an accurate characterization of the study, this may not be unique to a UBI; it is wholly plausible that if Iran was already under-providing welfare, introducing a targeted/conditional welfare program would have been more effective at increasing enrollment rates. In other words, the benefits may not have been unique to a UBI program specifically; traditional welfare might have been better for the situation.
1.1.5.1.3. Con: If higher education provides value to society, there are ample market incentives to furthering education. If, however, if this higher education is simply the self-indulgent study of topics without value to society, then it is not the role of the state to subsidize such unproductive activities.
1.1.5.1.3.1. Con: Education is nearly always of indirect if not direct benefit, as more knowledgeable people can provide or apply their knowledge in any situation where it becomes relevant \(consider someone with medical training acting as a first responder in a medical crisis despite not currently being "on-duty", or someone with specialist training pointing out a hazard which is not immediately apparent\).
1.1.5.1.3.2. Con: Education provides self-improvement and personal growth benefits from the outlay of effort and self-discipline and dedication, as well as the new information acquired allowing people to change or add to their worldview.
1.1.5.1.4. Con: But, with a UBI, there are fewer incentives to get a higher education because you'll make money no matter what.
1.1.5.1.4.1. Con: Incentives don't disappear because you have the money necessary to take care of your basic needs.
1.1.5.1.4.2. Con: Human beings are naturally curious; that is what led us to the point we are now. Having basics needs met won't change that — it is more like the opposite we will have more time to make questions.
1.1.5.1.5. Pro: People will be able to do longer studies, or retake studies at some point in their life, knowing they will have the resources to live during that time. The possibility of taking studies anytime can encourage some to acquire skills in fields with huge demand.
1.1.5.1.6. Pro: Early [YC UBI study participants](https://www.thebillfold.com/2017/09/y-combinator-is-expanding-its-basic-income-project/) are pursuing innovative projects, enrolling in career expanding education and proving the concept can work for a wider audience.
1.1.5.1.7. Con: If this is truly valuable and would be supported by a UBI, then targeted welfare could likely do this nearly as well if not better. Targeted welfare is, by design, targeted towards specific programs; UBIs may ultimately end up being used for other, less-valuable purposes.
1.1.5.1.7.1. Con: The Universal scope of a UBI allows it to support a greater range of endeavours without causing social backlash - it is the targeted nature of welfare that is precisely what prevents it from rewarding 'extraneous' activities such as education or art. When only the lower classes receive a benefit then it is framed as a handout and only designed to tide people over while they go back to traditional employment.
1.1.5.1.8. Pro: A UBI frees individuals to train and learn at any time in their life without fear of not being able to sustain themselves. People will no longer be locked into careers they found out too late that they do not like due to the inability to attain the high level of learning required to do something else
1.1.5.1.9. Pro: At the lowest end of the job market, workers often have to put in so many hours that they have little time for anything else, especially self improvement. Self improvement can only come from being able to study and or experience a greater variety of things which means either increased free time or jobs that are particularly well rounded.
1.1.5.2. Pro: A UBI makes it more likely that people will attempt to start their own companies, or become self-employed.
1.1.5.2.1. Pro: [Sixty-one percent](http://www.gallup.com/poll/15832/majority-americans-want-start-own-business.aspx) of employed Americans want to start their own business, preferring to work for themselves than for someone else. This becomes possible with a UBI.
1.1.5.2.1.1. Con: Start-up failure rate in the US after 4 years is between 42% and 63%. This shows that this is not a long-term sustainable pathway for most people. \([StatisticBrain](http://www.statisticbrain.com/startup-failure-by-industry/)\)
1.1.5.2.1.1.1. Con: [Twenty-nine percent](https://www.cbinsights.com/research-reports/The-20-Reasons-Startups-Fail.pdf) of start-ups fail because they run out of funding. A UBI can offer additional capital to help businesses succeed.
1.1.5.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Start-ups that fail for lack of funding usually need more than the small influx of cash that one individual's UBI payment would generate.
1.1.5.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: If a UBI can provide all citizens with a trickle of disposable income then it provides an environment that is suitable for crowdfunding like Kickstarter or micropayment subscription services.
1.1.5.2.1.1.1.2. Pro: If we focus on sustainability instead of profitability, then the UBI could itself start and sustain a small business.
1.1.5.2.1.2. Pro: We can help more people with a profitable and growing business than with a tax that has to pass through a bureaucracy to get to the people who need it.
1.1.5.2.1.3. Con: UBI is designed for welfare, not venture funding. Those who start a business are involved in serving the public which is a large responsibility. Those that businesses that do not serve the public must be allowed to fail quickly.
1.1.5.2.1.3.1. Con: Businesses that do not serve the public effectively will still fail or perform poorly in the presence of a UBI, as their more effective competitors will outperform them and substandard products and services will be recognized and rejected by consumers.
1.1.5.2.2. Con: Any UBI is likely barely more than poverty line; as such it is barely enough to live on let alone provide resources enough to start a business with.
1.1.5.2.2.1. Con: Removing the risk of abject poverty as a result of failure could very well be a motivating factor for quite a few lower-income people deciding whether or not to risk entrepreneurship.
1.1.5.2.2.2. Con: The largest barrier to starting a business is time. Most people cannot quit their job for long enough to get a business up and running, or the consequence of failure is to lose everything. You still require food, housing and transportation while starting a business. A UBI would allow most people to survive while also focusing on starting a business.
1.1.5.2.2.3. Con: Traditional welfare specifically prohibits doing productive work while receiving money. So while you have money to survive, you will lose it if you attempt to start a business. If you have enough to live on while staying at home surfing the internet, you could start almost any internet business for example.
1.1.5.2.2.4. Con: Some potential entrepreneurs cannot afford to leave their current employment to work on projects they hope will pan out to successful businesses.  A UBI would allow such entrepreneurs to take extended time off of paid to work to invest in their startup.
1.1.5.2.3. Pro: A UBI would enhance entrepreneurship, sustaining people while they develop projects that may take time to generate profits.
1.1.5.2.3.1. Pro: UBI pilot studies in Zambia and Mexico showed increased entrepreneurial activity. [oecd.org](http://www.oecd.org/officialdocuments/publicdisplaydocumentpdf/?cote=DCD/DAC/POVNET\(2008\)8&docLanguage=En) \(pg. 10 of the attached policy guidance note\).
1.1.5.2.3.2. Pro: Entrepreneurship and invention are in short supply in the population because there are opportunity costs associated with working a job. Jobs come with significantly more income and health and other benefits that most people can't forego. While one can create in their off-time, most people don't because the cultural norm is to focus on the paid job. The numbers of innovators would increase if the requirement to have a job in order to live were eliminated.
1.1.5.2.3.3. Pro: A UBI increases entrepreneurialism, new business ventures are more likely to be undertaken when there is some income security.
1.1.5.2.3.3.1. Pro: A UBI could free people up to create independent locally owned businesses, the real job creators in our economy. There's a solid conservative argument for this policy if they truly believe in entrepreneurship. \(I got this published in a letter to the New York Post, no less\)
1.1.5.2.3.4. Pro: Assured income to maintain a basic livelihood is the basis for most people to undertake their own enterprises. Thus it could \(and it would\) combat unemployment directly since there would be a big growth of small businesses. These businesses provide additional employment and income for people. Concerns about people not wanting to work are ungrounded, since people favor work over a life without meaning.[jamanetwork.com](https://jamanetwork.com/journals/jama/article-abstract/1105077,), [academic.oup.com](https://academic.oup.com/epirev/article/30/1/118/622529/The-Relation-between-Work-related-Psychosocial), [news.gallup.com](http://news.gallup.com/poll/171044/depression-rates-higher-among-long-term-unemployed.aspx), [hemelraadenkeijzer.nl](http://www.hemelraadenkeijzer.nl/opinie/zakelijk/ondernemer/risicos-in-kaart-bij-start-eigen-bedrijf), [theatlantic.com](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2017/09/sweden-startups/541413/)
1.1.5.2.3.5. Pro: UBI means that those looking to start new businesses or become self-employed can do so without the risk of losing the ability to pay for living costs. New business owners need not fear that if their business or project fails that they will be left without money to pay for rent or food, they will be protected with a UBI. This allows for trial and error with relative safety for budding entrepreneurs.
1.1.5.2.3.5.1. Pro: This allows business owners to bolster their profitability by not drawing a wage for themselves during the initial set up years when capital expenditure is high.
1.1.5.2.3.5.2. Pro: UBI would encourage more entrepreneurial activity in non-welfare states since you won't lose your healthcare if you quit your job \(you can pay for insurance with your UBI\).
1.1.5.2.4. Con: A UBI greatly increases the chances of societal [financial ruin](https://www.marketwatch.com/story/70-million-americans-teetering-on-edge-of-financial-ruin-2015-06-23) because the vast majority of people are financially irresponsible.
1.1.5.2.4.1. Pro: College students are one of many groups becoming [less responsible](https://www.usnews.com/news/blogs/data-mine/2015/04/02/college-students-becoming-less-financially-responsible-study-says) with their money.
1.1.5.2.4.1.1. Con: UBI is proposed for all citizens. College students, if they are becoming less responsible, require better education and less reliance on unaffordable credit; a problem UBI might help to solve.
1.1.5.2.4.2. Con: Financial irresponsibility may lead to financial ruin, but UBI does not lead to greater financial irresponsibility.
1.1.5.2.4.3. Con: The ability to manage finances is not dependant on receiving, or not receiving, UBI.
1.1.5.2.4.4. Con: Poverty itself is one of the greatest risks for financial irresponsibility. Not having enough money to make a balanced responsible budget, only leaves the option of an irresponsible budget. Thus a UBI might lead to more financial responsibility and less financial ruin.
1.1.5.2.4.5. Pro: There may be other studies but one example is lottery winners. [Lottery winners penniless](https://www.businessinsider.com/lottery-winners-lost-everything-2017-8#lara-and-roger-griffiths-bought-their-dream-home-and-then-life-fell-apart-1)
1.1.5.2.4.5.1. Con: A UBI is distinct from lottery winnings as it is paid in regular instalments.
1.1.5.2.4.5.1.1. Pro: The single, large sum paid out by a lottery \(and the improbability of the victory\) produces excitement and the perception of sudden wealth, which makes frivolous spending more likely.
1.1.5.2.4.5.1.2. Pro: If earlier instalments of a UBI are misspent and wasted, especially if severe consequences are the result, the individual will both be incentivized and have opportunity to learn better financial management skills to use later instalments.
1.1.5.2.4.6. Con: A UBI may help to teach and encourage greater financial responsibility and more sensible spending.
1.1.5.2.4.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.4.5.1.2.
1.1.5.2.4.6.2. Pro: People who, due to receiving a UBI, feel stable and secure in their financial future may be more inclined to invest their money.
1.1.5.2.5. Pro: A UBI would enable a lot of people to do what they really like, instead of what they currently do for a stable income. Doing what you really like for a few years helps you master some skills which, together with great quality coming from your enthusiasm around that "hobby", might result in an increase of small businesses.
1.1.5.2.5.1. Pro: Enjoying or finding passion in one's work increases motivation and hence, willingness to put time and effort in and effectiveness.
1.1.5.2.5.2. Pro: Given time away from mundane work, a person may have more time to invest in creative hobbies which in turn can be profitable such as sculpting or carpentry, for example.
1.1.5.2.5.3. Pro: There is also no guarantee that "what one really likes" is unproductive, and indeed it is hard to find jobs \(or other activities!\) which are completely unproductive.
1.1.5.2.5.3.1. Pro: Even someone who does not produce goods which are directly valued in monetary terms or \(deliberately\) participate in intellectual or artistic endeavours still contributes to society if they interact with other people - they can provide knowledge and information, opinions and perspectives, friendship, listening and support, participation in shared/group activities \(which tend to improve with more participants and/or require a minimum/specific number of participants in most cases\)...
1.1.5.2.5.4. Con: If UBI is used for people to follow their passions, then we won't have people who will process the banalities of life. Not enough people dream of becoming a garbage collector.
1.1.5.2.5.4.1. Con: If this is true wages for banal occupations will need to rise or conditions for the workers will need to improve.  This may mean an increase in the costs of goods and services, but it will mean a decrease in people spending time in unpleasant jobs.
1.1.5.2.5.4.2. Con: Perhaps the creative energy of people freed from wage labor will be able to address some of the concerns caused by a lack of wage labor.
1.1.5.2.5.4.3. Con: UBI is intended to be sufficient to live on but not provide a luxurious life. There are likely to still be people willing to perform banal tasks in exchange for affording luxuries like international holidays.
1.1.5.2.5.4.4. Con: A pickier, reduced force will provide incentive for automation within these areas. Driverless garbage trucks for example.
1.1.5.2.5.4.5. Pro: When Spain stole Mexico’s gold [everyone there became so rich that no one would do any work](https://www.reddit.com/r/AskHistorians/comments/2d2z3e/how_did_the_gold_and_silver_spain_brought_from/). If the garbage collector has thousands of dollars in his pocket, why work? If they do not work, the garbage does not go away and life really sucks.
1.1.5.2.5.4.5.1. Pro: From the link: "essentially, Spaniards began to buy goods with this imported treasure instead of making goods themselves. As others have pointed out, this led to a fairly predictable result: prices rose \(inflation\), and Spain began to import far more than it exported." Thus it was having fewer workers, and more importing of goods that ultimately caused economic failure.
1.1.5.2.5.4.5.2. Con: UBI does not necessarily mean an increase of actual money, it's more like a re-distrubution of existing wealth. Also there is no evidence that UBI would greatly reduce the workforce. Therefore it is a false equivalence to compare UBI to the influx of new gold in medieval Spain.
1.1.5.2.5.4.6. Con: If immigration policy allows it, cheaper foreign labour would fill the low-skilled job gaps, leaving native workers to progress to higher skilled jobs.
1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1. Con: Universal Basic Income [creates fiscal incentives for a country to reduce immigration](https://www.pbs.org/newshour/economy/making-sense/how-a-basic-income-in-the-u-s-could-increase-global-poverty).
1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.1.
1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1.2. Pro: A UBI requires wealthy nations to maintain strict immigration policies to restrict access to citizenship from people from less wealthy nations. It would continue to incentivize the upward distribution of wealth \(specifically resources that provide wealth\) from poor nations to wealthy nations.
1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1.3. Pro: If every citizen is entitled to a UBI, that creates political incentives to discourage immigration. The biggest economic inequalities [are been rich and poor countries](http://timharford.com/2014/11/a-passport-to-privilege/), not within rich countries, and anything that could create big barriers to migration from poor to rich countries is counterproductive to global economic justice.
1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1.4. Pro: UBI increases the difference in standard of living between countries, thus creating unsustainable migration pressure \(that may lead to overpopulation or very strict border control\).
1.1.5.2.5.5. Pro: It would allow people to do their hobbies and travel, discover the world and not do undesired job just to make for living. And if a person would like to get more money, they can find work they like.
1.1.5.2.5.6. Pro: People would be able to be in a more expansive way of living, and to manage all the things that no one dreamt of doing: We could use a schedule of volunteering where we would help each other and the community we live in to keep going. As people would be more happier with their lives, it would be easier to manage the things that by now we average see as "problems that aren't ours" as managing garbage and keeping the city clean etc. We would be aware of our compromises with our community.
1.1.5.2.6. Pro: A UBI provides an instant safety net and thus reduces the need for overly-expensive, or exploitative credit.
1.1.5.2.6.1. Pro: Banks are more likely to grant lines of credit, as applicants have a UBI to fall back on as loan security.
1.1.5.2.6.1.1. Con: Banks want a good asset-to-debt ratio when assessing loans, and will likely calculate UBI payments as a negative factor in the same way they already count Social Security Income \(in the US\) as a negative.
1.1.5.2.6.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.1.1.1.
1.1.5.2.6.3. Con: If the UBI is utilized to generate a line of credit then it is not being used to cover essentials such as food, clothing, and shelter.
1.1.5.2.7. Pro: A UBI allows people to imagine and research innovative technology and to start businesses - the engines of economic growth.
1.1.5.2.7.1. Con: UBI does not hold people accountable for misguided, delusional, or lazy attempts at innovation and research.
1.1.5.2.7.1.1. Con: Failure is an important part of innovation.  You rule out what doesn't work and are left with what does.  Permitting more people to fail at different pursuits will be a boon to innovation.
1.1.5.2.7.1.2. Con: It is not within the scope of a UBI to hold people accountable for innovation and research. This is the responsibility of other legislation.
1.1.5.2.7.1.3. Con: The lack of UBI does not actively discourage misguided, delusional or lazy attempts at innovation and research.
1.1.5.2.8. Con: Starting business is itself a risky financial proposition; something that a UBI shouldn't be encouraging for people who are on low incomes.
1.1.5.2.8.1. Con: UBI does not encourage low income individuals to start businesses directly. It simply provides more start-up capital to those who may already want to but do not have enough funds.
1.1.5.2.8.1.1. Con: A UBI might have laws that prevent it from being able used as such, thus it might not provide any start-up capital at all.
1.1.5.2.8.1.1.1. Con: A UBI is specifically designed \(and named\) to be an income that is both universal and unconditional. Any conditions on how it's spent would make it conditional, and thus also not a normal income either.
1.1.5.2.8.2. Con: A universal basic income is given to everyone, across the spectrum of earned income. Therefore, middle class people can also use their UBI to start a business.
1.1.5.2.8.3. Pro: More capital means more \(and greater\) acceptable risks can be taken by a new business owner. More risks being taken will eventually lead to more businesses entering the marketplace. This might not be a net benefit for UBI pensioners, but for the marketplace it absolutely would be.
1.1.5.2.8.4. Con: UBI provides a safety net, allowing people to try something without the fear of failure - encouraging innovation and amazing things.
1.1.5.2.8.5. Con: There is many low risk, low capital business options that can raise someone out of poverty that could be explored with a UBI in place. For example, if someone wants to become a gardener, cleaner, software developer, translator. There is a development period where the small business is only looking for customers but has no money in place. UBI would bridge that gap where as many now don't earn enough to save enough or borrow enough to bridge the 6 to 12 months to build a client list and earn more
1.1.5.2.9. Con: A UBI would also likely lead to increase income or business taxes that make it harder to start businesses or maintain employees.
1.1.5.2.9.1. Con: A UBI is agnostic to how it is funded. Increasing taxes on businesses is a choice separate from UBI.
1.1.5.2.9.2. Con: Does increasing income taxes really affect employment?
1.1.5.2.9.3. Con: With a UBI in play, businesses which provide a humanistic service would be able to claim charity exemptions \(and/or obtain volunteer work\), while businesses which do not would have to adapt to the market.
1.1.5.2.10. Con: A UBI would mean people are less likely to pursue profitable business ventures in favor of business ventures that they personally enjoy, which leads to less of society's needs \(e.g. plumbing, massage therapy, elderly care\) being met in favour of more people pursuing unsustainable businesses like party planning, book writing, etc.
1.1.5.2.10.1. Con: But if the number of people pursuing business ventures goes up and the profitable ventures are still available, there will be more people motivated to start a business who also personally enjoy making money. Supply and demand will also only make these ventures more profitable if their demand is left unmet over time.
1.1.5.2.10.2. Con: The fact that people are not forced to do unwelcome and poorly-paid jobs in order to survive does not mean that people will not do those jobs if there is a comensurate reward. If society has need of goods or services that are underprovided those goods or services would become more valuable and so able to demand a greater reward, providing a greater incentive for people to provide them. Inequality is driven by people being forced to accept unfair deals rather than be destitute in a buyers market.
1.1.5.2.10.2.1. Con: Although higher wages would still be incentive, people would still have the ability to fall back on the UBI if they run into financial trouble, which would decentives people from choosing safe jobs. This could lead to both a lot of high risk reward jobs such as philanthropy and low paying but entertaining jobs such as in the art field.
1.1.5.2.11. Con: A UBI does not provide the motivation to start businesses. It will be redirected to ease a person's suffering which are usually small vices like tobacco, alcohol, entertainment, food, etc. Starting a business or self employment requires hard work which requires motivation by a goal and paradoxically, less available money in the hopes that there will be even greater rewards in venture
1.1.5.2.11.1. Pro: A UBI erodes the personal and societal incentives for financial responsibility, self-improvement, and hard work.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1. Con: This claim has been [proven false by many studies.](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aIL_Y9g7Tg0)
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1. Con: None of these studies were carried out in Westernized countries. Cheap entertainment such as streaming services like Netflix could have a serious drain on the sort of "future-ward thinking" that is touted in this talk. Working pathetically low hours at a part-time job can pay for Netflix: what does that give society, except further enrichment of large corporations?
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.3.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.2. Con: The work they do at that part-time job would presumably produce something, otherwise paying them would be economically wasteful and thus disadvantageous to the business which does so. Beyond that, if they can afford their entertainment, they have either contributed enough to earn it, or are living frugally in other areas - thus reducing their burden to society there.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.3. Con: It is implausible that even with a UBI, someone could spend the majority of their time consuming. Either they would have to consume cheap/free goods and services, and/or make their consumption last - thereby not taking much from society, even if they don't give much in return either - or they would have to work for additional income in order to fund their consumption.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.3.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.4. Con: Not everybody is interested in spending their days on idle entertainment, which would in many cases get rapidly boring, especially where more meaningful options are available.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.5. Con: There have been UBI [pilots and studies all over the world](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_income_pilots?wprov=sfla1).
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.6. Con: "None of these studies were carried out in Western countries" is entirely untrue. This subject topic needs to be dramatically edited in order to be valid. Ref: [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_income_pilots#Europe) and [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_income_pilots#North_America)
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2. Pro: Human ingenuity has been existing long before financial incentives came into place.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.1. Con: The needs these financial incentives represent still existed, and did so long before human ingenuity came about. Human ingenuity evolved to help meet those needs: “necessity is the mother of invention” as the saying goes. Money is the grease in the wheels of economy; not the origin of it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Healthy competition in the global market paired with intrinsic human curiosity could provide enough incentive to innovate.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.2. Pro: Many inventions where not made to achieve financial gain but to statisfy the exploratory spirit of mankind.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.2.1. Con: Historically, there was no time for anything but necessities. Only with the advent of civilization were resources plentiful enough to provide time for intellectual exploration. And even then, it was only a very few special people that advanced human knowledge based on their "exploratory spirit". Most of us are simply incapable of such and, of those that can, many are uninterested in devoting their lives to the task without compensation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.2.1.1. Con: "A very few special people advanced human knowledge" because of "historically" the lack of education and wealth disparity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.2.2.1.2. Con: If people are uninterested in devoting themselves to a task without compensation, it's because they have been growing in this state of mind.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.3. Con: The speaker states that a UBI is utopian, but that utopias do happen, pointing out the end of slavery, equality for women & men, etc. However those circumstances are not utopias - UBI is pure utopia.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.3.1. Con: UBI is not utopia; it is a realisable goal and a necessity due to technological change within society. Surely the greater good, the avoidance of mass poverty, is a goal that ought to be pursued. Mass poverty will see standards of living, and consumption drop, which will lead to weaker economies.
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.4. Pro: Brazil, which is a country that shares a lot of the cultural characteristics common to Western countries, has a program called "Bolsa Família" \(Family Allowance\), which is basically UBI but only to poor people. Although it is not exactly UBI, there were enough studies in Brazil to prove that the described social program did not make, with some exceptions, people lazy. In a lot of cases, the exact opposite was observed: people worked harder to pursue their dreams. [scielo.br](http://www.scielo.br/pdf/ecos/v19n3/08.pdf)
1.1.5.2.11.1.1.4.1. Con: There are many countries which provide help to less-wealthy or unemployed people. Perhaps Brazil is not the best example to follow for Western countries: we're talking about a country whose wealth is [almost entirely being held by a fraction of the population](https://www.nationsencyclopedia.com/economies/Americas/Brazil-POVERTY-AND-WEALTH.html) and the less wealthy have to manage to make it out there. In that sense, a basic allowance does make sense for Brazil. It's not the case in European Western countries \(e.g. [France](https://www.nationsencyclopedia.com/economies/Europe/France-POVERTY-AND-WEALTH.html)\) where this gap - although it does exist - is much smaller.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2. Pro: A UBI grows people's sense of entitlement and makes it harder for them to see the value of hard work.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.1. Pro: Research shows that people derive a great deal of happiness from work. In particular, for men, identity is embedded in work - consider names like Fisher, Farmer, Taylor, Cooper, and Smith. If a UBI scheme leads to increased unemployment, it will create an unhappy underclass of citizens with few incentives to become employable.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.1.1. Con: UBI will not be seen as an opportunity to stop working, but as an opportunity to do better work. More people will have more time and resources to complete projects they are passionate about and that are important to the communities in which they live.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.1.2. Con: Voluntary work is already a thing, and with the increased freedom of time and to purchase resources provided by a UBI, it is quite likely to increase in prevalence.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.1.3. Pro: It would create a permanent underclass of people who opt for disengagement from society for psychological or pseudo political reasons. This would be inherently divisive and lead to conflict between the underclass and everyone else.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.1.3.1. Con: People like this already exist. Giving them a basic income may give them enough comfort and reason to go out and take part rather than remain a recluse.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.2. Con: This claim and the basis for this claim are only true if a UBI policy would increase unemployment, however providing security could just as easily increase a persons drive to seek creative, innovative, and hardworking employment, because if you have economic security you will likely seek and be more willing to take economic risk, like investment, startup and employment and etc.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.3. Con: As sentient beings, it can be argued that human beings do have certain entitlements they should assert and fight for.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.4. Con: This is merely conjecture, with no evidence to support it. Until enough longitudinal studies have been conducted, you cannot assert that the UBI does anything. The recent Finnish study will end at the end of 2018.[Finnish study](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/finland-first-results-basic-income-pilot-not-exactly/)
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5. Con: Not everybody considers hard work as a virtuous value, to some it is a sad necessity to be minimized.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1. Pro: The puritan work ethic of modern capitalism propagates a harmful ideology that equates ones self-worth and self-actualisation through productivity and achievement.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.1. Pro: John Stuart Mills was an early advocate against the 'gospel of work'; that work should be considered just only if instrumental to some greater object; [not as an end unto itself.](http://abolishwork.com/2014/01/15/some-anti-work-comments-from-john-stuart-mill/)
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.1.1. Pro: If we had UBI we would be a lot more productive and creative as we would not have to worry about where our next meal was coming from nor would we be tied up in menial, soul-destroying labour simply to make others richer.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.2. Con: One's worth to society is shown through how they contribute to the society. It's not harmful, but rather fair, because the more you contribute to society, the more you get out of society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.2.1. Con: What is here said, and what is perceived are very rarely the same thing, even if it is not the intent to communicate these harmful ideas, the harm still occurs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.2.2. Con: There are innumerable contributions made to society that are not recognized monetarily, from raising children to the passive and understated, yet undeniable and subtly powerful information transfer in every conversation, to the increase in morale \(including workers' morale!\) provided by free entertainments and collaborative \(or competitive\) leisure activities, to free mental and physical health care provided by friends and family.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.2.2.1. Con: In a super crowded world, having children is not a valuable contribution, it just creates another person to consume our limited resources. It shouldn't be endorsed.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.2.3. Con: By that same logic we ought not care for the elderly and those unable to work, or tie down children with a social debt they have to pay off upon reaching adult hood.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.2. Pro: The richest value [working smarter](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/anthony-w-orlando/the-rich-arent-rich-becau_b_4791626.html), not harder. They leverage their time and skills in order to provide the highest output with less time, resulting in more time for enjoyable activities, or work if they choose.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.6. Con: The UBI may indeed grow the sense of entitlement, but this sense is not necessarily false. There are many things that certain people get for free while others have to pay for with their hard labor. The earth and its resources should in principle be shared equally between all inhabitants; our civilization is a product of generations and its fruits should not be in hands of few. This realization has nothing to do with how we approach hard work: some value progress while others do not.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.7. Pro: A UBI seems like a typical "western medicine" not focusing on the source of the problem. If we put into equation our future technological advancement and the fact that UBI might grow to a decent salary. The average Joe will not take this as a life opportunity to do something meaningful for himself and/or help his community; instead becoming even more passive and indulging in lazy behavior such as video games, porn, and fast food.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.7.1. Con: Our future technological advancement should also be considered in future opportunity, the opportunities may not be the same as they have been. So to say that the average joe will not take this as an opportunity is not a good argument since we simply can't say what people would do given the kind of freedom this would give.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.8. Pro: For some of us part of our sense of self worth depends on actually contributing something to society. We would not want to feel like another government owned drone.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.8.1. Con: This suggests nothing outside of paid labor has a positive effect on society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.8.2. Con: These are the kind of people who would benefit from a UBI. You would be able to pursue whatever meaningful work you wanted to, but also receive the UBI on top of those wages.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.8.2.1. Pro: Jobs that make one feel like they lack agency could be avoided in favour of jobs one actually wants to work when a UBI is available.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.9. Pro: People will be incentivized to not work. Why will you work 180 hours a month for $2,000 when you can get $1500 for 0 hours?
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.9.1. Con: Because with $3500 \(your salary + UBI\) you buy a lot more than only with the UBI. On the other hand, simple welfare has the problem you point: only the ones not working are eligible for help, therefore sometimes it looks more appealing to just get government aid instead of working
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10. Pro: A UBI will cause different professions of clearly different value to suddenly have the same value. For instance, a waiter and a cook require very different skill sets and time devotion, and therefore they get paid differently. However, with a UBI, the value of that work done by the cook is thereby deemed equal in value to that of a waiter, which is not at all the case.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.1. Pro: A UBI will incentivize people with higher paying jobs, like that of a management job, to greatly reduce their quality of work. If a manager gets the same UBI payment as their employee, but the manager does a lot more work, the manager will not have any incentive to do a good job because, no matter what, they will always be paid the same.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.1.1. Con: People in higher paying positions likely won't want promotions less just because people who aren't in their positions are given enough to get by. Its not in that type of persons nature to do so, their goal is to make more.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.1.2. Con: Managers are motivated by a high salary, irrespective of whether they receive a UBI; compared to a manager's salary, a UBI is a very small amount.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.2. Con: There are different skill sets among many minimum wage jobs, also UBI doesn't effect differences in wages.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.3. Con: A UBI is also given to doctors and CEOs. If this effect exists it would surely raise the average apparent value of low income professions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.10.4. Con: UBIs will not replace wages paid for work; they will be additional money paid to every citizen, on top of what they earn from their job.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.11. Con: A UBI creates a new baseline that ensures everybody has their basic needs met. Hard work, competition and ambition can still proceed from that new baseline.
1.1.5.2.11.1.2.12. Pro: In Spain, state pensioners are protesting that their pensions are too meagre and pressure the government to guarantee inflation-indexed updates, \([Euronews](https://www.euronews.com/2018/03/17/thousands-of-spanish-pensioner-s-protest-over-pensions)\) even though state pensions are among the highest in Europe, typically exceed contributions made by the pensioner while working, and crucially, demography shows that such a guarantee will be unsustainable in the long run \([El Pais](https://elpais.com/elpais/2018/01/29/inenglish/1517221833_150575.html).\) This suggests that people receiving a UBI may feel a similar sense of entitlement and disengagement from society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3. Pro: UBI's don't address the right problem, it's not people not having enough money. The problem is deeper than that. Human beings are beasts of burden, if people aren't provided a place where they can accept social responsibility they degenerate, especially conscientious types.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.1. Con: There is an entire [Ted Talk](https://www.ted.com/talks/rutger_bregman_poverty_isn_t_a_lack_of_character_it_s_a_lack_of_cash/reading-list) and associated reading list disproving this exact claim. A lack of cash is actually specifically a cause of stress that forces poor decision making, and international studies suggest that a UBI will create a happier, more satisfied population.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.2. Pro: The core problem is money creation, and the fact that each is not enfranchised in the process. Money is loaned into existence, and interest paid to maintain the value. That interest is not directly distributed to each, even though each is compelled to accept the money in trade for goods and services. This largest stream of money, the interest paid on global sovereign debt, represents the financial security provided by states, and should be equally shared with each
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.3. Pro: A UBI would hurt those individuals who are suffering from depression and mental illness by giving them the easy out to leave the workforce which is usually a help to their coping skills, self esteem and social well being.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.3.1. Con: Mental illness would be greatly helped by a UBI, allowing an unwell individual to focus on self-improvement instead of trying to just maintain survival.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.3.2. Con: Depression and mental illness can be caused by seemingly \(or actually\) hopeless situations - or situations where every outcome is a poor or undesirable one. Such situations would be made less likely by a UBI, as the money could always be used to ensure one's survival and a certain level of quality of life, or avoid/rectify specific issues or situations.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.4. Pro: Work is one of the most normal ways for a human to find meaning. With working being the cause for the consequence that is feeding your family/providing and education and shelter, there is a deep sense of meaning and responsibility in doing so. Once taken away, a void of meaninglessness is created, and we don't know how to fill it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.4.1. Con: A UBI would allow people to work towards additional purchases or benefits for their family \(e.g. working a paid job to provide luxuries for their family\), directly work towards things of value for their family \(e.g. hand-crafting stuff for them or doing domestic labour\), or contribute to the broader community rather than being "trapped" thinking at the "local" level of maintaining their own family.
1.1.5.2.11.1.3.5. Con: The parts of western society that have a growing amount of austerity and lack of income are the exact people that degenerate for an inability to provide social responsibilities and connect with local communities. Money for the average jo is limiting not just on an economic level, but proven to be a burden on a psychological level too; the UBI devalues that money in order to help the individual move past it and contribute something more than just consumption.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4. Pro: A taxpayer-funded UBI acts as a state-sponsored "Robin Hood" system, without actually motivating people to be productive in order to provide their own incomes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1. Pro: Money is worth a certain amount due to the amount of effort required to make it; it is a measure of the worth of a person's time, resources, and effort toward making something of value to others. To take this money from a person and give it to someone who hasn't taken the time or effort to earn it disincentivizes productive work.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1. Con: This argument can be equally applied to all forms of taxes. Yet taxes exist in nearly every society because their collective value overrides the individual harms.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1. Con: The question is, however, whether the problem lies in the argument, or in the assumptions made on the necessity of those taxes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1. Con: Few people would argue for the removal of the military, the police, public road, or the sewer, and most of those that act upon this belief quickly die as a result of their lack of these facilities. Maintaining those takes money, and that money has to come from somewhere.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Many services have historically been provided through collective action. These kind of systems could return or be rejuvenated.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: There have been historical cases of polycentric legal systems, where even the writing and administering of law was in essence [privatized](http://www.tomwbell.com/writings/JurisPoly.html).
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: These laws always existed in addition to, not instead of, actual law.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Commercial examples are effectively contracts, and as such require consideration not only for a person to be entered into the contract, but for the existing members of the contract to grant outsiders the protections of this contract, if these are not granted, abuse will happen.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.3. Con: Mutual respect doesn't keep criminals from robbing one's house, murdering you, or just about anything else, it has no enforcement mechanism for failure cases, and relying solely on it would be a disaster.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.1.3.1. Con: There are many thieves, but few murderers. Clearly there is at least some aspect of mutual respect common to humanity that means we rarely cause each other grievous harm.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: The warlords of Japan and Europe might have repelled foreign invaders, but they definitely killed, and took advantage of those they 'protected'.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con: If warlords did not draw some profit from those they protect, they could not afford to offer that protection.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.3. Con: There has also been someone that [tried to privitize public parking](https://www.npr.org/templates/transcript/transcript.php?storyId=412240643). Just because a thing can be privatized does not at all mean it is best for the public.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4. Pro: Roads have often been built or maintained by collective action, often in the form of toll roads.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4.1. Con: Toll roads are often disliked by the public.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: Observation shows that putting a toll on a road reduces usage. See, for example: [Lindsey R. "Congestion Relief: Assessing the Case for Road Tolls in Canada." C.D. Howe Institute](https://cdhowe.org/sites/default/files/attachments/research_papers/mixed//commentary_248.pdf)
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4.1.2. Pro: Protests against tolls are not uncommon. For example, in the first month of 2018, the following protests were reported: [Lekki Toll Hike Area Boys smash phones and cameras as Lagosians protest new fares](http://www.pulse.ng/news/local/area-boys-disrupt-lekki-toll-protests-in-lagos-id7959535.html), [Gurugram residents take to streets to protest against toll plaza](http://www.timesnownews.com/mirror-now/civic-issues/article/gurugram-residents-take-to-streets-to-protest-against-toll-plaza/190828), and[Bayside Group To Protest Gov. Cuomo's Congestion Pricing Plan](https://patch.com/new-york/bayside/bayside-group-protest-gov-cuomos-congestion-pricing-plan).
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4.1.3. Pro: Political parties often try to win votes by promising to reduce \(or subsidise\) tolls. For example, in order to win support in Western Sydney seats, the NSW Labor Party, in Australia, committed the State to providing a cashback on tolls that has, so far, cost over $1.5 billion AUD.[Taxpayer bill for cashback scheme on Sydney toll roads hits $1.5 billion - and climbing](http://www.smh.com.au/nsw/taxpayer-bill-for-cashback-scheme-on-sydney-toll-roads-hits-15-billion--and-climbing-20161207-gt6hiq.html)
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.4.1.4. Con: When costs are concentrated and benefits are spread thin and wide, those carrying the cost are usually louder than those who enjoy the benefit, which distorts perceptions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.5. Pro: Power companies - like Edison's electricity company - have often been private, even when building substantial public infrastructure.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.5.1. Con: Power companies such as Edison were dangerous monopolies that ignored public safety to the point of giving [the Dodgers their name](https://gizmodo.com/the-la-dodgers-got-their-name-from-brooklyns-deadly-str-1687077696), and [suffocating innovation](http://jawadonweb.com/?page_id=900), all while using public resources.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.1.1.5.2. Con: The claim that "power companies have often been private" \(true though it is\) does not support its parent claim \("many service has been provided through collective action"\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.1.2. Con: Yes but your con is being applied to the argument that taxes are unjust in a general sense, and does not take into account the context of the tax \(e.g., to pay for UBI\). Your claim is infact a pro to the above claim, not a con.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.2. Pro: It could be equally claimed to be illegitimate to take income from A to help pay for B's security; for the courts used to enforce their rights; or for the use of other public goods that are available to all but are used disproportionately by some.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.2. Con: However, the amount of actual work that is required to make money actually reduces in proportion to the amount of money you already have, as you have a greater ability to loan, invest, and take financial risks.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.2.1. Pro: You can make money without working by loaning out existing money. Active investment does take effort, but even that effort can be bought for others for less than the proceeds from investing the capital.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.3. Con: This argument assumes that 100% of the production comes from human activities. This has already changed with massive automation and will change further with more and more AIs in charge of production. In that case, the "net payers" would not be humans, but automated systems and therefore there would be no loss of freedom for human actors.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.4. Con: What capitalism rewards often has no relation to its actual value to society. CEOs are paid hundreds of times more than the average worker, but they do not provide hundreds of times the value than the average worker does.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.5. Con: What constitutes "productive work"? Not all forms of work exist in the market. For example, child rearing and other forms of domestic work performed by family members goes uncompensated, yet is vital to the function of society. The productivity of work is not dictated merely by the "free" market.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.6. Con: Money is not the only resource of value to people and society;and, whilst some value it as the only resource, some require a UBI to pursuit the creation of resources. Ideas, innovation and creation may be born of adversities, but those with nothing cannot be expected to contribute let alone create until we give them the basic means to do so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.7. Con: A homeless individual without a roof or a bed has not the energy to work, let alone be productive, from lack of sleep; same also for those unable to afford food. Exhaustion and starvation don't just disincentivize work, they make work almost impossible to undertake. The exhausted and starving will be more productive with food in their bellies and a good nights sleep thanks to a UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.8. Con: The parent claim describes the Labor Theory of Value. It holds that the value of a product corresponds to the amount of effort that went into creating it. By contrast, the Subjective Theory of Value holds that the value of a product is determined by the importance that each individual market actor places upon it and is willing to bid. Following the Labor Theory, a hole that someone has dug and filled back in 100 times would be highly productive even though it offers  no value to society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.8.1. Con: The amount of labour that goes into something is not completely irrelevant to the market value of something - whilst it is not the only factor, there can be recognition of the amount of labour that went into something, and the fact that the market has both sellers and buyers; there may, of course, be no market for something at the price that the seller wants, but equally, there may be no seller who is willling to sell at the rate that the buyer\(s\) are offering.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.2. Con: [This study of an experiment with UBI in India](http://www.developmentpathways.co.uk/resources/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/Indias-Basic-Income-Experiment-PP21.pdf) points otherwise. Most critically, "production" can be measured in the short term and the long term, and the UBI moves the recipient's lens more towards the latter.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.3. Con: Contrary to the assumption of this claim, redistribution of wealth \(here phrased as "a state-sponsored 'Robin Hood' system"\) is good because it allows for a more egalitarian society, therefore [reducing numerous social problems](https://www.equalitytrust.org.uk/spirit-level).
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.3.1. Con: The study used as basis doesn't address the downsides of egalitarianism such as the decrease in productivity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4. Con: Forcing people to work under threat of starvation, homelessness, and/or death is fundamentally barbaric.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.1. Pro: Once the state claims the rights over lands, water and any useful resource, and prevent people to use those to survive, the minimum that the state could do is to provide the basic needs to it's population.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.1.1. Con: The basic needs of people differ on an individual basis. For example some people have need for medical treatment that is not needed or deemed as a basic need by most.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.1.1.1. Con: This does not mean that the state should not provide things which are basic needs for all. Specific programs can also be put into place to address specific needs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.1.2. Con: The state does not claim rights over land, water or any useful resource. Individuals buy these resources and trade it among themselves, if the government truly owned these resources like they do in communist countries, than yes it would be the responsibility of the state to distribute these resources to their citizens
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.1.2.1. Con: In most countries, government legislation permits and manages the claiming, purchase, and sale of such resources.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.2. Con: Starvation, homelessness, and death all happen in nature if one does not work towards obtaining resources for their own survival. Therefore not providing resources for free isn't barbaric.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.2.1. Con: Nature is barbaric - the point of a society is to do better than that.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.3. Con: If people do not work for the food or land they are consuming, than they are actively consuming the goods produced by someone else without compensating that person. The state is not forcing the individual to work to eat food the state creates, the state is protecting the individuals right to sell the fruit of their labor to their neighbor and receive compensation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.4. Con: Forcing someone to work for food and then giving half of what they worked for to someone who does nothing is barbaric. Requiring that people function as adults and do their part to earn their own food is called participating in society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.5. Con: People are motivated to be productive by more than just money. After meeting basic needs, almost everybody seeks a sense of purpose, and of social value. Money alone is a poor way to achieve these.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.5.1. Con: There has never been a time in human history where all basic needs have been met without having additional responsibility. Purpose has always arisen out of responsibility. Even before money was invented, purpose and societal value was found in cultivation and trade.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.6. Con: Poverty is not an incentive to work and be productive, it is a barrier to it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.7. Pro: Anti-meritocratic systems are by definition unfair. Merit has benefits and therefore should be rewarded.
