1.1.5.2.11.1.4.7.1. Con: Merit and wealth are two separate things. A UBI doesn't necessarily eliminate merit itself, it simply stops wealth as being the primary measure of merit. Contribution to others could just as easily be used.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.7.1.1. Con: Wealth is purported to reflect the exchange of earnings based on competency and therefore merit. The two concepts are closely associated.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.8. Con: A UBI would not necessarily have to be funded by taxes. It could be funded by a sovereign wealth fund that is sustained with the proceeds of the use \(or abuse\) of our natural resources, eg mineral leases, public land forestry, fines on polluters. This money is our birthright, literally the “commonwealth” the founders envisioned.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.9. Pro: Humans are too complex to suggest that a UBI, untethered from providing value to society, would be beneficial to all. Some would benefit and find their own way to/continue to contribute and some would quickly devolve into hedonism and/or nihilism. This will include the lazy and unmotivated and also those whose upbringing and/or culture did not instill altruism or accomplishment as important values. It will create a self-perpetuating underclass.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.10. Pro: A UBI will promote the false idea that there is such thing as a free lunch because people will constantly receive something for doing nothing. The renowned economist Milton Friedman wrote a book about how there is no such thing as a free lunch-[amazon.com](https://www.amazon.com/Theres-Such-Thing-Free-Lunch/dp/087548297X). A UBI appears to be ‘free’ but it will in reality be very costly, to one group or another.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.11. Pro: UBI will produce freeloaders who do nothing and contribute little to society. Without the incentive of having to making money, the freeloaders will never try to improve life for themselves or for others around them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.4.12. Pro: A UBI promotes stagnation and irresponsibility among the poor. After welfare reform in the 90's limiting who could receive welfare, and making it more of a temporary thing poverty rates and unemployment rates fell. [Poverty & Unemployment Data](https://fee.org/articles/undeniable-welfare-reform-increased-work-and-reduced-poverty/)
1.1.5.2.11.1.5. Pro: When a basic standard of living is available to all some people will not contribute to its upkeep. This selfishness creates [resentment in those who do contribute](https://books.google.com.au/books?id=QORW1i6XDKgC&pg=PA192&lpg=PA192&dq=welfare+resentment+surveys&source=bl&ots=CfzLza5DRM&sig=FgGV1gEjyqMN_IWkZonVnSGO82s&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjw9ZanoefWAhWJTrwKHfwVAyoQ6AEIPDAF#v=onepage&q&f=false); an aspect of human nature that a UBI plays out on a much larger scale than previous redistribution schemes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.1. Con: When a person has just enough money to provide themselves shelter they will still strive for self development and contribute to society out of boredom and loneliness.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.2. Con: Some UBI plans are designed around the principle that people economize: they seek to expend the least-means for the most gains. These plans assume landlords and vendors will try to make profit, while consumers will try to spend as little as they can. Profitable prices within the buying power of a UBI capture a market otherwise inaccessible. Without this feature of human nature, such plans fail, and so it is obvious the planners understand the fundamental tendency toward self.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.3. Con: Population levels and labor force expand in abundance, and so a baseline of unemployment exists. A small percentage being simply unmotivated to contribute only shifts them out of the labor force, and the overall situation goes unchanged.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.3.1. Con: The labor force is rapidly [diminishing](https://ideas.ted.com/the-rise-of-the-useless-class/) for the foreseeable future as a result of reducing incentives for the provision of labour. Therefore a larger percentage of \(often helplessly\) unmotivated contributors will be created.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.4. Con: People who have a good life do not have as much incentive to resent others, as they have less stress and thus less reason to seek a place for blame.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.4.1. Con: Resentment, stemming from jealousy, is a common human emotion which isn't eliminated by higher socioeconomic status. Crucially, the prominence of higher opportunity at a lower rate of investment fundamentally disregards the meritocracy of the traditional 'good life', which generates an exceptionally higher incentive for jealousy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.5. Con: A policy is not measured by what people think about those who benefit, but rather to the benefit to society. Given a UBI is an economic policy it should be measured by its economic benefit.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.5.1. Con: It is also a humanitarian and welfare policy. This makes it a moral issue in a way most economic policies are not. It is a responsible government's duty to look out for the best interests of all its populace, whether they are wealthy and economically powerful or not. A UBI could be argued to be a way of doing so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.5.2. Con: A policy that creates widespread resentment could result in a net negative for society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.6. Con: That resentment in and of itself is incentive to work. Perception in the social order, and class struggle are also elements of human nature.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.6.1. Pro: Social pressures can be a very effective way to change behaviours.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.7. Con: Human nature is as diverse as humans are and you cannot ascribe values to such a vague and ill-defined concept.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.8. Con: Those who contribute will earn more, therefore, there will not be any resentment.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.8.1. Pro: As long as those who work are compensated fairly then they will be happy to bask in their higher place in the hierarchy. UBI does not eradicate inequality, it eradicates need-based incentives.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.8.1.1. Pro: The lack of need-based incentives enables individuals to accept only jobs that are meaningful and will be worth the time spent doing them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.8.2. Con: Wealth disparities are not the only cause of demographic adversity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.9. Con: Once the State claimed the right over lands and sea it prevented the people to search for alternative ways to survive. Having no State is also dangerous because then only the laws of the strongest prevail. Thus given the necessity of its existence, the State has the responsibility to provide the means for its people to survive.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.10. Pro: Everything in our world has evolved through the process of "trial by fire" or "survival of the fittest". Nowhere in nature do you see any means of "welfare". In animal groups, each animal does their part. In the plant world, they die off if conditions become too harsh. While mankind is more intelligent the principles of the earth are the same.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.10.1. Con: Selective pressures are a property of a circumstance in which there is adversity to begin with. In a developed society, with reduced adversity by increasing automation, the framework of analysis inherently pushes away from taking action against selective pressures.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.10.2. Con: "Nowhere in nature do you see any means of 'welfare'." [African Wild Dogs](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=82kQTkWnKwg&t=2s) bring back food for the old, sick, baby sitters, and pups. Not all nursing mothers stay behind, just a few stay to feed the pups.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.10.3. Con: What is natural is not necessarily good.
1.1.5.2.11.1.5.11. Con: A basic standard of living is already available to all in the form of Prison. People that contribute don't appear to be resentful of those living in Prison.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6. Con: This presupposes that those are virtues. While some consider self-improvement to be virtuous, it doesn't need to be considered as such. It's merely a tool that allows people, to improve their living conditions or accomplish a goal when needed.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.1. Pro: A UBI instead frees people to find other ways to self-improve by removing the forced correlation between being able to eat and submitting to a job. A UBI would free people to make better contributions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.1.1. Con: Too many people would instead take the opportunity to explore what they consider their undiscovered artistic genius. Combining a UBI with turnkey self-publishing could create a nation full of subpar poets and writers, indistinguishable from simple layabouts.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.1.1.1. Con: Artistic works contribute to society by allowing not only enjoyment, but new ideas to spread. The inspiration thus created could lead to many other positive results.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.2. Pro: Different cultures have different perspectives on what is virtuous, so it is difficult to bring virtue into an objective argument.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.3. Con: Financial responsibility is most definitely a virtue in any society. This is globally accepted by economies.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.3.1. Con: Economies base their virtues on how much profit society can make, but humans shouldn't base their virtue on economies but rather on the happiness of living for everyone.
1.1.5.2.11.1.6.4. Pro: Hard work is not an intrinsic utilitarian or moral goal. For example, we embrace labor multiplying technologies such as tractors, dishwashing machines, laundromats, automobiles, and other tools and social systems that facilitate radical reductions in labor without a corresponding perception of moral failure or laziness.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7. Pro: UBI kills the initiative and incentives of people who are productive.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1. Pro: The point of a UBI is to eliminate the perverse incentive to receive welfare rather than work, but the level of taxation that it demands creates an even larger perverse incentive not to work.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.1. Con: With a UBI in place, tax brackets can be adjusted up, in order to produce incentives to perform work in the lower brackets.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.2. Con: With an UBI in place, taxation can be applied more heavily to activities which are no longer performed by humans due to automation and AI proliferation, creating incentives for new and innovative human activities which are virtually tax free.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.3. Con: The point of a UBI is supposed to be to ensure fair and reasonable living conditions and a fair share of wealth intrinsically generated by a country, while also improving both living and working conditions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.4. Con: The point of a UBI is to help give everybody the same set of starting conditions. Then meritocracy can play out fairly.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.4.1. Con: This is harmful because it fails to recognize the systemic and structural inequalities that make meritocracy an untenable concept.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.4.1.1. Con: Systemic inequalities rooted in discrimination \(e.g. racism or sexism\) are difficult to tackle and thus a perfect meritocracy is indeed hard to achieve, yet that is not reason enough to reject a policy that could at least reduce some forms of inequality \(income and class\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.2. Con: If people want to— or already do — live in locations with higher standards of living they will work hard to get there. A UBI will not cover, only supplement, such high costs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.3. Con: Productivity is not dependent on salary and compensation. Happy, healthy people are far, far more productive than  miserable, yet well payed people.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.4. Pro: A fundamental economic principle is to add value through work. When income is given for free without work this does not add value to the economy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.4.1. Con: There are no things like "fundamental economic principles", they are observations that have been valid in the past. They are traditions in the sense that they have been repeated so often people take them for laws. E.g. there could be so many opportunity costs that are avoided that the net turnover will be positive \(think of health, less pollution, etc.\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.7.5. Con: This is a generalization, many people are interested in being productive or good at something for the enjoyment of it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.8. Con: This would suggest that all forms of assistance or help that keep you from dropping into a hole are wrong and should be eliminated. Not all forms of assistance are bad, therefore UBI could be a way to provide it \(and in a proactive, before the tragedy, way\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.9. Con: By the same argument you could say that being rich removes our societal incentives for financial responsibility, self improvement and hard work. But no one would suggest that we outlaw wealth in order to incentivise society by having everyone live on the edge of poverty.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1. Pro: Personal responsibility and accountablity does not rely on the possibility of becoming completely poor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1. Pro: A UBI increases the chance of a person being more motivated and productive, as less pressure can make a person more happy and willing to contribute to society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1. Pro: [Financial stress](https://www.shrm.org/resourcesandtools/hr-topics/benefits/pages/employees-financial-issues-affect-their-job-performance.aspx) can affect health to the point of decreasing productivity. A UBI can alleviate this stress, thus indirectly increasing productivity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1.1. Pro: Increasing amounts of physical illnesses are being found to be stress-related, as stress weakens the immune system and negatively affects the physical body in other ways.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1.2. Pro: UBI reduces levels of stress and anxiety that result from an insecurity in wages and allows a person to build psychological resources that enhance possibility for upward mobility.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.2. Con: Positions that are necessary for society to continue functioning should be remunerated adequately. Implementing UBI is a band-aid solution to a systemic problem.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.2.1. Con: A UBI would not prevent remuneration for essential positions from being provided.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.3. Pro: This assumes that the only reason to work hard is for financial gain. Actually there are many incentives to work hard like personal satisfaction of contributing to your community, doing something you love, creating something, being part of a team achieving something. We can find motivation to work hard for so many reasons, and if the motivation is not financial we are likely to worker harder and with more passion.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.2. Con: Wealth is created by working hard and/or developing and investing in products and services that raise people's living standards. Outlawing wealth would remove the incentive for people to work hard and invest, eroding living standards over time.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.3. Con: Being rich does remove societal incentives for financial responsibility for many individuals. Being poor is a great motivator for financial success.
1.1.5.2.11.1.9.4. Con: Those who are rich \(not because of their parents etc. but by work\) have very often already shown successful behavior which is aimed for. Further incentive to bring them to work does not seem necessary, contrary to the prior claim of making lower earning or non-working classes aim higher. Therefore the same argument does not apply here as the direction of impact is inverse.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10. Con: This argument can only possibly apply to a very small subset of people who are living day to day on handouts - and are one wage check away from starvation. They are certainly motivated by poverty but I would suggest that the resultant worry, desperation & anxiety would outweigh any positive effects that we would consider 'motivation'. It's like saying we shouldn't ban disease because it motivates people to get better.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1. Pro: When weighing the consequences of poverty and homelessness against a desire to foster personal responsibility, human suffering has to take precedence.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.1. Pro: We may value the mindset of responsibility, but if people are needlessly suffering for what's essentially a question of ethics, there should be no question—save one's fellow man first, then begin building a sense of community and accountability. As [per Maslow](https://www.simplypsychology.org/maslow.html), the hierarchy of needs necessitates food and shelter be tackled before refining a sense of responsibility.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.1.1. Con: Saving your fellow man is only a worthwhile burden to carry when the man being saved is taking whatever responsibility they are able to take in their rescue. The responsibility for one's own life rests first with the individual, and with the community and government second only when full individual effort is not sufficient.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.1.1.1. Con: The stated conditions are often being met by the group in question.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.1.1.2. Con: Theoritically this is foolproof. But in practice, how do you establish when one's "effort" to save their lives is sufficient or not ? At the core of this claim, there is the idea that ensuring responsibility can be more important than saving a life. Human solidarity exists in part because personal will doesn't always suffice againts natural and societal odds. This is partly belief, but one person's will has less power than an entire  society's rules and culture.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.1.1.3. Con: Some people are not in a mental state to make their full effort, and this is not always correctly diagnosed. Third-party evaluators can also be incorrect about someone's capabilities, such as in the case of "invisible" disabilities, and demand unreasonable or impossible efforts. UBI allows for both of these factors.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.2. Con: When poverty and homelessness are the consequences of continued lack of personal responsibility, human suffering is simply a consequence of a personal decision. Thus the Universal word on UBI is the great issue here.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.2.1. Con: Calling poverty and homelessness consequences of a lack of personal responsibility ignores centuries of systemic oppression and presumes that people can pick themselves up by their own bootstraps. Those that have are exceptions to the rule, and should take care to avoid survivorship bias.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.2.1.1. Pro: Structural social and economic conditions are the main factor for poverty/wealth.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.1.2.2. Con: There are many reasons to believe most victims of poverty and homelessness are not suffering from the continued lack of personal responsibility, but rather have been forced into that situation by other factors.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.2. Pro: Some people might have disabilities which prevent them from being responsible for their financial well being and might not be picked up by current social security.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.2.1. Con: This problem is not efficiently addressed by an universal instrument such as an Universal Basic Income. UBI should be considered with the goal of serving a broader audience.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.3. Pro: This truly depends on the height of the UBI and the financial mobility of all kinds of groups within that country. If the UBI would merely ensure the affordability of only essential services & products that are considered primary life needs \(such as a house, food,medicine, et cetera\), there would be plenty people that would seek income of their own to afford pretty much everything else that one wants and needs as a citizen during their daily lives. Because of this, their incentive would not die.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.3.1. Pro: Basic Income can be incremental — the amount that defines one 'mans' basic is another's luxury.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.4. Con: It also applies to everybody who measures success in dollars - even if they don't actually care about the dollars themselves.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.5. Con: Claims have been made that UBI gives people more freedom to select jobs or to go jobless for a time rather than search desperately. To the extent those claims are true, UBI could reduce your motivation to keep a job or to work as hard as you can even when you aren't living paycheck to paycheck.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.6. Pro: Avoiding destitution is not the reason most people work and cannot be the basis for a fulfilling or productive career. Financial incentives contribute to social status, which is a reason why people strive for higher-paid work, but changing those incentives would be a benefit of UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.7. Pro: A UBI only erodes incentives rooted in fear of poverty and death. These incentives are only useful in creating a desperate slave workforce, as compared to healthy incentives like actual passions or an authentic drive to contribute to society and the local community from a position of empowerment, health, security, and consent.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.7.1. Pro: This is where civic duty must be rewarded. In essence, the consumer of the provided service should be public. As such, those performing greater good should receive higher rewards. Uncertain who gets to decide what is right or wrong.
1.1.5.2.11.1.10.8. Con: Individuals who are disenfranchised are the least likely to be apathetic towards their jobs. The one saving grace from starvation \(a paycheck\) does not reduce incentive to prevent starvation \(getting a paycheck\). Poverty is not the subject of interest in the analysis of extra opportunity space \(the root argument which does or doesn't decrease productivity\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.11. Con: This depends on the amount of money offered by the UBI. If low enough, there is plenty of incentive to get "more", while still freeing people from thinking of what to eat next or where to have shelter.
1.1.5.2.11.1.11.1. Pro: The difference between a UBI and a minimum salary has to be regularized, in order to avoid mechanisms where not working is more remunerative than doing so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.11.1.1. Con: This will lead to a general decrease in job positions, as with the same money an entrepreneur will be able to hire fewer people.
1.1.5.2.11.1.11.1.2. Pro: This could force large company enterpreneurs to invest more of their capitals in working force.
1.1.5.2.11.1.11.2. Con: A too-low UBI would not be helpful. Instead of keeping it too low in comparison to the least paid job, we should increase the lowest possible pay.
1.1.5.2.11.1.11.2.1. Con: Agreed: A too-low UBI is not helpful, it should be good enough for making a living. The attack goes to "we should imcrease the lowest possible pay". With a high-enough UBI, there is no need for a lowest possible pay: people would be happy doing a work they love almost for free, if they have a living and a meaning in life.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12. Con: Existing welfare schemes also erode personal responsibility and accountability; often to a much higher degree because of the high effective marginal tax rates. A UBI would actually reduce this effect relative to the status quo.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1. Pro: Existing welfare schemes reduce people's incentive to work because earning more money reduces the number of benefits they accrue. A UBI fixes this and actually provides [a higher marginal return to earning an income](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/).
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.1. Pro: In the US, the effective marginal tax rates on the poor [are often as high as 109% of their income](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/) because of the corresponding reduction in welfare benefits as they earn more.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.2. Pro: The small increase in income from getting a minimum wage job, at the cost of hours of free time, often does not motivate unemployed people to find one. A UBI, retained when entering the workforce, provides better incentive.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3. Pro: A UBI will fix the threshold and [poverty trap](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/p/poverty-trap.asp) effects induced by the current means-tested schemes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.1. Pro: Welfare is a system that is built to merely keep people on sustenance, and never provides them with the resources to pull themselves out of poverty.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.1.1. Con: The alternative to a UBI is not just status quo welfare but rather using all of the money that would go towards a UBI to massively expand welfare. This means welfare could include things such as education, which would allow people to escape poverty.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.1.2. Pro: Benefits received from the Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program \(SNAP\) are [not enough](http://www.npr.org/sections/thesalt/2016/03/03/468955099/the-snap-gap-benefits-arent-enough-to-keep-many-recipients-fed) to keep most beneficiaries fed. Many families are still forced to visit a food pantry each month.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.2. Pro: Most existing means-tested anti-poverty schemes exclude people because of their complexity, or because people don’t know how to apply or whether they qualify. With a UBI, people currently excluded from benefit allowances will automatically have their rights guaranteed.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.3. Pro: [The Speenhamland system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speenhamland_system) was a form of outdoor relief intended to mitigate rural poverty in England at the end of the 18th century and during the early 19th century. It was the first UBI experiment and it was at the time very successful.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.3.3.1. Con: The speenhamland system \(Berkshire Bread Act\) was a welfare system, not a UBI. Poor families were given extra money by the parish to top up wages enough to buy bread.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.4. Pro: UBI does not punish those of lower classes who begin to earn more money, but instead keeps the incentive to move up in income to the point where UBI is no longer necessary for the individual. Therefore this saves the government more money over time in welfare spending.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.5. Con: Even if the existing welfare schemes are \(supposedly\) worse than UBIs, welfare schemes could be improved so that the disincentive to increase income is not as steep as the [\(1999\) source](https://www.epionline.org/wp-content/studies/shaviro_02-1999.pdf) of the [parent's source](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/) illustrates. In other words, traditional welfare programs could be fixed rather than eliminated and replaced with UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.5.1. Con: This argument is a status quo bias. It adresses only one of the current wellfare system's shortcomings but maintains that it should be preserved to avert change.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.5.2. Pro: If the main problem with current welfare is the disincentive to work, a reworked version of [workfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workfare) can solve that problem without the need for the high cost of a UBI. Requiring some level of employment before social benefits supplement that income \(for those physically and mentally able\) incentivizes employment and reduces overall welfare costs because the government is only supplementing, not providing the income.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.5.2.1. Con: Invisible disabilities, or those who have difficulty proving their disability \(or do not have full information about the availability of schemes\), could easily slip through the cracks. A UBI allows no cracks by including every individual.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6. Pro: UBI would correct the unintended consequence of broken families and encourage marriage and stable family structure. Current welfare discourages marriage as evidenced by the rise in single mother households whereas UBI would not penalize a single mother for the added income of a spouse but instead encourage the combining of incomes through marriage.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.1. Con: Providing for the people through government is destructive of families, as it directly competes with or purports to replace [the responsibility](https://www.lds.org/scriptures/ot/gen/3.19) of [fathers to provide](https://www.lds.org/topics/family-proclamation?lang=eng&old=true) for their families.
There is [no more economical way](https://www.forbes.com/sites/aparnamathur/2015/10/30/the-family-foundations-of-economic-growth/#3a60007a2a9b) to live than in intact families as the fundamental provident unit of society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.2. Con: Traditional welfare systems can exist which are neutral or even incentivize marriage.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.2.1. Pro: In the UK it was historically very beneficial to be married, although it is now rather neutral, [due to](https://www.money.co.uk/guides/how-will-getting-married-affect-your-finances.htm) married couples/marriage allowance, tax bracket sharing, and tax free gifts.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.2.2. Pro: Many countries, [like Germany](https://www.expattax.de/marriage-tax-classes-germany/), have joint taxation. This benefits married couples who can choose to submit taxes together, meaning they can often share unused tax credits.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.2.3. Pro: Proposed changes to the Swiss marriage tax model make getting married [more financially beneficial](http://www.loc.gov/law/foreign-news/article/switzerland-abolition-of-marriage-tax-penalty-proposed/) than being an unmarried couple.  It also adds financial incentive for couples with children to get married.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.3. Con: Much of any marriage penalty comes from tax changes, rather than welfare, because most couples are not on welfare. 

UBI would not affect the taxation system, and therefore would not have as much of an impact on marriage incentives as a tax reform world.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.4. Con: Increasing numbers of single mother households just suggests that relationships are not lasting as long.

If a correlation was to be drawn between current welfare systems and lack of marriage, we should look at numbers of unmarried couples, not single mothers.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.5. Con: A UBI would not encourage combining of incomes through marriage, as any family would get the same benefits even if they stayed as an unmarried couple.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.2. Pro: Personal and societal incentives for financial responsibility are only eroded if programs are designed for people to become not-eligible after they surpass a certain income. Since UBI is free from this restriction, people will always have the incentive to get more income than the basic, for the same reasons that most people in society do not settle for minimum wage jobs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.3. Pro: People would still receive an incentive by the extra income generated from their job.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.3.1. Pro: A UBI is unlikely to inhibit someone like Steve Jobs or Bill Gates from their passions. If there are creative people out there, a UBI would give them time to work on what is important to them so they are free to be more creative, instead of working an unrewarding job.
1.1.5.2.11.1.12.4. Pro: On the contrary, a UBI incentivizes financial responsibility since it is a minimal amount to survive, it allows and empowers self-improvement allowing people to survive through re-training and education periods when they are not productive to the economy, and it empowers those who want to start their own businesses with a survival cushion that sets entrepreneurs free to create new businesses.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13. Con: The claim a UBI erodes personal incentives for self-improvement and hard work ignores the psychological and/or sociological components of these traits. It assumes such desires are externally motivated. These traits are likely a mix of internal and external factors. For those highly internally motivated, UBI is less likely to substantially change their behavior.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.1. Pro: There are other incentives for self-improvement, such as a sense of accomplishment or mastery.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.1.1. Pro: However, a UBI would force us to think about other options than financial incentives like personal growth and benefits for society .
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.1.2. Pro: Not all people are motivated or incentivized by profit.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.2. Pro: The thesis presupposes that human beings are primarily motivated by their own consumption - that they only do anything if it increases their ability to consume. But humans are primarily motivated by an existential calling. After all, one would not be interested in sustaining their  life if it did not have meaning for them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.2.1. Pro: Most people do not consider the meaning or purpose of their life to be to accrue wealth, and instead accrue wealth in service of other goals.
1.1.5.2.11.1.13.3. Pro: In the last years the concept of gamification emerged. It shows that motivation often has nothing to do with money, but rather people can develop a desired behavior through an activity that is meaningful. This can reward people without necessarily using money to do so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.14. Con: It depends in what kind of society you live. In a society where the will of the collective comes before the will of the individual, your claim may show itself fail. How could your claim even explain volunteer work if all that matter is money? There are more them just money that leads people to produce something, or "work" for those who prefer this word.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15. Con: This claim presumes that "financial responsibility" and "hard work" are essential to the functionality or benefit of society. However, it ignores the growth of automation of jobs. As automation increases, and unemployment grows, UBI will be necessary to forestall catastrophic economic inequality and to enable personal growth and flourishing.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15.1. Con: While "financial responsibility" and "hard work" may not be as useful in the future as they are now, it would be foolish to abandon them before we actually have the automated infrastructure to safely let them go as virtues.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15.1.1. Con: Yes, it would be foolish to let go of such virtues, but this could be tacked by, educating the masses on how to cope with the major shift and how to focus more on self development, now that they are free to do so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15.2. Pro: UBI creates the space for "hard word" and "financial stability" to be replaced with "creative output" and "network solutions". Rather than amassing more things, we can strive towards a society that is highly resilient and self-sustainable. The churn of automation would be less of a threat, because more people would be safe to pursue self-employment.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15.3. Con: UBI is simply a mechanism to maintain the rich/poor divide and not an attempt to bridge it. UBI will only prolong a dependence on a financial social ranking. Society has surpassed the need to rank individuals by wealth however we/the world still rely on this outdated system.
1.1.5.2.11.1.15.3.1. Con: Not having money is maintaining the rich/poor divide. Ubi wouldn't change it completely, but it would at least give the people a chance to move up.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16. Con: Some of the hardest working jobs have the lowest pay. And the very wealthy have to do relatively little to invest their large capital in such a way as to get large returns. Hard work and reward do not correlate.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.1. Con: Hard work is not limited to physically hard work. Achieving "large returns" on investments consistently is very hard work, although not physical.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.2. Con: “Hard work” is the wrong term. Work which has higher societal benefits would be more accurate. Athletes \(professional game players\) produce no material goods, but they are paid millions because people enjoy watching them. Investment banking isn’t physically hard work but is an important engine in growing the domestic economy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.2.1. Con: Investmant bankers spurred the 2008 financial crisis. Collateralized debt obligations and preditory lending allowed bankers to leverage their risk onto the pockets of tax payers in pursuit of personal profit. Their actions did not help American society. Skilled trades people help grow society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.3. Con: "Hard work" is an ambiguous term. It can mean physically taxing work - this indeed does not correlate with pay. But "hard" can also mean "hard to learn/acquire necessary skills" - in this meaning hardness does correlate with pay.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.4. Con: If individuals are resentful of the current circumstance for reasons like low-return labor, then reducting that resentment by eliminating those positions with a UBI does not contribute to decreased productivity and societal incentive. The threat to societal incentive pertains to increased opportunity space.
1.1.5.2.11.1.16.5. Pro: Additionally, there are many high earners in rent seeking industries like lobbying and arguably, to some extent, investment banking. These roles do not contribute to the social good and more often than not, detract from it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.17. Con: A UBI is a fairer alternative to most welfare programs because everyone gets it — not just the people who need it the most which could be interpreted as rewarding incompetence/laziness.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18. Pro: A work guarantee is preferable to a UBI because work can and does give people a sense of purpose and meaning in life, identity, self-worth and social contact.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1. Con: A UBI reduces the stigma of social welfare, because the payments are distributed to everyone in society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1. Pro: Those most in need are spared the degrading experience of pleading with welfare officers on a regular basis that they fit into specific categories.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1.1. Con: Given the harmful effects of poverty in general, having to justify your need \(even in a degrading way\) is far from the biggest issue facing the poor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1.2. Con: It is often quite simple to prove that one is entitled to a welfare payout by providing proof of unemployment, or showing an insufficient income.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1.3. Con: Communities are organized geographically based on socio-economic conditions and it is often an entire neighborhood that is on welfare. Proving one is part of a specific category is less degrading, because perceptively it is seen as a common experience, rather than a uniquely degrading one.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1.4. Con: We can likely tailor the current system to make admittance into a welfare program a more private affair done electronically or by mail.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.2. Con: Stigma is caused by a certain thing's association with low status individuals or groups: in a society with a mature UBI tradition, and perhaps in one where UBI is new as well, being known to rely on UBI would be just as shameful as relying on welfare is in the real world.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.2.1. Con: Money is inherently fungible, so the source is readily obscured where necessary, while foodstamps are obviously foodstamps.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3. Con: Stigma likely [arises](https://books.google.com.sg/books?id=SOPSL7y4cRYC&pg=RA1-PT121&lpg=RA1-PT121&dq=welfare+stigma+taxpayer+resentment&source=bl&ots=LKEKmQKU_Z&sig=1hRl3JJ3nS6Mv5H6GOjzM96EEDQ&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiXrMzPosDQAhUOR48KHYepDzMQ6AEIRjAG#v=onepage&q=welfare%20stigma%20taxpayer%20resentment&f=false) from individuals being unable to work or being perceived as taking more from the collective resource pool than they give. This perception is getting traction in states experiencing an electoral backlash against liberal values.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1. Pro: A UBI will entrench stigma against entire groups rather than remove it. Middle class individuals who imagine themselves to be hardworking will feel resentment at having a vastly increased tax rate to fund UBI payments for discriminated minorities.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.2. Pro: The nature of [government spending means that](http://thespinoff.co.nz/featured/31-03-2016/i-love-the-idea-of-a-universal-basic-income-but-heres-the-problem/) a UBI could be only two of: 1\) Generous enough to substitute for existing welfare systems, 2\) Have low phase-out rates that do not heavily penalise work, and 3\) Not cost an order of magnitude more than current welfare programs. Given most UBI's are strictly universal \(2\) either \(3\) or \(1\) must apply.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: For the UBI to not leave the most needy welfare recipients worse-off it would need to cover the costs of overlapping entitlements, which would cost far above the poverty line — [up to $22,000 in the case of New Zealand](https://thespinoff.co.nz/featured/31-03-2016/i-love-the-idea-of-a-universal-basic-income-but-heres-the-problem/).
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.2.2. Pro: An 'affordable' UBI that does not fully replace the existing targeted means of welfare distribution \(i.e. one that is supplemented by other targeted schemes\) retains many of the existing issues with poverty traps, inefficient distribution and high costs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.3. Pro: Many democracies have shown themselves to be chronically unable to raise taxes as they distribute benefits. Policymakers want to be seen as the bringer of carrots rather than the wielder of sticks.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.3.1. Pro: As governments fail to raise taxes, this would lead to an ever-increasing debt, allowing for interest to build, meaning that eventually, not even basic services could function without more tax hikes, and whomever was left with the fiscal mess would receive all the blame for it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.4. Pro: Targeted welfare often entrenches Darwinian narratives about those in poverty, particularly where ethnic minorities are overwhelmingly overrepresented in those statistics. This increases discrimination in social interactions and also reduces political capital for useful measures.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.4.1. Con: It is more realistic that targeted welfare entrenches the narrative that there are specific groups of people who are in special need of assistance, and that it is the state's obligation to assist these people, not that this assistance is a result of some kind of evolutionary deficit.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.4.2. Con: Seeing which minority groups correlate with the type of, and need for, welfare, provides us with valuable data about how much inequality still exists in society. A UBI limits our ability to gain and use this data to fight said inequality.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.4.2.1. Con: Government studies and surveys about income distribution across the population are still a powerful tool to determine inequality. Those studies can still be conducted with a UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.4.2.2. Con: A UBI may limit data that demonstrates inequality, but only to the extent that it actually reduces inequality.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.5. Pro: Middle class voters are more likely to accept a tax hike for a UBI because they actually see some return from this policy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.5.1. Con: If the tax hike is higher than the UBI payout, middle class voters are paying more than they are receiving, and will know that their money is going to people who may not even be working. This in turn reinforces stigmas against the poor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.6. Pro: This benefits the people who need it most \(those that do not have money or capabilities to access welfare programs\), because the program goes to them instead of them going to the program.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.7. Pro: People often do not take governmental assistance, because there is a stigma associated with receiving them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.8. Pro: The reduction of social stigma might help people get jobs more easily, as employers might not hire people who receive governmental benefits.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.8.1. Pro: An employer might not hire someone if 1\) if the applicant cannot manage their lives, then they cannot handle a job, 2\) an applicant receiving benefits might make the company look bad \(like the company does not pay enough, which is why they're on stamps\), 3\) the applicant cannot pay for the job's image \(like business suits, cars for transport...\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.9. Con: A UBI assigns a specific and clear monetary value to welfare, which feeds into classist rhetoric, making easier for some people to complain about wastefulness.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.10. Pro: Existing welfare systems have a burdensome set of conditions and application processes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.10.1. Pro: Conditions in social payments are a source of inefficiency, since they amount to qualifications which need to be validated.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.11. Con: On the other hand, everyone would be wards of the state, inverting the relationship between the People and the federal government established by the Constitution.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.2. Con: Currently unpaid roles such as parenting and caring for loved ones/family, would continue to be unvalued with a work guarantee. The UBI would give carers this sense of purpose, self-worth and social contact that they deserve.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.2.1. Con: A UBI would not directly change the social status or recognition of unpaid family roles.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.3. Con: A UBI is preferable to a work guarantee because it allows people to define for themselves what they see as a sense of purpose, meaning in life, identity etc. whether that be work or something else which is most likely still useful.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.3.1. Pro: People will work harder and be more motivated when working towards goals they personally understand, identify with and/or see the value in.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.4. Con: With increasing automation, it may not be possible to give meaningful jobs to every person in a society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.5. Con: A work guarantee will create distortions in the marketplace and waste of human resources as it necessarily requires the government to create otherwise unneeded jobs.
1.1.5.2.11.1.18.5.1. Con: There would be no shortage of necessary jobs if the government prioritized sustainable energy solutions and crisis relief.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19. Con: This is assuming societies' priorities should be the same as capitalism. Priorities shift towards societal & human gains.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.1. Con: Without capitalism, society would not be in a position to shift its priorities towards societal & human rights. Without capitalism there is no high standard of living.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.1.1. Con: Capitalism is only one factor in the global evolution of the human condition; factors like democracy, culture, and societal values are also highly relevant, as are scientific and technological advancements. It is inappropriate to attribute today's quality of life entirely to capitalism.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.1.2. Con: This is true until automation becomes the new standard. Then the society standard of living will need to change.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.2. Pro: These incentives are not needed by our society anymore as norms and customs change over time.
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.2.1. Con: While it is true that changes in norms produce \(and demand different incentives\), the economic structure of developed countries \(and human psychology\) has not changed anywhere near enough to deprecate these fundamental incentives
1.1.5.2.11.1.19.3. Con: Self-improvement and hard work are not essential tenets of capitalism: they are consequences of it. We can have a conversation about disciplining an exercise routine using the same tools of language as in developing a venture capital and both practices can be objectively irrespective of each other.
1.1.5.2.11.1.20. Con: If there is some requirement to contribute to society in some way \(work, volunteer, go to school, create art, start a business, teach, mentor people, join the military, etc.\), the government could use the program to encourage people to contribute to society in specific ways.
1.1.5.2.11.1.20.1. Con: If work is required for a UBI, it goes against the premise of a UBI which is provide a living wage for all without work needed. It therefore becomes a “fast track” of sorts to get a job. However, we currently have more positions available for work than unemployed. If the person wanted a job, the opportunities are available right now and a UBI wouldn’t be needed.
1.1.5.2.11.1.20.1.1. Con: In Australia, the ratio of unemployed workers to jobs can get as high as [18:1](http://unemployedworkersunion.com/job-seekers-v-job-vacancy-data/).
1.1.5.2.11.1.21. Con: A UBI encourages personal an societal incentives and encourages self-improvement and responsibility because it provides the ability to develop those values instead of requiring people focus on mere survival.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1. Pro: A basic income would provide a person with multiple chances to become financially literate. Currently, without affluent parents or any other sturdy safety net, one or two bad decisions or unlucky outcomes can be a slippery slope into debt and/or poverty.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.1. Con: UBI is unnecessary when it comes to teaching someone personal financial responsibility. Any interaction with money is a learning opportunity, especially when the person has earned it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.1.1. Con: People should not be penalized, or worse, suffer, for the rest of their lives over mistakes made early - which is possible without a sufficient safety net like a UBI or sufficient welfare.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.2. Con: UBI doesn't prevent a person from spending beyond their means or accruing debts they are unable to pay.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.2.1. Con: It does, however, provide a safety net for gradually recovering from the results of doing so, and provides a second chance once the lesson is learned - which is what the original claim is arguing.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.2.2. Con: Unless you restrict what people are allowed to spend their money on, this applies equally to any other income, from employment or otherwise.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.3. Pro: With the basic needs of the people covered by UBI, they can focus more time on experimentation and gathering experience.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.4. Pro: UBI provides people with economic security which allows them to follow their dreams easier and also invest more. Thats because if they fail they will still have the basic finances needed to survive.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.4.1. Pro: A UBI assists small businesses by allowing genuinely interested parties to devote more of their time, effort, and attention to the business rather than having to worry about their own employment and financial security.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.5. Con: Good decision makers should not be forced to provide financial support to poor decision makers.
1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.5.1. Con: Humans should not be damned to suffering and hardship for single mistakes, or even a series of mistakes. "Rescue" and education efforts should always be made.
1.1.5.2.11.1.22. Pro: People will lose motivation for work. As a citizen of a developed country, it would be perfectly possible to take the UBI and move to countries with lower standards and live on it without contributing anything.
1.1.5.2.11.1.22.1. Con: Being able to recieve UBI from one country while living in another is a problem that would be easy enough to solve.
1.1.5.2.11.1.22.1.1. Pro: UBI would have to be either universal for the entire planet and thus invalidate the claim, or would be governed by individual countries and could be dropped for those who are not there.
1.1.5.2.11.1.22.2. Con: This can be remedied by only providing UBI to citizens currently staying in the country.
1.1.5.2.11.1.22.3. Con: It is possible for sure to be an ex-patriot and scam the system, but it won't be easy for someone with little money. The whole point of UBI is to motivate people to be able to put themselves in a better place in society. \(E.g., 1. A female can leave abusive husband because UBI provides necessary costs. 2. A graduate with student loans who accepts any job to be able to pay the loan back could instead focus on getting a job they went to school for.\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.23. Con: It could actually do the very opposite. By giving people financial safety but still fixed, yet good amount, people will have to be very responsible and more understanding with their money from a younger age.
1.1.5.2.11.1.24. Con: Free Market Capitalism is based on “meritocracy” and hierarchies. It is the desire to reach the top of a given hierarchy that drives the needle of progress and innovation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25. Pro: A UBI is immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.12.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.2. Pro: A UBI takes resources from one individual to give them to another with no product or service in exchange, and without permission. That is the definition of theft; theft is immoral. Therefore a UBI is immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.2.1. Con: There is a service in exchange: Poverty is reduced. Please bear in mind that poverty is not only the sum of missing money of the poor people, but there is also a huge amount of money spent for poor people \(health, infrastructure, in rare cases even crime cost\). Investing in giving poor people a life with more dignity would prove money well spent.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.2.1.1. Con: Whether it would be money well spent or not is a matter of opinion, not fact.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.2.1.2. Con: Poverty being reduced is not a service to the person whose money is confiscated to pay for it. It's a service to the poor. The discussion isn't about whether helping the poor is a good thing or not, it's about a specific method of helping. If someone wants to help the poor, they absolutely should do so through free will charity. If they don't want to, they shouldn't be forced to. A UBI is how that force is applied, and it's immoral to do so.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3. Pro: A UBI incapacitates citizens as it creates a \(financial\) dependency on the state.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.1. Pro: The poor will be more reliant on the state if the only method they have for reducing inequality is through state provision. This creates a perverse incentive to not challenge the state or demand social change.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.1.1. Con: A UBI would not disincentivize the poor to challenge the state, rather they might have new challenges that do not concern economics anymore, but rather social or political issues.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.1.2. Con: People retain all the mechanisms to enact social change that already exist; this policy does not limit their abilities.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.1.3. Con: The poor are already reliant on the state, and usually have to "earn" their welfare through makework, such as seeking and applying for jobs that they have no chance of getting. Removing this requirement makes them less dependent on the state, because they can take chances on jobs or enterprises without facing destitution if they fail.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.2. Pro: Citizens will become reliant on the UBI once they start using it to support ongoing business or personal projects. This creates a dependency and thus a bargaining chip for governments.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.3. Con: Modern social welfare structures currently exist, with strings attached — you can't be homeless and get housing assistance, you need to be in college to get pell grants, and you must be working to get the earned income tax credit etc. By equalizing the payments and removing the strings attached to social programs, the influence of politicians and bureaucrats is lessened relative to the status quo.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.4. Con: Public subsidies for business \(like arts programs\) are currently used as bargaining chips by governments. Restructuring those into a UBI \(welfare for people instead of corporations\) would remove bargaining chips from the hand of governments.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.4.1. Con: Social programs addressing the needs of individuals are also used as bargaining chips by governments.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.4.1.1. Con: As a UBI is universal and unconditional, it could not be used as such.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.5. Con: Every single entity within a society is dependent on the state. It is called the social contract.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.5.1. Con: The degree of the dependency varies strongly.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.5.1.1. Con: Even the wealthiest of people are at the mercy of chance, nature, and crime without the state making sure they are not victims and/or helping them recover fro such events \(e.g. rebuilding disaster zones, policing, firefighting etc\). People tend to underestimate these guarantees given by the state and take them for granted thinking they are not dependent on them when in fact everyone is just as dependent. If anything the dependence of the wealthy could be even greater as they have more to lose.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.6. Pro: Multiple generations of UBI could lead to dependence on a delicate system that requires a lot of social cooperation to sustain. If this system ever collapses because subsequent generations take it for granted, the effects could be disastrous.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.6.1. Con: This argument applies to many other systems, like schools, firemen, police, social security, etc. That did not stop previous generations from creating those systems. There are cases around the world where these systems have indeed collapsed, with disastrous consequences; but the people recognize they are necessary and try really hard to re-establish them. A UBI would probably enter in that category if it is created.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.6.2. Pro: This is already happening with social security and other entitlement programs. UBI could follow suit.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.7. Pro: A UBI creates a fragile core centered around global government in a high-tension time in history. This core could collapse at any moment and create catastrophe.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.8. Pro: A UBI would disallows an individual to attain a higher standard of life by trapping them within a limited income and lifestyle. By issuing a limited income, people will come to rely on that income solely. Assuming that welfare would remain in many countries, there would be even less incentive to improve ones life and lifestyle. Many poor and uneducated are content to keep status quo, but the ones that would "Dream" of something better, would ultimately lose.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.8.1. Con: Individuals desiring a higher quality of life or additional goods and services could always still earn money through paid employment.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.8.1.1. Pro: This would be actively advertised and encouraged by businesses seeking employees, who must now compete for labour as people are not beholden to them merely for their living expenses.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.9. Pro: In essence, a UBI creates a [nanny state](https://www.thefreedictionary.com/nanny+state) through finances.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4. Pro: A taxation-funded UBI is an involuntary wealth transfer, and welfare redistribution schemes are equivalent to crime.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.1. Con: If taxation is equivalent to, and enforced as a crime, no modern state would be able to function.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.1.1. Con: A state that sustains itself by violating the rights and freedoms it was built to defend undermines its purpose.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.1.2. Con: Read the claim carefully, taxes for an involuntary wealth transfer are equivalent to crime, not all taxes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2. Con: Given taxation and redistribution policies are widespread, there is no reason to hold a UBI in particular to a different standard.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.1. Con: Prevalence is no proof of legitimacy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.2. Con: Taxation and redistribution policies are unethical, as they confiscate the individual's property without the it's consent. UBI is based on taxation, and is a kind of redistribution policy, so it is unethical too.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.2.1. Con: Permitting suffering and death due to poverty is cruel and immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.2.1.1. Con: UBI is economic suicide and will put more people in poverty when it quickly becomes unaffordable and the country defaults on its debt.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.3. Con: This discussion is about a UBI though. No different standard is held, all wealth redistribution programs are immoral and equivalent to theft.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.2.4. Con: Not all taxes are immoral. Defense is a service rendered to all people, and it would be near impossible for the military to selectively defend people. Therefore all people should have to pay for that service. Infrastructure, like roads, are a good that all people benefit from, therefore all people should have to pay for that good. Taxes for these purposes are not immoral. but taxes for a wealth redistribution scheme like UBI, welfare, etc. are. UBI isn't being held to a different standard.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.3. Con: The nature of political power in democratic society is that it involves individuals giving up power over one aspect of your life in order to gain benefits in the other.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.3.1. Con: Individuals don't give up their power voluntarily, even if they are immensely better off with a democratic society. This is unethical and immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.3.2. Con: Not all wealthy countries are democratic.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.3.3. Con: No benefit is gained by the person paying the tax for the UBI. Nor any tax based wealth redistribution program for that matter.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4. Con: Taxes aren't crimes. They are simply citizens paying for the services their goverment provides.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.1. Con: Taxes are the involuntary apropriation of a part of an individual's wealth. A theft is the involuntary apropriation of a part of an individual's wealth. Taxes equal theft, and if society legitimates taxes, it is legitimating theft.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.1.1. Con: Taxes are the legitimate exercise of one of a government's legitimate functions, which is to provide public services from public funds for the maintenance of society. Members within a society exist in that society, not in isolation, and benefit from their position within that society. It is reasonable and ethical for individuals to contribute, and be expected to contribute, towards maintenance of the society they are a part of and benefit from.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.2. Pro: Governments defend people's right to private property, which could otherwise be breached in any number of ways \(theft, financially motivated murder...\) without consequence. In order to provide this protection, it is necessary for governments to source funding somehow. This is the principle behind taxes, which are not immoral in this light.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.2.1. Con: But you've created a false equivalency and a straw man. The claim is that "Taxation for a wealth redistribution scheme is equivalent to crime". Not that all taxes are equivalent to crime. You've chosen a specific tax that actually provides a service to the person paying the tax. In the case of a UBI, the person paying the tax receives no good or service in return.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.3. Con: Note the claim states that taxation specifically for involuntary wealth transfers are EQUIVALENT to crime, in that case specifically theft. More specifically, even if theft wasn't considered a crime for some odd reason\(anarchic society\), it would still be immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.5. Con: Taxation is not theft given democracy defines the rule of law. Our law defines what is criminal and what isn't, meaning that legal taxation cannot be seen as a crime.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.5.1. Pro: The costs and benefits of taxation can hardly be crimes if they exist by the assent of the majority of the voting population.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.5.1.1. Con: This assumes that democracy and criminality are equivalent to morality. Democracies can and do act in immoral and criminal ways.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.5.1.2. Con: The majority should not be able to decide what to do with an individual's resources. That's immoral, and demotivational. The US founding fathers recognized that they had to do something to protect minorities, that's why the senate exists.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.5.2. Con: The claim you're attempting to refute states it is equivalent to crime, not that it is crime. It also doesn't state that all taxation is equivalent to crime, but that wealth redistribution schemes are equivalent to crime.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.6. Pro: Poverty is the default state of human existence; confiscating property from one to give to another is immoral regardless of good intentions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.6.1. Con: It could be argued that the very basis of property is immoral, and baseless, but it does make for a useful idea. Taxes do as well.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.6.2. Con: Allowing preventable harm is also immoral. A UBI is one way of addressing the preventable harm caused by the current financial system and wealth distribution.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.6.2.1. Con: Allowing preventable harm is not immoral in every case.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.6.2.1.1. Pro: Were it different, nobody could in practice be moral - as there's always somebody in need of help from harm. It's an impossible standard.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.7. Pro: A UBI is set on the premise of a right to another person's work without their consent, i.e. slavery, and is therefore unethical.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.8. Pro: A UBI is a "means to an end" policy, where stealing is justified on the basis of knowing better and only the possibility, but no guarantee, of achieving an ends.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9. Pro: A UBI cannot exist without confiscating money \(at gunpoint\) from some people and giving it to others. It is theft, and therefore 100% immoral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.1. Con: The criminality of a tax would necessarily translate to all forms of taxes.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.1.1. Con: Not true, that's like saying the criminality of an action would necessarily translate to all forms of actions, therefore if murder is illegal, then so is jumping. No, a tax specifically for wealth redistribution schemes is immoral and should be criminal. A tax for a public good\(one that is nonrivalrous and nonexcludable\) is fine.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.2. Con: Unlike other groups of people, the wealthy have the luxury of choosing where they live, and moving if they disagree with a countries' policies.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.2.1. Con: In the case of a UBI, it's not just the wealthy that end up paying for it though. Wealth distribution roughly follows a Pareto distribution, meaning that even the lower middle class will end up paying. Furthermore, if everyone above a certain income line left to avoid the tax, the UBI wouldn't have the wealthy to draw funding from and the middle class would have to pay even more, causing more people to flee, until all your left with is the poor that couldn't afford to leave.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3. Con: Forcing people at gun point to live in a system increasingly rigged against them until they descend into slavery, which is the current state of affairs, is more immoral then some think taxes are.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3.1. Con: You're assuming the system is rigged against someone.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3.2. Con: You're assuming that a system rigged against someone, will become further rigged against them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3.3. Con: You're assuming the conclusion, descent into slavery, and declaring that the current state of affairs without any supporting evidence.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3.4. Con: The proper way to fix immorality isn't to create another.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.3.5. Con: There is no law forcing someone to live in poverty.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4. Con: There is no inherent right to property, especially when that property wasn't earned by the one who holds it, as is often the case of the wealthy. Taxes are merely the government ending its lease on communal property lent to an individual.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.1. Pro: These leases encourage people to develop the property in question, and facilitates optimization of resources.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2. Pro: A UBI isn't about an inherent right to property, it's about an inherent right to life. Life isn't possible without shelter, food, health care, personal fulfillment. One may disagree about certain components, but a UBI facilitates life not property.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.1. Con: The right to live only means no one can take life from you; not that everybody has to guarantee you the necessities for living.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.1.1. Con: It could be argued that humans have a moral obligation to look out for the lives and healths of those less fortunate. The UBI would be a government sanctioned and facilitated way of doing so. The government is, after all, representative of the people \(under an ideally functioning democratic system\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.1.1.1. Con: By definition any moral actions can't be obligatory, since morality is the act of choosing the action which gives the greater sum of happiness when you have the choice to choose other actions with lower sum. Moral obligations are a contradiction.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Moral obligation is a common concept.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.1.2. Con: Many believe that humans should be entitled to the basic necessities of life.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.2.2. Con: The right to live is included in property rights, as your body is your property. As Henrique Campos has said: "The right to live only means no one can take life from you; not that everybody has to guarantee you the necessities for living".
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.3. Con: Property must be an inherent right. You own yourself. You own the things you make. You own the things you trade for. What you do with what you own is your choice and no one else's.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.4. Con: This claim "There is no inherent right to property" is extremely dangerous, as it tries to legitimate slavery. The only people on earth that do not have an inherent right to property are the slaves.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.4.4.1. Con: No. If nobody has right to property, nobody has right to own anyone else. One can oppose both property and slavery.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.5. Pro: All tax collection, ultimately, is enforced by the threat of State violence if people refuse to comply.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.5.1. Con: All of society is enforced by a [monopoly on state violence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monopoly_on_violence).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.6. Con: Governments can in fact print more money, although it's true that if this was the sole source of the UBI, it would be unsustainable.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.6.1. Con: Printing more money directly causes inflation, a UBI based on inflationary printing of money would only make everyone poorer. Then again, I suppose that's what a UBI is supposed to do, make everyone equally poor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.7. Pro: A universal welfare could only be funded with significant sales and/or income taxes. Such taxes require throwing anyone who refuses to disclose how much currency they received in private trade, and to hand over a share of that currency to the government, in prison, where they are kept in a small enclosure and subjected to dehumanizing conditions.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.7.1. Con: If a UBI was similar in outcome to current welfare rates, and taxes were modified to alllow for the UBI, the effect on taxes would be neutral, and may indeed save a little as there would be no need for the apparatus of checking that a potential recipient is destitute or otherwise entitled to receive assistance. The second part of the claim is an attack on the concept of taxation in general, and irrelevant to discussion of UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.7.1.1. Con: Assuming the UBI was similar in outcome to current welfare rates, then no modification to the taxes would be required.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.7.1.2. Con: If you remove the apparatus of checking that a potential recipient entitled to the payment, and assume the UBI was similar in outcome to current welfare rates, then you're splitting a payment that currently only those who are entitled to welfare get out to everyone. Those currently on welfare\(the ones a UBI is designed to help\) would be worse off. Those currently not on welfare would be better off, basically receiving a refund on their current tax rate. Where do I sign up?
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.8. Pro: The means by which a UBI is procured and redistributed, through involuntary taxation enforced by threat of government violence, is unethical.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.8.1. Con: Taxes are not ordinarily enforced through threat of government violence - taxes intended for a UBI should not be any different, unless individuals initiate armed resistance.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.8.1.1. Con: In this case, you're assuming that imprisonment isn't a form of violence. All taxes both the moral and immoral ones are collected by threat of imprisonment.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.9. Con: Tax money is not generally obtained through threat of violence, rather legal means.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.9.1. Con: You're playing a semantic game, the phrase "at gunpoint" here is perhaps heavy handed, but is meant that the government will punish you if you don't comply. As in if you refuse to pay the tax, you'll go to jail. So you're options are to have your money confiscated unwillingly or go to jail. Try to argue the merits rather than the semantics.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.10. Pro: It's unethical to use force as a means of redistrubing private property. UBI can only be provided by massively increased taxes, denial to comply would result in use of force. You don't have a right to the fruits of other peoples labor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.10.1. Con: All fruits of peoples labor occur in a context of state investment. Whether that be roads you use to get to work, the education you utilized to give you those skills etc.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.10.1.1. Con: The state is funded by the people, e.g. roads are paid for by the very people that use them and education is paid for by the same people that get the education. In fact, it would probably be more efficient to remove the state from the equation, and have privately funded roads and privately funded education. In a way roads and education are a UBI used only for those specific purposes, but when those that have money fund the roads and education of those without it's still unethical.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.10.1.1.1. Con: Wealthy people pay more for society than poor \<- this sounds very ethical. If poor people would have to pay the same, this sounds unethical. \(Also most religions state the same.\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.9.10.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.2.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.10. Pro: A UBI further consolidates goverment's power which in turn robs the citizens of theirs. A gap in power always leads to a gap in wealth. Consider the federal government has already targeted its citizenry with the IRS without consequence.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.10.1. Con: The government is the people. It being stronger gives the people more power against over-bloated corporations that attempt to control their lives without their input.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.10.1.1. Pro: Corporations serve no interest but profit. The government should at least in theory be subservient to the wishes and best interests of the general public.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.10.2. Con: It has not, so far, been possible to operate a society without some form of government, and for that government to have expenses. It may be that those expenses could be met by voluntary contributions, but there does not seem to be any society where this has successfully happened.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11. Pro: Freedom is the state of not being imprisoned or enslaved and being able to act think or speak as one wants. UBI requires the enforcement of taxation, which by definition, impairs freedom.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1. Pro: The additional purchasing power that net beneficiaries gain from UBI has to be taken from those who are net payers. The latter group's freedom is reduced because they have less control over the fruits of their labor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.1. Con: For most state's considering implementing a UBI, a social welfare system in already in place. If those systems are replaced with a UBI, the net payer's group freedom is not reduced any more than it already is. And the beneficiaries gain the freedom to choose how their UBI is spent.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.1.1. Con: This is only true if taxation stayed the same as without UBI. However, most UBI models require substantial increase in spending compared to the current welfare state, and thus would require more taxation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.1.1.1. Pro: Tax reforms to support a UBI would need to be highly progressive and focus on taxing luxury items.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.1.1.1.1. Con: This could be argued to be a desirable and effective way for the tax system to operate, as it would tax the wealthy who could afford it to support those who are in need for essentials.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.2. Con: There is no such thing as the isolated fruits of ones labour. Is buying shares on the stock market not a labour comparable to someone making minimum wage on 60 hours a week cleaning toilets? Society is contract, not a mass of individual interest.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.3. Con: These individuals often rely on a market of consumers. The UBI would act as an economic stimulant that would benefit them too, in the long run.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.4. Con: Net tax payers still owe society money in exchange for having an educated population that consumes their goods/services.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.1.5. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.3.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.2. Con: Not being able to afford a decent quality of life, often due to life circumstances not reasonably within your control, is not a dignified way for a human to exist.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.11.3. Con: Spending the majority of your time merely trying to survive in an unfair \(and stressful\) financial situation does not offer many opportunities for freedom.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.12. Pro: The only way to guarantee one person an income is to take from another through coercive force. Government is the only legitimate user of physical force. It should only be used to protect, not to take. To believe it is okay is to not believe in moral absolutes. In other words, anything is okay that one deems so within their conscience based on their subjective views, rather than the objective facts of reality.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13. Pro: Armed robbery is pretty efficient, too. That doesn't mean it's fair to take the fruits of another's labor. The issue isn't whether it's efficient or not. It's whether it's moral or not.  What Bastiat called "[Legal Plunder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legal_plunder)" is not moral.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1. Con: It could equally be argued that leaving people to suffer and die in poverty is equivalent to a collective societal crime.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1.1. Con: This is not a necessary consequence of forgoing a tax-based welfare program. These kinds of programs interfere in the voluntary non-profit/philanthropic sector, as well as the commercial market sector. There is no telling what altruistic and possibly more effective uses a person would have had for that money had it not been taken away from them via taxation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1.1.1. Con: The private market and industries cannot be relied on to ensure the provision of basic human rights and reasonable quality of life.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1.2. Con: Rights, responsibilities, and things like crimes can only truly be applied to individuals, not societies. Societies are made up of individuals, but are not the same thing.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1.3. Con: Currently speaking, theft is on the books as a crime, not being charitable isn't a crime. So I'm going to guess that your claim is really something along the lines of "People should be charitable". Which I would agree with that sentiment, but I don't agree that a federal UBI is the way to handle it. Also, forced charity isn't really charity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.14. Pro: The State is unethical, because it's existance is based on the illegitimate monopoly of coercion, and it's source of income is based on theft \(taxes\), so it shouldn't exist at all. Therefore, UBI shouldn't exist too, because it's execution is dependable on the State's abillity to steal it's citizens \(or collect taxes\).
