1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.14.1. Con: Most people prefer living in a stable and orderly society to anarchy where force or coercion may be used by any individual against any individual and individual rights do not exist or are not enforced.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.15. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16. Pro: The only right anybody has in regards to income is the right to keep what they have worked for and legally obtained. A UBI robs people of their labor and gives it to those who haven't earned it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1. Pro: Life isn't free. Life requires work. A UBI pretends no one has to work, when clearly many have to work and a UBI prevents those who do work from benefitting from their work, since their production will be taken to support the unproductive and irresponsible. A UBI fundamentally reverses the consequences of good and bad choices that promote or inhibit life, which is to forcefully institute an anti-life system under the guise of being life-supporting.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.1. Con: Taking the position of "life requires work" might be seen as pretending that there is a job out there for everyone.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.1.1. Pro: With current levels of technology, it does not take one person's work to feed another. Consider a combine harvester reaping a field. It takes only one person to drive it, and a specialist to maintain it \(who also maintains other machines\). However, this field may produce enough grain to feed hundreds or even thousands. And while other work such as irrigation is also required to reach this output, that work is equally enhanced by technology, such as massive irrigation arrays.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.2. Con: During the great depression, [unemployment peaked at 25%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Depression#United_States) in the U.S. This contributed to the creation of social security, not a "let's deport all these guys to international waters" system. There is truth to "life isn't free", but past behaviors show that it isn't the sole / dominant philosophy by which we include or exclude people from our society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.2.1. Pro: Even in the event of exclusion, we still provide sustenance to imprisoned inmates.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.3. Con: In the hypothetical case of [half the population](http://www.businessinsider.com/over-half-of-jobs-in-the-eu-at-risk-2014-7) or in some cases [two thirds of the population](https://futurism.com/un-report-robots-will-replace-two-thirds-of-all-workers-in-the-developing-world/) being at risk of job loss, a UBI reverses consequences that weren't linked to either good or bad choices. In such a scenario, even many of the productive and responsible would benefit from a safety net.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4. Pro: The free market is a system where [everyone needs to contribute to society](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fJr2RO7g7jI) in order to be able to take from society. UBI would allow people to take much more than their fair share.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.1. Con: The term "fair share" is an subjective notion that establishes an arbitrary bar for economic participation. It is entirely possible for a person to contribute to a society without engaging in what some might deem an acceptable economic activity because of prejudices.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.2. Con: The free market is flawed because nobody can contribute from day one, and people can temporarily lose their ability to contribute through no fault of their own \(and do not deserve to die for this\). There is also the issue of suitably valuing contributions, with prices being easily perverted from the \(nigh-indeterminable\) "true" value of a contribution by any number of factors.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.3. Con: The social benefits of a UBI are far higher than its taxation detriment even for the richest. \(e.g. rich people don't live in Congo even though it has low taxes, it is just too dangerous\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.4. Con: The free market system is not based on the premise of contributing anything to society.  All one does is buys and sells goods or labor. The invisible hand does the rest.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.5. Con: The government already redistributes income through taxes and spending on social programs.  A UBI just makes this redistribution more rational and efficient.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.5. Con: This presumes a constant state of efficiency. Reality has demonstrated that society has become more efficient and this means it is entirely possible to reach an almost null efficiency in regards to human labor versus output.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.1.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.6. Con: This makes an assumption that UBI will be set at an enormously high level - a level that would certainly not be practical or feasible given the economic realities and the budgets of state and federal governments.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.6.1. Pro: UBI will most likely be set at levels that allow for the assurance of fresh food, water, electricity, and housing. Done right, UBI can be set at levels low enough.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7. Con: Not if it is financed from sources other than income taxes \(machine tax, carbon tax, value-added tax, financial transactions tax....\)
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1. Pro: Taxes other than income taxes could be raised in order to lessen the economic impact on individuals.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1. Pro: A UBI wouldn't have to be paid for by taxes. As with other forms of government spending, a UBI could be paid for by fiat spending \(i.e. 'printing money'\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.1. Con: Issuing enough currency to fund a UBI would add over four times the yearly increase in total income, causing runaway inflation and requiring even more new currency and new inflation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.1.1. Con: The inflationary constraint upon fiat currency is not some arbitrary number, but instead the real productive capacity of the economy in question. Given the profound levels of current waste and untapped potential, we are nowhere close to maxing out our real productive capacity. The best way to avoid critical inflation from fiat spending is thus to spend it on supporting real productive capacity, rather than upon speculation or rent-seeking \(title-income, interest, etc.\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.2. Pro: An economy using a fiat currency is not revenue constrained. The issuer of the currency doesn't need to "get" currency from anyone to spend. Taxes only serve to drive a currency's demand and to draw down currency to prevent inflation. Only currencies pinned to a commodity \(gold\) or fixed exchange rate are dependent upon revenue.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.3. Pro: The US dollar is a sovereign fiat currency. Congress creates it by decree with spending and can afford anything for sale in dollars with no structural revenue constraints or the threat of involuntary default on any obligation denominated in dollars. The only constraint on a fiat currency is inflation, not the ability to "fund" anything with taxation. Taxes are not "revenue" for fiat system, as the issuer of currency has no need to "get" currency to spend. Affordability is not on the table.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.4. Pro: The US dollar is a sovereign fiat currency that we can never run out of and we can never involuntarily fail to pay any obligation denominated in it. This takes "affordability" off the table.[youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=i35uBVeNp6c&t=169s) [neweconomicperspectives.org](http://neweconomicperspectives.org/2012/03/where-did-the-federal-reserve-get-all-that-money.html)
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.5. Con: Any fiat spending must be balanced against the effects it has on the economy - buying too much of something will cause it's price to increase, for example.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.1.6. Pro: In the future, countries will afford the UBI, as part of a [macro economic process](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/) when unemployment becomes too high and the economy is near collapse and/or major deflation. Modern monetary theory itself can prove this affordability, especially when a country has sovereignty over its' own currency. Please don't forget new currencies and international influence in regards to the UBI that will help other countries afford this, if the need arises. [rooseveltinstitute.org](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/)
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.2. Pro: Respectively lower income taxes for the top 1% and a lowered company tax rate a two areas of taxation that could be amended to ensure a USI, amongst other Universal services, could be adequately funded.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.3. Pro: A special automation tax on profits from the increased productivity of humanless automation can partly cover the cost of UBI.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.4. Pro: UBI through a tax on energy resources and financial transactions makes an increase on the income tax unnecessary, while making society more equal.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.5. Con: The costs of those forms of taxes are all ultimately met by Citizens; even if indirectly.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.6. Pro: If higher tax revenue is required, a [Land Value Tax](https://www.kialo.com/should-a-land-value-tax-be-introduced-to-replace-other-taxes-8742) could be used. Such a tax does not harm the economy and has other localized benefits beyond simply raising revenue.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.8. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.1.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.9. Con: UBI does not prevent people who work from benefitting - you're thinking of people on welfare. They will invariably find that by going into work and coming off welfare, they end up with less money than had they done no work at all.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.2. Con: This argument is based on a vision of human nature that is not further elaborated upon. Instead of advocating for a greedy nature, I would plea for a social nature that is core to human being. In the end, people do like to share what they have both with their beloved ones and strangers. A UBI would not kill productivity or rob the laborer, but instead answer to a key feature of human nature.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3. Con: In nature, only the strongest person has that right — they can take anything from anyone else. Societies exist because of the collective recognition that such a state of anarchy is unjust and unproductive; and so they construct a legal system that protects the vulnerable from random acts of violence. The laws that enable these protections are the only 'rights' that exist.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.1. Con: Capitalism is not a zero-sum game. Everybody gets something from an exchange. Even those you would call "weak."
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.1.1. Con: Assuming rational actors, both only benefit if the opposite party has no control over what you can put on the negotiating table. This restriction is something we have written into the law, the courts, and the application of that law.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.1.1.1. Pro: The fact that exchanges within the system is better for everyone, says nothing about the system as a whole. 
Capitalism removes access to most property from most people by allowing individual ownership of all things. So while two people trading to get what they want, more than what they already have, makes both better off, the property system that dictates the distribution of property makes most people worse off prior to the trade.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.1.2. Con: Trades or market transactions, are both not unique to capitalism. Capitalism is the result of our property laws.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.2. Con: If society as a whole decides what constitutes a law, then we cannot critique any of the rights it creates — anything that the collective implements is thus tautologically good.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.2.1. Con: This is a non sequitur. If there is a Constitutional majority that decides on what is a "right", it becomes one. The original claim was about what was A right, not what was right.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.2.2. Con: Society's laws are based on authorities such as divinity, or reason, and through those authorities it can be attacked, but these systems don't argue for unchangeable property rights to support people to the determent of all.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.2.3. Con: Society may be persuaded to change its mind. Most societies permit discussing forbidden acts  — at least up to a point.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.3.3. Con: You don't have to construct a "legal system" which upholds "rights" either. At least you don't have to use that language. There can be a group of people who organize a self-defense squad to protect each other and their property.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.4. Con: In almost every UBI proposal, laborers stand to gain the most, and those looking for labor are provided freedom to seek it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.4.1. Con: This generalizes labor. Some laborers stand to 'lose' \(with higher marginal tax rates\) and some laborers stand to 'win' \(those who are at dead-end jobs to make ends meet\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.4.1.1. Con: Not many people are too concerned about slightly inconveniencing rich people, even if they are working rich people.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5. Pro: UBI may sound nice in theory, but any political theory which is developed without putting human nature into consideration can never successfully be employed. Given people's inherent selfishness, the better idea is to let people keep what they've earned as much as possible.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.1. Con: Human nature includes both competitive and cooperative aspects as outlined by [game theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game_theory). So while this statement considers the compete nature of humanity it fails to consider the cooperate nature of humanity as well.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.2. Con: The notion of "letting people keep what they've earned as much as possible" has led to the macroeconomic situation for which we propose the remedy of UBI in the first place.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.3. Con: Progressive taxation for the purpose of redistribution is being employed successfully nearly everywhere in the world.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.4. Con: It may not be possible for people to earn much in the first place, even if they put in a lot of effort and time towards such an earning, due to random chance \(like getting hit by a car in winter or cancer\). A UBI helps soften these blows from random chance by providing at least some form of security to the populace.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.5. Con: Part of "human nature" is the ability to reason, think rationally and change one's own mind; a capacity for improvement which could be used to accept and embrace a UBI, if indeed one wasn't part of the many who would already welcome and embrace such a thing.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.6. Pro: As nature does not guarantee human beings subsistence without labor, a UBI is a violation of natural law and fosters complacency. This leads to social disorder.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.6.1. Con: Nature supplies water and food without labor, it isn't a necessity in nature so UBI wouldn't violate any law. Theres no reason to think UBI would lead to social disorder behavior if those peoples life is provided by that same society.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.5.6.2. Con: Humans already employ a wide variety of "unnatural" technologies and techniques in their day to day lives. There is no reason for humans \(or human societies\) to deliberately restrict themselves on the grounds of what is "\(un\)natural".
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.6. Pro: UBI requires redistribution of fairly earned income from those who require "assistance" to those who do not.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.6.1. Con: Not if it is financed from sources other than income taxes \(machine tax, carbon tax, funds from social security programs that would become obsolete, taxes on financial speculation, stimulus packages that are supposed to "trickle down" to the middle class\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.6.2. Con: This statement effectively says that those with income are the ones that need assistance. The fundamental tenet of civil society, and ultimately its purpose, is that we help each other survive. We do that by giving of ourselves when we can to others that need it. Meaning we take from those that can earn income and give it to those who can't. This is either poorly written or idealogically incongruent with the goal of living together.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.7. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8. Pro: A UBI, like other welfare programs, involves many taking money from a few. The many have no right to take from the few just because they want it.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8.1. Con: The few don't have the right to take the amount of money they do from the many, that doesn't stop them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8.2. Con: Capitalism is based upon expoitation of workers, as exploitation is the only way that a firm can make a profit \(see Marx\). Under our current laws, the few have the "right" to take from the many, but this is far removed from what is ethical.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8.3. Con: This assumes 'rights' are god-given, and not enshrined under law \(51% of people\) or Constitution \(67% of people\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8.4. Con: There are many ethical and philosophical systems which believe the greatest good for the most people should be served as much as possible.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.8.5. Con: 'Just because they want it' is not the principle upon which UBI rests. Rather, it rests on the principle that all economic surplus was created in part with some externalized costs \(unfairly\) borne by the populace as a whole. Thus, some of all wealth earned should be redistributed across the population in order to balance this inequality. UBI is a direct means to do this.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.9. Con: In a world in which labor is replaced by capital \(machine, robots, etc.\) in production processes, an ever greater share of income is generated by machines. These can be taxed heavily without robbing people of their labor.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.10. Con: If 'worked for' is a precondition to income, then inheritances should be outlawed. Earnings from dividends are also called into question, since it's the labor of others utilizing your capital that is creating the surplus - not your own labor. You would have to eliminate these forms of income \(and others\) to use this argument.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.10.1. Pro: Investment and speculation falls into a similar "unearned" category, by this logic.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.10.1.1. Con: Liberal ideology would suggest that investment returns are a result of risk managing.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.11. Con: Workers are robbed of the fruits of their labor each time a profit is earned by a boss.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.11.1. Con: Workers sell their time. By definition, no-one would buy that time, unless they can make a profit, so employment is most commonly a win-win exchange, not a win-lose exchange. This doesn't mean that employment cannot be exploitative, but profit is better understood as one party's share of benefits jointly earned. Even if that share is larger than would be fair, that still falls short of being theft.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.12. Con: Society affords its members certain benefits. Taxes, to a degree, can be considered payment for these favorable circumstances provided to the individual.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.13. Con: "A UBI robs people of their labor and gives it to those who haven't earned it" 
That's capitalism. The current winners are capitalists. Under UBI, the winners will be, on the whole, closer to 'everyone.'
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.14. Pro: If our economic system is inherently unjust, we should work on ways to improve how our economy rewards good behavior and punishes bad behaviour; rather than seeking to force a minimum standard of living in a way that decouples economic reward from productivity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.14.1. Con: The only way to have control over what our society deems worthy is through state intervention. This would lead to less freedom not more.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.14.1.1. Pro: But if you believe that human freedom is a core ideal, than oneself should be the ultimate decider on what behavior contributes to society. A UBI would provide precisely that freedom to people.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.14.2. Con: Seeking a minimum standard of living for everybody is required to comply with human rights and has nothing to do with economy.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.14.3. Con: Not everyone will agree on what constitutes "good behaviour" and "bad behaviour", and to impose such standards would often lead to violations of freedom.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.15. Con: Suggesting that people are only entitled to what they have worked for only produces a reasonable outcome in a society with no involuntary unemployment.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.13.
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.6. Con: Not helping the poor is immoral. A UBI helps the poor \(and \(thats the point of this discussion\) does it more efficiently and more gracefully so than other welfare schemes\).
1.1.5.2.11.1.25.6.1. Con: There are ways to help the poor without a UBI or welfare scheme, for example free-will charity. The poor are helped, no one is forced to give. Trading one immorality\(not helping the poor\) for another\(UBI\) when a solution exists that is completely moral\(charity\), is immoral in itself.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26. Pro: A UBI, by eliminating the need to work, strongly decrease the incentives that promote innovation.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1. Pro: UBI will negatively impact incentives for technological and economic progress. Be it fame, fortune, or philanthropy, we have all the great things in our life readily available because the brains behind them had something motivating them.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.1. Con: Innovation requires the ability to pursue avenues that might include risk. With no reasonable safety net, these risks may be avoided instead of pursued.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.2. Con: Motivations can be more than money. Warren Buffett is one of the richest people in the world, and still working at an age many people have retired.[g.co](https://g.co/kgs/Cwy8m9)
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3. Con: Money is not the motivator behind innovation. The great innovators in history were all driven by a will to help people, or by their own passion for their subject.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3.1. Con: The belief that all innovators in history were not driven by money may be hard to back up. If someone asked people like Thomas Edison, Steve Jobs, Elon Musk, Henry Ford or the Wright Brothers about their drive to go from a neat idea to a scale that could benefit society, money would likely be a leading factor. Useful innovation needs scale and scale requires a lot of work and money. Payoff must be greater than cost for innovation. Here is a good article about driving innovation [sciencedirect.com](http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S004873331730104X)
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3.1.1. Con: Thomas Edison claimed credit \(and eventually copyright\) out of greed for work that Nikola Tesla did in order to benefit humanity.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3.1.1.1. Pro: Nicola Tesla did work for the good of mankind, as can be seen in his plans for [universal energy](https://www.theguardian.com/science/2003/jul/03/research.science1), something that would be almost impossible to commercialize.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.3.1.1.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.4. Con: Much of the greatest technological innovation has been made possible through general research - research that was subsidized through public funds with no direct expectation of profit. The motivator here is not something that a UBI would destroy: human curiosity and the possibility of changing the world.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.4.1. Pro: Those motivations don't magically disappear because we get a little bit of money.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.4.1.1. Pro: Very few people who achieve fame and fortune cease pursuing those goals once they achieve a moderate amount of wealth. This shows that, to an extent at least, incentives to achieve those forms of success go above and beyond just getting rich.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.4.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.4.1.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.5. Con: Many mathematicians and scientists of the past were born into wealthy and affluent positions affording them the freedom to peruse their true interests benefiting society and not what keeps bread on their table.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.6. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.6.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.7. Pro: UBI reduces incentives for creating new products and advancing our civilization. Many good inventions and products were driven by the desire to become wealthy, if much of that wealth was taken away and redistributed to people not working, many would not try as hard to make more money.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.7.1. Con: Wealth will still be possible to accrue \(and thus still a motivation\), as income beyond your UBI can still be attained through work or the sale of goods, ideas, and services.
1.1.5.2.11.1.26.1.8. Con: [The idea that money is a motivator is untrue,](https://youtu.be/dgKKPQiRRag) and studies have proven that money only motivates up until a living wage, after which competence is a greater motivator.
1.1.5.2.11.2. Con: A UBI will help facilitate the starting of businesses by people who are already motivated for their own reasons but lack the means.
1.1.5.2.12. Con: A UBI would reduce the incentives for starting a business by \(potentially\) taxing business owners to the point that becoming a business owner would no longer be worth the risks.
1.1.5.2.13. Con: A UBI by definition is enough to cover the total cost of all the essential resources that an average human adult consumes in one year. Therefore, UBI could not allow for a person to pursue an education or start a business. If it did, it would be providing more than the essential resources that an average human adult consumes in one year.
1.1.5.2.13.1. Con: Under a UBI existential stress is lowered and free time is increased, giving the person time to complete their studies using free resources such as the library and the internet.
1.1.5.2.13.1.1. Con: While a self-educated individual can be incredibly intelligent. An education not obtained through an accredited institution such as a college or university has little impact on the person's hirability. A freely obtained education therefore might distract a criminal personality, for a time. However because it ultimately won't change the situation for most people, it will most likely not prevent much crime, and might produce smarter and thus more dangerous criminals.
1.1.5.2.13.1.1.1. Con: Under a UBI being hired to a job is not a requirement for survival.
1.1.5.2.13.1.1.1.1. Con: This is circular reasoning, the above claim was in response to a UBI providing the ability for people to get an education, the idea being that an education improves a person's ability to rise above and no longer need the UBI. If it's just about a person becoming educated with no intention of applying that education in the economic system, then the fact that person gets an education has no bearing on the discussion at hand.
1.1.5.2.13.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Economic attainment is not the only worthwhile pursuit in life.
1.1.5.2.13.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: The state shouldn't fund non-economic pursuits.
1.1.5.2.13.2. Con: Under current welfare programs, it is not possible to accumulate any extra wealth as any and all liquid wealth is seen as a signal the claimant does not need assistance and thus benefits are reduced. Under a UBI if you are smart about your finances, you can accumulate money as there is no means testing which means there is no cap on how much you can accumulate. If you can live frugally, you could accumulate a large amount of money and thus start a business.
1.1.5.2.13.2.1. Pro: This is really an important point. There are other claims that state people would work less \(less incentive\) with a UBI in place, but really, with other welfare programs that are gone if you work and earn something additionally, those are more discouraging people to work.
1.1.5.2.13.2.2. Con: As the parent claim states, "By definition a UBI is enough to cover the total cost of all essential resources". If there was anything left over to save, then you're being paid more than the total cost of all essential resources. If you're capable of accumulating anything the UBI is too much.
1.1.5.2.13.3. Con: Starting a business would still require savings for the initial investment. The UBI would just be a safety net, and cover personal expenses while the business is not yet making money.
1.1.5.2.13.3.1. Con: In order to get those initial savings, a person would have to be above the UBI level. Therefore, that person is then taking a risk by starting a business, if the risk pays off that person could become very wealthy, if it doesn't they may end up with nothing. Society shouldn't be responsible for funding people's risk taking behavior, even moreso if they don't get to share in the rewards. If they want to take a risk, they should take the responsibility too.
1.1.5.2.14. Con: Extending government grant and support schemes would better support new businesses, and also cost less.
1.1.5.2.14.1. Pro: For the same cost of a UBI, much more support to new businesses could be provided through targeted government grants and loans. The money saved from not paying 'wasted' UBI to the wealthy could go into initial grants for new small businesses, to get them off the ground.
1.1.5.2.14.2. Pro: Government support would be time bounded, which would give the necessary drive for businesses to work hard or fail. A UBI could indefinitely prop up businesses which may have no productive future.
1.1.5.2.14.3. Pro: Support could be conditional on a good business plan. This helps make sure that people are working on a business with actual potential, which would reduce stress and the [high chance of failure](https://www.fundera.com/blog/what-percentage-of-small-businesses-fail).
1.1.5.3. Con: Crime is an economic opportunity, as well as a social opportunity. The UBI isn't even remotely considerable enough to engage in either of the stated opportunities listed.
1.1.5.3.1. Con: It does, however, create an alternative option and opportunity - it may not be enough on its own, but would be a factor in tilting the scales away from crime and making it seem less necessary or appealing, the risks less proportionate to the rewards.
1.1.5.4. Pro: A UBI could prevent government entities \(like counties or states\) from [turning to illegal or crime-encouraging activities out of desperation to repair their insubstantial economy, such as marijuana and gambling \(i.e. casinos\)](http://www.newsweek.com/unexpected-side-effects-legalizing-weed-339931).
1.1.5.5. Con: Not all crime stems from a lack of economic opportunities, for example white collar crime.
1.1.5.6. Con: Crime is always a personal decision. Blaming lack of economic opportunity disposes with personal responsibility for one's own actions.
1.1.5.7. Con: In wealthy countries, like the U.S., there are always economic opportunities. They may be hard to take advantage of, but not impossible. See any rags to riches success story for anecdotal evidence. Which is sufficient for this point, if it's possible, even for one person to actually accomplish, then it's possible for others to follow in their footsteps and duplicate their results.
1.1.5.8. Con: Not all poor people are criminals, thus it must be possible to be poor and not turn to crime. In fact, most poor people aren't criminals.
1.1.5.9. Con: There are criminals that aren't poor, many that are already super rich.
1.1.5.10. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.13.
1.1.6. Pro: If a UBI replaced benefits that have restrictive limiting criteria, then it would reduce crime caused by benefit fraud as you would not need to notify changes of circumstances as extensively. For example, [this would not have happened](http://www.tenby-today.co.uk/article.cfm?id=112354&headline=Suspended%20sentence%20for%20benefit%20fraud&sectionIs=news&searchyear=2017).
1.1.7. Pro: A study by [Akee et. al \(2010\)](https://www.aeaweb.org/articles?id=10.1257/app.2.1.86) shows that a government cash transfer might significantly increase education for children coming from poor backgrounds as well as decreasing the probability of minor criminal offences.
1.1.7.1. Con: A cash transfer reducing future criminal offenses is unrelated to UBI. If a complex welfare system different from UBI injected more money into poor background children education than UBI, this argument would be against UBI.
1.1.8. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.
1.1.9. Con: A UBI forces hard working people to give money to the least-deserving individuals that they try not to give to normally, like [criminals, especially hard ones](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/30/business/stockton-basic-income.html) \(evidenced by being rejected from jobs they apply to\). Since people are still rewarded after committing a crime with a UBI, people will feel less consequences in committing crimes, which can drive crime up.
1.1.9.1. Pro: This is especially true with UBI experiments, because the [unemployed criminals](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/30/business/stockton-basic-income.html) most likely will get first benefits from a UBI. Criminals will get a greater benefit from a UBI than hard-working individuals, at least in the beginning of the process.
1.1.9.1.1. Con: Criminals would receive the same amount as everyone else. Universal basic income isn't given in different amounts or at different times.
1.1.9.1.2. Con: This focuses more on not helping criminals, universal health care is allowed to criminals too.
1.1.9.1.3. Pro: This is especially true with UBI experiments, because the unemployed criminals most likely will get first benefits from a UBI, due to trials. In trials, entities need to be selective, so they pick the strongest candidates possible, which is more likely to be criminals than an workers. Thus, they get picked and attain benefits before anyone else.
1.1.9.2. Con: UBI programs could be designed exclude criminals \(especially while in or at least a year after getting out of prison\).
1.1.9.3. Con: This might be the case if UBI is considered a reward but that isn't what it is. To assume that UBI has an impact on consequence for crimes is also quite a stretch.
1.1.9.4. Con: A UBI would be no greater reward to criminals than different welfare systems already are. Besides, this "reward" seems irrelevant compared to the punishment of being in prison.
1.1.9.5. Con: You will be hard pressed to find a better option to deal with criminality than to give them a baseline, not giving them anything will drastically increase their chance of repeated crime.
1.1.9.6. Con: When a person has served their time in jail, it is best for the nation to accept them as complete citizens again. In this way they are able to break free from any elements which lead them to criminal actions. Economic opportunity is a large element of recidivism, and adding barriers to re-entry to society only perpetuates that cycle. This includes being denied jobs for previous offenses \(barring crimes of trust for positions of trust\)
1.1.10. Con: Criminals would be more likely to get away with the crimes they commit.
1.1.10.1. Pro: If given money through a UBI, criminals will have more power in getting out of their punishment \(like being able to pay for [bail more easily](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IS5mwymTIJU)\).
1.1.10.1.1. Con: Being able to pay bail does not mean they get away with a crime. This could only be the case if UBI is not taken into account when bail is determined.
1.1.10.2. Con: The opposite might happen where if funds are diverted away from the justice system to pay for a UBI; then more people could be convicted of crimes they did not commit due to a lack of a proper defense \(such as from a [lack of properly-funded public defenders](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=USkEzLuzmZ4) to help them\).
1.1.11. Pro: A society’s progress is both measured financially and morally. The poorer the society the higher the crime and moral decay rate. UBI could indeed help societies to address low-income citizens’ sense of helplessness thus providing them with hope and life meaning. UBI would also provide these people with some basic financial means, which will help them leave a decent and more secure life.
1.1.12. Pro: UBI reduces stress which leads to less mental illness and the crimes associated with mental illness.
1.1.12.1. Pro: Unresolved, compounded [stress](https://www.nimh.nih.gov/health/publications/stress/index.shtml) can lead to mental disorders like depression and anxiety. With money being a [major stressor](http://www.apa.org/news/press/releases/2015/02/money-stress.aspx) of 64% of Americans-particularly in the lower income bracket-a UBI can decrease this stress by increasing the amount of money contributed to a household.
1.1.12.2. Pro: [Financial stress](http://www.acseduonline.com/blog-stress-causes-crime-95.aspx) can cause crime when there is a lack of qualifications and social support for at-risk individuals. A UBI provides extra financial support that may relieve this stress.
1.1.12.3. Con: Implementing a UBI would require massive increases in taxation on the middle class, resulting in greater stress for a large swath of the population.
1.1.12.3.1. Pro: A UBI requires higher tax rates that harm the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.1. Con: The bulk of tax increases could be levied upon the super-wealthy. In the US in particular this would only marginally shift that top tax rate [back to its historic norm of 50%+](http://www.ctj.org/pdf/regcg.pdf) which did not seem to overly constrain growth between 1917 and 1987.
1.1.12.3.1.1.1. Pro: There already are initiatives founded by [millionaires](https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2016/mar/21/40-millionaires-ask-new-york-raise-taxes-wealthy-1-percent-plan-fairness) to raise the tax for the rich. This shows their willingness to contribute more to society.
1.1.12.3.1.1.1.1. Con: There several offshore tax schemes - such as those discovered by the [Panama Papers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panama_Papers) - that prove wealthy individuals are willing to try to hide their assets to contribute less.
1.1.12.3.1.1.2. Con: As the very richest are able to [shape tax policy](https://www.nytimes.com/2015/12/30/business/economy/for-the-wealthiest-private-tax-system-saves-them-billions.html?_r=0), it is unlikely they would accept extensive tax increases.
1.1.12.3.1.1.2.1. Con: That doesn't mean that it can't happen in a democracy.
1.1.12.3.1.1.3. Pro: A UBI would have a much greater positive impact on the economy that tax cuts, as giving $1 to those with lower incomes has three times the growth effects on GDP as compared to giving the same $1 to high income individuals, due to the economic multiplier effect. [huffingtonpost.com](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/the-economist-just-came-o_b_7447312.html)
1.1.12.3.1.1.4. Pro: There would be enough wealth if it were to be distributed down from [billionaires](http://time.com/money/5112462/billionaires-made-so-much-money-last-year-they-could-end-extreme-poverty-seven-times/). No one needs that much money \([apparently](https://qz.com/1211957/how-much-money-do-people-need-to-be-happy/) money doesnt increase happiness after $105,000 per year\). \(There should be a cut off point; if you make more than 500,000 a year \(and slowly decrease it to 105,000 to reduce shock\), you only keep 200,000, the rest goes to the government, who can make much better use of it.\)
1.1.12.3.1.1.4.1. Con: There would be difficulties implementing this, as many wealthy people would feel robbed of the fruits of "their" hard work, ignoring or overlooking the advantages of their privilege.
1.1.12.3.1.2. Pro: In the short run, tax increases have a negative impact on demand. As households and companies have less available income and capital due to higher taxes, they will spend and invest less.
1.1.12.3.1.2.1. Con: A UBI as additional form of income mitigates this negative effect, especially for those with lower incomes.
1.1.12.3.1.2.2. Con: However they will have paid a fair tax rate that benefits all citizens. Instead of low taxes lining the pockets of investors and private companies delivering the universal services a government should provide its people.
1.1.12.3.1.3. Pro: In the long run [high marginal tax rates](http://www.taxpolicycenter.org/briefing-book/how-do-taxes-affect-economy-long-run) have a negative impact on the supply by discouraging work, saving, investment, and innovation.
1.1.12.3.1.4. Pro: A UBI will increase taxes for those making money, thus pushing capital-makers to move elsewhere, and making the UBI financially unsustainable
1.1.12.3.1.4.1. Pro: If the scheme is national/regional only, and that net cost to some is too high, they may take their financial and/or other resources elsewhere. Would the benefits of living and doing business in a UBI society outweigh the higher costs in tax?
1.1.12.3.1.5. Con: Tax rates will not increase in an economy-hurting way, as simultaneously other taxed-based welfare programs can be reduced.
1.1.12.3.1.5.1. Con: The marginal incentive not to work for lower income workers, coupled with the need to find more tax revenue, results in a clear need to raise taxation. To replace grants for the poor with an income for everyone implies that cutting old social spending projects will be insufficient.
1.1.12.3.1.5.1.1. Con: A marginal incentive not to work does not equal not working. This could even make working hours more efficient due to increased drive of efficiency in the marketplace.
1.1.12.3.1.5.2. Con: Reduced spending on things like food stamps reduces effective income. This reduces the effective gains of a UBI.
1.1.12.3.1.6. Pro: Increased taxation and reduced spending on targeted welfare will result in increased pressure on the incomes of the poor.
1.1.12.3.1.6.1. Con: A UBI is inherently additive to the incomes of the poor, as long as they are working and the minimum wage is kept to inflation, they have increased income from a UBI.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2. Con: With a progressive taxation system that redistributes wealth, individuals with a low socio-economic status would likely receive more than they put in.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1. Con: A UBI would take money away from the rich, which would greatly decrease job creation and economic growth.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.1. Pro: With a decrease in overall wealth comes a decrease in overall jobs because the wealthy employ everyone! It's a slippery slope that we're better off not starting down.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2. Pro: But this [hurts the rich](http://www.ncpa.org/sub/dpd/index.php?Article_ID=25285). Which, in turn, destroys jobs and creates more poor people.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.1. Con: This is the problem with any redistribution scheme: the rich can't be hurt because they control the prices and the jobs.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.2. Pro: The rich can't be hurt; they control the prices and jobs available in their industry.  This can reduce jobs once they start compensating for the economic shift, however.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.2.1. Con: Powerful industries can be hurt. For example, this occurs through the effectiveness of anti-trust laws when they were properly enforced.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.2.1.1. Pro: Boycotts of a sufficiently large scale can also hurt a company or industry, as can strikes and industrial action.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.3. Con: Middle class people, not the rich, are more likely to create the small businesses that generate new jobs.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.4. Pro: If these rich backers of UBI are so enthuastic, they should use their own money to demonstrate proof of concept.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.2.5. Con: The rich can only benefit from a wealthier middle and working class. With more money being spent businesses will have higher profits in turn giving the rich more money.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.3. Con: A UBI may decrease job creation and even decrease the number of existing jobs. But it will not decrease economic growth: it will stimulate the development of automation as low paying jobs disappear. If anything, the rich who invest in automation will stand to profit heavily from this development.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.3.1. Con: More people replacing their entire income with UBI would create a large amount of "stagnant" capital that never got invested or put toward anything. Some individuals may do well entrepeneurially when they couldn't before - but it is unproven that this is the majority case.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.3.1.1. Con: if people are living off capital, it is not stagnated, it is being used, capital only stagnates when it is saved.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4. Pro: Income inequality is not inherently bad because it provides good economic incentives. The promise that you can make it to the top if you work hard makes it more likely that people advance their economic position.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.1. Con: These are the twin myths of meritocracy and trickle down economics, neither of which have ever been proven in a real world setting.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.2. Con: Rich people save a higher amount of their income relative to poor people. If you want to show that trickle down economics work, you need to show how burying money would stimulate the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3. Con: The average person should ask if the reason they are not rich is due to a lack of trying, and evaluate the above claim based on their conclusion.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.1. Pro: The old adage of "it's not what you know, it's who you know" often proves true; growing up and/or living in wealthy circles makes one more likely to receive opportunities for further wealth.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.2. Pro: Investment is easier when one has greater access to starting capital and/or loans.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.3. Pro: People who are already among the wealthy \(or who belong to families which are\) have access to information and strategies which are not commonly taught or provided to the lower classes. Even if they can eventually gain access to this information \(through courses, etc.\), it is not a routine part of their formative years and education.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.4. Con: This argument asks us to ignore the sloth, and underhandedness characteristic of many, if not all, members of the upper class.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.5. Con: This asks people who know that they work hard long hours to accept that the only reason they are still in poverty is that they aren't working hard enough. Anyone who does not see the problem here is encouraged to go out, and try doing this directly.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.6. Con: If that were true, we would have miners and road workers as presidents.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.7. Con: If this promise is a false promise, as there is reason to believe, it effectively amounts to deception at best and slavery by deception at worst.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.8. Con: Income inequality is an inherently bad thing, as it leads to stress, relationship breakdown and crime amongst other things.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.9. Con: The [great gatsby curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Gatsby_curve) shows that the greater inequality is, the lower social mobility is - so that even though there might be greater incentives, its actually much harder to get to them.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.10. Con: People like all animals do not need to "better themselves". We need to have needs met such as food, water, community, etc.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5. Pro: It doesn't help the economy to reward low-paid jobs, but [higher paid jobs](http://www.nytimes.com/1990/01/17/opinion/the-reagan-boom-greatest-ever.html). Higher paid jobs require ambition, motivation, and hard work. It's only worth it if there are incentives to do it.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.1. Con: Trickle down economics don't work. We've tried them before, and come up with nothing. Even in theory, the richer you are, the higher a proportion of your income you save. Insisting they work at this point is nothing but a bold faced lie.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.2. Con: Higher paying jobs still reward people more then lower paying ones.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.3. Con: Motivations are worthless if you don't have the ability to act on them. You can't start a business without an office you can't afford; you can't work hours of manual labor if you are starving; you can't get a job as a teacher if you can't afford to learn the knowledge you wish to impart.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.3.1. Con: Although opportunity is not perfectly equal, a degree of equality is offered through the [ability to receive investment/loans](https://www.inc.com/guides/2010/07/how-to-finance-your-business.html). Even if someone is very poor, if they have a very good business idea, they will likely be able to receive some startup money.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.3.1.1. Con: It seems like every head of a successful business has a story about how they were poor, and destitute, and someone gave them a shot. But these businesses are far from the total of the story as most people, even with great ideas, don't get those loans and their stories are buried by survival bias.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4. Con: Higher paid jobs are not the result of some 'virtue' poor people lack, but opportunities they were given and poor people weren't — like an education or an inheritance.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.1. Pro: Some jobs are also undervalued because they don't produce physical products, such as education and childcare.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.2. Pro: Members of wealthy families often have the opportunity to gather capital very easily in order to found a business or make investments.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.1.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.2.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.3.3.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.5. Con: This argument mirrors almost perfectly the [gospel of property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prosperity_theology), [which tells people with cancer should seek treatment by giving money to a rich person who merely talks about how rich he is, instead of seeking the medical attention they need](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7y1xJAVZxXg). Both put people with wealth on a pedestal, and lie to the poor and desperate that if they just give to these 'purer' people \(in this case, by working for them\) they can be better and more successful people.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.5.1. Con: This misses the point of the grandparent, which partially emphasizes that higher-paying jobs tend to be more valuable to + lacked by society, since the price signals of the market \(as established in economics 101\) indicate what society wants more or less of.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.7. Con: Money does not measure the worth of a person, their contribution to the society does. An individual's monetary worth measures only their talent in amassing a resource and is a solitary act benefiting only one individual. An act that in turn deprives others of access to that  resource and impedes contribution by the many to society. If money is the only measure of personal worth, then one's life is a meaningless pursuit of coin and paper that ceases to have value upon one's death.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6. Pro: UBI inadvertently makes the economy less efficient as it takes money from those who would otherwise invest and save that money and who have the most efficient network and resources with which to invest that money. It entrusts those who could not otherwise procure that money, to save or invest it in a way that adds value to the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.1. Con: There's a break-even point where the contentment of the people is necessary to keep a stable market. Automation is coming so labor demand will slowly evaporate. In that light it's not always less efficient.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.2. Pro: Buying more consumer items such as TVs, sports tickets, etc \(the most likely use\) doesn't create value — investing does.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.2.1. Con: Under driving capitalist theory, consumption actually does create value: [the people spending that money value the items they are consuming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dollar_voting), so those companies get the money to grow, since they make valuable products.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3. Con: Saving does not grow the economy. The money sits there in the bank not being used, maybe, just maybe, some of it is loaded out to pay for an investment, but for the most part it just sits there, doing nothing.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.1. Pro: [Seventy percent](http://money.cnn.com/2015/06/01/news/economy/american-consumer-saving-not-spending/index.html) of economic activity comes from American consumers. With more people saving money, less money is in external circulation resulting in a slow-growing economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2. Con: The saved funds are not simply put under the mattress but become reinvested in order to achieve a profitable return. Therefore, the saved proportion is available as loanable funds adding fresh capital stocks to the markets and as a result, increase the markets' [productive capacity](https://mnmeconomics.wordpress.com/2011/07/17/why-the-saving-rate-is-important/).
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.1. Con: Reinvestment is not as good for the economy as actually spending money is. An estimate of [the consumption multiplier is somewhere above 1.36](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiscal_multiplier#United_States_of_America), while [the savings multiplier, even if we assume the highest interest rate on a savings account is undervalued by a factor of 20, is 1.26.](http://money.cnn.com/2013/10/01/pf/savings-account-yields/index.html)
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.2. Pro: Money in savings accounts is used by banks as collateral in their loaning and investment activities. In this way it doesn't 'sit there' but rather enables other productive uses.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.1.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.3. Pro: This would be true only if savings were stored in a vault or under the bed, but this is false if savings are in the bank. If banks did not benefit in any way from savings accounts, they would have already discontinued this service. Savings accounts in banks do indeed grow the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.1.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.4. Con: Investing in new ventures is good and all, but if consumers can't afford to buy your product, it's not going to grow the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7. Pro: The rich employ everyone else. So rewarding higher-paying jobs is, actually, much better for the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.1. Con: Rich people do not employ everybody, or even most people: ["Small firms accounted for 64 percent of  the net new jobs created between 1993  and 2011 \(or 11.8 million of the 18.5  million net new jobs\). Since the latest  recession, from mid-2009 to 2011, small  firms, led by the larger ones in the cat - egory \(20-499 employees\), accounted for  67 percent of the net new jobs."](https://www.sba.gov/sites/default/files/FAQ_Sept_2012.pdf)
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.1.1. Pro: Businessmen \(or entrepreneurs\) of all types employ everybody. Big businesses have more jobs, there just aren't as many of them.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.1.2. Con: A UBI would destroy small businesses, because it would take people out of the workforce and require these businesses to pay higher taxes and fees.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.4.1.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.7.2. Con: In the current age of information technology, a very small amount of people can gather a huge amount of money without any need to significantly increase the employee base of a company. Any major website company is a perfect example of that.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.8. Con: Automation will greatly decrease job creation anyway. The rich will invest in automation, not new jobs.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.9. Pro: A UBI would require the government to collect a huge amount of taxes. Since, depending on how much the UBI is going to pay, has to be payed by the richer half of the working population, that would likely be diminished through emigration, experiencing a high utility loss.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.10. Con: Job creation is driven by demand of goods and services, not the amount of wealth the rich have. UBI would increase such demand, and so creates jobs.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.10.1. Con: Only the "rich" have the capital to start such enterprises as to produce these new products and services you are suggesting. Starting business isn't a cheap thing, you need tens of thousands to hundreds of thousands of dollars of liquid capital for a bank to even consider giving you a loan to help you get started.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.11. Pro: Forcible income redistribution from those who produce wealth to those who do not leads to a less optimal allocation of wealth for the purposes of growing the economy, and economic growth has many positive externalities that make it the primary vehicle for raising the standard of living.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.12. Pro: The harm done to the poor as a result of the economic growth lost to the higher tax burden would outweigh the benefits the poor would derive from higher welfare benefits, because the former is compounding while the latter is not.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.13. Con: This depends on the taxation scheme in operation, in a progressive taxation system the effective rate of taxation can be closely mirrored by a UBI system for all incomes above a certain point. [These graphs](https://codepen.io/adamdharrington/full/GdpMyG/) reflect Ireland's tax system and can be adjusted to different UBI levels and tax rates.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.14. Con: Since job growth resides in implementing good ideas \(like inventions\) that make money, placing money into the hands of more people, allow more ideas to grow and thus stimulate the job creation/economic growth. Monopolies/oligopolies \(with their copyrights\) tend to stymie the economy rather than grow it.
1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.14.1. Con: If copyright laws are in effect, the masses cannot implement anything they create anyway. That makes the economy not able to grow, even when money is placed in the hands of inventors. In the end, the rich still get richer.
1.1.12.3.1.7. Pro: The reduced incentive to work for those who are guaranteed an income will reduce government tax revenue.
1.1.12.3.1.7.1. Con: Income taxation is not the only source of government revenue. Money is also made from exports, property taxes, the sale of bonds, and government investments.
1.1.12.3.1.7.2. Con: Simultaneously, the revenue from sales taxes is likely to increase as a UBI provides a secure source of income for citizens that can be used for consumption purposes.
1.1.12.3.1.7.3. Con: Most people do not work to accomplish a standard of living that consists of basic subsistence. The overall incentives to work do not change because of a UBI.
1.1.12.3.1.8. Con: The IMF recently released a report which claims that higher tax would not negatively affect growth [theguardian.com](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/oct/11/imf-higher-taxes-rich-inequality-jeremy-corbyn-labour-donald-trump)
1.1.12.3.1.8.1. Pro: Misinformation by government that lower taxes are beneficial to society and the citizenry is a fallacy perpetrated to ensure votes at the ballot box.
1.1.12.3.1.9. Pro: Any democratic country that implemented a UBI would be forced to raise taxes on its citizens. Governments do not produce money, they only have the money that is taxed from the citizenry. Therefore, in order to come up with the money to pay for the UBI, taxes would have to be raised.
1.1.12.3.1.9.1. Pro: A UBI is fiscally unsustainable due to the government's expenses. Either, we'll have to universally increase taxes \(which would be impossible, because lots of countries already have incredibly high tax rates \[Belgium, Sweden,...\]\), or countries will have to cut costs in certain departments, which would not be a very popular decision.
1.1.12.3.1.9.2. Pro: Discussing a UBI as a benefit to a population without  acknowledging that it creates an equal obligation to that same population is not intellectually honest. If one is born with a right to UBI then one would also be born with an equal obligation to pay for it. It's another not-so-clever scheme for income redistribution. If a UBI was renamed "Selective Additional Tax" for some citizens, it would not be such a popular topic.
1.1.12.3.1.9.2.1. Con: The obligation that comes with UBI is like that of defending the nation \(the draft\) and upholding the law \(jury summons\). Normally these would be a violation of individual liberty but they are not because they are required to maintain the nation and the nation is required to maintain social spending. Citizens subject themselves to the possibility of obligatory service in exchange for receiving entitlement spending.
1.1.12.3.1.9.2.1.1. Con: A nation needs protection and borders to prevent, or at least deter, external attacks. Nations do not need a UBI.
1.1.12.3.1.9.2.1.1.1. Con: A UBI specifically might not be necessary but it seems most nations consider social safety nets are as essential as defense. For example, the U.S. spends [more on entitlements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_federal_budget#/media/File:CBO_Infographic_2016.png) than the military. That safety net could be UBI or something else, but for the sake of this debate we are assuming social spending is necessary.
1.1.12.3.1.10. Con: With the introduction and proliferation of automation and AI into the production systems, higher income tax rates for businesses would mostly affect activities which are not performed by humans. This would not have a negative impact in the economy.
1.1.12.3.1.10.1. Con: Without humans being a source of production, there is little incentive for governments to support humans over simply putting the money back into AI and other productive sources.
1.1.12.3.1.11. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.
1.1.12.3.1.12. Pro: UBI would create a vicious circle: As the after-tax income of more and more economic activities falls below UBI, taxes on the remaining workers must rise, marginalising the next layer of workers. The number of dropouts from the production system will rise until final economic breakdown and total poverty everywhere.
1.1.12.3.1.13. Con: Tax is largely raised when money moves from one individual to another. This regular cash injection will boost the economy and provide more tax back as a result, limiting the need for higher tax.
1.1.12.3.1.14. Pro: It's not per se that meeting poverty is too expensive, but that to perpetually prevent poverty would be an ever increasing cost \(poverty can't be eliminated once and for all\).
1.1.12.3.1.14.1. Con: If the UBI rate was explicitly targeting poverty it could easily switch to using an absolute, rather than relative, definition of poverty to account for the distortions in income a UBI would provide.
1.1.12.3.1.14.2. Con: This would not be true if UBI was funded by taxation and redistribution. Taxation does not inherently cause inflation.
1.1.12.3.1.14.3. Pro: UBI simply moves the poverty line to a higher income, because poverty in a country is usually measured at 60% of median income. Adding x-amount of money to all income simply increases poverty treshold. The 60% idea is based on the principles of social inclusion, and the idea that producers market towards median income to maximise sales.
1.1.12.3.1.14.3.1. Con: Objective standards of living, however, can be improved.
1.1.12.3.1.14.4. Con: This depends on the definition of poverty used. A UBI may be used to ensure an acceptable standard of living for all members of the community in a country.
1.1.12.3.1.15. Con: Taxes will be more happily paid since UBI contributes to the well-being of everyone in society. This will reduce the incentive for people to avoid or evade tax. This additional tax income will reduce the necessity for tax increases. UBI allows the liberty for people to spend it as wishes; quite different nowadays you pay and the Government decides what to invest it in.
1.1.12.3.1.15.1. Pro: It removes the paternalistic role of Government to say how taxes should be invested since everyone would spend their UBI in what they prefer. Given that a certain percentage of taxes still would go to public services enjoyed by everybody.
1.1.12.3.1.15.2. Pro: It would lower [tax resistance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tax_resistance).
1.1.12.3.1.15.3. Con: The more "money productive" people would be at a loss and discouraged to be as useful as s/he was to exchange valuable work with other people. It would increase tax resistance in some groups.
1.1.12.3.1.16. Con: By allowing people to live healthier lives, there will be major cost savings in the healthcare sector, which is publicly funded in many developed countries.
1.1.12.3.1.16.1. Con: Even if the population is healthier, the cost of general care \(regular check-ups, vaccinations, maternity, etc.\), emergency care \(traffic accidents\) and [elderly care](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2016/feb/01/ageing-britain-two-fifths-nhs-budget-spent-over-65s) will still be very high and may be crippled by a UBI. To avoid that, an increase in taxes may still be necessary.
1.1.12.3.2. Con: There are alternative sources of financing, such as public dividends, that may be used to sustain UBI without creating extra pressure on the layperson's economics.
1.1.12.3.3. Con: The tax effects on people who are already well-off and stable would not be as significant as the benefits to people whose quality of life and/or prospects would be significantly improved by a UBI.
1.1.12.4. Pro: Stress has been affecting the lives of those living in the cities [so much](https://www.stress.org/daily-life/), mainly work, money and the future, that it is literally [changing the pathways in our brains](https://www.nature.com/articles/nature10190) and there is [data proof](http://www.gwern.net/docs/nature/2010-peen.pdf) that living in the cities or urban areas in general has a direct impact on our mental health and how prone we are to disorders, in developed countries.
1.1.12.4.1. Pro: That is [debilitating to society](http://psycnet.apa.org/buy/2004-15935-004) as a whole, no matter who you are as [stress is infectious in and of itself](https://www.healthline.com/health-news/stress-cues-from-others#2) AND can make you more [susceptible to other diseases](https://www.clinicaleducation.org/resources/reviews/chronic-stress-increases-infection-susceptibility/).
1.1.13. Con: Many crimes do not occur due to economic problems. For instance many crimes stem from issues with [drugs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drug-related_crime), [mental illness](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0093854807312851), or [relationships](http://law.jrank.org/pages/1177/Family-Relationships-Crime-General-socialization-crime.html).
1.1.13.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.1.
1.1.13.2. Con: There is a [positive relationship](https://borgenproject.org/addiction-poverty-connected/) between poverty and substance \(drugs\) abuse.
1.1.13.2.1. Con: Positive relationship aka correlation doesn't imply causation. The second sentence in the linked above article states: " The direction of causation is unclear". The strongest claim that article makes is that poverty cause drug abuse is: "...and perhaps even some causal effect." Also it completely ignores that the can be no casual effect in any directions i.e. that there is other hidden factor\(s\) that has casual relation to both poverty and drug abuse.
1.1.13.3. Pro: UBI is most pressing to individuals below the poverty line. Some of these individuals are below the poverty line because of addictions. The UBI would enable addictive behavior.
1.1.13.3.1. Con: This suggests that not giving them this benefit would stop them from being addicted. There is no reason to believe this.
1.1.13.3.2. Con: Staying off of addictive substances is a real struggle, and one needs the means to be able to do it.
1.1.13.3.3. Con: Some people in poverty may have addictions, but not all. There are many other causes of, and contributors to, poverty.
1.1.13.3.4. Con: It may be worth inadvertently enabling some addictive behaviour in order to deliver the other benefits of the UBI, such as assisting those in poverty for other reasons.
1.1.13.3.5. Pro: UBI would provide addictive, impulsive personalities with an endless source of income - enslaving them to impulsivity and rendering change impossible for many.
1.1.13.3.5.1. Con: If someone could not change with the advantages offered by a UBI, it's unlikely they would change while suffering the disadvantages of living in poverty.
1.1.13.4. Con: A UBI could also give people with drug addictions or mental illness the ability to pay for assistance with the issue.
1.1.13.5. Con: -> See 1.1.12.
1.1.14. Con: Most major economic crimes are motivated by a greed for acquiring wealth above a subsistence level. A UBI is unlikely to decrease this drive.
1.1.14.1. Pro: Four of the five most committed crimes in the US are [property crimes](http://www.criminaljusticedegreehub.com/what-are-the-most-common-crimes-in-the-united-states/), despite the existence of existing social safety nets.
1.1.14.1.1. Con: This could equally be evidence that existing social safety nets are significantly insufficient.
1.1.14.1.2. Con: The US social safety net is [one of the lowest in the world](http://siteresources.worldbank.org/SAFETYNETSANDTRANSFERS/Resources/281945-1124119303499/SSNPrimerNote25.pdf).
1.1.14.1.3. Con: The type of crime committed in the US has no bearing on this discussion unless you can provide a link between that type of crime and receipt of benefits
1.1.14.2. Pro: In Queensland, Australia, 81% of criminals charged with property crimes [said they did so to support a drug habit](http://www.aic.gov.au/crime_types/property%20crime/drugs.html).
1.1.14.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.13.2.
1.1.14.3. Pro: Organised crime groups attempt to get rich from crime; not merely subsist. Internationally their acts [are worth $870 billion USD a year](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2012/jul/16/organised-crime-worth-560bn).
1.1.14.4. Con: Petty crimes can be more dangerous to individuals' health and feelings of safety, however. Such  crimes would be more intuitive to desperate individuals: a knife-point mugging is more likely to occur to them than major fraud.
1.1.14.5. Con: Even if true, a lack of reduction in economic crime does not mean the UBI is not effective in reducing other types of crime, namely the ones committed to acquire basic sustenance.
1.1.15. Pro: A UBI serves as an economic cushion and a de-stress factor, by providing society with alternatives to the most common problems found in current society with the financial safety it provides. That means less stress cycling through from person to person during interactions, which also betters behavior as there will be less stressed people to interact with.
1.1.16. Con: Rising poverty causes more crime. With an UBI, the economy will enter a vicious  circle where more and more workers will continually drop out of the productive sector as post-tax post-inflation incremental income from working continually falls. The nominal UBI will buy less and less real produced goods, poverty will rise, and crime will reach unprecendented levels.
1.1.17. Con: There is a higher correlation between unemployment and crime than between income level and crime. If the economic impoverishment thesis for crime holds, unemployed people receiving the UBI would still be equally likely to commit crime. \([Hooge et al, 2011](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/262933140_Unemployment_Inequality_Poverty_and_Crime_Spatial_Distribution_Patterns_of_Criminal_Acts_in_Belgium_2001-06)\)
1.1.17.1. Con: This effect is likely to be because it is quite difficult to both obtain and stay on welfare. Thus the unemployed are likely to turn to crime out of economic desperation; a factor that would not occur given a UBI.
1.1.17.1.1. Con: The study in question focuses on Belgium, one of the few countries that offers [unlimited](http://ec.europa.eu/europe2020/pdf/themes/2015/unemployment_benefits_20151126.pdf) unemployment benefits. There is little difficulty staying on welfare there.
1.1.17.1.1.1. Con: According to [the European Commission the payment of benefits in Belgium](http://ec.europa.eu/social/main.jsp?catId=1102&intPageId=4425&langId=en\)) is limited to three years. No unemployment insurance scheme exists for self-employed persons, with the exception of a bankruptcy allowance which is not paid by unemployment insurance. The claim that it is 'unlimited' does not seem to hold.
1.1.17.1.2. Pro: In many countries, the benefits system was [designed to be punitive and onerous in order to deter people from claiming without real need](https://www.sociology.ox.ac.uk/working-papers/do-punitive-approaches-to-unemployment-benefit-recipients-increase-welfare-exit-and-employment-a-cross-area-analysis-of-uk-sanctioning-reforms.html). This model is outdated and does not reflect the goals of an enlightened society.
1.1.17.2. Con: While there was a small rise in unemployment under a UBI in [Manitoba, Canada,](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mincome) during the 1970s, it was uniquely limited to teenagers and new mothers. Both of these were for assumed reasons other than laziness- teenagers were focusing on school, and new mothers were able to provide for their newborns more effectively. Other demographics did not see a major change in employment rates.
1.1.17.2.1. Con: Existing studies on UBI \(in Hamilton, Ontario, Canada for example: [www.cbc.ca](http://www.cbc.ca/news/canada/hamilton/basic-income-cheques-going-to-400-households-as-project-gets-off-to-slow-start-1.4329212\))\) did not compare giving cash to receiving other government benefits of equal value. They gave a test group additional cash without taking away existing benefits. These studies don't address the UBI proposed here where the cash benefits are instead of, not in addition to, existing government benefits.
1.1.17.3. Con: Unemployment leads to crime due to economic desperation and alienation. A wealthy unemployed person is not likely to commit crimes.
1.1.17.3.1. Con: A wealthy person is more likely to commit white collar crimes. Less likely to be prosecuted, and more likely to be able to afford a competent defense. They have more to loose, much more leverage, and their criminality is more impactful.
1.1.17.4. Con: Welfare as it stands creates a "perverse incentive"  not to work as especially with low wage jobs, individuals can find themselves worse off. The UBI does not create this perverse incentive.
1.1.17.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.
1.1.17.5. Con: If UBI is irrespective of crime \(criminals will still commit the same crimes as before, with or without a UBI\), then whether a UBI is implemented or not would not matter from this perspective, because the crime rate would be the same. Because of this, it would not hurt to try out a UBI, because people would not be worried any more about increasing crime than now.
1.1.17.5.1. Con: Crime may still increase if people find a UBI to be inadequate.
1.1.17.5.1.1. Con: This is not likely to be a statistically significant increase over current rates of economic crime, and may in fact be a decrease due to people in desperate financial situations who would otherwise prefer to be law-abiding no longer having to risk crime, as they can instead use their UBI to rectify their financial situation.
1.1.17.6. Pro: In this instance, the person doesn't change, just the crime. Instead of welfare fraud, people might commit UBI fraud instead.
1.1.17.6.1. Con: UBI is difficult to defraud due to being universal and unconditional.
1.1.17.6.1.1. Pro: One of the very few ways to defraud a UBI scheme would be to create fictitious claimants, which would be far easier to detect than people simply lying about their circumstances to falsely claim welfare payments.
1.1.18. Pro: UBI reduces the chronic high parental economic stress that daily impacts children and positive outlook that motivates productivity and health.
1.2. Pro: A UBI is more efficient and effective than traditional welfare programs.
1.2.1. Con: It is unfair that people are not receiving benefits on the basis of who is the most vulnerable or in need; the distribution of societal resources should be targeted [at those most in need](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Rawls) of opportunities and support.
1.2.1.1. Con: It is unfair that those who are successful in life are treated unequally and excluded from governmental grants.
1.2.1.1.1. Con: Those who are successful do not need these government grants to continue to be successful. Those who are struggling do. In either case, the passion and drive to better or add to the world should be the motivating factor, not the money. Thus, a UBI lessens the barrier to entry for such individuals that they may contend with those who are already successful. If they are truly successful on their own merits, it would indeed be fair on the grounds of keeping them honest.
1.2.1.2. Con: Because almost all tax systems are progressive, those who are vulnerable and in need already receive more benefits than those who are better off. If anything a UBI makes this distribution even more progressive.
1.2.1.3. Pro: Giving a UBI to wealthy citizens is a waste of means. The money is way better used to help those in hardship.
1.2.1.3.1. Con: The assumption that wealthy citizens will "waste" the money given to them is not implicit. On the contrary, it is possible that, on average, wealthy citizens will contribute more per "dollar" received than citizens in need.
1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: [That wealth individuals waste their money by saving it](http://www.nber.org/papers/w7906) [is not an assumption.](http://factmyth.com/factoids/people-with-more-money-save-more/)
1.2.1.3.2. Con: Wealthy citizens will pay more in tax than they receive from the UBI.
1.2.1.3.2.1. Con: This tax would have been paid anyway, so should not be considered when deciding between a welfare/UBI system.
1.2.1.3.3. Pro: As rich people save more money relative to their income than poor people and savings are macroeconomically bad UBI is inefficient.
1.2.1.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.6.3.2.
1.2.1.4. Pro: Some people will need more support then others, some will need less; a flat UBI check is probably going to run into a similar problems as flat taxes, and similarly simplistic tax codes. The lack of discretion will hurt those that need more help.
1.2.1.4.1. Pro: UBI doesn't account for differences of need between people from disability or illness.
1.2.1.5. Con: Targeted benefits inevitably have arbitrary cutoff points for eligibility, excluding some people who have essentially the same need for support as others, but fall just beyond the cutoff, or who have other extenuating circumstances that aren't addressed by eligibility criteria.
1.2.1.5.1. Con: Cutoffs could be easily removed by using a sliding scale, where applicants can get a variable amount depending on their needs, instead of being eligible for all or nothing depending on a cutoff point.
1.2.1.5.2. Con: This can be addressed by taking care over the criteria, and making sure they are appropriate. In theory the cutoffs should be a good thing, preventing those not in need taking resources which could go to those in need. If this isn't the case in practice, then the criteria simply need revisiting.
1.2.1.6. Con: The UBI does not exclude extra benefits to those who may need them.
1.2.1.7. Pro: A UBI 'takes' money from those who need it to 'give' to those who do not. Those who need money most are given proportionately less than under other welfare distribution systems.
