1.2.1.8. Con: Providing the same benefit to every citizen—even wealthy ones—makes it more likely that the UBI will remain dependable. Everyone who receives the UBI has a stake in keeping it going, so the more people who receive it, the more people will vote for officials who support the UBI policy. Social Security uses this strategy, and it's proven impossible for any politician to cut or even reduce benefits.
1.2.1.9. Con: This compromises democracy and encourages a client-patron relationship between groups and the politicians that secure their official "group in need the government must help" status.
1.2.1.10. Pro: Treating unequals, equally, is not egalitarian and will not address current inequalities or produce a more equitable distribution of capital and income wealth as an outcome. Universal access to a basic living wage above the poverty line that uses a simple and automatically-administered mean test would offer all the benefits claimed by a UBI without the cost burden of payments to people who currently don't need any financial assistance.
1.2.2. Pro: Current welfare systems are [complex](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/268176421_Complexity_in_welfare_systems_governance_and_the_changing_borders_of_the_third_sector_researching_two_local_multi-sectoral_partnerships) and fragmented between a range of issues and services. This necessitates \(and has resulted in\) a significant [bureaucracy](http://www.dailykos.com/story/2010/9/6/899569/-) which is highly cost ineffective and reduces the proportion of the welfare budget that is actually distributed.
1.2.2.1. Con: True, but it does not necessitate UBI as a solution \(with new unknown issues\).
1.2.2.1.1. Con: While UBI might not be the only solution, it does have one big advantage: simplicity. It would apply to everyone, so no need for huge administrations handling huge amounts of paperwork, and people wouldn't have to 1-know that they can apply for it \(not always obvious, especially for isolated people\), 2-prepare complicated applications \(which once again can exclude the people who need the most assistance\), 3-wait for said applications to come through \(even though their need might be urgent\).
1.2.2.2. Con: Current welfare systems may or may not be complex/fragmented and require more bureaucracy, but these extra costs should be weighed against the UBI's extra costs: money which is paid to the 100 million \(or so\) people who would already be well off without an income boost. These costs could easily be as high as $500 billion or more per year \(just assuming a mere $420/month payment\).
1.2.2.3. Con: A UBI could be rife with weaknesses compared to traditional welfare programs—or problems of its own—requiring a whole new \(i.e. unfamiliar\) bureaucracy. As a result, the supposed efficiency increases might be nonexistent or likely minimal at best.
1.2.2.3.1. Pro: It is difficult to account for geographical disparities with a blanket dividend such as a UBI. Some areas have higher living costs than others, and with a single UBI amount, you will either not be giving enough to recipients in some areas, or you will be giving proportionally too much to recipients in other areas to account for geographical differences.
1.2.2.3.1.1. Con: People can choose where they want to live, balancing tradeoffs such as cost of living vs services available and lifestyle. A UBI would prevent government from implicitly subsidizing certain geographic areas.
1.2.2.3.1.1.1. Con: The cost to move is finite. Unless a newly implemented UBI accounts for this cost, some people in high cost of living areas will be trapped.
1.2.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con: It would be up to an individual to work a job to earn the funds to make a move to a city with lower living costs where the UBI will carry more financial weight. Undeveloped regions would begin to grow as a result of this incentive.
1.2.2.3.1.2. Con: It is difficult, but it is not impossible. A UBI could be tied to the productivity of the areas and the total amount of the UBI payments for certain areas could be used to encourage or discourage migration.
1.2.2.3.1.2.1. Con: There is no support that a central regulating body would be capable of making such complex analyses; on the contrary, it seems unlikely that such regulating bodies would be capable of efficiently/accurately doing this.
1.2.2.3.1.3. Con: Perhaps it would actually help reduce disparities: if everyone had a basic safety net they knew they could count on, it would make it easier to look for a good job \(even if they need to quit a bad one first\) or to start a business, which is especially difficult in historically underprivileged areas where there are almost no jobs around.
1.2.2.3.1.4. Con: A UBI could account for geographic disparities in cost of living \(COL\) which would have the advantage of enabling people to move to cheaper areas with far less risk and allowing for income to be injected into areas with no current job market. UBI would therefore help alleviate COL disparities and create many new markets.
1.2.2.3.1.5. Con: UBIs would encourage people who can work remotely to move to areas with low costs of living. This could lead to a repopulation of neglected rural areas and take housing pressure off cities.
1.2.2.3.1.6. Con: Cost of living is easily and regularly calculated by the Bureau of Labor Statistics. [bls.gov](https://www.bls.gov/cpi/)
1.2.2.3.2. Pro: A UBI is subject to fraud.
1.2.2.3.2.1. Pro: In the US, [federal agencies](https://www.downsizinggovernment.org/fraud-and-abuse) are often sloppy in their administration of programs, and Congress provides little serious oversight.
1.2.2.3.2.1.1. Con: A UBI is much easier to administer than other welfare programs — all it requires is that the government send a check. This has little margin for error or exploitation.
1.2.2.3.2.1.2. Con: By the same argument, the military has documented waste in the billions, year after year. Yet it is tolerated in the name of the greater good.
1.2.2.3.2.1.2.1. Con: It is not well tolerated by some; there are plentiful criticisms of the American military, both inside and outside America.
1.2.2.3.2.1.2.2. Con: This claim aides that the central government presiding over the military will have difficulties preventing UBI fraud if the same government wastes billions on other programs.
1.2.2.3.2.1.3. Con: This is just as much an argument for no UBI as it is for a UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.1.4. Con: This is a systemic issue of congress and federal agencies, and shouldn't relate to UBI specifically. This is an issue which should be addressed regardless of subject matter.
1.2.2.3.2.1.4.1. Con: If we accept that congress is sloppy, etc. then to say that we should implement this policy without first fixing said issue seems ill advised.
1.2.2.3.2.1.4.1.1. Con: Using this argument we should stop building schools because some construction companies are corrupt.
1.2.2.3.2.1.4.2. Con: We are discussing UBI specifically. The systemic issue existing does not detract from its affects on UBI and fraud.
1.2.2.3.2.2. Con: Whereas conditional income assistance can be exploited by taking the money without a genuine effort to move out of the program, a universal system has no such conditions and thus there is less room for exploitation.
1.2.2.3.2.2.1. Pro: [In Kenya,](http://basicincome.org/news/2016/11/kenya-unconditional-cash-transfers-towards-unconditional-basic-income-randomized-controlled-trial-come/) the move from conditional to unconditional payouts is underway, and the effects are mostly positive, with little abuse of the system.
1.2.2.3.2.2.1.1. Pro: A 12 year basic income experiment in [Kenya](http://nordic.businessinsider.com/kenya-village-disproving-biggest-myth-about-basic-income-2017-12) shows that people typically spend this money on necessities like education, medicine, and food.
1.2.2.3.2.3. Con: Because a UBI is unconditional, benefit fraud will likely decrease. The need to forge documents or misrepresent the truth in order to obtain benefits will become obsolete.
1.2.2.3.2.3.1. Pro: Because everyone knows exactly how much and how often they should be receiving their payments, there is almost no room for corruption.
1.2.2.3.2.3.2. Con: Not obsolete - the room for fraud/corruption would be almost entirely limited to attempting to claim for another person - alive, dead, or fictional.
1.2.2.3.2.3.2.1. Con: Which is likely still more easily uncovered than most welfare fraud committed today.
1.2.2.3.2.3.3. Pro: UBI registration will encourage a more accurate population count, thus removing the need for an expensive census. The stringency of registration rules would affect the subsequent rate of fraud within the system.
1.2.2.3.2.3.3.1. Con: The census data is richer than that collected via a UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.4. Pro: Fraudulent use could include people creating false documentation in order to get paid more than once.
1.2.2.3.2.4.1. Con: In order to avoid overspending on fraudulent persons, there is an incentive to monitor for fraud more carefully.
1.2.2.3.2.4.2. Con: The inventives for identiy theft/fraud are already strong in developed economies. It is unclear that extracting traceable regular, relatively small UBI payments will be more attractive than e.g untraceable one-off, high value credit card fraud.
1.2.2.3.2.5. Pro: Fraudulent use of the funds could include people claiming the UBI payments of care-dependent or deceased relatives.
1.2.2.3.2.5.1. Con: Deceased people can easily be removed from eligibility lists when they are registered as dead.
1.2.2.3.2.5.1.1. Con: Family members may just not register someone as dead and receive their benefits indefinitely.
1.2.2.3.2.5.1.1.1. Con: The difficulties involved in hiding a death would rapidly make this infeasible - funerals, closure of accounts and estates, the deceased's disappearance from public and social life, grieving of friends and families, etc. would all be telltale signs. Furthermore, the steps necessary to conceal each of these things \(even temporarily; they likely could not be hid indefinitely at all\) would be unlikely and difficult for a grieving family to implement, even if deliberately pre-planned.
1.2.2.3.2.5.2. Con: Care-dependent people still merit a UBI payment. If those providing their care are collecting it and spending it on their behalf - with their permission - it is unclear why this is a problem.
1.2.2.3.2.5.2.1. Con: The problem is that the family members may not actually be spending it on behalf of the care-dependent; they may just be taking it for themselves.
1.2.2.3.2.5.2.1.1. Con: This would constitute financial abuse, which already happens with present-day pensions and disability and care payments. It is a separate issue, not an argument specifically against the UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.5.2.1.1.1. Con: It is an argument specifically against the UBI because prevention methods would be less effective with UBI. That is to say, even if stronger laws/enforcement against this kind of financial abuse are enacted, the introduction of a UBI would increase the amount of financial abuse by making it far easier and widespread/applicable.
1.2.2.3.2.5.3. Con: This is already a problem with traditional disability benefits, and is not an issue specific to UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.6. Con: UBI reduces the types of fraud possible to just identity fraud. This makes it much simpler and easier to fight than many types of fraud: the only question is "Are you who you say you are?"
1.2.2.3.2.6.1. Pro: Most countries have a national ID number, and UBI could certainly be implemented alongside a stronger national identification database.
1.2.2.3.2.6.2. Con: That is not the only question that needs to be asked; people must also ask "is the person still alive," "are they actually receiving the money or is a family member taking it without their consent," etc.
1.2.2.3.2.6.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.5.
1.2.2.3.2.6.3. Con: It's easier to fake an identity, than to fake an identity and make it look qualified to whichever entitlement program is being defrauded.
1.2.2.3.2.7. Con: All government benefits are subject to some degree of fraud. A UBI is less subject to fraud due to its universal availability and equal distribution. The need to commit fraud is reduced along with the bureaucracy required to manage social benefit programs.
1.2.2.3.2.7.1. Pro: Controlling one universal income which applies to everyone with no distinction is easier than multiple sources of financial help that depend on the person's characteristics.
1.2.2.3.2.7.2. Con: All government benefits are subject to fraud, including a UBI. Therefore no government benefit program should exist, including UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.7.2.1. Con: Ignoring the plight of large numbers of poor people can lead to rioting and other disruptions of peace.
1.2.2.3.2.7.3. Con: The universal availability and equal distribution would actually make it more susceptible to fraud, because there's less scrutiny so it's easier to just invent a fake identity and collect multiple payments. With the current entitlement system, in addition to creating the fake identity, you have to make that fake identity look eligible for the entitlement.
1.2.2.3.2.8. Con: -> See 1.1.6.
1.2.2.3.2.9. Con: Converting a welfare system to UBI, would reduce welfare fraud. Most fraud is not from the benefit recipients, but companies that are contracted to provide services. UBI makes the individual responsible for selecting the provider.
1.2.2.3.2.9.1. Pro: Allowing recipients to select their own providers can save money on contracting and oversight for the companies selected to provide traditional welfare services.
1.2.2.3.2.9.1.1. Pro: It also allows for competition in service quality even when offering services to the less wealthy.
1.2.2.3.2.9.2. Con: Just because the individual is responsible for selecting the provider it doesn't mean that they will make a good choice.
1.2.2.3.2.10. Con: All economic activities are subject to fraud. With each method of potential for fraud a subsequent law can be created to address this issue.
1.2.2.3.2.10.1. Pro: Fraudulent activity can happen in just about any domain \(e.g. [fish fraud](http://nationalpost.com/life/food/mystery-fish-study-finds-rampant-fish-fraud-in-ottawa-restaurants-and-sushi-spots)\). This does not invalidate the domain - i.e. fish fraud doesn't invalidate the fish trade.
1.2.2.3.2.10.2. Con: A good law is one which doesn’t require additional laws to enforce it. If a new law will throw the governed into a quagmire of elaborating laws, and new regulations, perhaps that is criteria enough for not passing the law?
1.2.2.3.2.11. Con: [Blockchain](https://futurism.com/the-first-blockchain-based-universal-basic-income-experiment-just-launched/) can be used to set up a transparent, immutable UBI system, that can be verified by anyone. [Grantcoin](http://www.grantcoin.org/) is an example of an UBI project that runs on the blockchain. It has been running for more than a year.
1.2.2.3.2.11.1. Pro: Part of the answer to the problem of income inequality and meeting basic human needs will come from technology. Block chains could be a cost effective platform to create better mechanisms for managing public welfare spending.
1.2.2.3.2.11.2. Con: Implementing UBI on a blockchain would need to go together with major social education and implementation of matching payment methods.
1.2.2.3.2.11.3. Con: Blockchain contracts are also vulnerable, and can fail in spectacular ways. [A hacker stoles $31 M of ether](https://medium.freecodecamp.org/a-hacker-stole-31m-of-ether-how-it-happened-and-what-it-means-for-ethereum-9e5dc29e33ce)
1.2.2.3.2.12. Pro: A UBI would incentivize certain people to have children irresponsibly so they can receive more money "for them".
1.2.2.3.2.12.1. Con: The amount it takes to have, and raise a child far exceeds any realistic universal income people would receive by doing so.
1.2.2.3.2.12.1.1. Con: This cuts against the purpose of the UBI, which is to ensure that no one falls below a basic poverty line. It's important that parents of many children aren't significantly worse off than childless adults.
1.2.2.3.2.12.2. Con: This same incentive also exists under traditional welfare programs, most of which provide additional benefits to dependents.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3. Con: The [annual cost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cost_of_raising_a_child#United_States) of raising a child is likely more than a base income of, for example, ten thousand dollars. Welfare policies give benefits to parents, but that happens only because governments acknowledge the immense cost that goes with it.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3.1. Con: Even if provided with a UBI, people would continue to work in order to ensure better education and resources for their children.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3.2. Con: A UBI is not meant to cover all familiar needs, but gives an incentive to parents that otherwise could not afford raising a child only with their jobs.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3.3. Con: No welfare policy is given solely out of recognising immense costs. The government would not be financially incentivising something it does not want to encourage.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3.3.1. Con: Medicare and other provisions of healthcare are given out because the government recognizes the immense costs of healthcare for the elderly and disabled.
1.2.2.3.2.12.3.4. Con: Children would likely be included in a UBI. An extra income would cover many of the expenses related to a child.
1.2.2.3.2.12.4. Con: Deliberately claiming another's money and using it against their best interests would constitute domestic abuse, which would be penalized and thus disincentivized.
1.2.2.3.2.12.4.1. Con: This would mean that a parent who buys food for their child \(paid for partly by the child's UBI\) would be commiting domestic abuse, which seems absurd. In practice, a UBI would probably allocate a children's income to their parents until they are 18 or not living at home.
1.2.2.3.2.12.4.2. Con: Irresponsible use of children's UBI payments by parents is too hard to police properly.
1.2.2.3.2.12.5. Con: This is nothing but supposition.
1.2.2.3.2.12.6. Con: This argument doesn't apply to a UBI. A UBI is generally considered to be an amount that is paid to everybody, and everybody gets the same amount. It is not dependant on individual circumstances.
1.2.2.3.2.12.6.1. Con: And because this amount is unconditionally paid to everybody, also to children, a UBI creates an inherent incentive to give birth to many children. As legal guardians, parents can freely dispose of the children's UBI.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7. Con: A UBI encourages more people to have children, solving the low birth rate problem that exists in many Western countries.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1. Con: Countries with relatively high Human Development Index \(HDI\) ratings experience a [demographic transition](http://www.economist.com/node/14164483): fertility \(defined as the number of children born by a woman over her lifetime\) drops from around eight to near one and a half. As a UBI would further increase the HDI rating of a country \(in particular the income per person aspect\) this trend is likely to intensify.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1. Con: One explanation of this phenomenon is the extensive entering of women into the [the workplace](http://www.economist.com/node/14164483). A UBI would mitigate the need for a job and enable women to focus on their motherhood, if wished. This could raise fertility rates again.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1.1. Con: This assumes women enter the workforce because they need to, rather than because they want to.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1.1.1. Con: Exactly how many people enter the workforce because they want to? Since when is joining the capitalist system vs being a marginalized "poor" outcast, a choice? People who "choose" to work are already either enjoying some kind of unearned income, or have lowered their quality of life to conform to what little they may legally get for free in the system.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1.2. Con: The financial independence would allow women to pursue individual enrichment, such as gaining education, a desired career, an artistic pursuit, or travel opportunities. This freedom will let them put off motherhood until they are financially secure; thereby reducing birth rates
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1.2.1. Con: Motherhood can be considered among the many forms of individual enrichment a woman might pursue.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.1.2.2. Con: That is not the case if having more children would increase the amount of money they would receive.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.2. Pro: If rich countries generally have [lower](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/population-paradox-europes-time-bomb-888030.html) fertility rates, childcare costs are not the only [factor](https://evolution-institute.org/article/why-do-modern-people-have-so-few-children/) decreasing fertility and thus a monetary increase like a UBI is unlikely to boost it.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.1.3. Con: This claim indirectly implies we should avoid developing countries \(or reducing poverty\) in order to increase the birth rates. This is barbaric.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2. Pro: The lack of [financial resources](http://www.rawstory.com/2016/08/the-real-reason-were-not-having-kids/) is an important reason why many individuals hold off on having children. A UBI mitigate this issue and helps the affected individuals to fulfil their desire to have children.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.1. Pro: The [average child](http://www.rawstory.com/2016/08/the-real-reason-were-not-having-kids/) in the U.S. represents an investment of $304,500 from the moment of birth to the child's 18th birthday. This is a significant amount.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.2. Pro: In the USA [child-care costs](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/wonk/wp/2015/10/06/the-staggering-cost-of-daycare-when-you-make-only-the-minimum-wage/?tid=a_inl&utm_term=.f500cb489981) account for at least 30 percent of a minimum-wage worker’s earnings. In New York and Massachusetts the cost rises to as much as 80 percent.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.3. Pro: Surveys have shown that [European](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/population-paradox-europes-time-bomb-888030.html) women want to have two or more children \(2.36 on average\). While countries in north-west Europe approach that number, in southern Europe they do not. Government action in the former, through child benefits, tax breaks, maternity leave or nursery provisions, may be a key factor.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.4. Con: UBI contributes to the planet's biggest future problem, that of over-population. By removing the responsibility of being able to afford having children, it will lead to a vicious downward spiral of starvation and violence as more people compete for less and less life-sustaining resources such as food and water.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.4.1. Con: Over population should be considered a serious problem but using financial positions to keep people from reproducing is perhaps an irresponsible approach. If we need to be careful with our population then that issue should be addressed directly and honestly and people should be informed of it rather than relying on an unrelated aspect of society to steer some people away.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.5. Con: That claim is very much contrary to the evidence of the people that have more children are generally low income.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.6. Con: It may not be a good idea for people who do not have moneymaking know-how or a sense of responsibility to raise children who would grow up to have similar traits.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.2.6.1. Con: In a society under UBI, "responsibility" would become far more diverse than simply "providing for yourself" and "not living beyond your means".
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.3. Con: The birth rate [isn't too bad](https://www.google.com/search?q=birth+rate+in+us&rlz=1C1CHBF_enUS750US750&oq=birth+rate&aqs=chrome.1.69i57j0l5.13972j0j7&sourceid=chrome&ie=UTF-8). This assumes that finances are the main reason people don't have more kids.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4. Con: There is no reason to assume low birth rates would be a problem.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.1. Pro: A birth rate below the replacement rate reduces human population \(without harming\), which is arguably desirable in this current state of overpopulation.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.2. Con: A low birth rate in one area or cultural segment will lead that segment to disappear in the long term, hence being taken over by other ways of thinking or living.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.2.1. Con: The disappearance of certain cultural segments is not necessarily a bad thing.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.2.1.1. Pro: The existence of certain cultural segments is not objectively good or bad for the society, therefore it is hard to argue with certainty that their disappearance will be bad.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.2.1.2. Pro: Many cultural segments have disappeared along human history and it is hard to quantify whether this fact was detrimental to humanity as a whole in terms other than intuition.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.4.2.1.3. Pro: The disappearance of certain cultural segments and the appearance of new ones is one of the factors that enables cultural evolution.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.5. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.12.2.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.6. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.12.3.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.7. Con: The World population \(and US population\) are climbing rapidly. China, India and Sub-Saharan Africa are currently overpopulated, requiring significant food imports to sustain the population.
1.2.2.3.2.12.7.8. Con: Lower birth rates might be a better way of conserving resources especially among those with greater difficulty with job retention strengths.
1.2.2.3.2.12.8. Con: A UBI incentivises individuals to pursue their own individual aspirations by eliminating the barriers of poverty. Those who no longer envision their lives as being stuck in a multigenerational cycle of poverty would be less inclined to limit their options through an accidental pregnancy and less inclined to see the benefit of having a child when they could pursue their individual goals instead.
1.2.2.3.2.12.9. Con: The distribution for having children could be based upon the marginal cost of an extra child as determined by the poverty guidelines. This would greatly diminish this effect.
1.2.2.3.2.13. Con: A UBI is less prone than other forms of welfare to fraud due to the fact that many points of interaction are outright removed, meaning many forms of fraud are outright impossible.
1.2.2.3.2.13.1. Con: As an attacker, the fewer points of interaction there are, the fewer interactions I have to successfully fake. This makes it easier to attack.
1.2.2.4. Con: Current welfare systems are already highly efficient. Empirical evidence shows that at [least 90%, if not 99%, of the capital used](http://www.cbpp.org/research/romneys-charge-that-most-federal-low-income-spending-goes-for-overhead-and-bureaucrats-is?fa=view&id=3655) for American welfare systems goes towards the beneficiaries.
1.2.2.4.1. Con: While the efficiencies of the current welfare systems are already high, that doesn't mean they couldn't be more efficient.
1.2.2.4.1.1. Con: With that high level of efficiency it is possibly less risky and costly to try something else than to further upgrade current system.
1.2.2.4.1.2. Con: The main issue addressed by the [grandparent claim](https://www.kialo.com/argument/1634.1730) is the misconception that traditional welfare programs are highly inefficient. It does not need to prove that traditional welfare is more efficient than UBIs; rather, the parent here needs to suggest that UBIs are more efficient than traditional welfare—and quantify that improvement, to the extent possible.
1.2.2.4.2. Con: This doesn't address whether the benefits are good value. For example, technically speaking most of the money spent on public housing goes towards the beneficiaries, but results in very poor quality housing compared to housing offered by the private sector for the same cost. [www.politico.com](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2017/07/20/how-san-francisco-turned-its-tenements-into-treasures-215391)
1.2.2.4.2.1. Con: While it may be true that means-tested welfare provides "perfect quality," UBI could be just as bad or even worse.
1.2.2.4.2.2. Con: [The politico article](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2017/07/20/how-san-francisco-turned-its-tenements-into-treasures-215391) seems to suggest that the public-private partnership system was a successful case of targeted/means-tested housing subsidies/benefits.
1.2.2.4.3. Con: Time spent and labor done by the beneficiaries must also be included when comparing costs of opt-in and opt-out systems.
1.2.2.4.3.1. Con: This is a valid consideration, but without quantification/support, we should not assume that factor is significant.
1.2.2.4.4. Con: This is of little importance if the capital doesn't achieve what it's meant to: helping people in need. This must be the primary measure; to what extent the capital improves people's lives.
1.2.2.5. Pro: Navigating bureaucracy takes time that a person can better spend seeking employment, caring for others, in leisure or in the community.
1.2.2.6. Con: While UBI may simplify many forms of welfare, a system to provide additional means-tested benefits is needed. To be fair the UBI sufficient for a single home-owning person will not meet the needs of a single person with children having to pay rent. Not all the bureaucracy disappears.
1.2.2.6.1. Con: Children would also receive a UBI.
1.2.2.6.2. Con: It would not have to eliminate other assistance.
1.2.2.6.3. Pro: UBI could replace the basic unemployment benefit but systems would still be required to provide other welfare benefits such as disability, child care, etc. The admin savings claimed may not be as great as claimed.
1.2.2.7. Pro: Income redistribution agencies absorb about two-thirds to three-quarters of each dollar budgeted to them in overhead costs. Using government data, Robert L. Woodson \(1989, p. 63\) calculated that, on average, 70 cents of each dollar budgeted for government assistance goes not to the poor, but to the members of the welfare bureaucracy and others serving the poor. Michael Tanner \(1996, p. 136 n. 18\) cites regional studies supporting this 70/30 split.
1.2.3. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.
1.2.4. Pro: The reliability of a UBI allows the poor the ability to better plan and budget their spending.
1.2.4.1. Con: Bad financial decision-making is part of the problem for many who might find themselves in need of welfare. Asking them to just fix that problem by making better decisions seems at best to be a utopian aspiration.
1.2.4.1.1. Con: The "poverty trap" often includes debts and excessive bills forcing desperate measures just to survive in the short term. Many objectively "bad" decisions are forced by desperation \(and/or impaired judgement from stress and the resulting exhaustion\), or seem to be the only/best option at the time. Alleviating that pressure would help them to make better decisions in a very practical way.
1.2.4.2. Pro: A UBI provides some income even if salary is being delayed for various reasons, mostly financial results of the employer. Welfare doesn't usually cover this.
1.2.5. Con: Because a UBI provides cash payments it becomes impossible for governments to direct welfare spending in order to promote positive choices.
1.2.5.1. Pro: For example, food vouchers can be used to direct the spending of the poor towards improving their diet and away from harmful choices such as drugs or alcohol.
1.2.5.1.1. Pro: At-risk families will tend towards addiction and spend a UBI on marijuana, drugs, alcohol and/or cigarettes. This would make these type of families more dependent on Social Services, costing the government even more. Funding like food stamps for all, is better as it restricts purchases to certain socially agreed tradable items. However recipients can still work and so earn money to purchase luxury items.
1.2.5.2. Pro: Welfare programs often offer direct rebates for purchases that aid in securing employment, such as buying professional clothes or repairing a car need for a commute.
1.2.5.3. Con: People do not use their transfers from the government to buy alcohol, tobacco or wasteful items, [the vast majority used the money responsibly](http://blogs.worldbank.org/impactevaluations/do-poor-waste-transfers-booze-and-cigarettes-no).
1.2.5.4. Con: Even targeted assistance programs only place soft limits on spending. Any assistance that can be used to purchase goods \(ie food stamps\) [can be easily sold on a black market](http://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2013/aug/19/food-stamp-black-market-rise/).
1.2.5.5. Pro: Effective use of welfare monies requires that individuals actually spend income on measures that improve their welfare. A UBI cannot guarantee this - which in the long run is more likely to perpetuate the social problem in question, compared to means testing or in-kind assistance.
1.2.5.6. Con: Increased financial security does not remove poor life choices. Disfunctional families with many kids and a low standard of education could potentially put enormous pressure on the service & society as a whole.
1.2.5.6.1. Con: There is no reason why a UBI cannot be implemented simultaneously with other programs designed to help prevent or minimize such occurrences, and/or their impact.
1.2.5.6.2. Con: If the same UBI is given to each person regardless of circumstances, people making poor life choices would not put any more pressure on the system than those making good ones.
1.2.5.6.3. Con: While it does not remove poor life choices, it does mitigate their impact by providing a financial buffer and safety net.
1.2.5.7. Con: It is not an absolute requirement of UBI that it is all in cash - and even if it was, who is to determine what are "worthy" purchases that poor people might be permitted to buy? The point of UBI is to restore individual agency, rather than prescribe how its recipients are to comport themselves.
1.2.5.8. Con: Unlike targeted welfare, the point of UBI is precisely to empower people financially and spend such money freely, not being forced to follow a specific worldview as to which spending is positive and which is negative.
1.2.5.8.1. Pro: It is not the role of the government in a free society to direct the life choices of its citizens, so long as those life choices do not infringe on the rights of other citizens. The point of UBI is that the citizens themselves, and not the government, are better at deciding how their aid money can be best spent to most efficiently help their lives.
1.2.5.9. Pro: Monetization of benefits makes subsidized sectors of economy \(healthcare, education, transport\) more vulnerable to market volatility.
1.2.5.10. Pro: Current social welfare programs are seen as entitlements by the recipients, rather than as welfare programs. The same will happen to UBI. Cash disbursements have a tendency to be misused. What is needed is are soup kitchens, night shelters, used clothing distribution, free schooling and free healthcare. Provide for basic needs; not cash disbursements.
1.2.5.10.1. Con: This claim contains no proof that its suggested methods would be any more effective than the provision of a UBI.
1.2.5.10.2. Con: Providing a UBI does not prevent any of these things from being implemented, and may in fact allow more time for individuals to provide labour to such causes.
1.2.5.11. Pro: With UBI, the poor will continue to spend their provided income in an unclever manner, and the rich will capitalize on the provided income and earn the poors' income in the end. Leading to no problems solved. A better solution is to provide services to those who need them.
1.2.5.11.1. Con: Some of the poor as well as some of the rich will continue to spend their income in ways which are not clever, regardless if they have an UBI or not. This means this is not a point in favor or against implementation of an UBI, because the UBI would indeed solve problems for those who do spend their income in clever ways. So, unless it can be demonstrated that all the poor would act in this way, this argument has no bearing on the outcome of the UBI.
1.2.5.12. Con: The government can still influence the spending by taxing undesirable items, such as cigarettes or alcohol, less favourably.
1.2.5.13. Pro: A UBI will have millions of dependents who will make subsequent changes or removal of the program impossible.
1.2.5.14. Con: "Positive choices" are, or can be, subjective.
1.2.6. Con: Traditional welfare programs \(e.g. social grants or infrastructure provision\) allow governments to target specific issues in specific communities, a benefit that is not available with a UBI.
1.2.6.1. Pro: There are groups in society who require more support from the state than others. For example, those with illnesses and disabilities who require specialist care cannot live off a small amount of money but have no ability to work to increase their income.
1.2.6.1.1. Con: In every case except for those who are not physically healthy or able, the UBI will be sufficient for living expenses because that is how the amount is determined.
1.2.6.1.1.1. Con: Cost of living differs drastically by region. For example, if a UBI in the United States is set at the poverty line, those who live in Manhattan will not be able to afford basic living expenses.
1.2.6.1.1.1.1. Con: Initiatives like the [London Living Wage](https://www.london.gov.uk/what-we-do/business-and-economy/london-living-wage) calculate how much more money people living in an expensive city need in comparison to other places. Although this is a voluntary approach for businesses, a similar compulsory measure could be included in a UBI.
1.2.6.1.1.1.1.1. Con: This would result in uneven wages, giving people incentive to move into cities. This would compound itself over time, making it more expensive to operate and more difficult to fund.
1.2.6.1.2. Pro: The loss of those extra support resources from targeted welfare schemes could lead to dire health problems for the chronically ill, who may no longer be able to afford adequate healthcare under private or increasingly privatized systems.
1.2.6.1.3. Con: The medical treatment of illnesses and disabilities is covered by health insurance. A UBI enables individuals to pay for theirs.
1.2.6.1.3.1. Con: If an individual is allotted $10k a year worth of UBI payments, it would be not enough to cover health care costs in addition to daily living expenditures.
1.2.6.1.3.1.1. Con: It would be $10,000 in addition to their employment income, which would be enough for people to afford most health care services.
1.2.6.1.4. Pro: Groups with special needs, such as the severely ill or disabled, are able to get greater levels of assistance under targeted welfare programs than what a UBI would offer.
1.2.6.1.4.1. Pro: Across [the many different welfare systems](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2015/apr/15/which-best-countries-live-unemployed-disabled-benefits), disability payments either exceed or top-up existing unemployment/welfare payments.
1.2.6.1.5. Con: It is right to defend a system where the poor are no longer forced into government endorsed establishments. Most benefits are in-kind: only 24% of US government benefits are direct cash assistance where governments have the authority to dictate what kinds of products the poor buy and who they buy those products from \([Brenkman, p. 125](http://bien2016.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/02/BIEN2016_Sess4_Brenkman_en.pdf)\).
1.2.6.1.6. Con: In systems with no public universal healthcare, the fact that the UBI helps the poor pay for their health will create demand for clinics that offer rates cheap enough for low income earners to afford.
1.2.6.1.6.1. Con: -> See 1.2.6.1.3.1.
1.2.6.1.6.2. Con: This requires doing so to be profitable, and it is not clear that even with access to a UBI, the poor in countries without universal healthcare - like America - would be able to pay enough to make opening low-cost clinics worthwhile.
1.2.6.1.7. Con: Under a system of in-kind welfare, governments are responsible for responding to the needs of constituents where they often inaccurately underestimate the needs of those with chronic illnesses. Market incentives provide competition and choice to people who are diverse with differential needs.
1.2.6.1.7.1. Con: The market does not care about those who cannot afford their products.
1.2.6.2. Pro: In Australia, parents who refuse to [vaccinate](http://www.cnn.com/2015/04/13/asia/australia-anti-vaccination-welfare-cut/) their children can lose up to $11,000 in benefits a year since this refusal is not supported by taxpayers or public policy.
1.2.6.2.1. Con: There is no reason why a UBI couldn't have basic requirements attached to them, such as vaccinations, schooling, and other public health and safety needs.
1.2.6.2.1.1. Con: Making UBI contingent on people obeying government mandates, no matter how reasonable, compromises the universal part: there's a real risk of it becoming the government paying people to make certain choices and not others.
1.2.6.3. Pro: Society has the principled right to build paternalistic incentives into its provision of welfare, since welfare is funded through taking away money from the rest of the taxpayers and giving it to a particular person. As such, taxpayers should be able to impose conditions on the receipt of that money.
1.2.6.3.1. Pro: When the rest of society grants money, charitably, to certain individuals, it is perfectly reasonable to design the conditions of that money to encourage the recipient to make socially optimal, rather than individually optimal, decisions.
1.2.6.3.2. Con: Foremost society has a duty to help underprivileged citizens according to the basic human rights in the constitution, instead of a right to demand certain behaviors.
1.2.6.3.3. Con: The structure of those programs is decided by people who have enough power to get a majority of the legislature to vote in their favor — and that is rarely the powerless. There's no reason to expect that such conditions, imposed by the powerful on the weak — would have the effect of protecting the weak from the powerful.
1.2.6.4. Pro: Even if some claim that people are fully rational actors, they may not fully understand the consequences of their actions - as, for example, we can see in parents who fail to vaccinate their children. Therefore, welfare programs are needed because they act as incentive systems.
1.2.6.4.1. Con: If welfare must be used to give people incentive to follow common sense then they should apply to everyone, not just the less fortunate. As it currently stands only low-income households are forced to vaccinate, leaving people with middle to high tier income to do as they please \(despite the potential harm to the child and surrounding community.\)
1.2.6.5. Con: There are better ways for the government to encourage behaviour such as criminal laws or broad country-wide incentives that falls on everyone.
1.2.6.5.1. Con: One of the biggest conditions for welfare is proving that you are looking for a [job](http://www.welfareinfo.org/). The government can not just make it illegal to not look for a job.
1.2.6.5.1.1. Con: That is the current status quo, yes. However I don't think that universal basic income is as entrenched in the idea that everyone should be working all the time as welfare currently is. Universal basic income is a system that should be applied as effectively in a fully employed community as one that has no one working. Welfare on the other hand was made as a hold-over for people between jobs, hence it needs to be proven you're actually looking for a job to get welfare benefits.
1.2.6.6. Con: People refuse vaccines, use drugs, or contribute to various 'issues' for reasons other than welfare. To punish those individuals by leaving them outside of the welfare system is a violation of their basic dignity.
1.2.6.7. Con: People living in poverty have problems related to a lack of money - a UBI provides it, precisely in proportion to the degree of poverty a person experiences. A UBI is as tightly targeted as a social program can be.
1.2.6.8. Con: The whole point of a UBI is to target poverty, whatever the cause.  If specific communities are poor, they will be implicitly targeted by a UBI; but targeting them explicitly is inherently discriminatory and generates ill-feeling toward those communities.
1.2.6.9. Con: UBI allows for the government to be able to cover almost everyone's needs using only one system. Subsequent welfare programs that are smaller in size can then be established to help certain individuals.
1.2.6.10. Con: Means testing is actually not a benefit, it's expensive to administer, inevitably "gaps" people, and generates public resentment towards the recipients.
1.2.6.11. Pro: Not every person has the same cost of basic needs \(i.e a large person has to buy more food and costly clothes\).
1.2.6.12. Con: Politicians focusing intervention on specific groups fosters corruption, as well as making those groups beholden to the politicians that keep them under the focus of welfare.
1.2.6.12.1. Pro: Out of a pool of equally needy people, politicians have incentive to enter into a cooperative relationship with the most organized, most politically active groups: this would basically amount to people who can otherwise not get enough money being forced to vote for whoever promises to keep them on welfare, as well as leave out people who might be needy, but are not a part of one of these client groups.
1.2.6.12.2. Pro: Welfare programs are there to support low-income people. Low-income people are the most vulnerable to be controlled through a program grant. Giving the individuals the possibility of deciding how to apply their income would prevent polítical or sectorial interests from controlling those people.
1.2.6.13. Pro: UBI trusts the poor to make informed choices, however, in books like [Poor Economics](https://www.pooreconomics.com/about-book) we come across how poor make unhealthy/imprudent choices \(such as bad calories American poor consume\) leading to a poverty trap. Targeted subsidies of goods/services that a particular income are likely to consume is a far better approach, especially to make education and healthcare accessible. However, a UBI may work in places where people are educated and / or aware above average levels.
1.2.6.14. Pro: There is a limit to how many jobs are available in most sectors. A UBI would allow some people to work in "uncapped sectors" that may not be as economically rewarding as a conventional job, but allows for people to take the jobs they leave behind in favor of things like painting, writing or teaching yoga.
1.2.6.15. Pro: When done properly, welfare programs can target specific issues in a community that UBI does not - In fact a UBI can exacerbate the problem e.g. giving a drug addict UBI instead of implementing opioid substitution programs.
1.2.6.16. Pro: If poor social outcomes are concentrated in certain geographic areas, governments can offer public housing in different neighborhoods. This incentivizes individuals to move out of poorer areas, more than cash handouts.
1.2.6.16.1. Pro: Universal basic income will lead to a sharp split in cheap and expensive housing. Those whom have trouble getting work or don't want to are likely to opt for cheaper, similar housing in 
whatever the housing in the Ubi's pricing bracket will be. Such a sharp split in housing quality is visually unappealing and enables class segregation.
1.2.6.16.2. Con: Moving out of poorer areas does not improve one's poverty status. However, a UBI can improve the economic status of those in poorer areas, allowing them to contribute to ameliorating the community.
1.2.6.17. Con: A UBI would allow people to choose freely which area of their life needs the most urgent improvement.
1.2.6.17.1. Pro: Food and health is not necessarily what everyone needs. For example, someone might want to pay for a suit so that they can excel in their interview and land a job.
1.2.6.17.1.1. Con: Some [needs](https://www.simplypsychology.org/maslow.html) are more basic than others. Without food or proper health it is unlikely someone will obtain or commit to a job.
1.2.6.17.1.1.1. Con: Even if these are more fundamental needs, the UBI does not preclude people from spending their UBI on these needs.
1.2.6.17.2. Pro: No one can target benefits more precisely than the beneficiary. Specially not a bureaucrat.
1.2.6.17.2.1. Con: People have a long history of acting against their best interests.
1.2.6.17.2.2. Con: Sometimes at least, bureaucracies use weighted measures  and careful consideration; their value should not be underestimated.
1.2.6.17.2.3. Con: Bureaucrats implement other people's policies, so they would not be deciding.
1.2.6.17.2.4. Con: People often don't know best what they need, or even what will make them happy.
1.2.6.17.2.4.1. Con: The clarity of a need is proportional to how pressing the need is; thus those most in need would be more likely to address their needs.
1.2.6.17.2.4.1.1. Con: This assumes that individuals will generally act rationality, but this is a flawed assumption. Given this, "free range" money reasonably may not always lead to better provision of needs than traditional welfare.
1.2.6.17.2.4.1.1.1. Pro: Gambling is generally an irrational activity: it is empirically and theoretically known to \(occasionally\) be addictive, financially destructive, and mathematically illogical. Yet people still engage in this activity.
1.2.6.17.2.4.1.1.2. Pro: All people exhibit some degree of cognitive biases and other basic "thinking mistakes." One of the most notable is that of planning failure, as exemplified through procrastination. Another common example is that of the [gambler's fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gambler%27s_fallacy), which causes some people to inaccurately assess the probability of crucial outcomes. Other important examples include [egotistical and confirmation biases](https://www.inc.com/sam-mcroberts/7-cognitive-biases-that-are-holding-you-back.html), such as where we choose to believe something simply because we want it to be true.
1.2.6.17.2.4.1.1.3. Pro: Hard drug usage \(e.g. meth, heroine, crack\) is generally considered to be an irrational activity, sometimes brought on by misleading environmental information. For example, if someone is misled by friends or the general culture he lives in that the drugs are not harmful—or that the habit is sustainable and the pleasure outweighs the harm—then they may irrationally turn to such drug usage.
1.2.6.17.3. Con: Some people are not well educated in how to save and accumulate wealth. Imposing a UBI without financial education programs runs the risk of being ineffective and providing only short term support for those in need of it.
1.2.6.17.3.1. Con: Short term support still helps to make situations less pressing, and is generally better than offering no support, as a lack of global welfare \(even just within a country\) often does.
1.2.6.17.3.2. Con: Given a UBI is a recurring payment program it offers much more than short-term support and instead allows people to learn how to manage their finances over the course of their lifetimes.
1.2.6.17.3.3. Pro: People do not need extra money \(such as a UBI\) if they could just save money by not buying goods they do not need. This decision-making comes with education.
1.2.6.18. Con: Overcoming shortcomings in certain areas, like infrastructure, is still a responsibility of the government regardless of whether a UBI is implemented.
1.2.7. Pro: In 2015 13.5% of the U.S. population lived in poverty \([U.S. Census](http://www2.census.gov/programs-surveys/demo/tables/p60/256/pov_table3.xls)\). This shows that currently existing welfare programs are not doing enough.
1.2.7.1. Con: Welfare programs are not intended to provide more than basic needs.
1.2.7.2. Con: The fact that current welfare programs are \(supposedly\) inefficient does not mean that a UBI would be more efficient.
1.2.7.3. Con: The fact that poverty still exists does not mean that existing welfare programs are ineffective. This is somewhat similar to saying that laws against murder "are not doing enough" since some murder still occurs.
1.2.7.4. Con: Poverty rates were declining decade over decade before the expansion of social welfare spending. Since the creation of the welfare state and its associated safety nets, poverty rates have ceased to decline in the US. This suggests that welfare has a harmful effect on poverty rates, and the answer is to provide less of it, not double down with a UBI. [Poverty rates - 1950 to 2015](http://granitegrok.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/07/failed-war-on-poverty.jpg)
1.2.7.5. Pro: Universal Basic Income is an important step towards eradicating World Poverty. People are poor not by choice. For example, people born into poverty, unable to find jobs, disabled & unable to work, etc., who therefore have no income or means of survival. On the other hand, there are those born with silver spoons in their mouths living in opulence without the need to work for an income. This is obviously "fated" and beyond the individual's control; "fate" has obviously not been fair. Needs action.
1.2.8. Pro: '[Workfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workfare)' programs require people to participate in cheap labour or volunteer work, as those who are unable to participate due to mental or physical disability often have their [welfare payments cut](http://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/entry/disabled-people-left-behind-equality-and-human-rights-commission_uk_58dab203e4b0cb23e65be5f2).
1.2.8.1. Pro: "Workfare" programs are also problematic because they allow businesses and organizations who can benefit from them to either cut paid work positions, or in the case of organizations which \(would otherwise\) employ volunteers, leads to them being overstaffed with people who would rather not be there, reducing opportunities to contribute for and pushing aside people who may genuinely want to volunteer there.
1.2.8.2. Con: Here in Canada people with disabilities get Disability Support. In Ontario here it's called Ontario Disability Support Program \(ODSP\) with monthly amounts already higher than welfare b/c disabled persons can't work, welfare folk can. And I know the US has Disability Support as well. [mcss.gov.on.ca](https://www.mcss.gov.on.ca/en/mcss/programs/social/odsp/)
1.2.9. Pro: Low-income government-[subsidized housing](http://www.urbancure.org/washington/article.asp?id=563&t=Government-housing-is-a-bad-idea) often leaves at-risks families no choice but to reside in crime-stricken 'pre-existing ghettos', which puts their safety and livelihood at higher risk. With a UBI, families will have a choice of where they choose to reside.
1.2.9.1. Con: UBI would most likely not be high enough to afford an opportunity to relocate to any chosen housing. Even if it were, then if all residents in such a community receive UBI, then all have equal opportunity to leave. Because the demand to relocate increases, so does the cost, and the opportunity to relocate decreases.
1.2.10. Pro: Current welfare schemes are inefficient at calculating and tracking benefits disbursement, resulting in double spending and wasted costs. A UBI represents a much more certain and definite cost increase for the government, as opposed to the probabilistically lower costs of welfare.
1.2.11. Con: A UBI encourages spending on unnecessary or luxury goods on the taxpayers' dime, something which a significant number of people would likely disapprove of.
1.2.11.1. Pro: Citizens consent to a means-tested social safety net with welfare programs in a way they do not with a UBI.
1.2.11.1.1. Con: Individual citizens have little to no say or control over whether there is a social safety net, or how it is structured. It is unclear how or why they can be considered to give more meaningful consent to one that is structured as a restrictive and limited insurance scheme, versus one that is structured as a universal minimum guarantee.
1.2.11.1.1.1. Con: Citizens have some degree of control over what sorts of social safety nets they want, through voting in politicians that advocate for the welfare structure they approve of.
1.2.11.1.2. Pro: Governments always have a mandate to provide a basic social safety net, because basic rights \(e.g. food, housing, education\) are a fundamental part of the social contract.
1.2.11.1.2.1. Pro: Welfare policies such as food stamps [have proven](http://www.vox.com/2016/7/16/11948800/robert-greenstein-basic-income) to be incredibly resilient, because they are fundamentally uncontroversial.
1.2.11.2. Con: In as much as a UBI encourages spending on unnecessary goods, this is also true of most existing welfare programs as they are predominantly cash transfers of a roughly similar amount.
1.2.11.3. Con: UBI might encourage spending more on luxuries but, someone has to make those luxuries hence providing economic growth and more jobs.
1.2.11.3.1. Con: That seeming "economic growth" is just the result of bad accounting/math. At some level, this "extra spending" comes from taxing someone who would have spent or invested that money elsewhere \(or by printing more money, increasing inflation/debt, which harms the economy\).
1.2.11.4. Con: [Statistics indicate](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2013/12/this-chart-blows-up-the-myth-of-the-welfare-queen/282452/) that this is simply not true. Families on some form of public assistance usually spend only a small portion of their money on luxuries, and it is reasonable to assume they'd do the same with a UBI.
1.2.11.4.1. Con: The cited statistics do not make it clear what is defined as "luxuries" or how persons are determined as belonging to the  "Public Assistance" category. As such it is difficult to evaluate what proportion of money is strictly spent on essentials.
1.2.11.5. Con: Some luxury spending is important to maintaining a happy and healthy life. [Drug use](http://www.dualdiagnosis.org/drug-addiction/economic-status/) often arises when individuals become so depressed and hopeless that they feel they have no other option. A moderate amount of luxury spending could offset a far greater problem.
1.2.11.6. Con: A UBI offers basic income for basic living. An individual would need to work in order to afford more than that - and do a significant amount of work in order to have sufficient for luxuries.
1.2.11.7. Pro: For example, Swiss voters [overwhelmingly voted against](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-36454060) a proposed UBI.
1.2.11.7.1. Con: The unpopularity in one country does not prove people would be more likely to spend money on luxury items.
1.2.12. Con: In some countries, governments guarantee a percentage of their salary to people who have been fired from their job. Often, that is a rate that would far exceed that of a UBI. This gives unemployed people of all incomes enough of a buffer to handle jobless periods.
1.2.12.1. Con: The duration of these guaranteed salaries is limited. A UBI would provide a source of income indefinitely.
1.2.12.1.1. Pro: Most targeted unemployment benefits have time limitations. Across Europe, only Belgium guarantees unlimited unemployment benefits. In other countries the benefits last from 3 to 24 months for a worker with 22 years of contributions \([European Commission, pg. 5](http://ec.europa.eu/europe2020/pdf/themes/2015/unemployment_benefits_20151126.pdf)\).
1.2.12.2. Pro: [In Germany](https://www.angloinfo.com/how-to/germany/working/unemployment/unemployment-benefits) the government guarantees 60% of their salary to fired people.
1.2.12.2.1. Con: However the high percentage of the former salary is only provided for some months. Afterwards the affected individuals experience a significant downgrade in payments.
1.2.12.3. Con: These programs operate like an insurance scheme - people must have been earning a salary in the first place, and thus contributing to taxation.
1.2.12.4. Pro: Unemployment [programs](http://ec.europa.eu/europe2020/pdf/themes/2015/unemployment_benefits_20151126.pdf) in European countries offer around [75 percent](http://www.iamexpat.nl/expat-page/official-issues/benefits/unemployment-benefit/requirements) of their last earned income rate for 1 to 2 years.
1.2.12.5. Con: This is only true for people who meet certain criteria, such as having officially worked at least 6 months out of the last 12 or having worked at their last job for at least 3 years. Also: these benefits are usually cut off the second this person accepts a job - any job, even one that pays 10 times less than the "poverty line".
1.2.13. Con: This is true only when talking about relative numbers, however it's the total cost of UBI that needs to be compared because \(as not everyone receives social welfare\) it is likely to represent much more spending overall.
1.2.13.1. Con: Since the money is transferred from tax payers directly back to them, it's not a question of "spending" but a question of wealth distribution. The question here is whether the amount of bureaucracy and case-handling will be larger or smaller.
1.2.13.1.1. Pro: Indeed, we tend to always see things under the perspective of "we're going to pay more taxes" when in truth, if everyone received a basic income thanks to these taxes, then it would only actually cost money to the wealthiest, who would give more than they'd receive. The thing to determine would be the exact balance between sums paid as taxes by each population category, and sums received by same categories. Or in other words, the exact amount of wealth distribution.
1.2.13.2. Pro: A UBI will eventually come from tax money. Our tax system have two goals, one is to provide public services, another is for redistribution. A UBI serves as a means of redistribution, then debating merely of its cost provide little value. It is merely a redistribution of wealth rather than spending.
1.2.13.2.1. Pro: Fairer distribution of wealth has shown to boost economic by increased domestic spending. [Better Economic Growth When Wealth Distributed Towards the Poor](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jun/04/better-economic-growth-when-wealth-distributed-to-poor-instead-of-rich)
1.2.13.3. Con: Because of increased job satisfaction, workers go on sick-leave less often and are more likely to work more hours or until a later age. This will increase tax payer spending and wealth, which will feed back into the system and possibly mitigate part if not all of the increased spending.
1.2.13.4. Pro: A UBI's effectiveness would vary depending on the specific nation's economic and social needs, and would not be a reliable long term means of a citizen's well-being.
1.2.13.5. Pro: A large part of the UBI money will go to people who are not in financial need. So it's hardly efficient.
1.2.14. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.
1.2.15. Con: According to the vast majority of experts, [replacing current welfare programs with UBI is a bad idea](http://www.igmchicago.org/surveys/universal-basic-income).
1.2.15.1. Con: This is a very biased survey, proposing to replace several essential services and valuable subsidies with a very low UBI. Many people who support UBI as a concept would vote against this particular proposal.
1.2.15.1.1. Con: The "very low UBI" in the proposal was roughly $13,000 per person. Across the entire United States \(assuming roughly 250M eligible recipients\), this would cost over $3,000,000,000,000 \($3 trillion\). 
If $13,000 is too low, it does not seem that anything "sufficient" would be affordable, as discussed by this [super-con.](https://www.kialo.com/argument/1634.1473)
1.2.15.2. Con: Policy effectiveness is a not a matter of voting. We rely on similar examples and previous studies to gauge the potential effects of new policies.
1.2.15.3. Con: This statement is misleading. A more accurate statement would read "A handful of experts don't agree that this particular version of a UBI would be successful."
1.2.15.4. Con: The thesis proposed in the survey tied UBI with elimination of basic public health insurance in the US. Health insurance is not a welfare program.
1.2.15.5. Con: This links to a relatively small panel of economists, not a sampling of all relevant experts across disciplines, making the wording of this claim highly misleading.
1.2.16. Pro: UBI is inevitable if modern society still believes its citizens have a right, at some point in their lifetime, to affect some form of 'retirement'. As people live longer and birth rates fall, social care in old age must change and we cannot expect falling numbers of contributors to finance increasing numbers of beneficiaries.
1.2.16.1. Con: Social Security already discourages saving for retirement. Enacting an even more expensive UBI makes retirement self sufficiency even more difficult and less incentivized.
1.2.16.1.1. Con: Nobody is "self sufficient" \(short of someone living in the wilds\), it's just a question of how any given individual depends on society.
1.2.16.2. Con: Retirement on other taxpayers' expense is not a right in itself. This is just something the wealthiest of societies have been able to afford for a brief period in history, namely the decades of post-WW2 economic growth and favourable demographic conditions. Although nice to have, a universal pension system has not proved its sustainability outside of perfect conditions.
1.2.16.3. Pro: If the life expectancy [keeps going up](http://emfsurveydallas.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/04/North-American-Life-Expectancy-Chart.jpg), companies let go of workers past a certain age \(see [IBM](http://www.dailycamera.com/boulder-business/ci_31771722/ibm-boulder-job-cuts-targeted-older-workers)\), social security runs out, and care-taking and medical expenses go up after retiring, then a UBI could be a viable option to assist seniors.
1.2.16.4. Con: If people are living longer, they will be able to work longer. Thus, they will not need a UBI to support them, as a job can.
1.2.16.4.1. Pro: This is especially true, since seniors can now pick up work-at-home and computer jobs \(compared to the hard-labor, industrial jobs of the past\).
1.2.16.4.2. Con: People do not always retain sufficient health to work full-time in their later years.
1.2.17. Pro: Employers will no longer be forced to play the role of social security guarantors. Employing will be easier, cheaper and therefore more accessible.
1.2.18. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.1.
1.2.19. Pro: Welfare programs are conditional in ways that are unfair and place unreasonable expectations and burdens on the poor they seek to help.
1.2.19.1. Pro: UBI provides a delivery mode that can provide money to those who cannot easily access jobs \(such as disabled or rural individuals\).
1.2.19.2. Con: Conditions such as demonstrating a willingness to seek a job in order to receive unemployment benefits seem fair and socially useful.
1.2.19.3. Pro: These conditions can include urine drug testing to access welfare checks or weekly unscheduled visits at the homes of welfare recipients.
1.2.19.3.1. Con: No explanation has been offered as to why this is "unfair" or "unreasonable"; on the contrary, many people would consider it reasonable \(at least on the surface\) that one must undergo periodic drug tests in order to receive government support.
1.2.19.4. Pro: Governments are bad at determining these conditions because the most wealthy in society hold the levers of power, thus they lacks empathy with those in positions of least influence in society.
1.2.19.4.1. Con: Governments are accountable to the majority democratically. Recent US election results suggest that the [working class](https://newrepublic.com/article/138754/blame-trumps-victory-college-educated-whites-not-working-class) have considerable voting power to hold elites to account.
1.2.19.4.1.1. Con: Many voices are part of minorities \(e.g. poor Black people\) that do not belong to the majority of working class.
1.2.19.4.1.2. Con: Governmental representatives have already shown that they are willing to disregard the voice of the majority in order to pursue their own interests. \(e.g. [Ajit Pai ignores outcry from all sides](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2017/dec/07/net-neutrality-fcc-ajit-pai)\)
1.2.19.5. Con: Many targeted welfare programs provide certain necessary goods \(food, medicines, education supplies\), unlike a UBI that will provide only money and will increase the number of things poor people need to worry about.
1.2.19.5.1. Con: Many welfare programs also offer cash that the recipients need to worry about distributing.
1.2.19.5.2. Pro: Poor people might experience anxiety from the necessity of managing money by themselves, as they were used to have a government providing goods for them.
1.2.19.5.2.1. Con: This is the same situation they'll be in when paid a wage.
1.2.19.5.2.1.1. Con: Even when being paid a wage the people may still be receiving some government benefits \(e.g. medicare\) that take some of the burden of budgetary planning off of the poor people's shoulders.
1.2.19.5.2.1.2. Con: Someone who is being paid a wage can be expected on average to be more fiscally/personally responsible than someone who is unable or \(perhaps temporarily\) unwilling to get a job. For example, someone may not be able to get a wage because they suffer from mental health issues—mental health issues which also may prevent them from budgeting well.
1.2.19.5.3. Con: Families know better than the state in which areas they need to spend, and in which they need to spend less.
1.2.19.5.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.6.17.2.4.1.1.
1.2.19.5.3.2. Con: The parent claim does not offer any empirical studies to support what it is claiming.
1.2.20. Pro: UBI is better than other ideas to improve economic conditions targeted for everyone, such as guaranteed jobs \([1](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/22/us/politics/democrats-guaranteed-jobs.html), [2](https://www.seattletimes.com/business/economy/guaranteed-jobs-may-not-solve-the-democrats-bigger-problem-with-many-voters/)\).
1.2.21. Pro: A UBI is portable \(especially with technology that allows benefits to travel with a person\), as welfare programs usually have a permanent location that a person needs to travel to in order to receive assistance.
1.2.21.1. Pro: A UBI helps people who move a lot, as those people would not need to keep finding new centers to receive the same benefits as before the person moved.
1.2.22. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.
1.2.23. Pro: A UBI affords choice and agency to individuals to make economic decisions that targeted welfare programs cannot.
1.2.23.1. Con: Choices can be facilitated in a variety of other ways. For instance [education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_voucher) and [housing](http://www.cbpp.org/research/housing/policy-basics-the-housing-choice-voucher-program) vouchers can be used to increase the choices between government services for poor individuals.
1.2.23.1.1. Con: Vouchers still limit choices to government approved options and make assumptions about how much people should and will spend on specific things. In this sense, a UBI provides a higher level of agency or accounting for relative differences in expenditures based on circumstance.
1.2.23.1.2. Con: The most direct and efficient method of facilitating choices in a society is through a common currency exchanged for goods and services within a free market environment.
1.2.23.2. Con: Government has greater access to research institutions and therefore macro-level information. This can be used to manage inter-generational dynamics better than an individual is able to.
1.2.23.2.1. Pro: For instance, moving young children into better areas has little effect on their parents, but massively improves their [expected earnings](http://scholar.harvard.edu/hendren/publications/effects-exposure-better-neighborhoods-children-new-evidence-moving-opportunity). Thus parents may be reluctant to move without information about how their futures will be affected, which governments are better placed to access.
1.2.23.2.2. Con: Macro-level information is useful for setting macro-level policy that impacts numerous groups. Less so for micro-level decision making where individual circumstance and chance play a massive role in resource distribution.
1.2.23.2.3. Con: Macro-level information is costly to collect and often out of date as soon as it is made available.
1.2.23.3. Con: It is disingenuous to claim that money is equivalent to choices. Behavioural evidence shows that people tend to be risk loving, discount expected losses, and are [unable to conceptualise distant future benefits](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/politicsandpolicy/book-review-poor-economics/). Often this results in people wasting money on unwise purchases.
1.2.23.3.1. Con: Money is equivalent to choice, as in many cases people can purchase goods or services that actually influence their own future choices towards wise outcomes. For example, the wealthy spend a lot of money purchasing [commitment devices](http://messymatters.com/akrasia/) that enable or incentivise self-control, such as medical insurance or a personal trainer.
1.2.23.3.2. Pro: People treat losses as less likely to affect them adversely than they actually are. This is why people drive drunk and choose not to wear seatbelts, when the rational choice is to do neither of these things.
1.2.23.3.2.1. Con: This is highly subjective. There is no actual evidence suggesting most people would take those risks.
1.2.23.3.3. Con: This is more likely to happen in situations where people are encouraged to think short term, and have no stability. People are more likely to consider long term impacts when they know their basic needs will be provided for as long as they make wise decisions.
1.2.23.3.4. Con: The behavioral evidence cited above has been contradicted numerous times, including in the science study referenced in [this](http://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2013/11/your-brain-on-poverty-why-poor-people-seem-to-make-bad-decisions/281780/) article, which notes that poverty and the lack of stability causes bad decision making rather than it being an innate characteristic.
1.2.23.3.5. Con: Money enables individuals to invest in education. Education extends occupational possibilities and choices.
1.2.23.4. Pro: Targeted programs are inherently paternalistic, insofar as they assume that the government is better suited than the disadvantaged themselves at making decisions benefiting their well-being.
1.2.23.4.1. Con: Most targeted programs only make minimal assumptions about the needs and desires of the disadvantaged: they provide things such as money, food, and childcare that are of universal value.
1.2.23.4.2. Pro: Welfare programs provide benefits designed for the average person who uses welfare. Most people do not reflect the average and have diverse needs, so their diverse needs can be better met by individuals making their own choices.
1.2.23.4.2.1. Con: Most welfare programs, as a baseline, provide money to those in need. Money can be used by people to purchase what they need, regardless of what those needs are.
1.2.23.4.2.1.1. Con: Some cash grants are not equivalent to a complete unconditional cash grant that offers full agency to those making decisions and not just partial agency.
1.2.23.4.2.2. Pro: A UBI helps decentralise the management of funds by allowing more local involvement in the process.
1.2.23.4.3. Pro: People are best at making economic decisions for themselves, as they know their own needs and circumstances and can thus better account for where to direct money.
1.2.23.4.3.1. Con: Dominant members of households can misuse UBI payments to further their own needs instead of providing for their families.
1.2.23.4.3.1.1. Con: This problem can apply to any family with capital of any kind.
1.2.23.4.3.1.2. Con: Considering everyone would be receiving the same UBI, it would be difficult for any individual in a family to totally abuse their power and waste money in a way that wouldn't be obvious to other members of their family.
1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1. Pro: With a UBI, women will have their own money and no longer be completely dependent on their partner's salary.
1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1.1. Con: Women are adults, if they are dependent on anyone's salary that was a mistake they made. Why should society have the pay for an individual's poor choice?
1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Many women were forced to make that decision before they were adults. Other women were encouraged or even coerced by society and even their families to make this decision regardless of their desires. An UBI would give these women the opportunity to reverse those circumstances that were forced upon them.
1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1.1.2. Con: Society is not obligated to pay for the mistakes of an individual, but it is certainly morally virtuous for society to do so. As with any other morally virtuous thing, it should be encouraged.
1.2.23.4.3.1.3. Con: The same is possible with welfare checks.
1.2.23.4.3.1.4. Pro: Comparatively, income is less accountable than conditional grants or particular items.
1.2.23.4.3.1.5. Pro: [Analysis of Canadian expenditure surveys](http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/1468-0335.00148/abstract) found that the gender of the recipient of a marginal increase in income predicted whether or not that income would be used to finance child care. This suggests that women should be targeted with grants to more efficiently improve social welfare, rather than everyone receiving equal incomes.
1.2.23.4.3.1.5.1. Con: The analysis is made with data from 1992, it is possible those gendered divisions have changed after more than 20 years.
1.2.23.4.3.1.5.2. Pro: In the National Rural Employment Guarantee Scheme \(NREGS\) in India female [NREGS](http://www.usc.es/economet/journals1/aeid/aeid14213.pdf) income goes towards children, while male income does not \(at least in the proposed categories, i.e. clothing and footwear\).
1.2.23.4.3.1.6. Pro: Often income would go into a shared bank account for a couple living together. Targeted welfare can often much more easily reach women or children directly.
1.2.23.4.3.2. Con: People tend to underestimate their future needs and often struggle to plan ahead. This is especially true when it comes to saving for future health problems because people often do not take seriously the possibility that they will be affected.
1.2.23.4.3.2.1. Con: National health care will still exist under a UBI model.
1.2.23.4.3.3. Pro: The needs of people are not static and there are unexpected costs that occur \(e.g. fees for a child's school trip\). Allowing people themselves to account for these changes is better.
1.2.23.4.3.4. Con: Targeted welfare, such as providing conditional or item-specific benefits, is not mutually exclusive to the poor also receiving cash grants.
1.2.23.4.3.4.1. Con: -> See 1.2.23.4.2.1.1.
1.2.23.4.3.5. Con: It is very easy for the government to analyse consumption patterns and account for seasonal changes.
1.2.23.4.3.5.1. Con: This kind of research is useful for analyzing broad trends, but it is inadequate to address different families with different circumstances and challenges.
1.2.23.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.9.1.
1.2.23.6. Pro: Welfare is only focused on "current state"  problems and that you must be in a "pit" that requires "assistance". A UBI is whole-life, and allows equal opportunities to make decisions that can help you plan, such as continuing education to ensure your skill set is current or providing the security to end an unhealthy relationship that you stay in for financial reasons.
1.2.23.7. Pro: In order for a state to provide housing, education and food for everyone, there would be needed a partly socialistic system, which althrough the history has been proven to not work. On the other hand, a state that supports private industries to help people with food, housing and education would be more viable.
