1.2.23.8. Con: UBI will dismantle not-for-profit essential welfare infrastructure eg: public food storage & distribution. This will lead to conflict and strife during times of shortage and waste in times of plenty.
1.2.23.8.1. Con: People freed from the requirement of performing paid work \(or provided with additional income\) by the existence of a UBI may - and many likely will - choose to devote their time and/or spare finances to altruistic or charitable projects, bolstering not-for-profit services.
1.2.23.9. Pro: A UBI shifts purchasing power from government to individuals thereby incentivizing what people actually want rather than what the majority wants them to want.
1.2.23.10. Con: UBI can be misused by lower income population due to lack of expertise in household financial management.
1.2.23.11. Con: This also affords UBI recipients the ability to exhaust their entire income on addictive substances \(sugary foods, alcohol, drugs, etc.\) which is why no one supports a full replacement of all social programs with UBI. There is no evidence that I am aware of that suggests that human beings make rational decisions when their incomes rise.
1.2.24. Con: A UBI will logically render void current welfare programs, and when people complain that they need more help, new welfare programs will be set up and will raise costs for taxpayers.
1.2.24.1. Con: This claim implies people should be ignored when they express a need for help, in the interests of retaining taxpayers' money, rather than the claims being investigated for veracity, and actioned if substantiated. The latter, not the former, is the way a humane society should operate. Taxpayers must accept that taxes are part of what maintains the foundation of the society they live in.
1.2.24.2. Pro: In [Ontario](https://www.ontario.ca/page/ontario-basic-income-pilot) a UBI pilot in a couple of located was recently terminated. It paid $17,000, and all existing supports remained in place. The cost would have been catastrophic for the provincial finances were it extended to all locations and made permanent.
1.2.25. Pro: A UBI would provide the threshold for all citizens of a nation. It would put the necessities for life of every citizen above the greed of the rich. Once all citizens are cared for, any excess can be fought over by whomever wants more than the basics.
1.2.26. Pro: A UBI allows labour to be more effectively allocated in the economy.
1.2.26.1. Pro: A UBI prevents people from self-development because it unconditional nature means it can't stipulate conditions \(such as job-seeking\) or provide incentives \(such as for retraining\). This means the labour market becomes less adaptive and flexible.
1.2.26.1.1. Con: This assumes that people's only motivation for self-development is to gain money. Humans seek growth and improvement with or without government manipulation for other reasons as well, such as for personal satisfaction or social status.
1.2.26.1.1.1. Con: This assumes that these self improvements will be beneficial to society, [when 60-70% of younger generations](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/01/17/well/family/is-your-child-a-phone-addict.html) are addicted to mobile devices and social networks. There is no reason to believe the statistics will improve, as social network addiction is claimed to be stronger than cocaine addiction.
1.2.26.1.2. Con: Working in a job that one hates, for very little pay, is not self-development. It actually traps them in a perpetual state of none-development.
1.2.26.1.2.1. Pro: Stress \(such as from a hated job or excessive hours\) can take time away from self-development, and force people to use all their spare time in attempting to survive and recover and/or escapism. The related exhaustion can also prevent them from thinking in the long term, beyond the immediate moment.
1.2.26.1.3. Con: The theory of self-determinism suggests humans have a need for competence in a skill or skills. A UBI would offer the opportunity to focus on competence rather than acquiring a basic level of income.
1.2.26.2. Con: Universal welfare would decrease the incentive to stay on top of changing market conditions and develop the skills and personal savings needed to stay productive and self-sufficient, because the guaranteed income provides a fallback option in the event that one becomes unemployed and financially depleted.
1.2.26.2.1. Con: This claim is only true if people are happy to take a hit in their standards of living due to not earning additional money.
1.2.26.3. Pro: A UBI will allow for a freer form of working where people can take jobs and pursue projects more directly related to their skills, while still having a stable standard of living while they wait on the fruit of their collective labors.
1.2.26.3.1. Con: If necessity is the mother of innovation, a lack of need may inhibit innovation by depressing motivation.
1.2.26.3.1.1. Con: The necessity there referenced is a task that is undoable, or needs to be done better, not a lack of means in general. For example, projecting ones voice thousands of miles almost instantaneously was impossible before the invention of the telephone, and being able to eat won't make working in the dark easier, but a light-bulb will.
1.2.26.3.1.2. Con: Or it may create the free time for a motivation or inspiration which would otherwise be repressed and ignored until it fades \(due to higher - or higher-seeming - priorities\) to instead be pursued.
1.2.26.3.1.3. Con: The need for food and shelter is only one need that might spur innovation. Other needs would arise when this most basic need is removed and hence spur wider innovation.
1.2.26.3.1.4. Con: Innovation is not necessarily an inherent good. Other goods may be preferred to an increase in innovation, such as a decrease in poverty, inequality, suffering, etc.
1.2.26.3.2. Con: This outcome can be achieved with a more traditional unemployment insurance scheme.
1.2.26.3.2.1. Con: Not all types of work are covered by these schemes — non-traditional pursuits in music, writing and art do not have a quantifiable average income which can be addressed.
1.2.26.3.2.2. Con: A traditional unemployment insurance scheme loses many people through the cracks but is also easily exploitable.
1.2.26.3.2.2.1. Pro: Furthermore, it only saves people from the stress of poverty after poverty is already present. Economic stress produces compiled anxiety in a household due to education costs and requirement for more loans and credit.
1.2.26.3.3. Pro: Without the burden of basic material survival, individuals have more time and energy to consider the wider issues relating to their jobs. This tends to lead to innovation, as well new possibilities. Freedom from material want allows space for perspectives to change in such a way that benefits society as a whole.
1.2.26.3.3.1. Pro: UBI would foster innovation by freeing people from toiling to meet basic needs.
1.2.26.3.3.2. Pro: One of the first thing large companies do for desirable employees is take money off the table by providing them with an income that allows them to be free from worry in order to pursue the objectives of their jobs. By expanding this to the general public, and taking away the worry of basic housing and nutrition, it allows for creativity and realization of potential. Most people will get bored of sitting around and doing nothing.
1.2.26.3.3.2.1. Pro: Universal Basic Income merely is Capitalism that doesn't start at zero dollars. It starts with food, shelter, and health.
1.2.26.3.4. Pro: Put another way it's [Seneca's barbell](https://plasubtil.wordpress.com/2014/07/23/antifragile-chapter-ten-seneca/) strategy. UBI's the cost of an option that protects from the downside of extreme harm \(the absorbing barrier of destitution\) and so affords the freedom \(survival and time\) to engage in activities that may bring upside to do more than merely survive. Conceived this way, you set a level that allows not only for the basics of healthcare, shelter, and food; but is additionally structured for the skills acquisition that upside depends on. Redundancy? Absolutely.
1.2.26.3.5. Pro: A UBI promotes a more motivated, productive workforce in employees free to choose work according to their talents, interest, or prospective benefits rather than taking something just to survive.
1.2.26.3.6. Pro: A UBI affords people time and financial freedom to diversify their skill set.
1.2.26.3.6.1. Pro: UBI allows young incoming workers to the economy the time and money to first figure out what they truly love to do and are gifted at so, when they do enter the workforce, they are happier and much more productive over their 40-plus years of employment.
1.2.26.3.6.2. Con: Some people will not use this time to diversify their skill set.
1.2.26.3.6.2.1. Con: It is up to each individual to choose, whether or not they want to work more, educate themselves or to do other things. It is the same today: some people work, some study, and \(like it or not\) some stay home on a welfare program.
1.2.26.3.6.2.2. Con: Some people may not use this time to diversify their skill-set, however, it is unclear whether it will be a significant percentage, enough to negate the benefits of those who would.
1.2.26.3.6.3. Con: Such extra skills will be worthless if people do not apply them \(on a job\).
1.2.26.3.6.3.1. Con: This assumes that the only things that add worth to society are jobs. Volunteers in many fields seem to disprove this.
1.2.26.3.6.3.1.1. Con: This discussion is about the economy. Volunteers may do a lot of good, but jobs do the most for economic growth.
1.2.26.3.6.3.1.1.1. Con: Our economy is driven by people working: people can't afford not too. Thus while jobs are the engine of the current economy, that doesn't speak to the possibility of an alternate economic system in which volunteer work forms the basis of work.
1.2.26.3.6.3.2. Con: In a time of exponential technology growth, it is unpredictable what jobs will exist in the future.  As people explore their previously-untapped skills \(such as creativity\), they could very well invent a job that's needed, rather than waiting for a government or corporation to discover that need.
1.2.26.3.6.3.3. Con: A "skill" that provides recreation has the benefit of increasing morale regardless of whether it provides an economic benefit or not.
1.2.26.3.6.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.1.1.
1.2.26.3.6.5. Pro: A UBI can allow people to pursue safer jobs \(they can avoid resorting to low-paid, unsafe ones due to a lack of money\), while allowing industries can automate [unsafe jobs](https://www.robotics.org/blog-article.cfm/Dangerous-Robot-Jobs/15) without impacting people's livelihood.
1.2.27. Con: A UBI means that any other welfare schemes that could help individuals with specific needs are either cut to fund the UBI, or become unpopular because the UBI will squander political capital and public goodwill. If the government truly cares for those in special need, it would support means-tested, targeted welfare over a UBI.
1.2.27.1. Con: With a UBI, the pressure on other social welfare programs is drastically decreased. In the US, the costs of social security, unemployment benefits, medicare and health rebates, and food entitlements [are approximately $2.2 trillion dollars](https://www.nationalpriorities.org/budget-basics/federal-budget-101/spending/). Cutting these programs would become feasible under a UBI, where it would replace most entitlement systems.
1.2.27.1.1. Pro: Even moderate reductions would provide feasibly close to one trillion dollars of freed government revenue.
1.2.27.1.1.1. Con: This would not be "new" government revenue; the "reductions" in traditional welfare program would be eclipsed by increases in spending on UBI.
1.2.27.1.2. Con: Without medicare, many low-income families would burn through their UBI very quickly, unless there's a medical system such as Canada has in place.
1.2.27.1.3. Con: If a $10,000/year UBI were enacted in the US, the total costs would be roughly around $2.5-3.5 trillion dollars \(depending on who receives benefits\). Thus, even if the traditional welfare programs were somehow fully cut, this would still represent an increase in overall spending by hundreds of billions of dollars.
1.2.27.1.3.1. Con: This [ignores the fact](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2017/01/why-we-should-all-have-a-basic-income/) that a UBI would also be implemented as a net transfer system: "In the same way it does not cost $20 to give someone $20 in exchange for $10, it does not cost $3 trillion to give every adult citizen $12,000 and every child $4,000, when every household will be paying varying amounts of taxes in exchange for their UBI. Instead it will cost around 30% of that, or about $900 billion."
1.2.27.1.3.1.1. Pro: $900 billion would be relatively easily accommodated through tiny tax rates increases \(5% for an extra $300b\) while also eliminating 25% of defence spending \($215b\), wage subsidies \($72b\), the home mortgage interest deduction \($70b\), and social security \($220b\).
1.2.27.1.3.1.1.1. Con: It is highly unlikely that there would be a 25% cut to military spending anytime soon.
1.2.27.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: There are already people who are significantly dissatisfied with the amount of military spending, and advocate for a reduction in it.
1.2.27.1.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con: It does not matter what a few people think; people have always wanted to cut the military spending. In the end, a 25% reduction seems highly unrealistic.
1.2.27.1.3.1.1.2. Con: 2017 tax revenues are $3.3 trillion, $300 billion is 9% of that, tax rate would have to be increased by 9%, which is nearly double the "tiny" 5% suggested, the math is off. Also, I'm not sure where these numbers came from defense spending is $590B, and Social Security is $939B, 25% of each is $148B and $235B respectively, $383B total, compared to the $435B for those two that's specified, this is off by more than $50B! Please link a source...[Budget Data](https://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/BUDGET-2019-BUD/pdf/BUDGET-2019-BUD-24.pdf)
1.2.27.1.3.1.2. Con: "Net transfer" systems lose efficiency when the people who create value \(and subsequently earn money\) are taxed to cover for those who do not or cannot. Otherwise, deep/extreme socialism could solve all of our inequity and inefficiency problems. Traditional, means-tested welfare systems, while imperfect, can strike the proper balance of redistribution.
1.2.27.1.3.1.3. Con: This ignores the fact that taxes would still have to be raised \(or other programs cut\) in order to afford this program. Many people might end up getting more money than they have to pay in new taxes, but overall taxation would have to increase by at least however much money is provided through the UBI.
1.2.27.1.3.1.3.1. Con: It doesn't matter if the taxes are higher, when you are given the money to pay those higher taxes up-front.
1.2.27.1.3.1.4. Con: The US population is estimated at 252 million adults, and 74 million persons under age 18. At the levels you suggest that would require tax revenues of $3.3 trillion, even if you then redistribute that. Current US revenues are about $3.3 trillion, so you're talking about doubling the current US tax rates.[Federal Budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_federal_budget#/media/File:CBO_Infographic_2017.png) [Population Data](https://www.census.gov/quickfacts/fact/table/US/PST045217)
1.2.27.1.3.1.5. Con: While it doesn't cost $20 to give someone $20 in exchange for $10, you still have to have the $20 to start with. You'd still have to collect the full $3.3 trillion. If you're arguing for negative income tax, I suggest you tack on to one of the already existing claims for that.
1.2.27.1.3.1.6. Pro: If we assume UBI level is $12000, and it's a negative income tax, and if you make more than $12000, you get nothing. Then the whole program would require extra revenues in the US of about $600B. [Income Data](https://www2.census.gov/programs-surveys/cps/tables/pinc-01/2018/pinc01_1_1_1.xls)
1.2.27.1.3.1.6.1. Con: $12000, is well below the US poverty line of $22500. At that level it would cost $1.6 trillion, and this argument is under the claim, "UBI's that meet the proverty line are too expensive for governments to afford". At some point, yes you can lower the number enough that some\(not all\) people won't argue with you about the funding anymore, at that point would you even be making things enough better to worry about providing the benefit.
1.2.27.1.4. Pro: In fact one of the very goals of a UBI is to obviate the need for any other social welfare programs. The saving it hopes to exploit is not just the money given to the needy in such a program but the entire overhead of delivering them \(including staff, real estate, and infrastructure\). All these savings go toward making a livable UBI feasible and attractive as an alternative to not addition to all other existing welfare programs. [Guaranteed Income as a Replacement for the Welfare State](http://www.fljs.org/files/publications/Murray.pdf)
1.2.27.2. Con: This argument supports the status quo which has demonstrably failed to help those in special need. A UBI would still allow for extra  targeted assistance for extraordinary need.
1.2.27.3. Pro: This is a concern for people currently claiming disability-related benefits in particular.
1.3. Con: UBIs that meet the poverty line are too expensive for governments to afford.
1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.2.
1.3.2. Con: Microsimulation from Barcelona University study shows that [only 3.4% of Spanish GDP](http://www.redrentabasica.org/rb/la-renta-basica-incondicional-y-como-se-puede-financiar-comentarios-a-los-amigos-y-enemigos-de-la-propuesta/) is needed to finance a sufficient UBI. This is without removing any funding for the army, health or education.
1.3.2.1. Con: Microsimulations cannot credibly simulate the effects of a nationwide UBI. Criticisms of UBI tend to center around its macroeconomic effects, which microsimulations do not address.
1.3.2.1.1. Con: Macroeconomic study shows that UBI would grow the economy. [rooseveltinstitute.org](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/)
1.3.2.1.1.1. Con: The study seems to improperly estimate investment and loan effects. That is to say: the study claims overall economic activity would increase because the proposal would be funded largely by taxes on the higher-income population, which the study states tend to save a higher proportion of their money. However, a decrease in savings decreases the ability to invest/acquire loans \(contrary to its assumption that "Generally, investment is determined by the level of economic activity"\).
1.3.2.1.1.2. Con: The study does not seem to account for decreased-incentive effects. That is to say, the study proposes a policy of higher taxes on the rich, but does not account for whether this might discourage the rich from working as much, offering as many investments/loans, and/or expanding their business.
1.3.2.1.1.3. Con: The study assumes that inflation will not significantly be affected by this change in the economy, even though it just seems that more people are spending, rather than the money being used more efficiently.
1.3.2.2. Con: Given Spain's GDP of [$1.2 Trillion \(USD\)](https://tradingeconomics.com/spain/gdp) and a population of 46.4 million, 3.4% of GDP equates to a UBI of only $879 per person.
1.3.2.2.1. Pro: Even assuming that $879/year in Spain equates to roughly $1160/year in the USA—since the cost of living in Spain is roughly 32% lower \(as suggested by [this site](http://www.mylifeelsewhere.com/cost-of-living/united-states/spain)\)—this is clearly an insufficient sum for a UBI.
1.3.2.2.2. Con: Not every single person in Spain would receive a UBI; such as non-citizens or children.
1.3.2.2.3. Pro: Going by Spain's standard 40 hour workweek and minimum wage of EUR 829/month, that's a yearly mandate of USD 12,173, way more than USD 879.
1.3.2.2.3.1. Con: It's not necessary to tie the minimum UBI to the minimum wage. Presumably collecting UBI costs less for the recipient than maintaining a job might cost for a worker. There's a reduction in clothing, transportation, education, childcare, etc. These necessary expenses might lead to a higher minimum wage compared to UBI.
1.3.2.3. Con: Expressing costs in terms of GDP is misleading, because GDP generally does not link well with government taxation/spending. For example, in the US, federal and total government revenue in 2016 [was estimated to be $3.27 and $6.13 trillion](https://www.usgovernmentrevenue.com/federal_revenue) \(respectively\). US GDP in 2016, on the other hand, was multiple times that: [nearly $18.6 trillion](https://www.bea.gov/newsreleases/national/gdp/2017/pdf/gdp4q16_adv.pdf). Ultimately, many government programs can be made to look small when expressed in percentages of GDP, but that is misleading when talking about government spending.
1.3.2.4. Con: This 3.4% will have to be ploughed out of some other thing it has been already allocated for, and possibly allocated year on year. That departments and services that would be would suffer as a result.
1.3.2.4.1. Con: Most UBI implementations will make other benefits obsoleete. Like unemployment benefits, or multiple child monetary support.
1.3.3. Con: If it were impossible for the government to afford, [Richard Nixon wouldn't have proposed](https://www.nixonfoundation.org/2017/01/nixons-vision-for-american-governance/) one during [the 70's](https://www.nixonfoundation.org/2014/06/family-assistance-plan-families-can-succeed/); a much less prosperous time. \(It was [shot down by the left](https://thecorrespondent.com/4503/the-bizarre-tale-of-president-nixon-and-his-basic-income-bill/173117835-c34d6145) because they thought they'd reject one version to approve a more generous one later\)
1.3.3.1. Con: The fact that he proposed one does not mean it was reasonable in the long run; it would not be the first time a leader implemented a policy that would ruin their country.
1.3.3.2. Con: The "Nixon Disapproves" criterion for public policy is not always reliable.
1.3.4. Con: A UBI offsets its cost by creating wealth and increasing the amount of economic activity that states can tax.
1.3.4.1. Con: A UBI does not inherently create wealth. It merely redistributes it.
1.3.4.1.1. Con: Wealth is based on value provided, so if the distribution of money sends it to a bearer for whom it has more value, then wealth is created.
1.3.4.2. Con: The degree to which it might "create wealth" to offset its costs is likely to be low/fractional. Overall, this in itself does not suggest that UBIs would actually be affordable.
1.3.4.3. Pro: The size of an economy is determined by two main factors: first, the total amount of money available. But, more importantly: the speed of monetary circulation between actors \(a factor which is commonly overlooked\). Again in a very simplified model, everyone can get rich on just $1 along as it is transferred very rapidly in exchange for goods. In fact, just handing money to people to buy things may be the best stimulus for the economy imaginable \(but cause inflation, over stimulus\).
1.3.5. Pro: For example, in the U.S. providing a UBI of $10,000 \(just below the poverty level\) to 300 million Americans would cost over $3 trillion dollars, easily [eclipsing current levels of tax revenue](http://www.cbpp.org/poverty-and-opportunity/commentary-universal-basic-income-may-sound-attractive-but-if-it-occurred).
1.3.5.1. Con: -> See 1.2.27.1.
1.3.5.2. Con: Many UBI programs do not pay out to children. There are only about 250 million adults in the USA. Some programs only pay out to adults over 21 years of age, thereby reducing the number of benefactors to 230 million.
1.3.5.2.1. Con: In the end, this is only a small difference from the grandparent. If the UBI were $10,000 annually and there were roughly 200 million benefactors, expenses would be roughly $2 trillion—half of [current federal spending overall.](https://www.thebalance.com/current-u-s-federal-government-spending-3305763)
1.3.5.3. Con: UBI could be scaled up very gradually over time. These small transfer payments would be more likely to be spent by the majority of citizens, who are lower income, creating the greatest possible economic multiplier effect for the amount of money given. When you hit the peak and the multiplier begins to diminish, you can slow down the increase in UBI levels and eventually stop the increase and hold it steady
1.3.5.3.1. Con: Any discussion of consumption multipliers must factor for how savings are [multiplied](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/m/multipliereffect.asp) by the fractional reserve system of banking. Decreasing savings to increase consumption very reasonably may have no net-multiplier effect \(possibly even a negative multiplier effect\), and/or also may reasonably decrease the efficiency of combined consumption + investment.
1.3.5.3.2. Con: This claim merely assumes we are not already at "the peak"; the balance between savings and consumption could reasonably already be at or near equilibrium.
1.3.5.3.3. Con: This claim merely assumes we will be able to accurately determine what/where "the peak" is, but such econometric analyses are not simple when conducted across an entire economy, in which there might be recessions/booms, policy changes, disruptions, imperfect reporting, or any other sort of interference/issue.
1.3.5.4. Con: [Here is an example](https://works.bepress.com/widerquist/75/) where it would cost $539 billion. It was made by a leading figure of the Basic Income Earth Network, based on data from the U.S. Census Bureau.
1.3.5.4.1. Con: The cited [source](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/much-ubi-cost/) is unreliable—even dubious.
1.3.5.4.1.1. Pro: The source makes an extremely dubious/misleading comparison between UBI and current entitlement spending. Specifically, the source says that the gross cost of a UBI program is "often-mentioned but not-very-meaningful," but in the very next part of the sentence, it compares the *net cost* of a UBI to the *gross cost* of current entitlement programs. If the source says that gross costs of a UBI aren't important, it shouldn't compare UBI's net costs to gross costs of entitlement programs.
1.3.5.4.1.2. Pro: The source itself describes its estimates as "back-of-the-envelope calculations," rather than even halfway rigorous economic models.
1.3.5.5. Con: This implies that the current taxation structure is the ideal structure. UBI allows the tax free threshold to be lowered, increasing revenue from the bottom, and a change to the tax structure for the top end of town more in line with the [New Deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Deal) could very well allow it to be sustainable.
1.3.5.5.1. Con: If the tax free threshold is lowered \(i.e. if poorer people could be taxed\) this would undermine the value of the UBI.
1.3.5.5.2. Con: If the tax free threshold were lowered, this might discourage poorer people from increasing their income \(e.g. by working\) in the same way that traditional welfare does \(to an extent\).
1.3.5.5.3. Con: This merely assumes that increasing taxes on a poorer segment of society will noticeably offset the spending increases. On the contrary, it seems unlikely that this will have significant positive effects on revenue.
1.3.5.6. Con: Providing a UBI, particularly when targeted at the consumer sector of the economy \(which traditionally has a very high multiplier effect on fiscal injections\) can also drastically improve economic performance to pay for increases in government spending.
1.3.5.6.1. Con: A UBI is not targeted toward the consumer sector of the economy; it is given to everyone equally.
1.3.5.6.1.1. Con: The vast majority of citizens are from the "consumer sector," so the program still has the highest impact on this sector.
1.3.5.6.2. Pro: Households in the US have very low rates of savings, and thus very high rates of spending. Even assuming a moderately high propensity to save, of 0.25 \(25% of every dollar of income saved\), the multiplier effect of additional money to households would sit around 3-4. The US currently collects around one quarter of GDP in the form of tax revenue, meaning that the UBI would come close to paying for itself in the form of tax revenue increases.
1.3.5.6.2.1. Con: Treating the UBI as a stimulus assumes that the poor are not already spending at similar rates through the status quo means-tested system, and any gains in pretax income given to the status quo middle class must surely be offset by any increases in the marginal tax rate that would result in this.
1.3.5.6.2.2. Con: -> See 1.3.5.3.1.
1.3.5.6.3. Pro: Even when not directly claimed by tax revenue, increases to national wealth that come from increased economic activity justify economic policies to increase government wealth, such as international borrowing or printing new money. When these policies are implemented as the result of increased economic performance, negative side effects such as inflationary pressures and loss of credit worthiness can be avoided.
1.3.5.6.3.1. Con: This assumes that a UBI increases spending among low socio-economic status people over the current means-tested welfare system, which already provides substantial capital to this group to spend on necessities like food and shelter.
1.3.5.7. Con: The United States has already gone through many years of tax reductions, as well as reductions in government spending. Congruently with this the income disparity has skyrocketed, and many already live below the poverty line. Therefore the United States may be a bad example to evaluate the feasibility of implementing a UBI.
1.3.5.8. Con: -> See 1.2.27.1.3.1.
1.3.6. Con: Germany is the most powerful state of Europe, but it [pays a basic liveable income of 404€ /month for each unemployed person living alone](http://ec.europa.eu/social/main.jsp?catId=1111&langId=en&intPageId=4556\]).
1.3.6.1. Con: This is a form of means-tested/targeted welfare, not a UBI. If Germany had implemented a UBI, it would have to pay far more people than just "unemployed people living alone"; these new costs very well may be unaffordable.
1.3.7. Pro: For example, in Sweden \(one of the largest welfare states\) if each citizen were given a basic liveable income of 12,000€/year, the annual cost of this policy alone would be as much as the [government spends in total](http://www.government.se/articles/2016/09/the-2017-budget-in-five-minutes/). This seems unaffordable.
1.3.7.1. Con: The government could just come up with a different tax structure.
1.3.7.1.1. Con: Tax cannot be higher than GDP \(that would mean 100% tax\). Taxation in many develop countries is close to \(or even above\) 50% of GDP. So taxes could never be more than doubled. [List of tax revenue to GDP ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_tax_revenue_to_GDP_ratio).
1.3.7.1.2. Pro: UBI doesn't have to be a new institution. It can be built into tax graduation or a "negative tax" for those who make less then UBI.
1.3.7.2. Con: It is in the largest welfare states that a UBI would be the most affordable because it would replace the already-expensive set of social services.
1.3.7.3. Con: 12,000 Euros is too high for UBI. $7,500 would be nearer the mark so 6,500 Euros or £5,500. The fact that the example states 12,000 Euros and goes on to state that this would be unaffordable is testament to the fact that it would not be set this high in the first place.
1.3.7.3.1. Con: Nordic countries, and others such as Germany, typically set some forms of unemployment benefits around [40-60% of average annual incomes](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2015/apr/15/which-best-countries-live-unemployed-disabled-benefits), which would be around 12,000 Euros, meaning this figure is not wholly unprecedented or unreasonable.
1.3.7.3.1.1. Con: This figure is rather inflated. Whilst UBI means to be constant and universal, mentioned unemployment payment are limited in time. Also only those who had worked are eligible for these high benefits.
1.3.7.3.2. Con: Sure, but that argument is also a straw man in the same respect. if saying  "UBI's are not affordable because the government will set them at levels which it cannot afford" is a bad argument, then so is saying "UBI's are affordable because the government will set them at levels it can afford" We ought at least to grant the premise that the UBI in question would address the financial needs of the recipients, and proceed the debate from there.
1.3.7.3.3. Pro: Given the poverty line for a household in Sweden is 11,142€ \([half median household income](https://www.thelocal.se/20130529/48194)\) a UBI set at 6,000€ would be enough to ensure a household of two people is not in poverty.
1.3.7.4. Pro: In Stockholm that income doesn't even cover the cost of a one bed flat. A typical small radhaus \(terrace\) costs over 20,000 euros a year in rent.
1.3.7.4.1. Con: While Stockholm is the most expensive place to live in Sweden, someone living on welfare in the existing system \(income ~€12000\) can't affort that anyway, so the point is moot.
1.3.7.5. Pro: In the best case scenario, the government would have to scrap many major welfare schemes in order to afford the cost of a UBI.
1.3.7.5.1. Con: -> See 1.2.
1.3.7.5.2. Con: That's the point. The idea is to replace means-tested benefits with a universal income that a person can always rely on having.
1.3.7.6. Con: The total cost of UBI is not equal to the amount of money paid to individuals - rather, it is the differential increase or decrease in the wealth of the nation: [scottsantens.com](http://www.scottsantens.com/the-cost-of-universal-basic-income-is-the-net-transfer-amount-not-the-gross-price-tag).
1.3.7.7. Con: Giving every citizen 12,000€ may be unaffordable, but a UBI could be set a lower level than that; say around 6,000€.
1.3.7.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.7.3.3.
1.3.7.8. Con: What if it would be set as a percentage of GDP and that people could choose if they want UBI, or not \(for the sake of others\). For instance - people with high income from their work could reject their UBI for government budget, charity, or someone else, or to make UBI higher \(could be done only once per year to lower costs of administration\). This could teach people solidarity.
1.3.8. Pro: Most countries are already in debt; a situation that the additional spending required for a UBI will only worsen.
1.3.8.1. Con: While a UBI will probably lead to more spending, that could be offset by potential increases in tax collection from wealthier tax-payers.
1.3.8.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.1.
1.3.8.2. Con: According to International Monetary Fund data released in 2017, the general level of government debt globally is projected to fall, on average, in the years up to 2022. [\(Source: IMF projections\)](http://bit.ly/2hMuYLM)
1.3.8.3. Con: A straight UBI cuts the costs associated with running a complicated welfare system, allowing more budget allocation to go directly to the people receiving welfare instead of it being sunk into administration.
1.3.8.3.1. Con: It is unrealistic to suggest that a decrease in administration cost for welfare systems will make a substantial impact on the total cost of the system.
1.3.8.4. Con: 1. Government debt itself is not inherently bad assuming that the debt is issued in currency it controls and that the increased money supply does not create excessive inflation. 2. Debt may, in fact, have a multiplier effect on the economy by creating a chain reaction of economic activity. In general, when money supply increases at the bottom or through direct spending \(like infrastructure\) the multiplier is greater because the money is spent faster than by tax cuts to the top.
1.3.9. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.3.
1.3.10. Pro: Forms of guaranteed income that are not unconditional would offer many of the benefits of a UBI but at a fraction of the price.
1.3.10.1. Con: Adding conditions inevitably requires bureaucracy and monitoring, which requires costs that do not directly go towards the paying of benefits, increasing the inefficiency of the system.
1.3.10.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.
1.3.10.2. Pro: A UBI represents higher taxes and a lot more government spending because you're paying everyone instead of just the people on welfare.
1.3.10.2.1. Con: Even if spends would be higher they would be more useful because it would cut down the costs of distribution —which in fact provide no income — and thus put more funds in areas providing at least some value.
1.3.10.2.2. Con: Taxes and spending are not that worrying when everybody gets UBI in the end; if it is done right, most people would receive as much or more than they give, and a small number of very wealthy people would receive less. It's not a matter of higher government spending, but rather of wealth distribution.
1.3.10.2.3. Con: This is solved by adding/increasing the progressive income tax, so that the UBI is paid back after earning a certain amount. The majority of working people will still pay more in taxes than they receive in UBI.
1.3.10.3. Con: The net cost of UBI isn't the UBI amount times amount of people, because everyone who works will have the UBI taxed back depending on income. The net cost is only the amount of UBI that is not taxed back, like when someone doesn't work enough for that to happen.
1.3.10.4. Con: If the income is conditional, then it is not guaranteed.
1.3.10.5. Con: If a conditional income is not fully automatic, it becomes a burden for the people that need it the most. The application and bureaucracy is a huge obstacle when you're not even sure where your next meal will come from.
1.3.10.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.1.1.
1.3.11. Con: A [Negative Income Tax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_income_tax) refund is equivalent to a taxed UBI, but cheaper to administer. In 2015, the US spent [$1.4 trillion on Social and Income Security programs](https://taxfoundation.org/how-government-spends-your-tax-dollars/). The [2016 census](https://www2.census.gov/programs-surveys/cps/tables/pinc-01/2016/pinc01_1_1_1.xls) counted over 200 million people with less than $30,000 in annual income \(including [61 million kids](https://factfinder.census.gov/faces/tableservices/jsf/pages/productview.xhtml?src=bkmk)\). If we refund those with no income $10,000 a year and graduate the refund down to zero for incomes over $30,000, [it would cost $1.21 trillion](https://docs.google.com/spreadsheets/d/1FQcVYx1-uYUqswytSM0R50BuICkct-cnKMU-4wVOESE/edit?usp=sharing), saving $200 billion. It's more affordable than the current situation.
1.3.11.1. Con: This would need to be modified so it is not paid in a lump sum, however, as many people are ineffective at saving money and financial planning.
1.3.11.1.1. Pro: The IRS already has already taken steps to move towards a more frequent calculation and payment system \(quarterly, monthly, or per-paycheck\), to dilute the impact of tax season on both taxpayers finances and the agency workload.
1.3.11.2. Con: The data you referenced to only counts people equal or over 15 years old, it doesn't contemplate children at all, if the social and income security programs meet children above this age, them there will be a loss for those out of the scope of this census.
1.3.11.2.1. Con: Parents have financial obligations to their children, discounting the children in this case reinforces parental obligations while reducing the cost of such a plan on the government's budget.
1.3.11.3. Con: These data includes all sources of income including social security, without it there would be many more people who need payment making this unsustainable. Mainly retired people, who receive retirement benefits from social security, and you counted as having income.
1.3.11.3.1. Con: My particular suggestion would replace many welfare programs, but the average income of those programs is less than $10,000. In other words, most people would receive a greater benefit under this scheme. Not that I'm suggesting we suddenly dump all those programs and replace them with this. We'd have to phase the programs out and a UBI in.
1.3.11.3.1.1. Con: The [average retiree collects around $17,000 per year from Social Security](https://money.usnews.com/money/retirement/social-security/articles/2018-08-20/how-much-you-will-get-from-social-security), and they account for half of the $1.4 trillion total of Social and Income Security spending. Many of them also have other income sources \(interest on savings being the biggest\) and wouldn't qualify for a Negative Income Tax \(or a much smaller benefit\).
1.3.11.4. Con: This is close to just being a form of means-tested welfare \(albeit a very crude one at best\). Thus, it hardly supports the idea of a true UBI.
1.3.11.4.1. Con: Most proposals of a UBI don't exclude it from taxable income, so it absolutely is comparable to a UBI within a similar tax scheme. The math on how a UBI would be taxed depends on the rest of your income, just like a negative income tax bracket. Labelling this as means-tested is misleading. It's just a UBI that is taxed back if you don't really need it, just like any UBI that isn't tax-exempt would be.
1.3.11.5. Con: Some people may be depending on those $1.4T in social welfare benefits. If 1\) the amount of money provided is reduced by $200B and 2\) it is not concentrated among a more limited group of people, one can expect that some people who were already on the margins will fall below a subsistence or dignified levels.
1.3.12. Con: This is effectively saying that countries do not have enough money to avoid people starving. With very few exceptions, if a country's economy can create millionaires and even billionaires, it can afford to give everybody enough to eat.
1.3.12.1. Pro: Ensuring the life and health of our fellow man is one of our first moral obligations as humans.
1.3.12.1.1. Con: The moral obligations of humans are not the moral obligations of governments.
1.3.12.1.1.1. Con: Democratic governments are not only responsible for the people under their care, but also ideologically should be representative of them. Therefore, they should attempt to carry out their collective moral obligations, without descending into tyranny.
1.3.12.1.1.2. Con: Governments are made up of humans. Those men who are part of governments choose what rights should be defended by the law, so governments have many more moral obligations than normal citizens.
1.3.12.1.2. Con: We are morally obligated to ensure *opportunity,* not outcome—especially if people simply choose not to work. Traditional welfare can target those who are temporarily unfortunate \(e.g. family breadwinner dies\) or long-term disadvantaged \(e.g. disabled\), but a UBI provides a basic income to all people, regardless of their actual need.
1.3.12.1.3. Con: Although we are morally obligated to help others, this does not mean we should be legally compelled to do so, especially considering \(for example\) that what the government considers "need" and what is actually "need" may be 2 different things. Similarly, if the government already taxes its citizens without fixing the problems, it should be hesitant for blaming/shaming the rich for not providing even more.
Ultimately, the parent is missing a crucial link between moral principle and legal policy.
1.3.12.1.3.1. Con: Many people do not carry out moral obligations they do not immediately see/feel or are not aware of; legal mechanisms ensure moral obligations are fulfilled.
1.3.12.2. Con: An economy can have the potential to create a large amount of wealth, but still be unable to produce enough of a certain resource, such as food.
1.3.12.2.1. Con: Many of the countries in question produce enough food to comfortably feed more there population without any type of intervention.
1.3.12.2.2. Con: Whilst this is theoretically possible, the issue with millionaires in an economy where people are starving is that they represent a concentration of clout at the expense of others. When one person has a greater income in a day than another does in a year, it is not that the first is working harder: it is that they are profiting from the others work at their expense.
1.3.12.2.2.1. Pro: Investing does not directly produce any labour or products.
1.3.12.2.2.2. Con: Remuneration can be for work but for the most part in modern societies with free markets people are remunerated for their contribution to society. Those with the greatest contributions tend to be those who have produced innovation or improved efficiency. Thus much wealth transfer is a result of society "voting" for the contributions they value most by purchasing them in markets.
1.3.12.3. Con: In wealthier countries, very few people actually face even temporary starvation in a given year. In the U.S., for example,[less than 1% of the population](https://mises.org/library/america-struggling-hunger) faces hunger in a given year and [less than 4,000 people](http://www.worldlifeexpectancy.com/cause-of-death/malnutrition/by-country/) die from starvation or malnutrition. Estimates of how much a UBI would cost [vary](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/much-ubi-cost/) from 595 billion to over 4 trillion. Giving 1% of the population $150 a month for food to cover both starvation and food insecurity would only cost $5.9 billion.
1.3.12.4. Con: Most halfway-decent countries have enough money to "prevent people from starving," but the costs to maintain people above the poverty line are far more than just "providing enough money to prevent starvation." For example, people also need bare minimum levels of shelter, clothing, transportation, medicine, energy, and purpose/occupation—just to name a few examples. All of these expenses start adding up to thousands per year per person.
1.3.12.5. Con: The [grandparent claim](https://www.kialo.com/argument/1634.1473) is merely disputing that a UBI should be imposed instead of welfare. Governments are capable of providing welfare, but the costs of providing *Universal* basic income at a level which *meets the poverty line* are \(likely\) far greater than traditional, basic welfare for the needy.
1.3.12.6. Pro: Nations who control their own currency spend what it takes on what is most important to them. If the nation's priory is the dignity and productivity of its people, then it can't afford not to have a generous UBI.
1.3.13. Con: Implementing a UBI would allow governments to reduce spending in other areas; thus reducing its cost.
1.3.13.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.
1.3.13.2. Pro: A UBI would also allow the government to consolidate or eliminate negative income tax payments, such as the earned income tax credit in the US, [which currently costs between $60 and $90 billion](https://www.cato.org/publications/tax-budget-bulletin/earned-income-tax-credit-small-benefits-large-costs).
1.3.13.2.1. Con: $60-90 billion is a mere fraction of what a reasonable UBI would cost.
1.3.13.3. Pro: The estimated net costs of an UBI in the US add up to [$539 billion annually \($20,000/individual\)](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/much-ubi-cost/). The UBI would decrease the official poverty rate from actual 13.5% to roughly 0%. Currently, the US spends [$550 billion on poverty](https://www.forbes.com/sites/timworstall/2012/09/13/if-the-us-spends-550-billion-on-poverty-how-can-there-still-be-poverty-in-the-us/#5e6452d8fdf6) every year, achieving modest success only.
1.3.13.3.1. Con: This only considers the official poverty rate at the moment; if inflation \(or segmented inflation—i.e. inflation mainly just on low-cost goods and services\) were to occur, the official poverty line may rise, causing poverty rates to increase.
1.3.13.3.1.1. Pro: Segmented inflation realistically might occur since more people will be buying low-income goods/services that previously could not be afforded by so many people, such as basic foodstuffs, low-cost housing, cheap cars, etc.
1.3.13.3.2. Con: If people who acquire the money cannot or do not use it to meet their needs, such as if the people are mentally ill, struggling with addiction, elderly, disabled, etc., then the official poverty rate would be misleading, since some people would still be living in poverty conditions \(e.g. homeless, hungry\).
1.3.13.3.3. Con: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.
1.3.13.3.4. Con: If those poverty programs were not cut then the new total cost of social spending would be $1089 billion dollars.
1.3.14. Con: A UBI would enable other forms of revenue collection that could help offset its cost.
1.3.14.1. Pro: Companies that use robotics in lieu of humans should pay an "income tax" on the robots' work. This money would go directly to UBI.
1.3.14.1.1. Con: This may incentivize hiring unnecessary human workers \(and thus wasting man-hours\) rather than automating industry.
1.3.14.1.2. Con: This presupposes a society where automation has become very extensive; as of yet this is not applicable to society.
1.3.14.1.3. Con: Taxing robots would be a tax on private property which would be immoral. Private property is an inalienable human right and therefore shouldn't be taxed.
1.3.14.1.3.1. Con: Property taxes, such as local rates, are an established form of taxation, and not inherently less fair than any other form of taxation.
1.3.14.1.3.1.1. Con: Real estate is not private property. Ownership of real estate is defined by public registry \(a deed\) not by possession. Real estate ownership exists as a virtual concept within the boundary of a nation state. The unfortunately named "property tax" is therefore not a tax on private property. You "own" real estate because society says you own it and for that privilege, you pay a societal tax and thus it is inherently more fair than other forms \(such as "income tax"\).
1.3.14.1.3.1.2. Con: Property taxes are unfair to those on a fixed income.  Retired people are especially hit hard as taxes and inflation go up, but their income remains the same.
1.3.14.1.3.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.4.2.
1.3.14.1.4. Pro: UBIs or some equivalent distribution are inevitable because the world is becoming so rich that AI and robots will do all the work. Distributing their wealth equitably is the only way to avoid dystopic revolution and mass genocide.
1.3.14.1.4.1. Pro: Growth of world product per person is at about $10K and growing exponentially so by 2099 it may be at today's $100K. If it were distributed equitably now it could generate a lot of creativity.
1.3.14.1.4.2. Con: No matter how quickly or much the world's wealth grows, the elites will suck it all up  for their personal possession. The greed at the top is unquenchable, and their haughty, uncaring distaste for others is misanthropic.
1.3.14.1.5. Pro: A UBI funded by the corporations that benefit from automation could be a more viable route than strictly using government funds. UBI is an inevitability with automation's pace accelerating, and the companies that benefit from that should still be forced to pay some amount to the people that their increased productivity puts out of work.
1.3.14.1.5.1. Con: Companies have no inherent incentive to be altruistic, or give away any more of their profit than is necessary.
1.3.14.2. Pro: A government is the property of its people, much like a corporation where each citizen holds one share. Governments, like corporations, should pay a dividend to their owners - forming a UBI.
1.3.14.2.1. Con: Unfortunately, most of the governments are not making profit, but doing quite the  opposite: borrowing more and more money. In reality, this wouldn't work out.
1.3.14.2.1.1. Con: Government is not in the business of making money, so monetary measures of their success are not appropriate.
1.3.14.2.2. Con: This would incentivize the government to make a profit, which should not be its role, and may lead to harsher and more draconian \(or even exploitative\) economic policies being put into place at the expense of the wellbeing of the general community, such as cuts to essential services, other public services, government worker wages, etc. Governments should not function or be explicitly encouraged to be run like corporations.
1.3.14.2.2.1. Con: There would be no incentive for government to maximize profits because unlike a corporation, 100% of government revenue would be returned to the citizens in the form of a UBI. A corporation tries to give shareholders a maximum return but a government would only give citizens a minimum UBI —  so there's no reason for a government to ever raise taxes beyond the minimum amount necessary to pay UBI and cover the expenses of other mandatory functions.
1.3.14.2.3. Pro: A UBI is a rightful claim of the public on a part of modern productivity. Productivity gains thanks to automation were made possible to a significant extent by means of research carried out by publicly funded institutions like universities \(at least in Europe\). So the public should benefit from this gained productivity.
1.3.14.2.4. Pro: The UBI is a right derived from the principle that the earth belongs to all of us and all shall profit from its proceeds.
1.3.14.2.5. Pro: Society jointly owns the right to control its natural resources \(e.g. land, water, spectrum and air\). Governments are created to lease those rights \(via taxes\) to individuals who may \(or may not\) profit from the temporary, exclusive use of a resource. The tax revenue should therefore be distributed among individuals as compensation for their share of society's natural resources. After paying UBI, then society can fund schools, parks, roads, police, foreign aid, etc. Any other scheme is immoral.
1.3.14.2.6. Pro: The idea that wealth must be "earned" is based in the erroneous assumption that everything doesn't belong to everyone in the first place. All wealth is sourced from natural resources. The Earth belongs to all of us. The intellectual property that is used to transform natural resources into other types of wealth also belongs to everyone, as no idea is original or autonomous.
1.3.14.2.6.1. Con: If I build a house it doesn't belong to you. You can go out in the woods and cut down some trees and gather the rest of the resources available and build one by yourself, but in our society, we mostly have a division of labour, where we use money as a medium to exchange goods and services. No wealth is sourced from natural resources. All wealth \(as determined in voluntary exchange\) is sourced from human labour.
1.3.14.2.7. Con: Governments are not "owned", they are a way of organising rules in a society. A government might choose to put in place a rule whereby money is taken from some \(the wealthy\) and redistributed equally, forming a UBI, but that is a choice not a product of "ownership".
1.3.14.2.8. Con: Ultimately wealth comes not from resources but from the time spent by someone to make something of little worth into something of more worth. UBI forces people to give some of their time to others. This may or may not be a good thing, but understanding where wealth comes from is important.
1.3.14.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.7.1.4.
1.3.14.4. Pro: Many economists have suggested revenue neutral workable UBI plans that are affordable. For example, the [FairTax version of UBI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FairTax) has been studied by economists at the beacon hill institute and in-house economists with various government institutions and concluded that it would be able to cover 30% of the needs at the poverty level with no change to existing welfare programs. If we further assume that some portion of existing welfare programs would be reallocated, that percentage increases.
1.3.15. Pro: Since this would have to be funded by taxes, as more recipients began to rely on it as a source of income there would be increasingly less funds to pay for it setting up an inevitable unfunded deficit.
1.3.15.1. Pro: A UBI could not be successfully funded as employed taxpayers make up the base, therefore publicly funded programs will become ripe for private take over.
1.3.15.2. Con: This is not the case if the increasing gains from automation were also taxed.
1.3.15.2.1. Con: Taxing automation would discourage automation, which UBI fundamentally relies on.
1.3.15.2.1.1. Con: UBI is in no way built on or relying on automation. It was proposed way before industrialization even: [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_income#History)
1.3.15.3. Pro: If everyone got a paycheck whether they worked or not, there would be little incentive to pay the taxes required to disburse a UBI.
1.3.15.4. Pro: UBI requires funds to enact, and those funds must be generated before they're dispersed. If everyone is on a UBI, then no one is required to generate those funds, and it becomes a 'chicken or the egg' paradox. If only some are on a UBI, then non-UBI people will be forced to hand over a chunk of their generated income to fund UBI. There is no free meal ticket.
1.3.16. Con: A UBI need not meet the poverty line to be an improvement over status quo. The parent discussion over-specified what a UBI must be.
1.3.16.1. Con: Even if the UBI did not meet the poverty line—such as if it only met half of the poverty line—it would likely still be extraordinarily expensive while not even solving the issues at hand.
1.3.16.2. Pro: Many Americans work two or three jobs. Simply being able to quit one of these jobs would mean the world to millions of single working parents across this country.
1.3.16.3. Pro: Even though an affordable UBI may not be sufficient to prevent poverty by Western standards, it should still provide enough to avoid extreme stress and inequality. With additional income or provision possible under a UBI low-paid employment will become both feasible and attractive as a form of self-provision.
1.3.17. Con: Speaking for England: if the government gave everyone over 18 £6k p.a. and people paid for their own healthcare it could cut tax by half; back to pre-WWII levels.
1.3.17.1. Con: UBI is supposed to be in addition to services provided by the state \(eg healthcare, emergency services etc\) not as a replacement to it.
1.3.18. Con: While there may not be any country that can afford a UBI today, it does not mean a particularly wealthy country could never afford one. The parent question specifies "A wealthy country" but not how wealthy that country must be. The condition of any one country \(Or any number of countries\) does not in any way invalidate the idea of a UBI. Only that we are not ready just yet.
1.3.18.1. Pro: The march of automation and artificial intelligence will provide levels of surplus that make a UBI more readily affordable.
1.3.18.1.1. Pro: Automation means providing the same goods and services with fewer people. If a job is no longer necessary but society could support that person previously, why can it not support that person afterwards?
1.3.18.1.2. Pro: Increases in technology will eventually result in an excess of productivity. Government issued UBI is necessary to distribute the returns on this productivity equitably.
1.3.18.1.2.1. Pro: When [productivity reaches incredibly high rates](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/519016/stop-saying-robots-are-destroying-jobs-they-arent/), there will be no need to have as many people working. Instead, we could afford to distribute a UBI to the increasingly large number of people who would no longer work.
1.3.18.1.2.1.1. Con: The excess productivity could be incentive to layoff the amount of workers it would displace and maintain all other operations as normal. This would create even more profit for corporations due to no longer needing to pay wages and supplemental benefits.
1.3.18.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: Corporations look after themselves before their employees. If it is beneficial to them to be rid of employees, there will definitely be cuts.
1.3.18.1.2.1.1.2. Con: Those profits would be taxed at a high rate and distributed as UBI. By distributing buying power to laid-off workers, UBI allows the capitalist market system to survive under conditions where many people are not working most of the time.
1.3.18.1.2.1.2. Con: Productivity growth[has been stagnant or in decline](https://www.oecd.org/std/productivity-stats/) in most OECD nations. The [data shows no relationship between productivity growth and net unemployment](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/519016/stop-saying-robots-are-destroying-jobs-they-arent/).
1.3.18.1.2.2. Con: The returns on this productivity could equally be distributed by lowering tax rates for low/middle income earners or increasing public funding for existing welfare, education, or healthcare systems.
1.3.18.1.2.2.1. Con: The chain of command of a public administration has fixed costs, plus the variable one related to corruption. The use of unneeded intermediaries decreases efficiency and raises costs.
1.3.18.1.2.3. Pro: A UBI is necessary and plausible if AI is to take a majority of jobs on the market. It is necessary as a majority of people who do work now will no longer be able to find jobs. It is plausible as AI will reduce the cost of many products and services available to us, thus we will be able to enjoy more with less, in which case the total living cost will be reduced, thus the amount of UBI necessary will be reduced \(discounting inflation etc\).
1.3.18.1.2.4. Pro: UBI allows us to continue with technological advances without the worry of losing jobs. In fact, because of technology and the ability it has to reduce the work needed by humans, UBI is necessary. Imagine a farmer in the 19th century and the amount of work needed to sustain a farm compared to today. The same amount of time, effort, and work could now feed hundreds if not thousands and all families should reap the rewards of this advancement!
1.3.18.1.2.5. Pro: UBI is a necessity for the economy in the future. Due to increases in automation, there will be a shortage of suppy for the workforce. In contrast to this, the people at the top of the business will earn huge amounts of money; creating an income gap like we’ve never seen before. It might not be time for UBI right now, but it’s definitely a solution to future problems.
1.3.18.1.2.6. Pro: Global wealth is not a fixed quantity: as technology improves, the world economy sees an aggregate benefit without requiring anyone else's wealth to decrease commensurately. UBI is an appropriate response to the aggregate increase of global wealth because of technology.
1.3.18.1.3. Con: This is just another way to describe heavier taxation of industry, which eventually damages your economy's competitiveness and tax revenues.
1.3.18.1.4. Pro: A society in a position to implement a UBI presupposes automation and AI. With mechanized efficiency and without the cost of human labor it is necessary to reevaluate the costs of living in a post-scarcity society when determining the economic viability of a UBI.
1.3.18.2. Pro: The affordability problem can be avoided by implementing a UBI only when an economy is mature enough that it is beneficial. A society requiring only ~70% of its citizens to contribute to the labor force is by definition producing enough excess that it can fund some minimal standard of living for the remaining 30%. Finding the relevant line is important, but this is a problem regarding execution, not the concept itself.
1.3.18.3. Con: The question seems to imply whether a UBI should be implemented today as part of the discussion around how to socially adjust to AI. To say 'we are not there yet' still argues that this shouldn't be allowed as we are incapable of implementing the idea. If it is impossible to implement, then we shouldn't consider it.
1.3.18.4. Con: It is also possible that if/when governments can eventually afford UBIs they will also have better or more means-tested welfare, making UBIs still inferior. In other words, in the future it might be better to just stick with means-tested welfare which has been increased/improved.
1.3.18.5. Con: Such a hypothetical future where the economy has massive surplus potentially may not even be struggling with extreme poverty. Unless the claim/we identify what this hypothetical/theoretical future society looks like, it is difficult to evaluate how practical/beneficial a UBI would be in the society.
1.3.18.6. Con: UBI depends on an unsustainable level of global market wealth. If every economy achieved the kind of market success - that would be required to pay its citizens a UBI - we would need far more global growth. The Limits to Growth analysis \(see - [youtu.be](https://youtu.be/U7Z6h-U4CmI)\) clearly tells us this would require several more planets.
1.3.19. Pro: A country that introduces a UBI would see a great influx of immigrants seeking the UBI. Without tight immigration control, the costs of the scheme would run out of control.
1.3.19.1. Con: -> See 1.1.2.7.
1.3.20. Con: The US already pays nearly $10,000 to many taxpayers in the form of the standard deduction. Changing the standard deduction from a non-refundible tax credit into a refundible tax credit \(a one-line change to the tax code\) would constitute a basic income structure. It would then be revenue-neutral for the middle class, replace pell grants for college students, replace the earned income tax credit for many low-income families, and replace or overlap with social security for the elderly.
1.3.21. Con: A [UBI](http://morganfoundation.org.nz/why-the-guardian-is-wrong-on-ubi/) is a redistributive transfer; it is not a new cost the government must meet. Rather it’s a cost to some and a windfall to others - the net cost is zero.
1.3.21.1. Con: UBIs impose this cost on people who have worked to earn their money.
1.3.21.1.1. Con: That is true of all existing tax systems and of all existing welfare systems.
1.3.21.1.2. Con: The work one does today couldn't be done without previous generations' work, since fire handling to the first wheel, from writing to printing press, the transistor, internet, etc. UBI then, is paid thanks to all wealth created in history as a dividend. It's an effective way to make everybody access this heritage as a right.
1.3.21.1.3. Con: A UBI imposes this cost on those who have won the lottery of birth. The difference between an executive and an apple-picker is more likely due to systemic factors than a result of either person's choices.
1.3.21.1.3.1. Con: Of the 29 richest people in the US, [most were not born rich](http://www.businessinsider.com/richest-people-in-the-us-2016-1/#19-sheldon-adelson-11).
1.3.21.1.4. Con: This assumes people are getting money based on the actual value they're adding to the system. The redistribution would make up for some of the difference generated when someone gains capital without adding enough value back into the system.
1.3.21.1.5. Con: Many wealthy people have far more money than can ever have been earned through products of labor. Let's assume that someone is doing a job with such intense labor that they earn $6000 an hour. If this person worked 60 hours a week, every single week, for 50 years, and didn't spend a penny of their income on food, housing, taxes, or anything else, they still would not be a billionaire. Anyone with that much money has been exploiting those under them and their money should be redistributed.
1.3.21.1.5.1. Con: There are more sources of income than production and exploitation. Investing in somebody else their business is not exploitation and one of the more common ways of earning enough to become a billionaire. Running a successful business yourself is another way.
1.3.21.1.6. Con: This argument assumes that all income comes from human activity. With the introduction and proliferation of automation and AI, more and more of the income will be due to activities performed by non-human actors, effectively making an UBI a redistribution from non-human to human actors.
1.3.21.2. Pro: Most of those receiving a UBI also pay, on net, positive amounts of tax. Therefore, when calculating the cost of a UBI, it doesn't make sense to to calculate their UBI as a cost when it can easily be offset by a small tax increase.
1.3.21.2.1. Con: This would, in effect, invalidate the whole definition of "universal" basic income—this proposal essentially just says "we make it universal, but the rich don't get any benefits in the end." In other words, it would make more sense to only pay the poor, rather than also paying the rich.
1.3.21.2.2. Con: As per basic accounting, all the flows of money \(costs and revenue\) should be accounted for, or else people will be more liable to make accounting/policy errors. Thus, it may be true that these costs might be offset by "a small tax increase," but we still have to account for the total expenditures. Otherwise \(for example\), people might forget that taxes must be raised in order to offset the UBI for rich people.
1.3.21.2.2.1. Pro: It is particularly important to account for the payments to rich people because these payments previously were not being made \(e.g. under targeted welfare systems\).
1.3.21.3. Con: Redistribution reduces production by decreasing the incentive to work. Hence redistribution creates a net loss to society, not zero as claimed.
1.3.21.3.1. Con: A person needs a certain amount of income in order to be capable of productive work. Issues associated with poverty prevent people from entering the labour force.
1.3.21.3.1.1. Pro: Poverty is stressful, as is starting a new job. If someone cannot handle that stress, they can easily become trapped in poverty, unable to successfully commence employment.
1.3.21.3.1.2. Pro: Health issues can prevent one from being able to work, but may require costly surgery, medicine, or other treatment to successfully treat.
1.3.21.3.1.2.1. Con: Targeted welfare may be more effective at improving such situations, providing the needed medicine or treatment free or at a discounted cost.
1.3.21.3.1.2.1.1. Con: Targeted welfare requires identification of the cases where it is needed - creating "gaps" in the "net" which cases may "slip through", as well as requiring bureaucracy and administration and investigation/inquisition to find and confirm the cases that would require it. A UBI would avoid these issues by being unconditionally available to everyone.
1.3.21.3.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: If a targeted welfare program requires would-be beneficiaries to come forward to it, this creates several issues - would-be beneficiaries may not know about it, may not have the time or ability to do what is required of them to access it, or may feel that it is stigmatized or prefer to "deal with their own problems themselves". A UBI would avoid these issues also by being unconditionally available to everyone.
1.3.21.3.2. Con: Most people will still want to work at least part time to supplement their UBI, provided working conditions are safe and reasonable.
1.3.21.3.3. Con: [The multiplier effect](http://www.economicsonline.co.uk/Managing_the_economy/The_multiplier_effect.html) shows that the accumulation of capital harms the national economy. By redirecting money pooling at the top through a [continual redistribution](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/why-should-we-support-the_b_7630162.html) from the top-middle down to the bottom-middle of the income spectrum, the entire economy would actually expand while becoming more sustainable and more inclusive.
1.3.21.3.3.1. Pro: Consumption expenditure differs substantially between low and high-income groups: [it is estimated](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/why-should-we-support-the_b_7630162.html) that low-wage workers add about $1.21 to the national economy per dollar they have to spend, while high-income workers only add about 39 cents. Redistribution would therefore help expand the economy.
1.3.21.3.3.2. Con: Increased savings still operate in the economy because they become savings that reduce the costs of borrowing.
1.3.21.3.3.3. Con: Increased capital and investment leads to greater investment which in turn leads to increased productivity that creates more jobs.
1.3.21.3.3.3.1. Con: This [IMF study](https://www.imf.org/en/Publications/Staff-Discussion-Notes/Issues/2016/12/31/Causes-and-Consequences-of-Income-Inequality-A-Global-Perspective-42986) \(also covered [here](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jun/15/focus-on-low-income-families-to-boost-economic-growth-says-imf-study)\)shows that trickle down economics does not work.
Giving tax breaks to rich people/businesses actively harms economic growth and equality.
It goes on to say that if you give that money to the poor and middle class, the economy grows.
1.3.21.3.3.4. Pro: Better wages, conditions, and more money in the hands of low earners, has been shown to improve aggregate demand and therefore grow the economy. Therefore, reversing the tax cuts for the wealthy of recent decades, and redistributing that wealth into the economy will improve economic conditions, as opposed to having it languish in offshore investments and unproductive assets.
1.3.21.3.3.5. Con: Increased spending on consumption is bad for the environment.
1.3.21.3.3.5.1. Con: Green products, which have a more [positive impact](http://www.ripublication.com/gjmbs_spl/gjmbsv3n6_09.pdf) on mankind and the environment, cost on average [50% more](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/earth/earthnews/7785705/Green-goods-cost-nearly-50-more.html) than non-green products. With a UBI, more individuals will have more funds to purchase green products, resulting in a less harmful impact on the environment.
1.3.21.3.3.5.1.1. Con: Even if a UBI results in people having more money, that doesn't imply people would choose to spend the extra money on more environmentally friendly products.  For example, if a person is too poor to afford a car, they take the bus to work.  But once they have enough money they buy a car, which hurts the environment rather than helps.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2. Con: A UBI would be beneficial for those who fight for the environment's protection.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.1. Pro: A UBI would help NGOs that dedicate themselves to environmental protection, as they can save activists' salaries and thus have more available capital.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.2. Pro: Citizens who are concerned about the environment will be able to donate larger amounts with the help of a UBI.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.2.1. Con: Considering much of the redistributed income will go towards the poor, as the wealthy citizens' gain is marginal, it seems unlikely they will donate higher amounts to the environment. It is more likely they will spend the money on education, food, health care, and housing.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.2.1.1. Con: Lower Incomes donate a higher percentage of income  than any other income bracket. The IRS’s 2011 Statistics of Income file on individuals who itemize on their tax return shows a relationship between total adjusted gross income and charitable giving as a percentage of AGI. Those with income between $100,000 and $200,000 contribute, on average, 2.6 percent of their income, which is lower compared to those with income either below $100,000 \(3.6 percent\).
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.3. Con: Many of the wealthy who donated or invested much of their income to the protection of the environment will no longer be able to do so because of the increase in taxes.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.3.1. Con: If charity [donations](https://www.gov.uk/donating-to-charity/overview) are fiscally [deductible](https://www.irs.gov/charities-non-profits/charitable-organizations/charitable-contribution-deductions), a lot of wealthy individuals will still donate.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4. Pro: Currently, social workers and NGO workers, who are helping a lot of people, are paid very poorly for their meaningful work. This deters many people from entering the non-profit sector.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.1. Pro: Governments are [investing less and less](https://books.google.it/books?id=JcPiBwAAQBAJ&pg=PA298&lpg=PA298&dq=crisis+government+cut+social+aid&source=bl&ots=PUj8DMEcpk&sig=OEDtkapMpf1Efhh8BW8cXng47S0&hl=it&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiJoLnh563RAhVEVhoKHUnwApoQ6AEIWzAI#v=onepage&q=crisis%20government%20cut%20social%20aid&f=false) money for social aids, leaving to NGOs the important role to replace this obligation. A UBI would reward their work with governmental funds.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.2. Con: A UBI does not make this work relatively more appealing, as the bonus income will equally apply to all jobs.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.2.1. Con: There are people already choosing these jobs despite economical hardships, which points to greater motivations to do so.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.2.1.1. Pro: It is likely that once economic worries are eliminated, even more people would choose to dedicate their working hours towards a cause they resonate with rather than opting for whatever pays the most.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.2.1.2. Pro: Most people entering the nonprofit sector are driven by a greater purpose and are engaged by inspiring missions.
1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.3. Con: How do you define meaningful? In a capitalist society, a job is paid exactly what it's worth, if the person doing that job wants to be paid more all they have to do is ask to be paid more. If it turns out no one will pay them what they're asking for, then society has deemed it's not worth that much.
1.3.21.3.3.5.3. Con: If a UBI's implementation means [a shorter working week](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2016/dec/09/basic-income-short-working-week-green-institute-report), this will also reduce commutes, which will reduce emissions and be beneficial for the environment.
1.3.21.3.3.5.3.1. Con: Fewer working hours means more recreational hours and recreation has a higher environmental impact than working. For example, recreation in the UK already accounts for [20% of CO2 emissions](http://www.independent.co.uk/environment/climate-change/your-carbon-footprint-revealed-climate-change-report-finds-we-each-produce-11-tons-of-carbon-a-year-427664.html).
1.3.21.3.3.5.3.2. Con: A shorter work week leads to more commuting, as more people need to do the same work \(assuming the same work needs to be done\).
1.3.21.3.3.5.4. Con: With added income, more people will have the ability to pay for college, which will enrich the scientific labor force attempting to study and combat climate change.
1.3.21.3.3.5.5. Con: A UBI tied to a value based on the total sustainable natural services, including renewably-sourced human and animal products, of each region. This would solve both intranational ecological exploitation and international inequities due to colonialism/neoliberalism/etc. while providing a natural, measurable de-externalization of capitalist production costs.[Environmentally Sustainable National Income - A new alternative marcoindicator](http://www.oecd.org/site/worldforum06/38828016.pdf)
1.3.21.3.3.5.5.1. Pro: [Global economic enfranchisement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/User:Tralfamadoran777) accomplishes this by paying an equal share of the interest paid in money creation to each, a functional tax on all money, which is "a value based on the total sustainable..." and will also fill the data gaps referenced in the link, by providing data from each.
1.3.21.3.3.5.6. Con: Many people work jobs they know to be environmentally damaging in order to earn enough for a decent life. A UBI would allow them to stop.
1.3.21.3.3.5.7. Con: Having a higher base income allows people to more easily [make capital investments in green technologies](http://basicincome.org/news/2016/08/green-technology-another-reason-need-basic-income/) \(e.g. solar panels\) that would previously have been unaffordable.
1.3.21.3.3.5.8. Con: UBI helps address climate change by reducing commuting time, office space, and consumerism. It allows people to engage in enriching low-carbon activities such as small-scale farming, art, and study.
