1.3.21.3.3.5.9. Con: A UBI makes it possible to effectively fight climate change and environmental damage by loosening relationship between welfare and GDP growth. Societies will no longer have to rely on infinite, exponential economic growth and creating useless jobs that create no social value in order to ensure basic survival of individuals.
1.3.21.3.3.5.9.1. Pro: Instead, these useless jobs consume resources that might produce goods \(that also consume resources\) that no one can buy. A UBI would allow people to have the capability instead to get useful jobs \(because people will have enough money to apply to them and also because they will not be so strapped for cash that they resort to these useless jobs\).
1.3.21.3.3.5.9.2. Pro: Useless jobs have a negative social value \(at the individual level\). The more a person is at a useless job, the less skills they can gain towards a useful job.
1.3.21.3.3.5.9.3. Pro: Waste would not be such as big issue if capitalism did not pressure companies to overproduce goods. Instead of gauging and guessing at demand, but most likely getting it wrong \(overproducing or under-producing\), a UBI would let consumption switch to on-demand production.
1.3.21.3.3.5.9.3.1. Pro: For example, people could 3D print their own goods, however much or small, which can eliminate a lot of waste and unnecessary jobs \(especially since people would not need them anymore\).
1.3.21.3.3.5.9.3.2. Con: Some manufacturing methods of overproduction are unavoidable unless an improvement is made in production process. Mining raw materials is a good example, because some of these processing plants need to be continuously running in order to make the production economically viable.
1.3.21.3.3.5.10. Con: Not all consumption is bad for the environment. As we move further into the digital age, more and more of what people spend their money on is not physical.
1.3.21.3.3.5.11. Pro: [Families and homes with similar incomes tend to own similar homes and objects](https://www.gapminder.org/dollar-street/matrix). A low universal basic income give a lot of people and families a very similar low income which will lead to a strong increase of similar cheap products which can lead to [environmental complications](https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2015/09/150928153039.htm) and can have a [negative impact on climate change](https://mahb.stanford.edu/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/Meat-the-Biggest-Threat-and-Opportunity-to-Climate-Change-FINAL.pdf).
1.3.21.3.3.6. Pro: By redistributing wealth, UBI raises the proportion of the population capable of buying certain goods and services - this will stimulate the economy more than the same money sitting dormant in the savings accounts of the most well-off people
1.3.21.3.3.6.1. Pro: A UBI is good for the economy as people will generally have more money to spend and more time to spend it. This will mean most businesses will make more money.
1.3.21.3.3.7. Pro: -> See 1.2.13.2.1.
1.3.21.3.3.8. Pro: Once people can easily afford basic goods like food, water or shelter, or health, this would release public money from health-related spending like  disease prevention and boost the income of private companies with millions of new consumers. The company's new income would boost tax  revenue and help compensate the initial investment in the UBI.
1.3.21.3.3.9. Pro: Increasing the incomes of the poor could actually improve the economy by virtue of redistribution of wealth. The current wealth situation is not only abhorrently imbalanced, it actually harms the economy by locking up cash flow in the form of savings. A UBI might stimulate the economy, provided the tax is levied on those with income greater than 8 figures.
1.3.21.3.3.10. Con: This does not hold for permanent programs such as UBI. The long-term real GDP of a country is determined by economic inputs of capital and labor, not consumption. At full employment of a country's resources, fiscal stimulus will push up prices and imports, leaving real production unchanged. [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Keynesian_economics)
1.3.21.3.3.10.1. Con: A UBI would allow workers to exit the poverty trap, increasing the Aggregate Supply of the country and leading to increased economic growth.
1.3.21.3.3.11. Pro: If people do not need to hoard money for retirement or investments, then money could circulate better and increase the multiplier effect.
1.3.21.3.3.12. Pro: The increase in the multiplier effect by the UBI allows it to be sustainable, so it can pay for itself.
1.3.21.3.3.13. Con: A UBI could only ever cover basic living costs, meaning that those on a UBI could not enjoy all the benefits of life or be consumers of higher level goods, thus reducing demand, leading to even less people employed.
1.3.21.3.3.14. Pro: A UBI would be a boom to the economy. It would be almost like the opposite of trickle-down economics. Rich people can afford to save additional income but poor people have a more urgent need to spend the money. This increase in economic activity would spur hiring and investment.
1.3.21.3.3.15. Pro: UBI would also guarnteed a baseline of economic activity because there would be a highly reliable amount of demand that comes from a highly predictable income.
1.3.21.3.4. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.3.21.3.5. Con: There are plenty of non-financial incentives to work, which may in fact increase the incentive to work when one is not being forced by financial pressures into working a job they would prefer not to.
1.3.21.3.6. Con: A UBI will redistribute wealth and thereby give more opportunities to the poor, enabling the overwhelming majority of the population to be more productive.
1.3.21.3.6.1. Pro: A UBI will increase the purchasing power of the currently poor, thus increasing economic activity at the lower socioeconomic brackets and driving demand for goods and services.
1.3.21.3.7. Con: If the UBI is financed by a tax on the top 1% then for 99% the incentive to work will remain the same.
1.3.21.4. Con: Redistribution means disruption and causes changes in the marketplace. If it were a worldwide system, it would at least eliminate the flight of businesses to other countries, but issues would still remain.
1.3.21.5. Con: Redistribution has administrative costs for the government, besides indirect effects that could harm the economy thus reducing income from taxes.
1.3.21.5.1. Con: UBI redistribution has less administrative costs than other forms of welfare that require complicated and time-consuming means testing.
1.3.21.5.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.
1.3.21.5.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.10.
1.3.21.6. Con: It’s inefficient to take money from wealthy or middle class citizens only to give it back to them. It makes more sense to just let them keep it in the first place.
1.3.21.6.1. Con: This does nothing to prevent poverty or poverty-related suffering and deaths.
1.3.21.6.1.1. Con: Neither does giving money to rich people, which is what the UBI does.
1.3.21.6.1.1.1. Con: People who are wealthy today may not necessarily be wealthy tomorrow. In different stages of someone's life, they may do high-powered work, invest in businesses or enterprise, and/or subsist on UBI respectively. It is good for everyone to have the option available to them.
1.3.21.6.1.1.2. Con: The U in UBI is the reason for this. It's universal, which means that all people will receive it. This is mitigated with higher taxes on higher wealth individuals.
1.3.21.6.2. Con: It can be done in an extremely simple one line addition to the tax form.
1.3.21.7. Con: It is unjust for the government to take a meaningful sum from a class of people who disagree with how it would be used.
1.3.21.7.1. Con: Quite a few people object to either welfare programs, or military spending, but both of them are still very high, and at least to some degree, necessary.
1.3.21.7.2. Con: That class of people would not have been able to earn and/or inherit the money in the first place if it wasn't for the society that the government is tasked with protecting and developing. If UBI sufficiently enhances society then the government has the right to charge the people that benefit \(financially\) from the society the most for its continued upkeep and enhancement.
1.3.21.7.3. Con: This is logically flawed. In a democratic system, the government must fulfil the wishes of the voting majority. It is meaningless to refuse to pay tax because you, personally, did not vote for it. The policy is a national policy, voted by the country.
1.3.21.8. Con: The net cost would not be zero as there would be cost associated with managing the system.
1.3.21.8.1. Con: The cost associated with managing a UBI is \(almost by definition\) far less than the current cost of a selective welfare system, thus creating a net saving \(reduction in current costs\).
1.3.21.9. Con: UBI is made of two parts: 1\) collect money, and 2\) redistribute it. The first part is not redistributive by itself and has limits, you cannot confiscate with taxes more money than what it exist in the economy.
1.3.21.10. Pro: For instance, a 26.51% tax levied flat across all income levels in the US and distributed to all working age adults \(206,585,933 people\) would result in a $25,003 UBI for everyone after taxes \(based on a GDP of $19.484 trillion\). Those who earn little or nothing, such as college students, would make $25k. Someone who is currently at the poverty line \($19k\) would be topped up to almost $39k. Someone earning $58k would be topped up to more then $67k. 0% tax impact would be at $94k per worker.
1.3.21.10.1. Pro: Couple this with abolishing minimum wage requirements and US labor could become more profitable for businesses.
1.3.21.10.1.1. Con: Cheap labor being subsidized by a UBI could discourage companies from innovating more efficient ways of doing things, slowing growth.
1.4. Pro: A UBI makes it possible for some people to work less.
1.4.1. Pro: For the less advantaged in society whose jobs often involve physical labour, work is often a hard and unrewarding. A UBI helps relieve them of some of this burden.
1.4.1.1. Pro: Marx wrote that '[the realm of freedom](https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1894-c3/ch48.htm) actually begins only where labour which is determined by necessity and mundane considerations ceases'.
1.4.1.2. Pro: Higher levels of income and education [are associated](http://www.gallup.com/poll/18109/most-workers-positive-onethird-love-their-jobs.aspx) with higher levels of job satisfaction.
1.4.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.
1.4.2. Pro: If average working hours were fewer, it would enable them to be more evenly distributed; reducing both overwork and underwork. Sharing work more evenly reduces the load on those overworked and provides more opportunities for the underworked to do more.
1.4.2.1. Pro: Keynes saw a reduction in the work week as a way to [help combat unemployment](https://mronline.org/2010/07/22/the-keynesian-cure/).
1.4.2.2. Con: The amount of work is not a fixed figure. Working creates the goods and services that humans want, and human wants are unlimited; therefore, the amount of work needed to fulfill these wants is unlimited as well.
1.4.2.3. Con: The unequal distribution of work is a result of optimization with regards to the unequal distribution of expertise and specialization. Artificial attempts to change the current state of the distribution would cause an increase of labor costs, because the capitalist would be forced to create more jobs for the same level of output.
1.4.3. Pro: Efficient economies boost production and consumption, but beyond a basic quality of life an individual's happiness is mostly determined by non-material factors. If economic performance does not increase happiness we should curtail it.
1.4.3.1. Pro: A [75-year long study](https://www.inc.com/melanie-curtin/want-a-life-of-fulfillment-a-75-year-harvard-study-says-to-prioritize-this-one-t.html) came to the conclusion that good relationships are the most important factor to create sustainable and fulfilling happiness.
1.4.4. Pro: Working long hours is detrimental to one's health and encounters diminishing returns.
1.4.4.1. Pro: There is an [increased chance](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10618741) of smoking, depression, drinking, and weight gain for men and women who work 35 hours or more per week.
1.4.4.2. Pro: Working over 39 hours a week was shown to have negative effects on [mental health](https://www.minnpost.com/second-opinion/2017/02/study-links-working-more-39-hours-week-poorer-mental-health).
1.4.4.3. Pro: Many workers stay in jobs with unhealthy working conditions due to a tight job market. A UBI can alleviate this stress.
1.4.4.3.1. Pro: When workers are more able to leave bad jobs it leads to bad employers having persistently high turnover rates. This creates more effective pressure on the employer to deal with their problematic power dynamics and mitigate stressful working conditions.
1.4.4.4. Pro: Working [5 hours](http://www.inc.com/marla-tabaka/the-5-hour-workday-how-to-increase-productivity-by-working-fewer-hours.html) a day instead of 8 hours or more has shown to increase productivity, performance, and increased happiness.
1.4.4.5. Pro: Working [80 hours](https://hbr.org/2015/08/the-research-is-clear-long-hours-backfire-for-people-and-for-companies) a week does not result in increased output. Thus, working long hours is unnecessary and at the expense of the employee.
1.4.4.6. Con: Additional income isn't necessarily a motivator for people to work less. Some people want to earn as much money as possible no matter what.
1.4.4.6.1. Con: People who want to work long hours are free to do so, even at the expense of their health, but a UBI would allow those who do not want to do so securely.
1.4.5. Con: Working less will result in fewer benefits, like health insurance or contribution pension schemes. Even with a UBI, these benefits are still a necessary safety net for employees.
1.4.5.1. Con: People who are no longer mandated to work can make up for these benefits through vocations and other individual ways of making money.
1.4.5.2. Con: The main beneficiaries of a UBI \(the poor\) do not have much in the way of health insurance or retirement savings anyway.
1.4.5.3. Con: A UBI serves as a safety net for these employees. The income from their UBI payments can be put towards health care and retirement funds.
1.4.5.4. Con: Those benefits are not provided by companies for free; they just reduce the amount employees would earn from their salary anyway. It is unclear why working less would mean a drastic, rather than slight, reduction in those benefits.
1.4.5.5. Pro: Funds from a UBI are not likely to be enough to cover the rising costs of healthcare or emergency visits.
1.4.5.5.1. Con: In developed countries people on low-incomes do not typically pay for their own health care. Even in the US there are medicaid provisions and the [costs of emergency visits are passed on](https://insight.kellogg.northwestern.edu/article/who-bears-the-cost-of-the-uninsured-nonprofit-hospitals).
1.4.5.5.2. Con: A UBI could increase in line with an index that tracks the cost of living \(including healthcare\).
1.4.5.5.3. Pro: Emergency visits in the US on average cost [$1,233](http://blog.bcbsnc.com/2014/04/5-emergency-room-myths-busted/). This would take a substantial amount from the monthly UBI payments to cover.
1.4.5.5.3.1. Con: The state is not covering the cost of this care for the vast majority of people under the status quo anyway.
1.4.5.6. Pro: As some people are more likely to get diseases than others, they will be disadvantaged in terms of health care costs.
1.4.5.6.1. Pro: Some people who are for example ill, and depend on financial help, will need more than what a UBI can offer because they have large health-related expenses.
1.4.6. Pro: A UBI will allow individuals the opportunity to take fewer work shifts, giving them more time to pursue other interests they hold valuable.
1.4.6.1. Pro: Fewer shifts allows more time for children, family, and ensuring proper health outcomes. This leads to the development of healthy and meaningful relationships that better both a person's physical and mental health.
1.4.6.1.1. Pro: Supportive healthy [relationships](http://www.health.harvard.edu/newsletter_article/the-health-benefits-of-strong-relationships) have shown to reduce harmful levels of stress that negatively affect the immune system.
1.4.6.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.1.
1.4.6.1.3. Con: These precise outcomes can be achieved through something like an earned income tax credit for low-income individuals, without necessitating a UBI.
1.4.6.1.3.1. Pro: An earned income tax credit is also a lot more economically feasible, as it [costs less than a high UBI.](https://www.vox.com/2016/7/16/11948800/robert-greenstein-basic-income)
1.4.6.1.4. Pro: Additional time spend with children can reduce mental health problems of future generations.
1.4.6.1.5. Con: Work could be done in family businesses or at least with help of children in many cases, which would not only be good for relation, but also for the economy.
1.4.6.2. Pro: Reduced working hours allow more time for educational pursuits like earning a degree or taking classes in a subject of interest.
1.4.6.2.1. Pro: High and specialized education becomes increasingly important as more and more jobs are being replaced by machines, especially the lower-skilled jobs.
1.4.6.2.1.1. Pro: Between 2000 and 2010 the US lost 5.6 million [manufacturing jobs](https://www.ft.com/content/dec677c0-b7e6-11e6-ba85-95d1533d9a62) and 85% of these losses can be attributable to technological change \(mainly automation\).
1.4.6.2.1.2. Con: Moral-panic around increased mechanisation of the labour force has existed since before the industrial revolution, yet never actually come to fruition.
1.4.6.2.1.2.1. Pro: Overwhelmingly, technological advancements lead to job creation in other fields, due to increased net demand as a product of economic growth.
1.4.6.2.1.2.2. Con: This is not correct. [Current trends](http://www3.weforum.org/docs/WEF_FOJ_Executive_Summary_Jobs.pdf) could lead to a net employment impact of more than 5.1 million jobs lost to disruptive labour market changes over the period 2015–2020, with a total loss of 7.1 million jobs.
1.4.6.2.1.2.2.1. Con: The very same study suggests that these losses can be offset by a ['talent revolution'](http://www3.weforum.org/docs/WEF_FOJ_Executive_Summary_Jobs.pdf) whereby strong leadership from government and business elites leads to job creation in new fields.
1.4.6.2.2. Pro: With a UBI, people will have more income to easily afford more enjoyable educational experiences, like studying subjects within their interests or learning through world exploration.
1.4.6.2.2.1. Pro: People are more likely to have better lives if they have better access to education and enriching experiences than if they spend their whole lives trying to maximize their productivity in a capitalistic sense.
1.4.6.3. Pro: A UBI reduces the effective risks of working part-time or on a seasonal basis; allowing workers to better choose a schedule that suits their lifestyle.
1.4.6.3.1. Pro: Our time is the most important commodity we each possess; allowing us to better allocate it is important to self-determination and happiness.
1.4.6.3.2. Pro: People's living expenses are relatively constant costs — rent, food, etc are all must be paid for on a regular basis. When people do not work consistently the need for 'income smoothing' becomes burdensome and risky as people must carefully budget for how future costs can be met with irregular incomes.
1.4.6.4. Con: Incentives to work long shifts exist even under a UBI, and employers can design the system to their advantage. For example, employers might hand out promotions only to employees who work long shifts. Thus, low-income individuals are pressured into taking long hours in any case.
1.4.6.4.1. Con: Under a UBI, the vast majority of people will have the option to not work 12 hours a day, even when bonuses for long hours are in place. Thus, a UBI offers more individual choice, which is likely to be used by many workers, not just those with low-income.
1.4.7. Con: Most employees already have the option for working less by shifting to less demanding \(or part-time\) jobs. That most people choose to work full-time suggests they value a larger salary over larger amounts of free time.
1.4.7.1. Con: It is often not economically viable to work part-time. Part-time work does not always cover the cost of living.
1.4.7.2. Con: For many professions it is difficult to find stable, part-time work. The 5-day week is the de facto work pattern and is the pattern that most employment opportunities offer.
1.4.7.3. Con: Government programs, like benefits that only kick in after 40 hours per week,  strongly discourage part-time work. Restructuring to a UBI would help level the playing field between part-time and full-time work.
1.4.7.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.
1.4.7.4. Con: Part-time jobs benefit the employer more than the employee. They end up having the same or more hours of work by hiring multiple people and paying them less.
1.4.7.5. Con: A UBI would not keep anyone from working a full time job. It would give them the option of taking a lighter work week if personal time is something they also value.
1.4.8. Pro: UBI can liberate brainpower and thinking time, producing additional forward movement for society.
1.4.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1.
1.4.8.2. Pro: With a UBI, many people would be less worried about money, and thus less mentally constrained.
1.4.8.3. Pro: People who receive UBI have more opportunity to search for meaningful and dignified employment than if they're struggling to survive; they can still be rewarded for their efforts.
1.4.8.3.1. Con: The only people who would 'benefit' are people who don't qualify for private charity, i.e. able bodied people who aren't looking for work.
1.4.8.3.1.1. Con: Private charity is overburdened, unable to keep up with the need of the groups it caters too, and is to unreliable to rely on.
1.4.8.3.1.2. Con: And people who are not technically unemployed or in need, yet are currently trapped in jobs or situations they would otherwise rather not be in, and are thus both suffering and performing less effectively since their work is not wholehearted and is stressful.
1.4.8.4. Con: People who have idle time due to UBI are not guaranteed to use it wisely. Without UBI people are guaranteed to use their brainpower to generate goods/services of value to other humans.
1.4.8.4.1. Con: Without a UBI, people are guaranteed to use some of their brainpower to generate goods/services. A stressed, forced worker will never be as productive as someone who does it voluntarily as a hobby and passion, without undue stress.
1.4.8.4.1.1. Con: Stress is in fact a strong motivator and often projects that are taken as a hobby or just for passion are abandoned or produce much less than projects that are produced unwillingly under stress.
1.4.8.4.1.1.1. Con: Stressed labour has physical and psychological costs that make it less productive in the long-term due to burnout, ill health, and resentment. Genuine motivation is far more sustainable.
1.4.8.5. Pro: Free time will drive people to work on personal projects - which will lead to an increase in innovations, allowing humanity's development to accelerate. Considering that each person has preferences for specific knowledge areas we can expect development in many forms.
1.4.8.5.1. Con: Perhaps, but there are other factors related to UBI which could cause the majority of humanity to simply spend more time enjoying themselves instead of working to improve. Artificial Intelligence would be one example.
1.4.8.5.1.1. Con: Likely true - but whether you view this as a negative or positive result is contingent on how you define benefit to society. 'Enjoying oneself' leads to happier, well-adjusted individuals with less aggravation and stress. A perfect personal climate to flourish curiosity-based learning and invention.
1.4.8.6. Con: Technological innovations and automation will lead to revenue being generated with less work, which will liberate brainpower and thinking time. Similarly to how hunter-gatherers have to spend most of their waking time in search of food, while modern humans can work for 8h per day and still have worked more than necessary for survival.
1.4.9. Con: A UBI, like welfare, denies our innate evolutionarily need to hunt and gather \(do work\). Humans that do not work are not happy.
1.4.9.1. Con: Work takes on various forms, as the people this would most directly effect are primarily physical labor. This frees them to perform more fulfilling tasks and allows automation to more readily take over such fields without casting many into poverty.
1.4.9.1.1. Con: Automation isn't all-pervasive, and will spread into different fields at different rates.
A UBI is a blunt and distortionary way of dealing with automation until such time as all fields are entirely automated, which may never happen.
1.4.9.1.1.1. Con: Automation can be all-pervasive in the near future and it can indeed spread into very different fields at a rapid rate. Waiting until this has happened before addressing the problem with a well considered and well implemented UBI may create great imbalances in existing power structures; creating social unrest at a massive scale as more and more people are pushed into poverty due to the lack of jobs.
1.4.9.2. Con: Humans are not bestial animals. Most people's reason to do work \(and feel good about it\) is either to do with survival, or a higher cause - whether it be providing for one's family, social status, profit as an end goal, profit to an end, or a genuine belief in the desirability and value to society of the results of one's work.
1.4.9.3. Con: Not all work is necessary. It would be at least equally correct to claim that the drive to say optimize transport by inventing the wheel was to reduce the need for work and effort, freeing up time for leasure.
1.4.9.4. Con: UBI does not deny this "innate evolutionary need", it only makes possible the choice of what kind of work the person desires to do.  The fact that there is no need to worry about surviving, people will be driven to work on whatever work field they want instead of working to attend to the desires of others.
1.4.10. Con: If the entire population works less on average, the amount of products and services generated decreases, decreasing overall wealth.
1.4.10.1. Con: Motivated people, that are not under stress, doing what they like, can achieve much higher production efficiency.
1.4.10.1.1. Pro: A UBI enhances working conditions.
1.4.10.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.4.10.1.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.3.
1.4.10.1.1.3. Con: Fewer workers means any spending to improve working conditions is less efficient. For example having to pay to make an entire factory safe for just one worker would create an impetus to find a way to circumvent having workers entirely.
1.4.10.1.1.3.1. Con: It is likely that any remaining humans in a factory setting, where the work is done by robots, would be in a supervisory/oversight function. This should be easy to ensure H&S as there would be little physical interaction with actual processes.
1.4.10.1.1.3.2. Con: Maintenance personnel would be necessary in any case, and a factory would have to be made safe for them. Providing safety in the event limited numbers of human workers are still required should not be so difficult as an extension for that. It is not like factories would otherwise be completely unsafe for humans to access.
1.4.10.1.1.4. Pro: UBI could help make employing and being employed more voluntary. If employment is driven by grave need, it becomes voluntary only in name.
1.4.10.1.1.4.1. Pro: There cannot be a free labour market without a UBI. When labour sellers are forced under pain of starvation to sell their labour to any available labour buyer there is no free choice and no free market. The labour buyers have no such urgency.
1.4.10.1.1.5. Pro: Competition would shift from businesses trying to entice consumers to buy products to trying to entice workers to help produce products. This would result in better wage competition.
1.4.10.1.1.5.1. Pro: It would also put pressure on excessive production of redundant products, thus forcing businesses that waste our resources out of the market.
1.4.10.1.1.5.2. Pro: A UBI will create a more competitive job market by allowing workers to be more selective of employment opportunities.
1.4.10.1.1.5.3. Pro: A UBI incentivizes employers to provide competitive pay and benefits for prospective employees, as employers would have to entice employees with more than just the promise of a basic living.
1.4.10.1.1.6. Con: Companies could use a UBI as a justification to offer lower wages.
1.4.10.1.1.6.1. Con: Companies do not need a justification to offer lower wages, the question is whether employees are willing to accept lower wages or not.
1.4.10.1.1.6.1.1. Pro: UBI would increase a low-skilled employee's leverage with prospective employers, and would also free workers to engage in efforts which might be marginally less profitable, but are also significantly more fulfilling or rewarding, such as creative endeavors or personally-preferred lines of work \(education instead of manufacturing; health care instead of construction; etc..\)
1.4.10.1.1.6.1.2. Pro: The wages might be too low to be worth the hassle of going to the job when all expenses are factored in \(education, driving...\). This could then lead to labor shortages.
1.4.10.1.1.6.1.3. Pro: The lower wages might not be enough to live on, even when combined with a UBI if either or both are too low.
1.4.10.1.1.6.1.4. Con: This is not only a question about employees, but employers too. Without stable employees working full-time, employers might not like lowering the wages of employees.
1.4.10.1.1.6.2. Con: Lower wages will improve productivity costs and increase production without affecting the general population income due to the UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.6.3. Pro: Companies are constrained in the current environment by people needed enough wages to, at the least, survive. If a UBI provides this level of subsistence instead then employment wages act as a marginal top up and can be offered at lower rates.
1.4.10.1.1.6.4. Con: Companies will still be restricted by their respective countries laws with respect to minimum wage.
1.4.10.1.1.6.4.1. Con: A UBI could allow the removal of the minimum wage if it were high enough, since it provides sufficient bargaining power to prevent exploitation.
1.4.10.1.1.6.5. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.6.4.1.
1.4.10.1.1.6.6. Pro: The money companies save from providing lower wages might go towards automating jobs, which would reduce a person's sources of income.
1.4.10.1.1.6.6.1. Con: Lower wages could help both companies and employees transition to automation smoothly as it would function as an intermediary step instead of facing instant jobs losses.
1.4.10.1.1.6.7. Pro: This might be beneficial, as people could then buy products at lower prices. With lower purchasing power the price of general goods would need to adapt.
1.4.10.1.1.6.8. Pro: Lower wages could improve a company's image if the drop comes from higher-paid positions, as it will create more equality within it.
1.4.10.1.1.6.8.1. Pro: [US companies should limit CEO pay](https://www.kialo.com/time-for-a-maximum-wage-should-the-us-limit-ceo-pay-2141/2141.0=2141.1)
1.4.10.1.1.6.9. Pro: If a UBI takes place in the US, it'll resolve the US's issues with outsourcing jobs by remaining competitive internationally with nations that do not offer a minimum wage. The US companies could offer a similar wage as those given overseas. This will help US companies grow as well as provide more US jobs.
1.4.10.1.1.7. Pro: Decreasing the available workers would create greater labor shortages. This would result in an increase in pay for those who wish to participate in the labor force.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1. Con: Decreasing available workers and increasing wages, especially in combination with a UBI would cause prices, especially of consumer goods, to increase, possibly even in step with wages.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1. Pro: A UBI will lead to greater inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.1. Con: The [increase of consumption](http://www.economicswebinstitute.org/glossary/cons.htm), as the largest GDP component, is great for the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.1.1. Pro: Keynesian economists believe that higher inflation [promotes employment.](http://www.forbes.com/sites/louiswoodhill/2014/01/23/why-higher-inflation-is-a-very-very-bad-idea/#1823c2bc7e10)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.1.2. Con: In case of the USA, a [stable dollar](http://www.forbes.com/sites/louiswoodhill/2014/01/23/why-higher-inflation-is-a-very-very-bad-idea/#191a89b37e10) was always better for the economy. The average real GDP growth rate is on its highest and the average unemployment rate on its lowest level in times of a stable dollar.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.1.2.1. Con: This argument already assumes inflation. In other words, there is the possibility of rising consumption and a stable dollar.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.1.3. Con: Inflation reduces the ability to consume more with each dollar.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.2. Con: There only is a [very limited positive correlation](http://www.usnews.com/news/articles/2015/05/20/higher-incomes-loosely-linked-to-more-spending-according-to-jpmorgan-chase-study) between changes in income and changes in consumption.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.2.1. Con: The researchers here are careful that the study would not infer macroeconomic trends \(inflation being a macroeconomic trend\). Those who already have a JPMorgan Chase account would not be those who go from being homeless to affording a mobile home, for example.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3. Pro: Assuming the vast majority of citizens will not stop working, the UBI is an addition on top of their incomes. This leads to an increase in the amount of money in the national economy.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.1. Pro: In addition to that, a UBI also boosts the spending power of the lower class, which intensifies the mentioned effect.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.2. Con: A UBI would not create money from nothing, but rather reallocate who has it to spend.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.2.1. Pro: The [UBI](http://morganfoundation.org.nz/why-the-guardian-is-wrong-on-ubi/) is a redistributive transfer, it is not a cost to the government. Rather, it is a cost to some people and a benefit to others; thus the net cost is zero.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.2.1.1. Con: It might not be a cost to the government, but it is an immense cost to taxpayers. Many people will have to pay a far higher tax-rate to pay for the large scope of the program.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.3. Pro: The price of a resource is set partly by consumers' ability and willingness to pay \(and partly by scarcity\). If you gave everyone on earth $1 trillion each tomorrow, the price of a loaf bread would rise to $millions, neutralising the effect. In states that offer unconditional housing benefit, landlords are able to demand at least the state benefit rate, causing average rent to climb. The net effect is a transfer of wealth from the state to landlords.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.3.1. Con: That would only be the case if every economic actor in this scenario started out with the same resources, so they would not be effectively richer. Now, when some have billions to begin with, and many have nothing \(or even less than nothing, debt\), it would bring new economic actors into play.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.4. Con: That is not a great assumption. There will be people who earn a small amount at something they strongly enjoy but have a side job to make ends meet. There will be mothers who would take time off they would not have taken to have children.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.4.1. Con: No reason is given why the majority of people would leave.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.4. Con: Demand is what creates jobs. Inflation is more-complex than simple demand.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.5. Con: It would require an insane level of inflation to occur for the benefits of a basic income to be strongly mitigated. [There is no evidence such inflation would occur.](https://medium.com/basic-income/wouldnt-unconditional-basic-income-just-cause-massive-inflation-fe71d69f15e7)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.5.1. Con: Even if the level of inflation under an implemented UBI was moderate, it might still be high enough to render the implementation of a UBI less efficient to a family than other models of welfare.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.5.2. Pro: UBI payments can be indexed to inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6. Pro: A UBI will raise the general demand in a national economy as citizens would have more available income that can be used [for consumption and investment \(saving\)](http://eml.berkeley.edu/~webfac/wood/e100b_f08/consumption.pdf). This process will lead to a higher price level and [thus inflation](http://www.frbsf.org/education/publications/doctor-econ/2002/october/inflation-factors-rise/).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.1. Pro: It does appear that UBI would increase aggregate demand, then indirectly increase inflation as per [demand-pull inflation](http://www.investopedia.com/articles/05/012005.asp).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.1.1. Con: This would only be the case in a scenario where offer was inelastic \(either because goods are too expensive to produce, thus unenforceable, or already at peak production, because all the land and resources are already destined to other more important products, making opportunity cost too high\), otherwise it would respond to the expansion in the consumer market by producing more, effectively offsetting inflation
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.2. Con: This would only be the case in a scenario where offer was inelastic \(either because goods are too expensive to produce, thus unenforceable, or already at peak production, because all the land and resources are already destined to other more important products, making opportunity cost too high\), otherwise it would respond to the expansion in the consumer market by producing more, effectively offsetting inflation
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3. Pro: There is, by now, considerable evidence that consumption appears to respond to anticipated income increases \([Jappelli, Pistaferri, p.502](http://web.stanford.edu/~pista/ann_rev.pdf)\).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3.1. Con: To say people would consume more based on their income is, beyond plain obvious, not the same as saying such an event would cause inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3.1.1. Con: [Increased spending results in inflation.​](https://www.economicshelp.org/macroeconomics/inflation/causes-inflation/)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3.1.1.1. Con: This is not a universal law, it is only the case in scenarios when the offer is bounded and inelastic; either because it is impractical to produce a good or because it is already being produced at maximum capacity where because  every land and resource is being directed at more important productions the opportunity cost would be too high. This is certainly not the case with a UBI because the offer could respond to the increase in the consumer market with new economic actors.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3.1.1.1.1. Con: In the short term supply is inelastic. It takes time for the market to react, and the market reacts primarily due to the greater incentives provided by increased prices.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.3.2. Con: This point is only valid in that the transition to UBI could be inflationary. It does not speak to long term inflationary trends once it is implemented.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4. Pro: A UBI will increase income, which in turn increases demand \(for better housing, better products, better foods, etc.\) which in turn leads to higher prices. Thus, the benefits of a UBI will normalize out within a few years — back to where we started, where a segment of the population falls at the bottom of the affordability scale.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.1. Pro: To give people a minimum amount of money without taxing others would inflate the money supply, and with such inflation comes increased prices, as more dollars chase the same number of goods and services in a society. And with increased prices, an increased minimum will be required to compensate.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.1.1. Con: A UBI would be paid for through taxes. The money supply would only inflate if the UBI were paid for through printing money, which is not core to UBI policy.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.1.2. Con: But in fact others should be taxed, especially those activities putting pressure on the system that make it harder and harder for people to find jobs \(e.g.  automation and AI-related activities\).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.2. Con: The UBI rate can grow in response to changes in the cost of living. [These problems have been faced, and dealt with with other welfare programs.](https://www.ssa.gov/news/cola/)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.2.1. Con: The impact of UBI will be much larger than existing welfare programs. So the cost of living adjustment will need to be quite large. That will add to the inherent infinite growth tendency of the market system: a UBI increases cost of living which increases in turn the UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.2.1.1. Con: This may indeed be a factor, but these same forces have the possibility of waging wages at a similar rate, while this, all together may well create an environment where saving is less favorable, which is different, it's hard to say it will damage the economy, or people's lives all that much. Lacking more data, it's hard to say for certain which of these two theories is more right.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.3. Con: This argument seems to suggest that we should neither increase income, nor demand for better housing/foods/products, as increasing this demand would result in normalization and pricing out. Aside from pinning UBI to inflation as a remedy, \(and the [work demonstrating](https://medium.com/basic-income/wouldnt-unconditional-basic-income-just-cause-massive-inflation-fe71d69f15e7) that this does not occur, as both Alaska and Kuwait experienced decreases in inflation after UBI implementation\), the premise that increasing both income and demand for better housing/foods is a bad idea, seems quite misguided.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.4. Con: While increased income may increase spending and increase inflation, it does not have to, per se. If supply rises equally as a result of higher demand, the price of a product 'should' not rise, thus no inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.4.5. Pro: UBI will not only create more inflation, but it will create a positive feedback loop. UBI will cause inflation, which will raise the cost of goods and services, which will lead to raising the UBI. Rinse. Repeat.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5. Pro: UBI payments enable many more citizens to acquire real estate, as they have a higher income and easier access to credit. This process leads naturally to increasing prices for property, until the new standard price is reached and the purchasing power of their UBI payments are nullified.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5.1. Con: The combination of purchasing power and freedom from work would enable people to live where they want, not where they must, normalizing the topography of house prices over land area.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5.2. Con: Any time wages rise, there is inflation, but it is less than the increase in wages, giving a net positive gain. A UBI works like an increased wage in the economy, even if it is unearned.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5.2.1. Con: The introduction of a UBI is of different quality than a mere increase of wages: A UBI affects the whole population \(citizens and/or residents\); A UBI provides a significantly higher amount of money compared to an average increase of wages; A UBI is permanent and therefore allows future planning irrespective of the labour market's situation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5.3. Con: That argument presupposes that offer would be inelastic, but it would actually respond to the increase in market demand, and production would be more substantial, for more people would be economic actors and able to buy, therefore offsetting inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.5.4. Con: This wouldn't increase inflation, It would just increase house prices - and only in areas where supply is not meeting demand \(eg major western cities, the UK etc\)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.6.6. Con: The hand that provides the resources for UBI will be the same hand that taxes the economy to generate the revenue. UBI is redistributive in its principle, so what purchasing power you take from one you given to another. Ignoring the intricacies of exactly how consumer goods markets will adjust, the net impact on inflation is likely to be neutral. Furthermore, successful implementation of UBI will require coordination between fiscal and monetary policy authorities.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7. Con: Inflation happens already, it is necessary to drive the incentives to continue to work rather than sit and stock pile cash. As inflation does occur, the amount of UBI given needs to increase over time; but this is an ongoing and healthy cycle.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1. Con: If producers and landlords are expecting a UBI increase based on the last year's inflation, \(in the same way they already predict other things, like a Fed rise in the prime lending rate\), producers will accelerate inflation by anticipating these changes and adjusting their prices in advance.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1.1. Con: The business adjustment argument was made against moving from the gold standard. We transitioned from the gold standard without issue.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1.1.1. Con: This example does not reflect the conditions surrounding the implementation of a basic income. A change to fiat currency did not redistribute money. A basic income would. This changes people's spending habits much more than predicted inflation. With a large redistribution, a rich man does not buy a yacht, and the poor man buys a washing machine. There are many more people who are in the latter category than the former category.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1.2. Con: This would only be true in the case of goods/services where demand significantly outstripped supply; in other areas, market forces would keep prices low as different suppliers competed wih one another for business. The big problem in the UK would be housing, but this is already a problem with the current welfare system and needs its own solution.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1.2.1. Con: This is dependent on how powerful brands are in that industry, the cooperation/cartelization among those market participants, and how quickly new market entrants can respond to such demand signals. For a good with lots of proprietary technology, such as microchips, that may be a significant period of time.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.1.2.2. Con: Saying housing is a problem without a solution yet does not remove it as an area of potential friction. Negative externalities - in any policy change - must be taken into account by any reasonable legislator.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.2. Pro: Inflation is necessary, and the important question here would be "how much"?[Sigh of relief from inflation](http://www.investopedia.com/news/us-inflation-hits-fouryear-high/?ad=dirN&qo=investopediaSiteSearch&qsrc=0&o=40186)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.2.1. Con: Inflation is not necessary. A good argument could be made that money ought to grow at the rate of the population growth, but the 2-3% target is far too much.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.2.2. Con: Even if inflation arrives at a moderate and expected pace, not all people can equally adjust to this. Someone with a fixed income would be at a loss vs someone with an accumulation of financial or real estate assets. Inflation takes from some and gives to others unfairly.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.2.3. Con: Unexpected inflation distorts behavior and creates mal-investments, increased market risk, and lower future growth.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.2.3.1. Pro: For example, inflation creates incentives to spend more in the present and save or invest less in the future, as returns are eroded by the declining real value of money.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.7.3. Con: Inflation happens but is not necessarily given; price inflation is a result of an increasing monetary supply, increasing propensity to spend, or decreased production. To propose that any of these instances will naturally occur in an economy and so can be accepted as a result of a welfare plan is fallacious.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.8. Con: Luxuries may be marginally priced out, but necessities wouldn't be. Especially given that both Alaska and Kuwait experienced [decreases in inflation](https://medium.com/basic-income/wouldnt-unconditional-basic-income-just-cause-massive-inflation-fe71d69f15e7) after versions of UBI were implemented, and that UBI is not additive but redistributive \(further mitigating fears of potential inflation\).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.8.1. Con: It may be redistributive, but wealthier people save more as a percentage of total assets. Any change to that would increase the total cash in economy and therefore inflationary pressures.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.9. Con: A dubious fear of inflation is not an adequate reason to continue allowing poverty. There must be a stronger imperative than the fear of, or harms caused by, inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.10. Con: Real world case studies suggest this is not the case. In [a study in Mexico giving out cash even had a lesser effect on the market than giving out necessary goods](https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2017/9/20/16256240/mexico-cash-transfer-inflation-basic-income). In this case food.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.11. Con: Current economic challenges are [too little inflation, not too much](http://www.investopedia.com/news/us-inflation-hits-fouryear-high/?ad=dirN&qo=investopediaSiteSearch&qsrc=0&o=40186).
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.11.1. Pro: Inflation can be countered through monetary policy. Interest rates are currently too low, as is inflation. Generating inflation would be good for the country, and interest rates could be raised to counter any risk of hyperinflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.12. Con: The amount of price inflation from giving money to the poor is less than the amount of income inflation they experience.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13. Con: Inflation would only occur if the money supply was increased to pay for a UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.1. Pro: Because a UBI is a redistribution plan, the amount of money that people have is, on aggregate, the same. Therefore, consumption will not increase.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.1.1. Con: [Redistributing money](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jun/04/better-economic-growth-when-wealth-distributed-to-poor-instead-of-rich) to the poor likely increases goods sales as the poor spend proportionately more of their income on consumables than on savings.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.1.1.1. Con: This is not because poor people consume more, but because they earn less. If they would earn more, it is likely that they could save more of their income as well.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.1.1.1.1. Con: Lack of financial security is often a barrier to achieving the better work that allows this. A safety net would allow people to take more calculated risks and self-development to finding better work.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.2. Pro: The [Quantity Theory of Money](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity_theory_of_money) states that inflation will only happen if money supply increases. With UBI, aggregate money supply in the economy does not increase, it just redistributes money from one part of the economy \(the rich, specific industries, specific economic activities, etc., depending on how the UBI was funded\) to the public.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.3. Con: Besides the quantity of money \(which affects general inflation\) the relocation of money creates localised inflation too. See for instance university costs in USA: as more money was made available for university costs \(loans, etc.\) the costs then soared way beyond that of general inflation. A UBI would create localised inflation rendering the income payments useless.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.3.1. Pro: An equal influx of money to all the population would create severe localised infaltion in consumer goods such as food, transport, housing, etc. as the purchasing power of everyone is shifted upwards.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.3.2. Con: An UBI would indeed cause some localised inflation, but it's impossible to make sweeping statements on whether they would offset the costs.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.3.3. Con: There's a critical difference between a UBI and the money made available for higher education: the latter is specifically purposed \(which logically increases the risk of inflation\) and the former is not. It allows people to spend the money on a large variety of things, greatly lowering the risk/amount of inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.13.4. Con: Inflation for individual products will increase faster for products generally bought by UBI recipients. Overall across all products inflation may remain the same.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.14. Con: Efficiency created by automation will offset inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.15. Con: The amount of inflation that UBI causes may not be much because it's aimed at getting people out of the poverty line. Net recipients of UBI transfers are going to be spending mostly on basic needs and thus have minimal effect on the money supply.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.15.1. Con: Not all goods are meat and potatoes. There are goods the poorest don't utilize which have constrained or sluggish changes in supply, such as housing.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.15.1.1. Con: This would only add to the monetary stress for those items, not divert money from them to goods that would grow the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.15.2. Con: Assuming what UBI transfers will be spent on is hearsay - one doesn't know and can't decide for others what that money will be spent on and it's just as plausible that the money could be mis-spent, requiring yet another safety net for those who waste their UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.15.2.1. Con: Giving even more money to people who will misspend it is redundant, as that money will also be misspent. Education is what is needed in those cases.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.16. Pro: An universal basic income doesn't guarantee the access to basic needs as inflation can eat away the purchasing power, specially if basic needs like health and education are paid for and market regulated. It's better to provide free public services and subsided housing, food, among others.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.16.1. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.5.2.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.16.2. Pro: Why not giving basic resources and services instead \(housing, internet, food, etc.\)? It will cost less, because of economies of scale, but it would decrease the GDP.
[www.independent.co.uk](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/universal-basic-services-idea-better-basic-income-citizens-social-housing-ucl-a7993476.html)
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.16.3. Pro: The two most common proofs for this proposition in the U.S. are education and healthcare. Both sectors are heavily subsidized and entitled; both sectors have seen steep [price inflation](https://images.google.com/imgres?imgurl=http%3A%2F%2Fblogs.reuters.com%2Fdata-dive%2Ffiles%2F2013%2F12%2FCPI.gif&imgrefurl=http%3A%2F%2Fblogs.reuters.com%2Fdata-dive%2F2013%2F12%2F17%2Fus-inflation-flat-historically-low-and-still-below-the-feds-target%2F&docid=VYwuC7J9zbkFQM&tbnid=qMIcT_Mx5KiiFM%3A&vet=1&w=700&h=615&source=sh%2Fx%2Fim) far in excess of the consumer price index for inflation.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.17. Pro: UBI would make the value of said basic income the new zero. If everyone has wealth, no one has wealth because the base value is whatever the least of us has.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.18. Pro: -> See 1.3.13.3.1.1.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.19. Con: In an era of accelerating technology, we should see negative pressure on inflation, meaning UBI could be funded by central banks printing money, a kind of QE for UBI. The great benefit of this is that if this assumption about inflation proves wrong, the policy could be stopped. It could also be phased in gradually, tested, and evaluated to see if the policy leads to inflation before increasing it.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.20. Pro: An economy is an allocation of resources, and money is just a signaling device and abstract store of value. Because a UBI simply distorts values and the signaling function, it cannot be effective in causing a permanent reallocation of resources, the only real justification for a UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.20.1. Con: As a UBI is funded by taxation, it can facilitate a wealth transfer from the wealthy to the poor.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.21. Con: UBI will only have an effect on inflation if it is set so high that demand outstrips the ability of the labor market and natural resources to meet that demand. It will likely have only a modest effect on inflation. Of course, until a basic income is truly universal, we can't know what the real effect on the economy will be.
1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.21.1. Pro: In most cases, supply can rise to meet increasing demand, undercutting any inflationary pressure. The obvious exception is real estate, but a UBI will allow more people to migrate away from concentrated population centers because they are no longer dependent on the jobs there. This will effectively reduce demand for real estate, countering any inflationary pressure. Renewable energy will counter the inflation of fuel prices.
1.4.10.1.1.8. Pro: The rise of [freelancing](http://www.afr.com/business/small-business/future-of-work-how-the-freelance-revolution-is-changing-the-meaning-of-a-day-job-20140508-jyw7q) and [casualisation](https://www.theguardian.com/business/grogonomics/2014/oct/17/the-casualisation-of-australias-workforce-is-nothing-to-panic-about) of the workforce is eroding the stability and financial security provided by full-time employment.
1.4.10.1.1.8.1. Con: Freelancing and casualization are indicators that people as a whole are now more capable of providing for themselves in the market than they were previously.
1.4.10.1.1.8.2. Pro: With the recent buzz of the [gig economy](https://www.forbes.com/sites/larryalton/2018/01/24/why-the-gig-economy-is-the-best-and-worst-development-for-workers-under-30/#7cc41d876d76), especially for younger people, a UBI would help these people get enough money until more stable work comes along.
1.4.10.1.1.8.2.1. Pro: Since the Great Recession, people do not have much choice with jobs, so people should not be punished for something out of their control \(a rough job market\). A UBI corrects for this issue.
1.4.10.1.1.8.2.2. Con: A 'worker stipend' \(money that fills in where the gig work lacks\) would be better for the economy, as it is less wasteful. It can target people who need the money the most rather than give everyone money whether they need it or not \(like a UBI\).
1.4.10.1.1.8.2.3. Pro: The issue with the gig economy is that it is a [large part of the workforce already and is expected to get bigger](https://www.nasdaq.com/article/the-gig-economy-2020-freelance-workforce-predicted-to-rise-to-43-cm803297). A UBI will help the economy prepare for that scenario.
1.4.10.1.1.8.2.4. Pro: Since [20 million gig workers would prefer a full-time job that they cannot find](http://money.cnn.com/2016/10/27/news/economy/gig-economy-workers/?iid=EL), a UBI would help them greatly.
1.4.10.1.1.8.2.5. Pro: Since the [gig economy is bad for the economy as a whole](https://www.forbes.com/sites/larryalton/2018/01/24/why-the-gig-economy-is-the-best-and-worst-development-for-workers-under-30/#7cc41d876d76) in the long term, a UBI may resolve the issues that the gig economy present.
1.4.10.1.1.9. Pro: With the insurance of a UBI acting as a safety net, workers can challenge their employers if they find their conditions of work unfair or degrading.
1.4.10.1.1.9.1. Pro: This serves as a check against the leverage typically held by employers. They now have a greater economic reason to ensure that their workers are satisfied.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2. Con: New ways of maintaining the power imbalance between the employer and the employee will be found.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.1. Pro: The imbalance of power serves to increase productivity, as hierarchical management serves to guide and govern workers with a centralized system of rules and goals.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.1.1. Con: It is morally abhorrent to use the threat of making someone jobless as a negotiating tool.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.1.2. Con: The imbalance of power could also create antagonistic relations between employee and employer, which diminish productivity.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.1.3. Pro: [Proper management techniques](http://www.forbes.com/sites/work-in-progress/2014/01/20/8-common-causes-of-workplace-demotivation/2/#390e499cf401) like rewarding success and addressing low performance increases overall motivation and productivity.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.1.4. Con: This argument sounds very similar to the one used to justify slavery. Slavery is wrong.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.2. Con: The power inbalance would be shifted towards the worker with a UBI, any countereffect on behalf of the employers is a seperate issue that could be argued in chicken-egg like fashion as the countereffect to the countereffect takes place.
1.4.10.1.1.9.2.3. Con: It would still increase the difficulty for the employer in maintaining said power imbalance, which is beneficial for the employees.
1.4.10.1.1.9.3. Pro: With a UBI in place, the power imbalance shifts: employment will be less of a necessity for some, making it easier to bring unfair treatment, wages, or working conditions to light.
1.4.10.1.1.9.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.4.3.
1.4.10.1.1.9.5. Con: Walking away is often too little, too late. For example, an unsafe workplace is often only exposed through serious accident. Proactive government intervention is better than retroactive employee action.
1.4.10.1.1.9.5.1. Con: Proactive government intervention can still continue even if a UBI is implemented.
1.4.10.1.1.9.6. Con: Everyone but the most poor have adapted to living beyond the meagre means offered by a UBI. This means that the income that an employer offers still affords them a large amount of power over employees.
1.4.10.1.1.9.6.1. Con: They can adapt to living on less just as much as they've adapted to living on more.
1.4.10.1.1.9.6.2. Con: Even so, the threat of losing your luxuries is not as grave as the risk of losing provision for your basic needs, or having to rely on welfare system that are not always certain or reliable, and often stressful or stigmatizing.
1.4.10.1.1.9.7. Pro: UBI gives workers more power at the negotiating table. The choice is "work or don't have extra benefits" instead of "work or starve."
1.4.10.1.1.9.7.1. Pro: Currently many workers hesitate to speak up for fear of losing their jobs and their family's livelihoods.
1.4.10.1.1.9.7.2. Pro: UBI allows less skilled workers to negotiate pay and working conditions with employers fairly, rather than from a position of desperation.
1.4.10.1.1.9.8. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.3.
1.4.10.1.1.9.9. Pro: A UBI would make wages depend less on bargaining power, and more on skill requirements and effort.
1.4.10.1.1.9.10. Pro: UBI helps employees make the decision to not go to work sick. Then customers feel better that they are not going to get sick as often and employees don't worry about infecting others. So many benefits can incur from savings in costs, lives, and jobs.
1.4.10.1.1.9.11. Pro: A UBI would make it easier for unemployed people to get back on their feet and rejoin the workforce without having to find ways to cope with debilitating poverty in the meantime.
1.4.10.1.1.9.12. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.9.7.2.
1.4.10.1.1.10. Pro: A UBI allows people to take the time to look for better jobs.
1.4.10.1.1.10.1. Pro: Having this time to find suitable work will stop the "revolving door" effect, where people on benefits are forced to take a job, hate it, and quit shortly thereafter, becoming a burden on the welfare system.
1.4.10.1.1.10.2. Con: The additional time that people take to look for jobs would result in reduced time spent performing productive labor.
1.4.10.1.1.10.3. Pro: More time gives citizens a greater chance to find a job close to their current home and social environment \(family, friends\), which has a positive effect on their quality of life.
1.4.10.1.1.10.4. Con: A UBI will make citizens very picky and demanding concerning their job choices. Some positions and jobs would be very hard, next to impossible, to staff.
1.4.10.1.1.10.4.1. Con: It is the companies' duty to create incentives in order to staff their positions.
1.4.10.1.1.10.5. Pro: People stay in unsatisfying jobs because they are afraid of losing their healthcare benefits. With the UBI, people can better afford to fund their own healthcare and be less tied to any given job just for the benefits.
1.4.10.1.1.10.5.1. Con: That is only true for systems where public universal healthcare is not provided and therefore can easily be solved by implementation of that, without need of a UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.10.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.1.1.
1.4.10.1.1.10.7. Pro: In the US, only 30% of workers feel engaged in their jobs; [leading to a likely cost of around $500 billion a year](http://screenwerk.com/2013/06/25/gallup-70-of-workforce-not-engaged-costs-billions-in-lost-productivity/).
1.4.10.1.1.10.7.1. Pro: Productivity [increases](https://www.fastcompany.com/3048751/the-future-of-work/happy-employees-are-12-more-productive-at-work) when people enjoy the work they do. This can have a positive effect on the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.10.8. Con: UBIs threaten corporations that depend on salaried employees that earn 100% of their income from them.
1.4.10.1.1.10.8.1. Pro: A UBI could lead to increased employee turnover because their reduced dependence on a salary enables them to quit at the slightest dissatisfaction. This hurts companies, and then in the long run, the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.10.8.2. Con: If a business can't survive paying a fair rate to it's employees, it shouldn't exist in the first place.
1.4.10.1.1.10.9. Pro: Taking renumeration to make ends meet out of the equation will also allow people to only pursue what is of interest to them. Counter-intuitively, the biggest impact will be in the job market, as [workplace efficiency was studied and known](https://www.emergenetics.com/blog/workplace-appreciation-gratitude/) to be proportional to the psychological effects of workplace appreciation & gratitude.
1.4.10.1.1.10.10. Pro: UBI means less direct marketing - door to door sales and cold calling. Most people only take these jobs when they are desperate and have no other option.
1.4.10.1.1.10.11. Con: This is not a feature unique to a UBI. It is something that targeted unemployment benefits manage to achieve quite well under the status quo.
1.4.10.1.1.10.11.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.12.
1.4.10.1.1.10.11.2. Con: While unemployment benefits help people seeking full time employment, they generally provide a disincentive to finding part time work, as reporting this work means losing part or all of their benefits for that period.
1.4.10.1.1.11. Con: When people no longer need to work in order to improve their economic circumstances, governments are less likely to legislate on worker protections.
1.4.10.1.1.11.1. Pro: If there are comparatively fewer worker protections, this will further incentivize people to stay unemployed.
1.4.10.1.1.11.2. Con: This is only a problem in the short term. As time passes, companies have to create new protections and regulations  in order to appeal to potential employees.
1.4.10.1.1.11.3. Con: If companies, or governments, do not establish worker protections, then employees are less likely to want to work for those companies. This could spur an increase in automation in order to meet unit demand, which would in turn would further necessitate a UBI so products can be consumed.
1.4.10.1.1.11.4. Con: The government legislates worker protections because of its duty to protect citizens. This duty is not erased if the way citizens interact with work changes.
1.4.10.1.1.12. Con: The sheer cost of a UBI would reduce economic growth and thus decrease wages and increase unemployment. This gives employers more power to exploit employees.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1. Pro: A UBI has negative effects on the national economy due to effects such as inflation and higher tax rates.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3. Con: A UBI better rewards low-paid jobs. That has a positive influence on the economy, as well as on society.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.21.3.3.5.2.4.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.2. Pro: As some companies cannot afford to pay high salaries, a UBI works as a subsidy. It helps the affected companies, e.g. start-ups, to develop and expand.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.2.1. Con: On the other hand, a UBI can be misused by employers as an excuse for low salaries.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.3. Con: There are more direct ways to improve this. A free market will appropriately compensate for the skill of labor given time to work out kinks. Labor laws and unions provide incentives to pay individuals just compensation. A job that pays more than it produces has a negative effect on the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.3.1. Con: There are many perverse factors and market failures that currently prevent the free market from working effectively as it should.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.4. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.5. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.6. Con: A UBI is actually probably worth just as much as those low paid jobs; thus it wont change a thing for people who want to contribute to society without receiving a universal benefit.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.6.1. Con: A low-paid job provides the same financial incentive to work and contribute to society whether a UBI exists or not; a UBI actually makes it more likely because benefits are not withdrawn when becoming employed.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.6.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.7. Pro: A UBI could help with space exploration where the economy has physical limits. Space explorers will need funding for setting up extraterrestrial civilization but have less opportunities to work \(not many options in a space capsule or a planet with no people\). Or they can support their family on Earth through a UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.8. Pro: UBI would achieve more employment in jobs that are necessary, while reducing conformity to unnecessary and wasteful employment because it allows us more freedom to choose our fields of work.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.8.1. Pro: People would be more apt to choose work that they believed to be important rather than just working for a paycheck.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.8.2. Pro: UBI would allow people less fear of unemployment to be able to do work that they are good at, rather than just working for a paycheck due to having less fear of losing their income.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.8.3. Pro: UBI would allow people less fear and freedom to discover the work that they are good at, that being their talents.  Thereby, better accommodating a wider and differing range of personalities.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.8.4. Pro: As population growth occurs, and times change, the number of different personalities will also increase. UBI better allows for the accommodation of these different personalities via reducing unnecessary conformity.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.9. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.7.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.10. Pro: As people become enabled to work less it will also enable them to spend more time volunteering, or offering their professional skills to causes they care about.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.11. Pro: A UBI would increase the demand for products and services people actually need \(e.g. housing, education, healthy food, health care\) but could not afford otherwise; resulting in a more efficient society-wise use of resources.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.4. Con: Giving the poor a higher baseline income allows them to better participate in price signalling \(relative to the middle/upper classes\) and [thus better drive demand for inexpensive but high-quality goods](http://ordinary-gentlemen.com/2016/04/22/the-unconditional-basic-income-and-the-hayekian-price-system/) that improve their lives.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.4.1. Pro: Conditional grants \(such as food vouchers\) are a mechanism that force consumer choice towards particular goods and services, rather than a market-driven aggregation of individual preferences. Allowing consumers more agency over how they acquire goods more efficiently directs the market to provide the goods and services that individuals actually value.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.4.2. Pro: A UBI would facilitate a more democratic capitalist society by providing all citizens with sufficient funds to 'vote' for the companies they want to exist through the purchasing of products and/or services. This informed consumption would provide a greater flow of capital to companies that meet the wants of the greater population forcing companies to adapt or be replaced. This is in contrast to a wealthy elite defining the direction and ethos of companies and thus the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5. Con: UBIs increase the productivity of individuals.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.1. Pro: [One reason](http://www.cnn.com/2015/01/12/opinions/schulte-leisure-productivity/) for low productivity is that people are unable to enjoy the benefits of the money they earn through their labor, since they have no time. A system that allows people to actually enjoy their life means that they are more productive at work, since they are more relaxed.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.1.1. Pro: This argument is [empirically supported](http://www.forbes.com/sites/elenabajic/2015/08/25/take-a-vacation-to-boost-your-productivity-at-work/#3a27c02c6955) by statistics in countries with more days for vacation.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.1.1.1. Con: There is no evidence that this is a causal relation as opposed to a simple correlation. It is possible that countries with the highest productivity can afford the most days for vacation.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.1.2. Pro: This is especially true in places where the cost of living is particularly high, pushing many workers to take on extra hours or additional jobs, which further reduces their access to leisure time away from work.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.1.2.1. Pro: This is can be found in the Hawaiian culture through the notion of "[living aloha](https://www.cbrigham.com/living-aloha-and-pono/)," which advocates building up the local community in order to maximize time spent with family in order to combat the negative effects of the lack of leisure time.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.2. Pro: People who do not work out of financial fear are [more productive](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2015/05/what-if-everybody-didnt-have-to-work-to-get-paid/393428/).
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.2.1. Con: The biggest motivation for me to earn a paycheck is the fact that if I don't work, I starve. If I don't work, I don't have a place to live. If I don't work, I can't support a family. If all of that were covered by a UBI, and my only motivation to work is extra "me money", then I am far more likely to do sub-par quality work or even quit because I don't need that job.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.2.1.1. Con: Most people would prefer not to be in that situation. We should therefore be working to avoid forcing anyone into that situation, rather than justifying it.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.3. Con: A UBI doesn't decrease societies overall workload. Only innovation does that.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.3.1. Con: It may help to redirect society's production in directions more genuinely valued, rather than forcing people to accept any job they can obtain, as current society does.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.4. Pro: Today, lots of people are unfit or unwilling to do their jobs, but need those jobs to get through the month, while lots of willing and ambitious people are sitting at home, searching for a job. With UBI, one could leave a job they are not happy with and still survive while searching for something else. In doing so their post could be taken by someone ambitious, which would lead to a further boost in productivity.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.5. Con: UBI is a productivity killer, in the sense that it gives incentives to things different than value-driven activities \(be it straight to the point corporate work, sports or arts production\). Society reached our level of complexity due to its incentives structure, changing this so broadly is, to say the least, risky.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.5.1. Con: We've changed social structures many times before: 'society' used to be only monarchs and lords who owned everything.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.5.2. Con: We have the ability to do controlled experiments of both size, and scale to see how this policy will effect us.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.5.2.1. Pro: [Finland](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/finland-universal-basic-income-experiment-lesson-tips-ubi-poverty-unemployment-a7871441.html), and many other countries, are already experimenting with a UBI under controlled conditions to rigorously determine its effects.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.5.3. Con: There is more to life than "productivity" and "value", and if there is no time to use and enjoy the utility we create, it is valueless.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.6. Pro: [Evidence](http://behavioralscientist.org/wisdom-universal-basic-income/) suggests UBI is good from a psychological well-being and productivity perspective.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.6.1. Pro: Current welfare systems induce a psychology of fear of ['earning too much'](http://reason.com/reasontv/2016/03/03/the-human-cost-of-welfare-how-the-system), which can hinder the potential for self-reliance and economic growth. A UBI would remove this fear, as it is not dependent on one's earnings.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.6. Pro: The effectiveness of UBI depends on the culture. In cultures that prize hard work a UBI will inspire greater work. In cultures that do not prize hard work \(say, [Saudi Arabia](https://www.forbes.com/pictures/eihg45gjhg/saudi-arabia-4/#574f01de7ae3)\), most of the actual work will have to be imported from outside.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.6.1. Con: It's not so easy to measure cultural laziness / work ethic. Saudi Arabia, for example, is a more difficult place to find work as it is usually defined.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.6.2. Con: Using Saudi Arabia or any other country that have to deal with physically crushing heat during the day that curtails daytime work outside of the confine of a well air-conditioned enclosure is not a fair example. This fallacy is too often and erroneously used to make cultures from hot country appear to be lazy. It is true however, that necessity is the mother of invention, thus people living in a harsh and scarce environment have a higher incentive to innovate.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.7. Con: A UBI guaranteed to any citizen of a country would allow its government to foster liberal economic policies without throwing people into economic and social insecurity.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.7.1. Pro: For example it could create regulations to the effect of allowing for more flexible jobs and hence some air for the businesses \(especially the small and mid sized ones\) or for creating a greater freedom of choice for individuals to choose their professionnal path.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.7.2. Pro: A UBI could remove the need for a regulated minimum wage and allow companies to hire lower skilled, lower experienced or lower productive workers at appropriate rates.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.6.4.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.7.4. Pro: A UBI, although a band-aid for the current economic model \(capitalism\), this temporary phase, could be just enough to be a stepping stone towards a more sustainable economy.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.8. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.
