Pro: The [Saudi Arabian](https://www.forbes.com/sites/jeffreydorfman/2016/10/03/saudi-government-budget-cuts-prove-basic-income-is-no-solution-to-slow-economic-growth/#1376eff54bbe) government provides its citizens with payments comparable to a UBI; however its economy \(outside of oil production\) is undeveloped.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.1. Pro: The Saudi government employs around two-thirds of [Saudi nationals](https://www.forbes.com/sites/jeffreydorfman/2016/10/03/saudi-government-budget-cuts-prove-basic-income-is-no-solution-to-slow-economic-growth/#4a0dcedb4bbe) in make-work jobs that provide a steady and reliable income regardless of an individual's productivity. Despite this free and unconditional source of income, people do not do more than required or use their free time for other productive activities.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.1.1. Con: The Saudi system \(as depicted above\) is a poor example since it's neither here nor there. UBI isn't about 'making fake jobs', its purpose and spirit is to free people up to live lives that are meaningful to them.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.2. Con: The Saudi economy is undeveloped because the sheer size of the oil economy means that no other forms of industry and services are necessary.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.3. Con: The Saudi economy is undeveloped because archaic social norms prevent half the population working, and normalise a lack of effort in the other half.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.3.1. Con: There is no evidence that women not working normalizes a lack of effort in men. Japan is a case and point in a male-dominated yet workaholic culture.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.3.2. Pro: Smart women in the workforce is better for the economy than smart women not in the workforce.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.4. Con: Providing make-work schemes to the general populace is not the same as a UBI; it is more similar to 'work-for-the-dole' schemes and suffers from the same problems: that forcing people into meaningless tasks is less efficient than allowing them the freedom to self-improve or produce value independently.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.5. Con: This payment is far from universal when you consider it is not available to [the 30%+ portion of the population who are foreign workers](https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/geos/sa.html).
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.6. Con: Saudia Arabia's economy has not suffered because of its UBI. Its GDP per capita is [comparable to the United States](https://www.wikiwand.com/en/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(PPP\)_per_capita)'.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.9.7. Pro: Saudi Arabia's economic woes have less to do with their welfare spending, and more an over-reliance on oil which proved to be their Archilles heel. The Saudi case says less about UBI, and more about the need for economic diversification.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10. Pro: There are a multitude of jobs that need to be filled that are not economic and that are also minimum wage, such as bush regeneration, farm labour or factory hand etc. It is possible that providing a UBI could potentially decrease the incentive for workers to take jobs that are unappealing and/or uncomfortable.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.1. Con: If those low-skill jobs are fundamental, but increasingly attract fewer workers, the jobs will then require higher wages until their attractiveness reaches an equilibrium.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.1.1. Pro: Many factories and warehouses pay double federal minimum wage for entry level. It isn't about the pay, it's about the culture of people not willing to work with their hands and get them dirty. It's about the culture that only office work is good work, and that actual labor is beneath the common man.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.1.2. Con: The equilibrium you talk about is bankruptcy for many small businesses that can no longer afford a waiter, a cleaner, or a dishwasher. This simply plays into the hand of well established businesses and makes the barrier to entry much higher for anyone looking to start something new.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2. Pro: A UBI is currently not sustainable as there would not be enough incentives for people to work in jobs which are fundamental to society, but unattractive \(e.g. garbage disposal\).
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.1. Con: When considering a UBI as a future solution, many of these types of jobs could be performed by machines, thus rendering low-skills employment unnecessary for humans.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.1.1. Con: Replacing fundamental jobs such as garbage disposal with machines is not, as of yet, a viable solution.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.1.1.1. Con: Technological development has increased a lot: we are about to make driving a useless human skill. People seems to underestimate the potential of the AI; we are just a few steps from a new industrial revolution.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.2. Con: If these jobs are truly fundamental, the compensation for them will increase to a new equilibrium.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.3.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.3. Con: Jobs that are fundamental to society should not be unattractive - for example, garbage disposal doesn't have to be so unattractive. If we all learn to reduce, reuse, recycle, compost, etc, there is not much left to dispose of. Some ancient societies built their homes in midden piles.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.2.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.4.5.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.3. Con: A UBI will decrease the supply of low-skilled workers. This dynamic will be counteracted by rising wages \(in low-skilled jobs\) and a surge in Automation \(to replace lost workforce\). A UBI therefore acts almost like a pigouvian tax on low-skilled work \(similar to a minimum-wage\).
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.4. Pro: A UBI would most likely mean workers subsidizing low wage jobs.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.5. Con: These jobs are not taken because they do not pay enough for someone to live on. And these jobs are better done with automation.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.5.1. Con: Some jobs are not currently possible to automate. Others are not economically feasible to.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.6. Pro: If there are any menial jobs that cannot be automated, it will be difficult to arrange to have them done. Menial labor, if not motivated by necessity or by the hope for better opportunities, doesn't have what little appeal it had in the first place.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.7. Con: A UBI will force businesses to pay workers fairer wages, particularly for menial jobs less people want to do, since it will reduce the amount of people looking for this type of work and leave it to the employer to attract workers \(with higher pay, more rights etc.\).
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.10.8. Con: A UBI does not need to be set so high that everyone is satisfied, only enough that their needs are met at a minimal reasonable level.  Anything beyond that would still require a job.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.11. Con: -> See 1.3.21.3.3.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.12. Con: A UBI makes everybody economically "productive" by giving them money to spend on goods and services, thus driving demand.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.12.1. Pro: UBI will stimulate the economy, as wealth scarcity reduces cash flow. UBI will improve cash flow and thus encourage people to spend more freely, especially those who work and educate for extra wealth privileges.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.12.2. Pro: This also has benefits on the supply side, as when people cannot buy enough goods, it prevents the need for [government intervention due to market failures](http://www.econlib.org/library/Topics/College/marketfailures.html), as a UBI would prevent them.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.12.3. Pro: In a world where automation is going to remove more jobs than it will create, a UBI will be needed to ensure the  liquidity of money still has value. Otherwise, money will be hugely devalued to a point much worse than stagnation/devaulation and right or left wing politics of today which are focused on the creation or redistribution of wealth become irrelevant to the masses and lead can to a population with no sense of purpose except for day to day survival; leading to a rise in Fascist beliefs.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.12.4. Pro: -> See 1.3.21.3.6.1.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.13. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.4.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.14. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15. Con: Widely respected economists, such as free market capitalist [Milton Friedman](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xtpgkX588nM&t=50s), were in favor of a basic income guarantee in the form of a negative income tax and saw it as having positive effects on the national economy. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milton_Friedman#Social_security.2C_welfare_programs.2C_and_negative_income_tax)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.1. Pro: Having a UBI for 8 years will grow the USA GDP by 2.5 trillion dollars. [www.cnbc.com](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/08/31/1000-per-month-cash-handout-would-grow-the-economy-by-2-point-5-trillion.html)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.1.1. Con: An increase in the GDP is not in itself a good thing, especially when it just seems to be "more money is spent" \(as the study seems to suggest\); one must link to how quality of life is affected by a change in the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.1.2. Con: The linked study makes multiple questionable/disputed assumptions.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.2. Pro: A study shows that UBI would grow the economy. [www.vox.com](https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2017/8/30/16220134/universal-basic-income-roosevelt-institute-economic-growth)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.3. Con: Friedman preferred no welfare at all, and simply thought an equal payment to all would be more efficient than payments dependent on income level. That implies he would not support massively increasing welfare expenditures to institute a UBI. [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milton_Friedman#Social_security.2C_welfare_programs.2C_and_negative_income_tax)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.4. Con: Friedman reiterated these arguments 18 years later in Free to Choose, with the additional proviso that such a reform would only be satisfactory if it replaced the current system of welfare programs rather than augment it.[en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milton_Friedman#Social_security.2C_welfare_programs.2C_and_negative_income_tax)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.15.5. Con: It's inaccurate to claim Friedman supported a UBI. Negative Income Tax is very different from UBI in that UBI gives everyone the same check regardless of means or a change in circumstances, whereas NIT is predicated on how far off from a pre-determined level of income the recipient is.[Milton Friedman on Negative Income Tax](https://www.youtube.com/results?search_query=milton+friedman+negative+income+tax)
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.16. Con: People tend to [spend less and save more](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradox_of_thrift) when there is no security net. The economy would be better of having people circulating their money back into society.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.17. Pro: A universal basic income would cause marked reductions in productivity while increasing public expenditure, harming the economy.
1.4.10.1.1.12.1.17.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.7.
1.4.10.1.1.12.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.
1.4.10.1.1.12.3. Con: Much of the money that would go into a UBI is already being spent on other programs. The net cost may actually be lower.
1.4.10.1.1.12.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.3.1.2.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4. Con: UBI would likely have a growth effect on the market by putting money in the hands of the impoverished, who statistically spend it most readily.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.1. Pro: Black Americans have low [saving rates](http://thepowerisnow.com/savings-rate-of-african-americans/). Thus, money received would be channeled into the economy directly.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.1.1. Con: As the [source](http://thepowerisnow.com/savings-rate-of-african-americans/) states, the most likely reason is that Black Americans do not earn enough to save. The money paid out to them might give them a chance to save.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.1.1.1. Con: Much of the money received may have to go to [debt settlement and other expenses](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2015/10/debt-black-families/409756/), with very little going into savings.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.1.1.1.1. Con: Even just clearing existing debts is a starting point for savings - when income is no longer eaten up by interest fees on debt, individuals can begin to save.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.1.1.2. Con: The money paid out to them would be taken from someone else, who would as a result save less than before. Moreover, the money paid to them would not be enough for them to create savings, otherwise they would not need it urgently, and the UBI would not have been enacted.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.2. Con: Increased spending means decreased saving, and therefore less capital construction. This makes society less wealthy in the long run.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.2.1. Con: More capital is constructed rather by re-investing than by keeping saved money-based capital. Saved capital is out of the market for all the time it stays saved.
1.4.10.1.1.12.4.2.1.1. Con: Money that is saved in a bank or other financial institution is not out of the market, but instead is now accessible to most anyone who wants to borrow. Money saved "under the mattress" does not contribute to economic growth, but is a negligible portion of all savings. It also plays an important role in the security of low income households \(which are most likely to practice it\).
1.4.10.1.1.12.5. Con: Poverty is very [expensive](https://www.economist.com/news/united-states/21663262-why-low-income-americans-often-have-pay-more-its-expensive-be-poor). Not having a UBI is more expensive than a UBI. Economic policies that create jobs are very expensive if we're creating those jobs mostly as an excuse to provide people with incomes.
1.4.10.1.1.12.5.1. Pro: The increase in economic contribution from those lifted out of poverty by UBI could be greater in many cases than the cost of providing those individuals a UBI.
1.4.10.1.1.12.6. Con: A UBI decreases the importance of wages and those who feel they are capable of living on the UBI will drop out of the labor market. This in turn decreases unwilling unemployment and increase the demand for labor.
1.4.10.1.2. Pro: There is an overriding presumption which suggests money is the only cohesive motivator to unified productivity. We use communism as the example, but that flawed system poisons other post-scarcity-like imaginings. It is difficult to prove, but is entirely conceivable that a UBI-floored system could foster more productivity and motivation.
1.4.10.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.
1.4.10.3. Con: Money is not the only factor that creates jobs. Freedom and creativity can create jobs often in spite of a lack of resources.
1.4.10.4. Con: -> See 1.4.7.5.
1.4.10.5. Con: In the 50's the [labor participation rate](http://static5.businessinsider.com/image/515ec6526bb3f73375000008-1503-1127-620-/labor-force-participation-rate-march.png) was 6-8% lower than currently, but the economy was good and income equality was much lower. With the gains in productivity we've achieved, everyone could live comfortably with fewer workers.
1.4.10.6. Pro: Society relies on economic productivity to tax and fund programs, such as welfare, education and healthcare. While people individually might be better off if they worked less, it would lead to less tax revenue and harm society overall \(even if those programs were replaced with a UBI\).
1.4.10.6.1. Con: Tax systems can be re-evaluated, corrected and improved to account for the effects of a UBI.
1.4.10.7. Pro: UBI is not a long term solution. Even in a situation where it is perfectly collected and distributed it would only work while the amount of working individuals out number the amount of individuals collecting UBI. There is a tipping point were the system would be unsustainable.
1.4.10.7.1. Con: That tipping point may not be reached due to other incentives to work.
1.4.10.7.2. Con: There could not be more people working than receiving - a universal income is universal. It may well be that there are more people making a net contribution than receiving a net income, once taxes, etc, are allowed for, but again this is not a given - there need to be enough people producing that they can meet the needs of the economy as a whole, but this is not just a matter of raw numbers of people, but of resources as a whole.
1.4.10.8. Pro: The point of a UBI is supposed to be that it doesn't disincentivise work. If it does, then it is flawed in the same way that welfare is; it's a distortion that leads to less work and hence less real wealth.
1.4.10.8.1. Con: If you define "incentivize work" as any lawful mechanism that makes people work more, then abolishing slavery disincentivized work. Any semblance of power \(aka more money, less unemployment\) that you give to the worker will also give them the ability to say no to work they don't need to do. You don't want to optimize for wealth created alone.
1.4.10.8.2. Con: Hours worked are subject to diminishing returns. At one point, working one hour more might be less beneficial overall, than for example resting. In fact, it's possible for a worker to be more productive spending that hour doing something that he is intrinsically motivated to do, like spending time with children, making food, cleaning at home etc.
1.4.10.8.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.7.1.3.
1.4.10.9. Con: A decreased working population pushes industry towards automation and higher incentives to attract workers within a more competitive market.
1.4.10.9.1. Pro: The amount of products and services that were generated will still be produced, only now under sophisticated automation, whether this will decrease overall wealth is dependent on the decisions and discussions surrounding wealth distribution.
1.4.10.9.1.1. Pro: Automation is expected to reduce more jobs than the jobs it will create, so we will propably need to find a way to work less and maintain a good standard of living. A UBI might be one of them.
1.4.10.10. Pro: This is the biggest issue facing the US economy today. Productivity IS down to where it was during the great recession. This is the biggest reason for low or no economic development in many sectors of the economy and subsequent stagnation of income.
1.4.10.11. Con: With automation progressing as it is, businesses will be able to produce the same output without having to pay as many employees \(or allowing employees to work fewer hours\) giving them a higher profit or higher wages. This will lead to some degree of increased wealth.
1.4.11. Con: Working, in general, should not be seen as a bad thing. The emphasis of government policy should be to improve our working life; not nullify it.
1.4.11.1. Con: Work takes time that can be otherwise used on good things, so unless it can be shown that it is valuable, it should be treated as a bad thing.
1.4.11.1.1. Con: The value of work can be determine by the amount paid for it. So only charitable work, in some cases, may be treated as bad work. Any other is valuable by default.
1.4.11.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.
1.4.11.2. Con: Even if work was a good thing, it is unclear why it should take the priority it currently does over other uses of time.
1.4.11.3. Con: We work quite a lot. It is unclear if it maintains any value and represents a good use of time given the amount of time we currently devote to it.
1.4.11.3.1. Pro: There are many jobs which are widely acknowledged as ["make-work" jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Make-work_job).
1.4.11.3.2. Pro: We spend 8 hours sleeping, probably at least 1.5 hours eating, 8 hours working, and around 1 hour commuting to and from work, all of which only leaves us 5.5 hours to actually live, create, or better ourselves at a maximum. It is hard to argue that social lives, self discovery, personal growth, and enjoyment are all so subservient to the 'duty' or 'responsibility' that is work.
1.4.11.4. Con: A UBI does not necessarily devalue work. It does give people a chance to find work they find intrinsically rewarding as well as financially. Particularly if they have lost a job to out sourcing or automation.
1.4.11.5. Con: Work is not necessarily a good thing. Most people hold jobs that are not rewarding, stimulating, or interesting.
1.4.11.6. Con: Working in itself may or may not be a bad thing, but the problem is when we are working more than is necessary/optimal. In the future, we very likely will be able to increase the efficiency of our work—decreasing the time required to work.
1.4.11.7. Pro: We define work in this discussion as a negative, but there are millions of options for "work" that are not only positive, but enjoyable.
1.4.11.7.1. Pro: Working in the creative or medical fields, teachers and professions dealing with children report [higher than average satisfaction with their jobs](https://www.usatoday.com/story/money/personalfinance/2015/03/08/cheat-sheet-happiest-jobs/24509095/). It boils down to liking what you do. Many people do things they don't like, so "work" becomes a pejorative, instead of the calling or passion that many people see it as.
1.4.11.7.1.1. Con: A UBI would not prevent people from taking jobs they find personally satisfying, and would in fact facilitate them to as they would not have to spend time and energy on jobs which are not satisfying or enjoyable to them, allowing them to devote this time and energy to training, searching for, and engaging in work which is. This would also increase worker engagement and effectiveness.
1.4.11.7.2. Con: -> See 1.4.11.7.1.1.
1.4.12. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.2.5.1.
1.4.13. Con: Only some groups of people actually need to work less — the overworked, those of ill health,  etc.
1.4.13.1. Con: Being able to work less is valuable to all people, regardless of need. Even if they do not take the opportunity, it works as a bargaining chip.
1.4.13.1.1. Con: What is worth for people is not for sure what is needed. If nobody would need less work, then they all would work same amount, so it would be nearly no change; or some would work less out of laziness or some other unimportant motivation and total production would suffer unnecessarily.
1.4.13.2. Con: The opinion exists that people are overworked in general in modern society.
1.4.13.2.1. Pro: The [United States](https://20somethingfinance.com/american-hours-worked-productivity-vacation/) often ranks the most overworked developed country in the world.
1.4.14. Pro: A UBI is the best way that we can arrive at the post-scarcity economy that advancing technologies have been promising since the dawn of the industrial revolution. If you want a future in which the average work day gets shorter because we have automation to do the work for us, you want UBI.
1.4.14.1. Pro: A UBI helps individuals to cope with the consequences of the [next industrial revolution](http://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2016/09/the-next-industrial-revolution/498779/) \(automation\) which could obliterate millions of jobs.
1.4.14.1.1. Pro: It has been estimated that [47% of US jobs are at risk of automation](http://www.oxfordmartin.ox.ac.uk/downloads/academic/The_Future_of_Employment.pdf) in the next 20 years.
1.4.14.1.1.1. Pro: In 2016, 45% of [McDonalds](https://www.thestreet.com/story/13511123/4/mcdonald-s-rolling-out-self-ordering-kiosks-in-europe-will-u-s-be-next.html) restaurants in Europe rolled out in-store kiosks to automate the ordering process. While convenient, the kiosks take the place of jobs with raising minimum wage costs.
1.4.14.1.1.2. Pro: Even creative domains are at risk of being automated.
1.4.14.1.1.2.1. Pro: There is [software](https://www.wired.com/2015/10/this-news-writing-bot-is-now-free-for-everyone/) that generates texts and stories and it is already used by the Associated Press to cover [sports](https://www.poynter.org/2016/the-associated-press-will-use-automated-writing-to-cover-the-minor-leagues/419489/).
1.4.14.1.1.2.2. Pro: A supercomputer successfully created a totally AI based [movie trailer](https://phys.org/news/2016-09-supercomputer-world-ai-created-trailer.html).
1.4.14.1.1.2.3. Con: The current methods for creating art using an AI are based on pattern and rule recognition followed by recombination of preexisting data. Artificial Intelligence currently has no idea of context and cannot create art without specific human direction.
1.4.14.1.1.3. Pro: Fast spreading new [technologies](https://www.ft.com/content/dec677c0-b7e6-11e6-ba85-95d1533d9a62) like advanced robotics or 3D printing will obviate millions of low-skilled jobs.
1.4.14.1.1.4. Pro: In the [medical sector](http://www.economist.com/news/special-report/21700758-will-smarter-machines-cause-mass-unemployment-automation-and-anxiety) deep-learning systems are already more accurate than humans in classifying malignant tumors from CT scans, or wrist fractures from X-ray scans.
1.4.14.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.2.1.1.
1.4.14.1.1.6. Pro: A new software by JPMorgan \([COIN](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-02-28/jpmorgan-marshals-an-army-of-developers-to-automate-high-finance?cmpid=socialflow-facebook-business&utm_content=business&utm_campaign=socialflow-organic&utm_source=facebook&utm_medium=social)\) for Contract Intelligence is able to replace whole teams of lawyers and loan officers, interpreting commercial-loan agreements faster and with fewer errors.
1.4.14.1.1.7. Con: [Very few occupations—less than 5 percent—are candidates for full automation today.](https://www.mckinsey.com/~/media/McKinsey/Global%20Themes/Digital%20Disruption/Harnessing%20automation%20for%20a%20future%20that%20works/A-future-that-works-Full-report-MGI-January-2017.ashx) A surplus of human labor is much less likely to occur than a deficit of human labor, unless automation is deployed widely.
1.4.14.1.1.8. Con: The Frey and Osborne paper ["is a set of guesses with lots of padding to increase the appearance of 'scientific precision'.”](https://robotenomics.com/2016/07/28/new-research-fears-of-technological-change-destroying-jobs-may-be-overstated/) Researchers at the OECD analyzed the Frey and Osborne study and conducted their own research on tasks and jobs and concluded that: “automation was unlikely to destroy large numbers of jobs.”
1.4.14.1.1.9. Pro: ATMs are substituting bank employees worldwide, the same as Home-banking is: nearly all done via technology.
1.4.14.1.1.10. Pro: It is estimated that [4 million jobs](http://www.marketwatch.com/story/hello-self-driving-cars-goodbye-41-million-jobs-2016-09-15) in the US will be replaced by self-driving cars over the next decade.
1.4.14.1.2. Pro: As many as [45 percent](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) of the activities individuals are paid to perform can already be automated by adapting current technologies.
1.4.14.1.2.1. Con: Fewer than [5 percent](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) of occupations can be entirely automated using current technology. Thus it is likely that positions will not entirely disappear, only the tasks and activities humans perform will change.
1.4.14.1.2.1.1. Con: Technological progress occurs rapidly and what is not possible today could very well be possible in a few years.
1.4.14.1.2.1.2. Con: As human-performed activities will be reduced, jobs will transform from full-time to part-time. This will result in lower wages and fewer benefits.
1.4.14.1.2.1.2.1. Pro: About 60% of all occupations could see 30% or more of their constituent [activities automated](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/where-machines-could-replace-humans-and-where-they-cant-yet) with today's technologies.
1.4.14.1.2.1.3. Con: Occupations do not have to be entirely automatable to result in significant job losses. An occupation that is, for example, 60% automatable, is likely to result in employers reducing their staff in that occupation by 60% and reallocating the unautomatable work accordingly.
1.4.14.1.3. Con: Technological innovations automatically cause [compensation effects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technological_unemployment#Compensation_effects) that strongly mitigate their consequences.
1.4.14.1.3.1. Pro: In the past, [new technologies](http://www.economist.com/news/special-report/21700758-will-smarter-machines-cause-mass-unemployment-automation-and-anxiety) have always created more jobs than they have destroyed.
1.4.14.1.3.1.1. Pro: New technologies lead to increased productivity which in turn creates more wealth, cheaper goods, greater spending power, and ultimately more jobs.
1.4.14.1.3.1.1.1. Con: Whilst this is true, in the US, for example, average incomes have been relatively stagnant for the last 30 years, whilst the National wealth has increased significantly. Wealth creation has always been transferred to the already wealthy, with little benefit for the middle, and working class.
1.4.14.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: This is untrue. From the mid 1800's until the mid 70s [productivity grew in line with real term wages.](http://www.outsidethebeltway.com/what-happened-to-the-wage-and-productivity-link/)
1.4.14.1.3.1.2. Con: [Automation](http://www.forbes.com/sites/eriksherman/2016/12/17/automation-has-created-more-jobs-in-the-past-but-will-it-now/#55efab0627fd) will not create new jobs like other technologies did.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: The loss of jobs due to automation will dramatically reduce social mobility as most individuals' skills will be rendered obsolete — particularly those who perform menial work.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1. Pro: [Automation](http://www.businessinsider.de/stephen-hawking-ai-automation-middle-class-jobs-most-dangerous-moment-humanity-2016-12?r=UK&IR=T) will accelerate the already widening economic inequality around the world as very small groups of individuals can make enormous profits while employing very few people.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Blaming technological change for inequality absolves the elites of their responsibility. Technology transforms production, but [power and politics determine](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/may/16/universal-basic-income-equality-tech-silicon-valley) how the dividends are distributed.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1.2. Pro: As automation replaces human workers, it's likely something like this will be used to create a new serf class, and a new land owner nobility, essentially repeating the middle ages. In other words, as more people are unemployed, the government will use UBI to prevent uprising and civil unrest against the economic elite. This is very likely, but it is not at all a good thing.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1.3. Pro: The exponentially accelerating progress in robotics and AI will inevitably result \(as we're starting to see already\) in the elimination of essentially all jobs and corporate shareholders reaping all the benefits. There are only two possible outcomes:  either we tax the corporate profits resulting from automation to provide a UBI, or there will be blood in the streets.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1.4. Pro: An UBI can unleash the creative forces once the need for basic survival imperatives and creature comforts are removed. An infrastructure must be created to capitalize and support on creative, innovative surge in new paradigms. Industrial monopolies would represent a danger to this system.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.2. Pro: A storybook "[rags-to-riches](http://www.businessinsider.com/rags-to-riches-stories-2011-11)" career will become next to impossible to achieve.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.3. Pro: This is due to the production of digital technologies that [eliminate the need](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/531726/technology-and-inequality/) for many middle-class jobs.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.2. Pro: Experts predict a "[jobless future](http://www.economist.com/news/special-report/21700758-will-smarter-machines-cause-mass-unemployment-automation-and-anxiety)" as most jobs can be broken down into a series of routine tasks, more and more of which can be done by machines.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.2.1. Pro: According to [Stephen Hawking](http://www.bbc.com/news/technology-30290540), full artificial intelligence could supplant the human race.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.2.2. Con: The fact that a computer can do something better than a human being doesn’t mean that the computer will replace the human being. The market will determine whether it is economical to do so, given the costs and perceived benefits.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.2.3. Con: When machines replace one kind of human capability,[new human experiences and capabilities emerge.](https://www.forbes.com/sites/stevedenning/2015/06/04/the-robots-are-not-coming/#574dc6131913) These might be mostly higher value and offer more interesting work than the experiences and capabilities that have been replaced by machines.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.3. Pro: The idea of automation and [artificial intelligence](http://www.forbes.com/sites/eriksherman/2016/12/17/automation-has-created-more-jobs-in-the-past-but-will-it-now/2/#33a8b61b23ae) is different than other technological inventions before as it merges the existing ideas of machinery, electricity and computing to something entirely new.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.4. Pro: General artificial intelligence will be able to learn at a faster rate than humans.
1.4.14.1.3.1.2.5. Con: Experts suggest that automation \(and AI\) will create more jobs than it will replace.  [www.cnbc.com](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/06/16/ai-robots-jobs-alphabet-eric-schmidt.html)
1.4.14.1.3.2. Con: However, some [economists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technological_unemployment#Compensation_effects) have concluded that those compensation effects are reduced or, at least, need to be studied [further](http://ftp.iza.org/dp6291.pdf) due to new technologies such as computerization.
1.4.14.1.3.3. Pro: In Germany, there is a huge number of robot-intensive industries and a high degree of automation. However, this does not negatively affect [total employment](http://voxeu.org/article/rise-robots-german-labour-market).
1.4.14.1.3.3.1. Con: Although total employment has not been intensively negatively affected, [roughly 275,000](http://voxeu.org/article/rise-robots-german-labour-market) full-time manufacturing jobs have been replaced by robots in the period 1994-2014.
1.4.14.1.3.3.1.1. Con: As those significant losses in employment could be offset by job gains outside manufacturing, robots and automation only have changed the employment market's[composition](http://voxeu.org/article/rise-robots-german-labour-market), but not the total employment rate.
1.4.14.1.3.4. Pro: New jobs always replace old. We’ll need automation maintenance & improvement, and emerging resource production \(such as algenol farms and silicon & lithium mining\).
1.4.14.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.14.1.3.1.2.1.1.
1.4.14.1.5. Con: The loss of a fulfilling job cannot be compensated for by UBI payments.
1.4.14.1.5.1. Con: Having more free time enables people to instead have a fulfilling hobby they can choose more freely than a job.
1.4.14.1.5.2. Pro: People relying on external motivation to feel fulfilled where monetary  gain is an important factor might not feel as fulfilled if they no longer need this mechanic.
1.4.14.1.5.3. Con: The loss of a fulfilling job also cannot be compensated for by living below the poverty line.
1.4.14.1.5.3.1. Con: The idea that we need a job to be fulfilled, that we are not complete without one, is toxic on the face of it. It creates the internal, and external idea that the unemployed are bad people, and that retired people are incomplete, both obviously absurd, and harmful ideas. This idea is horrible, and we should do what we can to eliminate it.
1.4.14.1.5.3.1.1. Pro: This further leads to any number of insecurities and causes of depression, as well as furthering judgmentalism.
1.4.14.1.5.4. Con: Only [around half of jobs are satisfying](https://www.statista.com/statistics/224508/employee-job-satisfaction-worldwide/) so a UBI would relieve people of unsatisfying activities.
1.4.14.1.6. Pro: Automation and the increased use of technology [affects](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) the whole socio-economic spectrum from low to higher-income professions.
1.4.14.1.6.1. Pro: UBI will become increasingly necessary as advances in technology render larger and larger portions of the population to be unemployable.  UBI would help us retain societal order, rather than mass unemployment causing chaos. [Humans Need Not Apply - CGP Grey](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Pq-S557XQU)
1.4.14.1.6.2. Con: Policy acceptance is more dependent on perceived vulnerability than actual vulnerability. Insofar as most higher-income professionals do not view themselves at risk, they are therefore still unlikely to support a policy that they see as irrelevant to their interests.
1.4.14.1.6.3. Con: Executive-level jobs tend to rely on more transferable general management and administrative skills. It may be true that single-industry workers are most at risk of obsolescence if their factory closes down whereas professionals, managers, executives and technical occupations have comparatively less trouble transferring to a laterally-related field.
1.4.14.1.7. Con: The belief that machines and automation on net balance will create unemployment is an "economic delusion" \(see Chapter 7 on [mises.org](https://mises.org/library/economics-one-lesson)\).
1.4.14.1.7.1. Con: Our current society generously rewards the insights and creativity of intelligent specialists.
If machines become more intelligent, draw insights from an infinitely broader dataset of field specialized knowledge and offer just as creative solutions at a faster pace and a cheaper rate; then society will less generously reward the outperformed traditional specialists.
1.4.14.1.7.2. Con: The quoted source focuses on mechanical machines \(which reduce repetitive manual labour\), leaving out artificial intelligence \(which reduces routine and even non-routine cognitive labour\).
1.4.14.1.7.3. Con: The author passed away in 1993 while even many contemporary economists don't understand the implications of a machine defeating world champion go players. It would be very hard for an economist to lay down principles that apply to our ever faster changing economy without having witnessed the mass adoption of cell-phones nor algorithms dominating stock exchanges.
1.4.14.1.8. Pro: With experts predicting 47% unemployment by 2040, there is no way for the law of creative destruction to catch up. The great depression hit its height at 30%, and UBIs are the only feasible solution to keep money flowing through the economy while we adapt.
1.4.14.1.9. Con: [Creative destruction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creative_destruction) is a natural economic transition; for example the invention and mass production of cars destroyed the horse and cart industry. Creative destruction forces people to refocus their skills elsewhere, thus encouraging innovation.
1.4.14.1.9.1. Con: Creative destruction of jobs is currently occurring \(e g. fossil fuels and auto industries\); however workers are turning to conservative politicians to keep their jobs open instead of moving to another industry or going back to school for new job training.
1.4.14.1.10. Con: It is too early to predict whether automation will actually take enough jobs that the economy couldn't support normal employment.
1.4.14.1.10.1. Con: Though we can't predict it with complete accuracy, there are observable patterns to follow. Not only can we vaguely see where technology is headed, we can observe the attitudes of various employers to new development to determine how employment is likely to rise or fall.
1.4.14.1.10.2. Pro: A future of massive job automation/displacement \(where new jobs aren't created, it's futile to retrain displaced workers, and indefinite unemployment benefits are their only hope to get by\) is extremely speculative/hypothetical at this stage. We should instead focus on optimizing our education system for the 21st century, so everyone \(all ages, all social classes\) have the opportunity to acquire in-demand skills...if jobs do become scarce, the "haves" must face fierce competition for those jobs
1.4.14.1.11. Pro: People without the high cognitive abilities \(e.g. because of their genetics\) required to do the remaining jobs will not be able to make a living.
1.4.14.1.12. Pro: UBI will balance out the missing consumption part of increased automation \(sadly robots do not have to buy anything\). Most western nations are heavily dependent on personal consumption for their GDP \(70% of US GDP comes from personal consumption\). UBI can provide for the missing consumption for the unemployed labor pool, while taxing owners of robots.
1.4.14.1.12.1. Pro: AI and automation are replacing jobs at a growing rate, leaving millions of capable people jobless with no alternatives. Even menial service jobs are becoming automated. A UBI would not only ensure the survival of the poor, it may become the only way to infuse cash into a high-unemployment economy.
1.4.14.1.13. Pro: As more labor is automated, there may be less work available to do for all, including many who want to work. Since the availability of paying jobs are unfairly distributed, the claim that those who are able to work are unfairly burdened with the needs of others is morally suspect. At the very least, the burden falls as much upon manufacturers who refuse to employ humans vs superhuman machines as it does on those who cannot find work.
1.4.14.1.13.1. Pro: In the near future, automation will increasingly displace human workforces. If the world still operates on a monetary system, these people will still need money, but jobs will be unavailable beyond automation maintenance and programming; everyone cannot do this, and everyone is not needed to do this. Thus, a UBI would allow economies to continue churning, while acknowledging the work done by automation on the human worker's behalf.
1.4.14.1.14. Pro: We can't predict the rate with which technologies such as AI could [evolve](https://deepmind.com/blog/alphago-zero-learning-scratch/). Having a UBI in place as soon as possible can contribute in having well prepared societies for that eventuality.
1.4.14.1.15. Pro: UBI democratizes the profits of automation.
1.4.14.1.16. Pro: UBI can address the negative impact of "creative destruction".  As new technologies displace older firms and their workers, income inequality between lower-educated and higher educated workers will grow with no short-term market solution.  UBI can remediate this growing inequality while preserving market-based incentives to pursue education and other means to greater productivity, if UBI is set high enough for basic subsistence and low enough to be unattractive as a stand-alone income.
1.4.14.1.17. Con: UBI ends up being duct tape for capitalism by mildly redistributing wealth in such a way as to render those with substandard living conditions complacent. It is like a robot tax, delaying the inevitable.
1.4.14.1.17.1. Con: The notion that capitalism is a major source of poverty is unproven. For rankings of present-day governments regarding degrees of political freedom and economic prosperity, see “The Human Freedom Index, 2016,” [object.cato.org](https://object.cato.org/sites/cato.org/files/human-freedom-index-files/human-freedom-index-2016.pdf). Figure 7 shows Average GDP per Capita by HFI Quartiles \(Human Freedom Index\). And Figure 1, 2 show correlation between personal freedom and economic freedom.
1.4.14.1.18. Pro: A UBI would allow labor forces unemployed due to structural changes, e.g. technological improvement, to cope with the physical and mental transition in finding a new remunerating occupation/job.
1.4.14.1.18.1. Con: Existing welfare systems already do this in most countries.
1.4.14.2. Pro: A UBI will decrease the need to keep obsolete jobs open which can result in an improved focus on technology. For example: machines can take over a position with zero hardship to the employee losing said job. The UBI removes fear of \(and indeed financial impact of\) losing a position to technological improvements.
1.4.14.2.1. Con: UBI wouldn't remove *all* financial impacts of losing a job; presumably, even with UBI we still earn more with a job than without.
1.4.14.2.2. Con: The worth of having a job is not limited to mere financial aspects. There is a huge moral and psychological boost from having employment.
1.4.14.3. Pro: UBI is a logical outcome in a world with advanced and generalized robots, 3d printers, and solar panels allowing for nearly nearly free, widespread work and energy. Heretofore humans have been responsible for all goods and services made and transported. With machines, our productivity has exploded while costs for basic goods have dropped.
1.4.14.4. Pro: UBI is the only proposed solution to a near future where a large number of people can work and want to work but there is nothing economically useful for them to do.
1.4.14.5. Con: This is a Malthusian fallacy, claimed during previous economic changes, and giving rise to the Luddite movement that wrecked mills because it was putting weavers out of work. In fact what happened was people ended up doing jobs that simply did not exist, while benefiting from lower cost output.
1.4.15. Con: The right way to create an environment where people work less is by shortening the amount of hours required to work at a full-time position.
1.4.15.1. Con: Reducing hours without increasing wages \(or alternate income streams\) will simply increase poverty, inequality, and/or the prevalence of working multiple jobs.
1.5. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.12.1.
1.6. Pro: A UBI reduces inequalities and enhances social mobility.
1.6.1. Pro: Government-funded research and education is a key driver of innovation, yet its returns mostly accrue to the owners of capital. By helping to circulate wealth from the rich to the lower and middle classes, a UBI helps recapture this public investment for the public itself.
1.6.1.1. Con: Those who are wealthy already benefit from technological advancement as they are the ones who own the means of production. The government does not also need to provide them with a UBI, that is just double dipping.
1.6.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.3.
1.6.1.1.2. Con: The money paid out to the wealthy through a UBI will be offset through increases in progressive taxes.
1.6.1.1.2.1. Pro: A UBI can be combined with shifting taxing schemes from income-based taxes towards capital-based taxes, which would increase the liquidity of money and ensure that capital has a reasonable return rate while also forcing those who own capital to invest it in value-creating activities instead of just "sitting on it".
1.6.2. Pro: A hypothetical $12k UBI if implemented in the US would be [enough to reduce its Gini index](http://www.scottsantens.com/does-basic-income-reduce-income-inequality-gini) \(a measure of income inequality\) from 0.6 to 0.46; bringing it on par with that of the Nordic states.
1.6.2.1. Pro: A $12k per year UBI would cover a large portion of my survival expenses freeing up the time I presently use working to meet my basic needs, to do other more effective and self-actualizing things.
1.6.3. Pro: Unlike current welfare schemes, which only benefit the most disadvantaged in society, a UBI will benefit almost everyone by taxing those who have accumulated large amounts of wealth over a long period of time.
1.6.3.1. Pro: The income gap continues to increase between the wealthiest 1% and the rest of the population. A UBI would not only help close the gap, but would also all but eliminate the constant debate of a liveable minimum wage we've been having for over a decade.
1.6.3.1.1. Con: Resource distribution in society will always be unequal. A UBI or any other welfare scheme does not alter this fundamental reality.
1.6.3.1.1.1. Con: A UBI may not eliminate inequality \(and total equality may not even be desirable\) but it will help to prevent inequality from reaching levels which create suffering, and create a safety net for those on the "short end" of the "inequality stick".
1.6.3.2. Pro: Rawls vs. Nozick rears it’s head again! Intellectualism lost the argument to populist politics. Fundamentally though, any sane, right thinking person would support John Rawls philosophy of Distributive Justice. Rawls suggested that vast wealth accumulated by a small minority should be redistributed; after all the majority likely created it for the minority. As we head towards greater mechanisation via AI and more people loose their jobs, the vastly wealthy minority have a moral obligation.
1.6.3.3. Pro: Universal benefits such as child benefits and pensions have strong support among the populace \([Allahyar, p. 18](https://www.academia.edu/8447710/Universal_Basic_Income)\) due to their universality. Therefore it is fair to expect similarly strong support for a UBI.
1.6.4. Pro: Most advocates of a UBI emphasize that the fundamental basis of a UBI is equality in terms of opportunity, saying that “each person should have equal opportunity to pursue a conception of the good life” \([Allahyar, p. 8](https://www.academia.edu/8447710/Universal_Basic_Income)\). This would decrease inequality, rather than increase it.
1.6.4.1. Pro: It is difficult for the poor to improve their position without a basic safety net that provides reliable access to education and income. A UBI will provide this and help close the gap in equality of opportunity between the rich and the poor.
1.6.4.2. Pro: Experiments with UBIs [showed](http://qz.com/765902/ubi-wouldnt-mean-everyone-quits-working/) that teenagers put off getting part-time jobs to focus on school, leading to a noticeable decline in high school dropout rates in Dauphin, Canada, and to double-digit increases in high school completion among participating families in New Jersey, Seattle, and Denver.
1.6.4.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.17.2.1.
1.6.4.2.2. Con: [Youth allowance](https://www.humanservices.gov.au/customer/services/centrelink/youth-allowance) fixes this same problem in a more targeted and efficient way.
1.6.4.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.6.4.4. Pro: Equality is by its nature unconditional and recognized as a [human right](https://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/).
1.6.4.4.1. Con: No benefit can be guaranteed unconditionally to some without enforcing an unconditional obligation on others. Before distributing money as a UBI, all the money must first be collected, plus the overhead required to complete the redistribution process. When the cost of enforcing the transfer is included, UBI is negative sum and constitutes Legal Plunder, as defined by French economist Frederic Bastiat in his 1850 work, [The Law](https://mises.org/library/law).
1.6.4.4.1.1. Con: This assumes that all the production comes from human activities. This is already changing with automation and artificial intelligence, which would push most of the income toward activities which are not performed by humans and therefore removing one side of the equation defined by Bastiat.
1.6.4.4.1.2. Con: The idea that the UBI is always a negative sum does not take into account the fact that people with less money spend most if it, therefore it goes back into the economy. In contrast, much of the money from the top 1% is not put back into the economy since it is saved offshore, used to speculate in stock markets \(not natural market forces\) and to unfairly influence governments.
1.6.5. Pro: Capitalism has a tendency to lead to the accumulation of capital into the hands of a few \([Piketty, 2013](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_in_the_Twenty-First_Century)\) leading to an inevitable stratification of income levels and the collapse of the middle class. A UBI is a way to redistribute wealth, stimulating consumer demand and businesses.
1.6.5.1. Con: UBIs are akin to medieval indulgences, but for modern elites. They're meant to tamp down support for more fundamental reforms like land redistribution, or like reformulating the economic system to reward something other than immediate self-interest. Previous welfarist proposals have been lauded by reactionaries as "[saving capitalism](https://www.hoover.org/research/how-fdr-saved-capitalism)". UBIs work the exact same way.
1.6.5.2. Con: Capital accumulation is not reduced by a UBI. By increasing [reservation wages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reservation_wage) and reducing the desirability of hiring workers, capitalists gain a justification to leave workers out of the market.
1.6.5.2.1. Con: A major redistribution between the population translates into a reduction of capital.
1.6.5.2.2. Con: By increasing the lowest wage which workers would accept, employers will expect workers to be highly competent and rigorous, stimulating work efficiency.
1.6.5.2.3. Con: Workers still need to be hired to fuel capitalism, so they cannot be left out of the market.
1.6.5.3. Con: Inequality can be reduced in less disruptive ways, such as by encouraging [inclusive growth](http://siteresources.worldbank.org/INTDEBTDEPT/Resources/468980-1218567884549/WhatIsInclusiveGrowth20081230.pdf) and economic participation.
1.6.5.3.1. Con: Income inequality being reduced in less disruptive ways does not solve for the root cause of inequality, but it rather runs the risk to lead to worse conditions where individuals can still use income inequality to oppress individuals that have a lower standard of living.
1.6.5.4. Con: The effect of stimulating consumer demand is mitigated by the extent to which prices are raised by businesses.
1.6.5.5. Pro: Consumer demand isn't stimulated when individuals do not have the autonomy to spend how they wish. With a UBI, individuals can finally choose what to invest in.
1.6.5.6. Pro: When robots take over the jobs, people will still need money to buy the products robots are making. A basic income is a great idea because people have more free time to start a business or become an artist or what ever and start earning more money with the things they like to do in life, this way more money will be created for the government by paying taxes on the extra income they create!
1.6.5.7. Pro: The profit employers make by effectivization tends to go to people with an already high standard of living rather than employees which are in more in need of the profit - which could be implanted through fewer working hours with sustained monthly wage. The free market's inability to deal with this calls for wealth-distribution measures, such as a UBI.
1.6.5.8. Pro: UBI allows those, who previously had no capital, to participate in capitalist endeavours of enterprise. This is preferable than being excluded from it and forced to rely on others because of their lack of opportunity.
1.6.6. Pro: Property rights and capital ownership are community assets. Individuals with no property or capital need to be compensated by those who are hoarding these assets.
1.6.6.1. Pro: UBI mediates between the natural right of every human to a part of the earth and its natural resources and the liberal concept of individual ownership of those goods.
1.6.6.1.1. Pro: A UBI is a small step towards remedying the exceedingly imbalanced distribution of wealth generated with Earth's resources.
1.6.6.2. Con: Property rights are community assets but capital and property itself are not community assets. Control of natural resources \(e.g. land, water, spectrum and air\) belongs to the entire society which then sells temporary rights for exclusive use to individuals. Private property is a natural, inalienable right that is not granted by society. Everyone has at least some property; their body. We trade our labor to acquire more property. Our property is not a community asset unless we are slaves.
1.6.6.3. Pro: A UBI is not the redistribution of income from those who earned it to those who did not. A UBI is the redistribution of accumulated wealth from those whose ancestors hoarded it to those whose ancestors have provided the labor and intellectual capital which has created that wealth seance the end of the stone age. A UBI is not a handout, it is inheritance from all of your ancestors for 10,000 years.
1.6.6.4. Con: There is nothing communal about property rights. Only individuals can own things. Even corporations and the government are reduced to individuals when presented as legal entities.
1.6.6.4.1. Con: While it's true that only individuals can own things, property rights are not "things" by any common definition of the word. For example, an individual might hold a copyright on a book but it's a temporary right granted for a limited time by society. Nobody can actually own the story because an idea is not a "thing". Likewise real estate rights are not things. They are a temporary right granted by society. If there is no community, then there are no property rights, there's just property.
1.6.7. Pro: UBI is possibly the most cost effective solution for addressing homelessness, poverty, and crime as the overall cost is less than the cost of policing, medical care and lack of opportunities induced by poverty.
1.6.7.1. Con: Universal Basic Income would not completely address economic inequality because it would not change the fact that at least some higher end services and goods would be too expensive for somebody on such income to afford such as private health insurance.
1.6.7.1.1. Con: Universal health care would not require a health insurance for basic health services.
1.6.7.1.2. Con: The desire to have access to luxury products and higher end services are still good incentives for work.
1.6.7.2. Pro: A UBI is a proactive approach to preventing abject poverty in the population, and proactive approaches are typically superior to reactive approaches. Studies have demonstrated significant anti-poverty benefits from the implementation of a UBI.
1.6.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.13.3.
1.6.7.4. Pro: A UBI would enable those who have lost their jobs to recover more easily and allow them a better quality of life.
1.6.7.5. Pro: An UBI prevents a major rise in poverty in times of financial crisis.
1.6.8. Pro: Only a UBI can guarantee that every person is provided the basic amount of resources needed to survive.
1.6.8.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.6.8.2. Con: Alternatively, basic resources could be provided in-kind \(government-issued food, health care, shelter\). A UBI is not the only way to accomplish that aim.
1.6.8.2.1. Con: To be provided in-kind by the government would require a much higher cost due to the increased bureaucracy than simply giving people the money to do this themselves.
1.6.8.2.2. Con: This would limit the services able to be accessed by lower income people to the government supplied and would remove the free market advantages and personal choice
1.6.8.2.3. Con: This would provide a similar stigma to current government programs such as 'food stamps'
1.6.8.3. Pro: Society expects individuals to be clothed, fed and housed. These cost money. It is preposterous to expect individuals to clothe, feed and house themselves without providing the means to do it.
1.6.8.4. Pro: A UBI provides security, allowing people to focus on long-term goals instead of their next meal.
1.6.8.4.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.6.8.5. Con: Each person's basic amount of resources needed can vary, especially when it comes to healthcare. A person may still be precluded from required, costly medical treatments if the UBI is not high enough.
1.6.8.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.5.5.
1.6.8.5.2. Con: In many developed countries, healthcare is free at the point of need. In others, UBI would enable more people to afford health insurance. Even though UBI will not solve every problem, it may still be much more effective than current systems.
1.6.8.5.3. Con: The UBI money would still provide part of the healthcare cost, thereby making the rest more affordable. It would be much better than nothing.
1.6.8.6. Con: Supplying basic resources does not guarantee happiness or contentment.
1.6.8.6.1. Con: True, but a lack of basic resources guarantees ill health and intense stress that prevents someone from persuing the endeavours that do make happiness and contentment more likely.  See [Maslow's heirarchy of needs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs#Hierarchy).
1.6.8.7. Pro: Universal Basic Income would allow everyone the opportunity to survive regardless of their ability to work and/or skills, training, etc.
1.6.8.7.1. Pro: Without work for income, one must resort to begging or theft to survive. If one is unable to even do those things, they would surely perish.
1.6.8.8. Pro: Capitalism is predatory by nature and requires poverty. One cannot be on top if there is no one at the bottom.
1.6.8.8.1. Pro: The US is the richest nation yet we allow resources to end up in dumpsters rather than give them to those who need them to survive. Further, waste is necessary in order to control the costs of production and market value.
1.6.8.8.2. Pro: When citizens have more money than can possibly be spent in several lifetimes, ending poverty is a question of morality, not resources.
1.6.8.9. Con: A UBI does not guarantee that for every person; only every citizen. The aim of welfare should be to provide every resident, including non-citizens, with the means to fulfill their basic needs.
1.6.8.10. Pro: UBI frees individuals from the constant worry of keeping their heads above water. This liberation from survival-mode encourages positive economic education, familial stability and increased joy. The psychic costs of a lifetime's survival  must be accounted for, and remedied.
1.6.8.10.1. Pro: It also encourages increased perspective of - and contribution to - the bigger picture and bigger-picture issues.
1.6.8.11. Pro: A UBI will give the most vulnerable and disadvantaged people in society a little bit of housing security, which many have never had.
1.6.8.11.1. Pro: We are in "winner takes all" societies. Top performers capture a disproportionate share of the value created. In many professions, such as those related to arts, sports, or entertainment, there is a long tail of people which can't afford basic needs such as housing.
1.6.8.12. Pro: According to the Center For Poverty Research UC Davis \([poverty.ucdavis.edu](https://poverty.ucdavis.edu/faq/what-current-poverty-rate-united-states)\) the poverty rate in the U.S between 1959 to present day fluctuates between 22%-11%. This constitutes millions of people struggling to eat and live. If we had a UBI in place people would ultimately be inclined to focus on other social issues instead of the struggle to 'make ends meet.' Financial responsibilities would be easier to accomplish. Citizens would have the ability to concentrate on building better lives.
1.6.8.13. Pro: Every human belongs to the same species \(Homo Sapiens\), so they have the same moral rights and one of these is the right to survive.
1.6.8.14. Pro: There is also little dignity in sleeping on the street or subsisting on trash, which is what at-risk populations are increasingly being driven to as the wealth gap increases.
1.6.8.14.1. Con: This is more an argument for voluntary charity or means-tested social programs as it is for UBI, because extreme cases of poverty are relatively rare, and could be better dealt with programs aimed at that specific situation.
1.6.9. Con: -> See 1.6.7.1.
1.6.10. Con: People don't want to create financial equality for all \(as a UBI would provide\) but instead financial fairness, [where their efforts are rewarded and opportunities are accessible](https://www.theatlantic.com/science/archive/2015/10/people-dont-actually-want-equality/411784/).
1.6.10.1. Con: UBI would not create financial equality for all, as those who worked for pay or sold desired goods, ideas, or services could earn money above and beyond the provision of the UBI. This would also allow for efforts to be rewarded and opportunities to be accessible.
1.6.11. Con: UBI merely addresses some of the harms of inequality \(people being on low absolute incomes\) rather than addressing the structural causes of inequality.
1.6.11.1. Pro: UBI adresses the symptoms of inequality, but not the root cause: poor education.
1.6.11.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.
1.6.11.1.2. Con: It is extremely presumptuous and probably inaccurate to definitely assign the root cause of inequality to one factor. If the problem were that simple, it would already be solved, or at least solutions would be in the process of being implemented.
1.6.11.1.3. Pro: UBI will not solve people making bad financial decisions. Financial education being taught in schools could resolve this issue. UBI could complement the education with practice. However people do not need money to make better financial decisions. They could instead figure out ways to save money.
1.6.11.2. Pro: A UBI reduces the political will needed to continue other welfare programs.
1.6.11.2.1. Con: Political events such as economic crises or right-leaning governments are inevitable and mean that the levels of welfare provided often follow a feast/famine cycle. Given this, a UBI provides a circuit-breaker solution that shores up some minimum level of support even as political will for welfare flucates over time.
1.6.11.2.1.1. Pro: When existing welfare programs are cut or restructured, having a UBI in place will help tide over those affected until the new programs are implemented.
1.6.11.2.1.2. Con: A UBI can also be dismantled or reduced. This is especially problematic because a UBI would be, by far, the biggest welfare program.
1.6.11.2.1.3. Con: Once established, a UBI changes the structure of the economy and societal psychology, and therefore is a commitment. If then, at any point, some serious crisis pushes the government to cut the UBI, it may be a deadly twist for the economy and people.
1.6.11.2.1.3.1. Con: This is true, but at the same time the changes to the economy and societal psychology could equally push people to care more about each other's welfare, be more interconnected, and respond better and with a more united front to any given crisis.
1.6.11.2.1.3.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.6.1.
1.6.11.2.1.4. Con: Unlike most welfare programs, UBI is easily reduced, altered, or removed entirely. All it would take to slash benefits for millions is altering a few numbers in a law code.
1.6.11.2.2. Pro: The all-encompassing and comprehensive nature of UBIs are unlike any other form of welfare \(which is much more targeted and small scale\). Given this, a UBI is likely to be seen as a complete solution to poverty and a replacement for current welfare policy.
1.6.11.2.2.1. Con: It should be seen as a complete solution for poverty and a replacement for our current welfare system. After all, the former is overtly stated in the name of the proposed policy "Universal Basic Income," and the latter is a core component of the policy.
1.6.11.2.2.1.1. Con: There are some difficulties which cannot be wholly addressed purely by providing money e.g. a lack of education, mental and physical illness, etc. Targeted welfare programs addressing these areas will still be required.
1.6.11.2.2.2. Con: Such a structure either gives incentive to produce and neglect children \(cash-for-children UBI\) or fails to provide childcare aid. Other welfare aid works in tandem with a universal benefit.
1.6.11.2.3. Con: Targeted welfare programs are subject to regulatory capture by self-interested voting blocks and lobbying interests.
1.6.11.2.3.1. Pro: In contrast, because a UBI is universal, all people who receive it have both an incentive to raise it, and share in the results of any lobbying to do so.
1.6.11.2.3.2. Pro: Because the middle class is an important voting block, they receive many benefits \(tax cuts, home buying subsidies, childcare services\) not because they need them but because of political pandering.
1.6.11.2.3.3. Pro: Pension programs \(unlike welfare aimed deliberately at the poor\) are generous and nearly-always [generously indexed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Pension_\(United_Kingdom\)#Pensions_Act_2007) because of a large high-turnout voting base. Having the UBI replace the pension reorients their political capital towards the needs of the poor also.
1.6.11.2.3.4. Pro: The poor lack the money to fund successful lobbying campaigns and the numbers to form a successful voting block. As such, they have little political capital to affect the existing welfare program.
1.6.11.2.3.5. Con: Having a single payment scheme that almost all voters have a self-interest in creates a strong incentive for politicians to increase the payout amount ever-higher. This would, in the long term, create unsustainable levels of government expenditure.
1.6.11.2.3.6. Pro: Political parties have incentives to continue to give specific benefits to specific groups. This means we would be stuck with both UBI and traditional welfare schemes. As a result, UBI would be underfunded and fail to accomplish its purpose.
1.6.11.2.4. Con: The amount of welfare might decrease, but given that there is often a welfare gap that systematically excludes certain individuals even in the best versions of welfare, a UBI is a more equitable distribution of resources which maximizes overall welfare.
1.6.11.2.4.1. Pro: Even if it has more monetary weight, a UBI cuts bureaucracy costs and removes the disincentives to work that afflict many welfare systems.
1.6.11.2.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.2.4.
1.6.11.2.5. Pro: The implementation of a UBI reduces existing welfare spending by a significant amount.
1.6.11.2.5.1. Pro: People will work less, which also decreases available public revenue. This leads to a large deficit which will make cuts necessary.
1.6.11.2.5.1.1. Con: This claim assumes that public revenue is necessarily tied to income tax. Public revenue can also be raised through a consumption tax, value-added tax, carbon tax, etc... none of which would be reduced by a UBI.
1.6.11.2.5.1.1.1. Con: All taxes take from value creators a portion of the value they created, so by discouraging the creation of value, a UBI has the continuing effect of reducing its own source of funding, thus destroying itself.
1.6.11.2.5.1.2. Pro: A UBI is not sustainable because it relies for its funding on the taxing of value creation while at the same time it discourages value creation by reducing or removing the incentive to work to create value.
1.6.11.2.5.2. Con: A large portion of existing welfare is conditional cash payments. Individuals would still get most of this money, if not more, under a UBI.
1.6.11.2.5.3. Pro: In order for a state to gather the immense funds necessary for a UBI, and to avoid "double spending", it will need to cut funding for other welfare programs.
1.6.11.2.5.4. Pro: A UBI will require large amounts of political capital that will reduce the amount of political capital that will be used to support others, e.g. programs to support those with disabilities.
1.6.11.2.6. Pro: More important than a flexible income are the necessities of life: access to clean food, air, and water, a doctor when you're sick, a roof over your head and a good education. We should fight for universal access to these goods first.
1.6.11.2.6.1. Pro: This fight should take place at an individual and community level. A UBI gives these communities more to work with.
1.6.11.2.6.1.1. Pro: A UBI would help people to find the time and means to engage in activism.
1.6.11.2.7. Pro: Because instituting welfare programs requires political capital and public goodwill, a majority of which will be used instituting a UBI, it is unlikely that the general public will be sympathetic towards additional welfare programs if a UBI is implemented.
1.6.11.2.7.1. Con: Numerous additional welfare programs will ideally not be required under a UBI system. This model is intended to replace the fragmented and often inconsistent programs that states have in place currently.
1.6.11.2.7.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.5.5.
1.6.11.2.7.2. Con: A UBI appeals across the spectrum - for example, to liberals who see a duty to help those in poverty without putting unduly restrictive conditions on that help and to fiscally concerned conservatives who object to the inefficiency and bureaucracy of current welfare systems \(Milton Friedman [supported a form of UBI](http://fivethirtyeight.com/features/universal-basic-income/)\). Therefore, a UBI is unlikely to undermine sympathy for other welfare programs.
1.6.11.2.7.2.1. Con: Even though a UBI appeals to both liberals and conservatives, their reasons are mutually exclusive. Because conservatives would see a UBI as a strategy to replace most of the existing welfare state, it would likely undermine their support for other welfare programs, whereas for liberals, a UBI would supplement existing safety-net programs \(e.g. childhood education subsidies, comprehensive health insurance etc\).
1.6.11.2.7.2.2. Con: It is likely that the implementation of a UBI would be fraught with division over where the funds would come from. For example, conservatives may not want increased taxes and liberals likely would, at least compared to cuts to public services.
1.6.11.2.7.2.2.1. Con: That is also true for any budget or tax reform discussion in any given government, not something specific to a UBI.
1.6.11.2.7.2.3. Con: While many may claim to support a UBI in principle, when it comes to implementing such a policy and very real sacrifices have to be made in the budget, it is likely that these opinions will change. Decreases in funding towards institutions like education, or the military could prove very unpopular.
1.6.11.2.7.3. Pro: For those in the middle and lower classes, there is unlikely to be buy-in towards a system that also distributes money to wealthy individuals who are not in financial need.
1.6.11.2.7.3.1. Pro: Wealthy people are able to meet their basic needs already. A UBI is not necessary for them to achieve adequate housing, medical care, education, or proper nutrition as it is with middle and lower class individuals.
1.6.11.2.7.3.2. Con: -> See 1.6.1.1.2.
1.6.11.2.7.4. Pro: The public is reluctant to fund welfare because our society has deeply ingrained the capitalist idea of rewarding individuals for effort and working hard. Hard work is seen as a virtue, and something deserving of reward, and those with wealth they have not themselves earned \(for example, the rich children of billionaires\) are vilified.
1.6.11.2.7.4.1. Pro: Rich people are likely to believe that those in poverty are receiving money for nothing. Even now, with welfare usually contingent on demonstrating that one is at least looking for employment, stigma against those who "leech off welfare" without contributing is rampant.
1.6.11.2.7.4.2. Con: It tends to be poor people who do not work that are discriminated against, while rich people, including [inheritors](http://www.executivestyle.com.au/heir-style-the-rich-kids-who-have-it-all-2xbt0) and rich children, can become idols.
1.6.11.2.7.5. Pro: In Switzerland, the concept of a UBI was rejected [by close to a 3-1 margin](https://www.google.com/url?q=http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-36454060&sa=D&ust=1479913397736000&usg=AFQjCNGl2VcGG-v8Z_Qa1w8vKXvWgh59Cw), reflecting how politically unpopular a UBI is, and how much it is likely to squander political capital towards welfare if instituted.
1.6.11.2.7.5.1. Con: While Switzerland may have rejected the vote, 64% of Europeans in general were in favor of a UBI according to [a 2016 poll](https://daliaresearch.com/two-thirds-of-europeans-for-basic-income-dalia-ceo-presents-surprising-results-at-future-of-work-conference-in-zurich/).
1.6.11.2.7.5.2. Con: [A recent survey](https://futurism.com/survey-reveals-nearly-50-of-americans-favor-a-universal-basic-income/) in the US, a country known to favour free-markets and oppose government policy that could be construed as socialist, found that nearly half of Americans would support a UBI.
1.6.11.2.7.5.3. Con: As Switzerland already has the second [highest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\)_per_capita) GDP per capita, its interest in implementing a UBI is obviously lower than that of comparatively poorer countries.
1.6.11.2.7.5.4. Con: Switzerland is an economically conservative country with a relatively small welfare system. More truly liberal countries such as Finland and the Netherlands are currently piloting the program with little backlash.
1.6.11.2.7.5.4.1. Con: One concern brought to light by the [Finnish experiment](https://www.jacobinmag.com/2017/01/ubi-finland-centre-party-unemployment-jobs/) is that a person's allotment of the UBI replaces their state benefits, which are controlled by labour unions. A person may be loth to accept UBI and be blocked out of his/her union as a result.
1.6.11.2.7.5.4.1.1. Con: This a situation entirely unique to Finland.
1.6.11.2.7.5.4.2. Pro: At the outset of the Finnish UBI experiment, [over half the population was in favour](http://yle.fi/uutiset/osasto/news/study_over_half_the_population_supports_basic_income_scheme/8583547) of the scheme.
1.6.11.2.7.5.4.3. Con: In the case of Finland, this is potentially due to the fact that their pilot program is only a 'partial basic income', available only to [unemployed](http://99percentinvisible.org/episode/the-finnish-experiment/) participants, who would likely not oppose an influx of free money.
1.6.11.2.8. Pro: A UBI would almost certainly replace other welfare programs because that would be the only way to pay for it.
1.6.11.2.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.
1.6.11.2.8.2. Pro: A UBI means significant expenditure increases \(United States\) to ensure edge cases are taken care of. Based on 2015 numbers, there are ~252 million adults \(18+\) with ~$1.468 trillion in social security, unemployment, labor, food assistance and other benefits. This only leaves $5,828.37 pp/yr. The poverty line is at ~$11,770 pp. [media.nationalpriorities.org](https://media.nationalpriorities.org/uploads/budget-graphic.png)
1.6.11.2.8.2.1. Con: The parent's dollar figures seem too low. Using the same [source](https://media.nationalpriorities.org/uploads/budget-graphic.png):

$986 billion: Medicare & Health
$895 billion: Social Security
$104 billion: Food Assistance
$36 billion: Unemployment
---
$2.021 trillion: Total

Divided by the same 252 million population figure, this comes out to $8,019.94 pp/yr, substantially higher than the parent's estimate.

And it could go even higher if other spending from the source such as Housing & Community, Education and the mysterious "Other" figures are included.
1.6.11.2.8.3. Pro: A UBI might lead to restrictions on other entitlements, given the budgetary constraints it would pose to the Federal Government.
1.6.11.2.9. Con: Whilst it is true that there will be limited demand for most welfare programs, some programs without the stigma of poverty will likely still have sufficient political capital: e.g. funding for children with disabilities.
1.6.11.2.9.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.6.9.
1.6.11.2.10. Con: Given that everyone receives something under a UBI, once normalised, it may actually increase the balance of political capital for welfare. Extreme cases would likely still have public support on top of the standard of living that a UBI guarantees for everyone.
1.6.11.2.10.1. Con: This argument is premised on the notion that individuals' utility from the UBI outweighs the loss of utility from a higher tax rate and perceived infringement on their quality of life. Middle-class individuals might receive more UBI back from the state than they have to pay in taxes, but the perception that their hard-earned monies are being taken away does resonate with voters squeezed by the changing economy.
1.6.11.2.10.1.1. Con: If a policy would financially benefit people, the government should work to fix people's perception of the policy instead of giving up on it.
1.6.11.2.10.2.
