Con: UBI neglects political reality because wealthy citizens receive payments regardless of whether the system is in the self-interest of any beneficiary; whether it be bureaucrats, special interest groups, or voters. All political incentive is driven by the pressure of beneficiaries. Since there is no beneficiary, there cannot be any political incentive and thus a UBI will not be ratified.
1.6.11.2.10.2.1. Con: There are people who will vote for a political entity who proposes to implement a UBI, whether because of personal self-interest, moral and ethical reasons, or other reasons entirely. The idea that people act purely out of self-interest is patently incorrect in practice.
1.6.11.2.10.2.1.1. Con: Politicking is inherently immoral. Politicking does not include the dissemination of policies, so movements such as the abolishionist movement are exempt from this issue. UBI, however, does not fall into that category. Political voting is at least amoral. Therefore, there being a sufficient motivation among citizens for UBI on moral grounds is unlikely.
1.6.11.2.10.2.1.1.1. Con: The moralities of the policies proposed by political parties and candidates do have an effect on which ones get voted for by citizens, so they are relevant.
1.6.11.2.10.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.6.9.
1.6.11.2.11. Con: If UBI does replace other welfare systems, it will likely be due to the success of the system, and could not be seen as a 'con' of UBI.
1.6.11.2.12. Pro: It may be a good idea in theory, but in practice UBI could potentially be used to cut benefits programmes that many people rely on to survive. Consider what's going on with Universal Credit \(where six programmes were slashed and replaced with one\), which is now being cut. When you get rid of multiple programmes and create one, it's naturally going to constitute less, and thus a smaller social safety net
1.6.11.2.12.1. Con: Giving the same amounts under fewer programs creates less bureaucracy, and thus less wastage and allows more money to be put into the program itself.
1.6.11.2.12.2. Con: If there are too many welfare programs, there is nothing stopping the government from cutting entire programs rather than merely making cuts to the fewer programs that exist. Larger numbers of programs is no protection if the government has the political will to cut welfare programs.
1.6.11.2.12.3. Con: If the government has the political will and intention to cut welfare programs, that is not the fault of how many welfare programs there are, but the attitude of the government and the people who put them there \(or the government not effectively representing the people\). Ultimately, the number of welfare programs is irrelevant from this perspective.
1.6.11.2.13. Con: Political will is not a currency; it can be renewed and expanded rather than permanently depleted.
1.6.11.2.13.1. Pro: It is the role of the Citizens to demand from their government those services that ensure equity. A person with a severe disability requires extra services to ensure individual equity. Political will stems from the voice of the citizens who choose their representatives. Political will for a social welfare safety net will not be found in those who would seek to place the fate of citizens in the hands of the corporate class.
1.6.11.2.13.2. Pro: One a UBI has been passed the political will needed to do so will quickly renew \(as the policy becomes normalised\) and can then be used to support other policies.
1.6.11.2.14. Con: If there continue to be deficits in the public economy that need addressing, such as daycare, or basic nutrition, or housing, then there will still be demand for targeted programs to support those societal needs.
1.6.11.3. Pro: UBI is simply a stopgap measure propagated by dominant corporations to make up for their monopolized contributions to income inequality. Automation is not the threat, it's the current oligopolistic structure of our economy - and that needs to be solved rather than providing simple UBI.
1.6.11.3.1. Con: If automation is not a threat, but rather the way things are, then we should treat the symptoms of this economic system. UBI is a potential way to do this.
1.6.11.4. Pro: UBI would exacerbate the structural forces that drive inequality, by making a larger portion of the population unemployed and dependent on the government.
1.6.11.5. Pro: Native American tribes with casino revenues are a harbinger for large-scale UBI programs. The Seneca Nation pays [$8000 annually](https://ifstudies.org/blog/what-can-we-learn-from-native-americans-about-a-universal-basic-income) to each tribe member and yet unemployment, poverty and theft continue to rise. The guaranteed, subsistence income seems to have contributed to the stagnation of their society.
1.6.11.5.1. Con: There are other factors involved in the underprivileged situations endured by many indigenous people, such as poor education and cultural integration.
1.6.12. Con: UBI falls victim to the real notion of perceived poverty - whereas in some countries those considered poor are often much better off than the poor of other countries, so too will those subsisting on the UBI alone end up perceived just as poor as those without it, and thus it will do nothing to fix society in a satisfying way.
1.6.12.1. Con: Relative poverty and absolute poverty are about numerical relationships, not perception.
1.6.12.2. Con: Providing a guaranteed payment to all citizens promotes the idea that human life is intrinsically valuable and should be supported.
1.6.12.3. Con: Perception is not the only problem, nor the greatest portion of it - actually starving to death, or even just being unable to afford healthcare it would be better for one to receive, are both objective states of being and far greater issues than the social status of the impoverished, and these are issues a UBI is likely to address.
1.6.12.4. Pro: It is a simplification to assume the difference between the rich and the poor is simply financial. To be richer than another you need to be able to buy/access things that poorer others are unable to. UBI will simply shift the margins but the differences will still remain. Prices of good/services will adjust \(obviously\) to soak up the UBI. This is less about inflation and more about understanding the concept that rich/poor is only measured by finance but exists beyond it.
1.6.12.5. Con: We have Americans, including veterans, that deal with food insecurity right now. The problem is not perception.
1.6.13. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.
1.6.14. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.4.6.1.
1.6.15. Con: Since a UBI as proposed does not yet include programs to guarantee jobs as well, it could create an environment where most people live at the poverty line - essentially helping no one.
1.6.15.1. Con: Part of the objective of a UBI is to move away from a work-based economic system.
1.6.15.2. Pro: The poor will use their UBI to buy apples which they consume, while the wealthy use it to buy Apple stock which further increases their income – and so grow wealthier. Thus over time, UBI mathematically increases disparity. Economist Pavlina Tcherneva writes extensively [here](http://neweconomicperspectives.org/2014/01/16-reasons-matt-yglesias-wrong-job-guarantee-vs-basic-income.html) about how a Job Guarantee offers a superior practical solution.
1.6.15.2.1. Con: This is a highly speculative claim, and most UBI plans suggest a very small amount of money per person, basically enough to live on and no more \(no more than current welfare\). In this case the increase to the wealth of people who are better off would be negligible. It seems highly unlikely that an already wealthy person's receipt of UBI would greatly affect how much money they invest, or anything else.
1.6.16. Con: A UBI does not share wealth because it gives a portion to those who are already wealthy. This does nothing to resolve financial inequality.
1.6.16.1. Con: The rebate offered to the wealthy by a UBI is not of consequence given that those people pay more tax than they receive in government services. It is just effectively a slight change in their tax rate.
1.6.16.2. Con: While this is technically true, the implementation of a UBI will most likely require tax rates to become more progressive overall, and thus levels of financial inequality will decrease.
1.6.16.3. Pro: According to the [relative income hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relative_income_hypothesis), consumers make purchases and save money depending on their percentile position within the income distribution. A UBI may increase spending, but does not improve the income gap.
1.6.16.3.1. Con: This does not account for the fact that a UBI is a redistribution program. The money that goes to those who are poor comes from those who have more, so there is a difference in relative income.
1.6.16.3.2. Con: These two points are unrelated. Whether or not UBI will increase spending \(unclear\) has no bearing on the income gap, which it does address.
1.6.16.4. Pro: This means that minorities are still in the same socio-economic position they were before relative to the privileged in society.
1.6.16.4.1. Con: This is an over simplification. When you have not enough money to make it through the month, your position is much more desperate that when you don't have money for leisure. To deny this basic comfort on the base of "relative wealth" is to forget that there is more similarity of experiences between poor people everywhere in the world than within the "steps" of a single social pyramid.
1.6.16.4.2. Con: Minorities are much better off when they have economic security.
1.6.16.4.2.1. Pro: Even groups who lack the social cohesion, self identification and social recognition of minorities will benefit from this. It will effectively surpass current efforts to increase the quality of life in the current narrative and support structure.
1.6.16.4.2.2. Pro: The idea of a UBI is that even the poorest would have enough money to cover basic expenses.
1.6.16.4.2.2.1. Pro: By choosing to work, they can earn additional income, which would be disposable income. This would never be possible without a UBI since all of their money would go towards making ends meet.
1.6.16.4.3. Pro: Minorities continue to experience a wage gap in the workplace. A UBI does not solve this discrimination.
1.6.16.4.3.1. Con: If someone is not dependent on a certain job, they can choose more freely to walk away from workplaces where they are not treated fairly with less risk of endangering their means for subsistence.
1.6.16.4.3.1.1. Pro: The fact that labor would no longer be forced into sub-poverty conditions by an employer or else face starvation and social chastisement should they "fail to keep a job" will change the job market drastically and empower everyone.
1.6.16.4.3.1.2. Con: This wage gap disparity is widespread. The likelihood of a minority finding a job with fair wages in comparison to those not considered a minority is slim.
1.6.16.4.3.1.2.1. Con: Minorities will be empowered to become entrepreneurs and their representation in companies will increase as a consequence of UBI. This will assist in the correction of the indicated problem.
1.6.16.4.3.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.6.16.4.3.2. Con: [Some feminists](http://basicincome.org/bien/pdf/munich2012/katada.pdf) see a UBI as an “emancipation fee”, and criticize the welfare state because they argue that it is largely based on a traditional model of male breadwinner and female housekeeper. They conclude that a UBI can be useful to overcome this form of discrimination.
1.6.16.4.3.2.1. Pro: A UBI is a way of reevaluating the economic value of the work women have primarily handled in the past, while also promoting the economic independence of women. UBIs are an “Emancipation Fee” paid to promote women’s liberation \([Katada, p. 3](http://basicincome.org/bien/pdf/munich2012/katada.pdf)\)
1.6.16.4.3.2.1.1. Con: UBI payments go to men as well as women, and regardless of contributions to housework, childcare, eldercare or other traditionally unpaid female labour.
1.6.16.4.3.2.2. Con: A UBI returns women to the home, and contributes to a backlash that runs counter to women’s liberation and independence.
1.6.16.4.3.3. Con: A UBI was never designed to — or claimed to — eliminate workplace discrimination.
1.6.16.4.3.4. Pro: [Gay](https://ecf.cand.uscourts.gov/cand/09cv2292/evidence/PX0605.pdf) government employees in the US earn 8% to 29% less than their heterosexual counterparts.
1.6.16.4.3.5. Pro: [Transgender workers](http://www.transequality.org/sites/default/files/docs/resources/NTDS_Exec_Summary.pdf) experience rates of unemployment at double the rate of the general population due to firing, wage discrimination, or harassment. This has a negative impact on their income.
1.6.16.4.3.6. Pro: The [racial wage gap](http://time.com/4390212/race-wage-gap-pew-analysis/) in the US shows that Black men earn only 73% of white men's hourly earning and Hispanic men earn just 69%. These numbers have not significantly changed in 35 years. Its unlikely that UBI will change this.
1.6.16.4.3.6.1. Con: Improved social mobility due to reduced paycheck-to-paycheck budgeting allows them to find better and less discriminatory opportunities, and will help close this gap.
1.6.16.4.4. Pro: Minorities will still be locked out of certain employment opportunities for prejudicial reasons.
1.6.16.4.4.1. Pro: There are [many accounts](https://www.revealnews.org/article/when-companies-hire-temp-workers-by-race-black-applicants-lose-out/) of temp agencies hiring out with direct orders not to hire Black Americans, despite equal opportunity employment policies.
1.6.16.4.4.2. Pro: [Hiring practices](http://thelasource.com/en/2011/10/31/university-study-finds-prejudice-in-hiring-practices/) in Montreal, Vancouver, and Toronto favored applicants with English sounding names over Asian sounding names.
1.6.16.4.4.3. Con: Whether or not this is true, this is no reason not to offer a UBI. If this is true it means we should be looking for ways to reduce this prejudice. It does not mean we should give up on other ways to help a given minority, i.e. it is no reason not to offer a UBI.
1.6.16.5. Con: While a UBI does not correct income inequality, it assures equality of opportunity.
1.6.16.5.1. Con: A UBI is not enough to create equal opportunities for both the poor and wealthy.
1.6.16.5.1.1. Pro: The wealthy have educational advantages as they can pay for prestigious university tuition and tutors.
1.6.16.5.1.2. Pro: Wealthy individuals often have networks with peers and good connections that make it easier for them to find jobs.
1.6.16.5.1.3. Con: There is a difference between equality of opportunity and equality of economic income. Equality of economic income would never be reached and would potentially lead to an economic paralysis, while equality of opportunity would be the incentive to make everybody participate in the work market.
1.6.16.5.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.4.2.
1.6.16.5.1.5. Pro: What about poor countries? If only wealthy countries will have this than we solved only a fraction of the issue, and we already have a system that resolves only a fraction of the problem globally.
1.6.16.5.2. Pro: A UBI provides people with a sufficient amount of money to allow everybody to have access to food, health, education and housing.
1.6.16.5.2.1. Con: Food, health & education - yes, maybe. But housing is a mostly fixed supply - regardless of how much money you give people, everyone cannot have access to housing unless there is more supply than demand. This isn't usually the case due to the inherent difficulty of creating new housing units.
1.6.16.5.2.1.1. Con: There are currently houses \(and units\) left unoccupied for economic reasons - and/or because the landowner prefers the loss of no tenant rather than the risk to their investment - in at least some countries.
1.6.16.5.3. Con: A UBI does not assure equal opportunity when the UBI does not cover it \(such as a [higher cost-of-living than a UBI can handle](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kl39KHS07Xc)\).
1.6.16.6. Con: Minorities have real problems that endanger their health and sometimes their lives, like living in very poor conditions, being malnourished, not having access to health care and education, etc. A UBI can afford them better health, education, and housing.
1.6.16.6.1. Con: There are [government public assistance programs](https://www.usa.gov/benefits) available that already provide benefits to improving health care access, education, proper nutrition, and housing.
1.6.16.6.2. Pro: A UBI will reduce unclaimed medical costs caused by malnurishment, dehydration and various homeless injuries. In turn, this will cause an overall increase in national health.
1.6.16.6.3. Pro: Eliminating these problems should be the absolute priority, even if this does not also solve discrimination and inequality.
1.6.16.7. Pro: A guaranteed UBI is incompatible with equal human rights. It separates society into net payers and net receivers.
1.6.16.7.1. Con: Inequality exists in all societal models and everyone within an economic system either has a net gain or a net loss. However, this has no bearing on fundamental rights and there is no visible human rights issue with the implementation of UBI.
1.6.16.7.2. Con: Many countries, like the US, already have an 'unfair' progressive tax system that seperate society into net payers and receivers.
1.6.16.7.3. Con: A UBI is one way of ensuring the right to life - including necessities and adequate shelter/healthcare/etc. - is met.
1.6.16.8. Con: Because a UBI is a flat rate, the portion it gives to the poor could double their current income. To the wealthy it would only be a marginal increase. In relative terms this is a huge decrease in inequality.
1.6.16.8.1. Pro: This is referred to as the [diminishing marginal utility of income](https://www.economicshelp.org/blog/12309/concepts/diminishing-marginal-utility-of-income-and-wealth/).
1.6.16.8.2. Pro: A person making $1000 and receiving a UBI of $500 would see their income increase by 50%;  a person making $10,000 and receiving a UBI of $500 would only see an income increase of 5%.
1.6.16.9. Pro: Since the UBI is provided to all, it would simply move the "0.00" income level higher for all people. This would result in no relative change since everybody is making \(Prior Income\) + \(UBI\) instead of just \(Prior Income\). It seems to be a mathematical illusion. How would UBI address income inequality if UBI is truly universal?
1.6.16.9.1. Con: Inequalities are both absolute and relative. If "moving the 0.00" level up by a few thousand dollars allows people to afford healthcare or to heat their homes it makes a huge difference in their quality of life relative to the middle classes.
1.6.16.9.2. Con: -> See 1.6.16.8.
1.6.16.10. Pro: A UBI would further increase the economic gap because the needy would use it for necessities and the rich would bank/invest theirs.
1.6.16.10.1. Con: A UBI would not be a significant increase to the finances of highly wealthy individuals, but would be a significant improvement to less well-off people - both those who would use it to eat and those who would use it to invest in owning their own home.
1.6.16.11. Con: Only by making UBI universal will the wealthier support the provision of such an income rise for the poorer. As the wealthier seem happy to pocket universal benefits too \(even when claiming to disagree with them\).
1.6.16.12. Pro: The wealth can be equally shared by effective taxation and welfare programs.
1.6.16.13. Pro: Giving everyone welfare, including the uber wealthy, is not the solution to problems with the way mean-tested schemes of welfare are calculated. The solution needs to be to change the way welfare is calculated.
1.6.16.13.1. Con: Removing  the concept of "welfare" will go a long way to solving perception issues such as this. The whole idea of a universal income is that it is earned by one's place in society and is not a 'handout'.
1.6.17. Pro: A UBI will reduce discrimination by empowering groups that traditionally lack influence due to economic barriers.
1.6.17.1. Pro: With a UBI, these groups now have the capital to receive an education and pursue high-skilled jobs. This will help remedy the lack of skilled professionals in minority communities.
1.6.17.1.1. Pro: Minority doctors are more likely [to return to their communities](http://commonhealth.legacy.wbur.org/2012/02/minority-doctors-diversity) and service those who traditionally do not have access to medical care.
1.6.17.2. Pro: A UBI improves the economic freedom of women.
1.6.17.2.1. Pro: Women would no longer have to stay in abusive relationships due to a lack of income.
1.6.17.2.1.1. Con: Women should not rely on the income of an abusive partner, they can leave and get a job if necessary.
1.6.17.2.1.1.1. Con: Doing so without an income stream of your own often means having to survive without accomodation or any form of financial security; making this highly difficult.
1.6.17.2.1.1.2. Con: For women to be able to leave an abusive partner they need a way to ensure they and their children won't be left in poverty and homelessness because of this decision. A UBI would provide that safety net for them to be able to change their minds and safely leave abusive relationships.
1.6.17.2.1.1.3. Con: In order to leave an abusive relationship in which they are dependent, women would often need to get a job even before they do so. Abusive partners often prohibit women from getting a job, making the jump difficult. An UBI would remove this control mechanism from abusive partners.
1.6.17.2.1.1.4. Con: Abuse can have many physical and mental effects which make it more difficult \(and often impossible\) to get a job, such as lack of sleep, \(often extreme\) stress, malnutrition, physical injury, shame and the desire to hide one's situation, the destruction of one's personal identity and self-esteem, the destruction of one's belief in their own competence, morality, and agency, and the destruction of one's belief in their worthiness of any form of achievement or reward.
1.6.17.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1.
1.6.17.2.3. Con: -> See 1.2.23.4.3.1.6.
1.6.17.2.4. Pro: A UBI can make a critical contribution to women's autonomy, security and flexibility \([Regehr, p.14](http://www.basicincome.org/bien/pdf/montreal2014/BIEN2014_Regehr.pdf)\).
1.6.17.2.4.1. Con: This can only be achieved by reducing the autonomy, security and flexibility of other women \(via increases their taxes\). To justify helping some women by harming other women is hypocritical.
1.6.17.2.4.1.1. Con: Given men earn, on average, more than woman the redistributive effects UBI would actually be a net benefit to women at the cost of men.
1.6.17.2.4.1.2. Con: Assuming proportionate taxation, the effect on taxpaying women will be far less significant than the effect on women who depend on or gain significant benefit from receiving a UBI.
1.6.17.2.4.1.3. Con: Taxes besides income tax can be used to fund a UBI, as can non-taxation methods of increasing government revenue.
1.6.17.2.5. Pro: A UBI guaranteed to any citizen of a said country may allow inequalities between men and woman to be slightly reduced. Not only women would be able to stop working more confortably during their pregnancy, but it would also allow them to be less vulnerable when they are students \(from miserable jobs often given to them in a society that still rewards more men than women on the employment market\). More generally, one more tool for their independance from the men is also one for their emancipation.
1.6.17.2.6. Con: A UBI is zero-sum and does not enhance the freedom of some without equal inhibition of freedom on others.
1.6.17.2.6.1. Con: There is no reason to believe that it is a zero sum game. Any policy that helps those who most need money raises the total amount of freedom in the economy \(the poor gain more than the rich loose\) more than just a simple redistribution would suggest.
1.6.17.2.6.2. Pro: If a person collects their UBI, pays for their necessities, and then sits on the couch watching TV for the rest of their life, they have gained the freedom to do so at the expense of all involved in the production of said entertainment.
1.6.17.2.6.2.1. Con: The person who sits on their couch watching TV will have used their UBI or some other form of income to pay for the programming they are watching.
1.6.17.2.6.3. Pro: Whilst the UBI may provide freedom for one group, it can only do so by reducing the freedom of another group.
1.6.17.2.6.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.1.3.
1.6.17.2.7. Pro: Unpaid work, such as supporting younger and older family members is often a burden disproportionately affecting females. The guarantee of a UBI reduces the risks from part time work, thus helping females enter and leave the work force as they need or want.
1.6.17.2.8. Con: The view that the UBI somehow levels the playing field in upper management can't hold true. The UBI would most likely serve to oppress women by citing that wage inequality is a non-issue in the first place.
1.6.17.2.8.1. Con: The fact that people could \(mis\)use a UBI as an argument against addressing other social injustices such as the gender pay gap \(which are related to factors beyond poverty and lack of funds\) is not an argument against implementing a UBI.
1.6.17.2.9. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.6.
1.6.17.3. Pro: Wealth is also a massive predictor in [civic engagement](http://www.people-press.org/2015/01/08/the-politics-of-financial-insecurity-a-democratic-tilt-undercut-by-low-participation/), insofar as it is able to grant stability to people from less advantaged backgrounds, it affords them the time and agency to push for the types of political change that wouldn't be possible otherwise.
1.6.17.3.1. Pro: Not only will more people agitate for change, but it will also change incentives for politicians who have to more actively target or incorporate traditionally non-politically enfranchised groups.
1.6.17.3.2. Con: They still only have a finite degree of political capital when engaging with elites. If wealthy people already feel as though they are contributing towards the UBI, they are less likely to support these kinds of initiatives.
1.6.17.3.2.1. Con: What the wealthy want and desire becomes less relevant in a world where massively more people are enfranchised.
1.6.17.3.2.2. Con: This same logic could also be applied to all of the social welfare programs, yet many wealthy individuals still distinguish between their own self interest and what is best for people broadly.
1.6.17.3.3. Con: States are also able to use the fact that there is a UBI to justify failing to engage with specific political change.
1.6.17.3.3.1. Con: If states fail to engage with specific political change, these disadvantaged groups are now considerably more able to hold them accountable due to increases in political awareness and activism brought about by less worry for basic daily struggles.
1.6.17.3.4. Con: NGOs and other special interest groups \(such as the NAACP\) are able to fulfill this form of engagement.
1.6.17.3.4.1. Con: These NGO's and advocacy groups are much stronger and greater political forces in a world where their constituents have more access to capital.
1.6.17.3.4.2. Con: These groups cannot \(and do not claim to\) speak to all of the diverse interests and desires of their own constituency. To claim that NGO's and hierarchical organizations can by themselves compensate for the political voices of millions is naive and dangerous.
1.6.17.3.5. Pro: By giving workers a wage security, many decision making processes such as voting are being freed from that economic incentive, leaving room for potentially more thoughtful debates.
1.6.17.4. Con: Contingent on providing wealth, an income just above the poverty line is neither meaningful wealth nor economic inclusion.
1.6.17.4.1. Con: A UBI ensures that our most important needs are accounted for in a stable manner. This is incredibly meaningful and comparatively leads to far more political engagement, even if not all recipients become dedicated activists.
1.6.17.5. Con: A UBI is not enough to make a person middle class, only enough to prevent poverty. Many of these benefits are better achieved by education and social benefit access than by income.
1.6.17.5.1. Con: Access to education and the ability to lift oneself from one class to another are more easily achieved when one has basic needs accounted for.
1.6.17.5.2. Con: An individual does not need to be middle class to have political efficacy and influence, they need time. However, in the status quo people on the poverty line are not only poor but have no energy to invest in the political process due to work and life demands.
1.6.18. Pro: Studies show that Alaska \(the only state with UBI at present\) is also the state with the lowest financial inequality. In [2015 Alaska had the 2nd lowest inequality, but today it has the least.](https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/jp5wdb/only-state-free-money-alaska)
1.6.18.1. Con: Alaska may offer a poor case test. It’s geographical and demographic markers, may make the state too anamolous — too much of an outlier — to properly transfer to the US mainland.
1.6.19. Pro: A UBI will benefit the middle class, by increasing median household income among the middle class.
1.6.19.1. Pro: [Alaska, the only state with a UBI](https://qz.com/1205591/a-universal-basic-income-experiment-in-alaska-shows-employment-didnt-drop/), has the [highest median household income for middle class](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/03/24/heres-how-much-money-middle-class-families-earn-in-every-us-state.html). Alaska's middle class make $80,000 per year, while middle class in all other states make less — up to $10,000 less.
1.6.20. Pro: -> See 1.4.14.1.16.
1.6.21. Pro: -> See 1.2.21.
1.6.22. Con: There could be an increased dependence of cheaper "non-citizens" to provide the labour force. Eventually leading to an elite/subordinate citizen/ non-citizen segregated society.
1.6.22.1. Con: Our moral values are strongly against such things, at least in the West. So it's unlikely to happen at least in the current Western nations.
1.6.22.2. Con: "Non-citizens" who provide labour could become citizens and therefore recipients of a UBI.
1.6.22.3. Con: A valid point but something that could easily be avoided by providing UBI to anyone living in said country. Today, in most industrialised countries there are welfare programs taking care of workers, earning too little to make a living, anyway. So this would be a different distribution pattern.
1.6.23. Pro: UBI compensates people for the injustice created by the institution of private property.
1.6.24. Con: It flattens the top on social mobility as those who become more productive would bear a greater burden for those happily being carried at the bottom. Someone pays for overcompensating less valuable labor.
1.7. Pro: A UBI better rewards meaningful contributions to society, such as domestic labour, art, volunteering, engaging with politics, and pursuing higher education.
1.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.
1.7.2. Pro: Domestic labour \(e.g. child and aged care\) are vital to the function of family and societal units. However, it is currently devalued by our income system and lacks financial recompense. Instituting a UBI creates compensation and assigns value to this labour, supporting those individuals who undertake it.
1.7.2.1. Con: There are experiments to deal with this, such as an old-age home in Holland that gives [free housing to students in exchange for time they spend with elderly residents](http://www.universityherald.com/articles/53852/20161207/dutch-university-students-free-housing-exchange-valuable-service-elderly.htm).
1.7.2.2. Con: This doesn't work in practice, as, for example, a stay-at-home mother who does full time domestic labour will still receive the same UBI as her partner who does none of it.
1.7.2.2.1. Con: A UBI can decrease the necessity to work long hours, thus providing working partners more time and energy for domestic labor efforts. This essentially helps both individuals.
1.7.2.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.23.4.3.1.2.1.
1.7.2.2.3. Con: A UBI is not a job fee, but it can balance out those unpaid jobs that are still not recognized by the State.
1.7.2.2.4. Con: The working partner will also receive the benefits of the external job, and in most households, will also be contributing to the labour of managing the houshold.
1.7.2.3. Pro: Unpaid labour tends to be highly gendered with the bulk of work expected to be fulfilled by women. In this context it is important to implement systems that support women in these roles and provide greater financial autonomy.
1.7.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.16.4.3.2.1.
1.7.2.3.2. Con: The fact that the labor is unpaid is a symptom of the destruction of the arrangement that exchanged provision for the household for management of the household, rather than the problem itself- in which when both parties are providing for the household, it simply becomes extra work for one or both providers, instead of compensated work in exchange for the others' provision.
1.7.2.3.2.1. Con: That arrangement was and is problematic. However, it leaves the unpaid worker vulnerable to abuse and otherwise at the mercy of the provider, who can provide exactly as much or as little as they please, unless the unpaid domestic worker has effective legal recourse - which often is and was not the case. Its destruction \(or reconstruction\) is a reasonable and desirable process.
1.7.2.4. Pro: The value of unpaid care in the US [is estimated at $691 billion](http://www.washington.edu/news/2015/06/16/study-reveals-surprising-truths-about-caregivers/); over 4% of GDP.
1.7.2.4.1. Con: Unpaid care could and should be addressed by targeted programs, rather than a UBI which just indiscriminately provides everyone with money.
1.7.2.5. Pro: A UBI alleviates the stress of insufficient [maternity leave](http://www.forbes.com/sites/ritarubin/2016/04/06/united-states-lags-behind-all-other-developed-countries-when-it-comes-to-paid-maternity-leave/#20f54bd05ada) for expectant mothers and their families.
1.7.2.5.1. Con: The problem of insufficient maternity leave \(which the article notes is a major problem in America\) could be addressed by increasing maternity leave support, rather than providing everyone with unconditional money regardless of whether they likely need it more or less than the average person.
1.7.2.6. Con: This has been historically managed by having one partner fully provide for all of the needs of the other who is in that doing the domestic work: in that creating the ends that financial recompense acts as means for. Separating these from each other destabilizes the family unit, rather than supporting its functions.
1.7.2.6.1. Con: -> See 1.7.2.3.2.1.
1.7.2.7. Con: There are more direct and effective ways to compensate for child/aged care, such as access to healthcare for children and aged populations as well as tax deductions or credits for having or adopting children.
1.7.2.7.1. Con: Those methods are not mutually exclusive with a UBI; moreover they only compensate for some aspects of raising a child \(healthcare, or 9-5 care\) rather than the entire endeavour.
1.7.2.8. Con: All UBI does is in this case, is not award good parents for being good or discourages bad parents for being bad, a reason why non-discriminatory systems don't work.
1.7.2.8.1. Con: The majority of parents are good; to punish them for the actions of the bad ones is unfair.
1.7.2.8.2. Con: There are plenty of discriminatory systems that can co-exist with a UBI, such as criminal sanctions, child services interventions, and the withdrawal of non-UBI benefits or privileges.
1.7.2.9. Con: If this work were of high value to parents, the caretakers of the aged, and the aged themselves, they would be willing to pay for it in proportion to that high value. The fact that their valuation of such services is not as high as some would like represents a valid difference in personal priorities and is not a structural imbalance in need of reform.
1.7.2.9.1. Con: People cannot be willing to pay money they do not have, as in the case of stay at home parents who either are not provided a fair portion of the family's income, or are simply in a lower-income socioeconomic bracket.
1.7.2.9.2. Con: Historically, caretaking roles have been undervalued for a number of reasons, including the expectation for women to perform them for free, merely in return for housing and basic necessities \(and often with a side of abuse\). This historical impact has not yet entirely faded.
1.7.2.9.3. Con: This claim assumes most people are enlightened capitalists able and willing to think in such terms. This is not true. Most people do not make rational purchasing decisions based purely on capitalistic logic and ideals.
1.7.2.10. Pro: A UBI benefits caregivers and gives a decent life to people with disabilities.
1.7.2.10.1. Con: -> See 1.7.2.4.1.
1.7.2.11. Con: UBI assigns the same financial reward to the selfless mother as it does a man who does nothing but play visual novels all day.
1.7.2.12. Pro: A UBI is a feminist issue. For too long the economic model has been proped up up the unpaid work of carers \(mainly women\).  This work is the real cost of doing business and needs just compensation; something a UBI can achieve.
1.7.2.13. Con: Babysitters are paid. If your argument is they aren't paid enough, in a capitalist society, they're paid exactly as much as they're worth, and aren't forced to work and do so willingly for the amount they are paid. If they want to be paid more, they should charge more, if no one will hire them at the higher rate, then they're obviously not worth the higher rate.
1.7.2.14. Con: Hospice care is a paid position. If your argument is they aren't paid enough, in a capitalist society, they're paid exactly as much as they're worth, and aren't forced to work and do so willingly for the amount they are paid. If they want to be paid more, they should charge more, if no one will hire them at the higher rate, then they're obviously not worth the higher rate.
1.7.2.15. Con: Being a stay at home parent is an option, with alternatives like working and letting someone else take care of your children. Many times this decision is made for one of two reasons: 1\) It costs more to pay someone to watch your children for you then you would earn working outside the home. OR 2\) You want to care for your children yourself, and thus choose to forego the income of working outside the home. Society shouldn't have to pay for your choices. You should.
1.7.3. Pro: Many forms of creative expression, such as art or writing, are not pursued because only the most successful artists or writers can generate a self-sustaining income.
1.7.3.1. Pro: Wikipedia and many other open-source community creations are an example of unpaid work that has great societal value. A UBI would help reward and sustain these valuable resources.
1.7.3.2. Pro: Artists, who have always struggled to find funding adequate enough to develop their oeuvre, will benefit greatly from a reliable income.
1.7.3.3. Pro: Even the pursuit of creative activities as a way to achieve a financial reward may in many cases lead to those activities [being enjoyed less and pursued less effectively](https://www.gnu.org/philosophy/motivation.html).
1.7.3.4. Con: The is no shortage of supply of "creative expression" in the today's wealthy countries. It far from obvious how increase in volume of "creative expression" produced will benefit society as whole. Or if additional volume of work produced will be "meaningful contribution" to society.
1.7.3.4.1. Con: Creative expression can function as critique of society, or otherwise provide moral lessons or other points to think about, and can inspire further artwork - in response or indirectly. This increased reflection and intellectual stimulus may lead to innovation, societal progress, or other benefits.
1.7.3.4.2. Con: Many people use artworks and works of fiction to relax and as a temporary reprieve. The stress-reducing and satisfaction/morale-increasing effects of this can lead to improved production in other areas, as well as a reduction in the negative effects of stress \(shorter-temperedness, stress-related illness\).
1.7.3.5. Con: Producing quality "creative expression" is a hard work. Assuming that introduction of unconditional income will increase the total amount of high quality works of art is unfounded and naive.
1.7.3.5.1. Con: "Practice makes perfect", or at least has a positive relationship with quality. Additional time permitted for artistic endeavour \(such as by a UBI reducing the stress of providing for basics\) will increase the quality of artistic production.
1.7.3.5.2. Pro: Simple probability statistics disagree with this thesis. A greater number of artistic creators, even using the Infinite Monkey Theorem as a productivity yardstick, will invariably increase the number of quality artistic works being created.  More artists means more art means more good art. While the average quality of UBI-funded art may be less, but more great art will invariably get produced if there are more artists making art.
1.7.3.6. Con: If artists struggle to earn enough money to make a living, this is a price signal that has a meaning: it means that, for people, that art has more costs than benefits; i.e. in order to allow that art to be created, people would have to give up things that are more important. \(So the fact that only few artists are unable to make a living is not false but irrelevant\).
1.7.3.7. Pro: An UBI enables artists to live without the need to commodify their art so that it can be sold.
1.7.3.7.1. Pro: Many contemporary artists receive pressure from producers to alter their work to better appeal to a mainstream audience, against their own desires or creative vision.
1.7.3.7.2. Pro: The current economy is based on the assumption that the average human can sell their work at enough cost to sustain themselves . This may not be true in the future.
1.7.3.8. Pro: A UBI will enable artists to do what they do best rather than having to scrape by, spending most of their time working minimum wage jobs. The benefit to society is that we have an infusion of arts and culture.
1.7.3.9. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.7.
1.7.3.10. Pro: A UBI will help to subsidize independent and critical thought and thus help bring new ideas into fruition.
1.7.3.11. Pro: Our brain can be on either survival or creative mode. Our default is to be creative. As children, we have more creative thoughts and we are more curious. But as we grow and see our world, we switch to survival, which focuses on getting a job to buy food, and have shelter, etc. UBI takes care of the basic needs so we can switch back to creative thoughts which gives us a natural incentive to create and invent more wherever is needed. We would get our kick by seeing the world become a better place.
1.7.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.1.
1.7.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.12.7.
1.7.6. Con: If a contribution is valuable to society, then market forces will already define a fair price for such a contribution.
1.7.6.1. Con: In the case of America the market system specifically does not define a fair price for such a contribution due to skewed distribution of wealth and price influence.
1.7.6.1.1. Con: Further skewing the market by use of involuntary wealth transfers would exaggerate this problem rather than fixing it.
1.7.6.2. Con: If you solve a problem for a rich person "society" rewards you much more than if you solve a problem for a poor person. Even if your contribution toward the poor person had a greater impact on their life. For instance, tutoring a poor kid or tutoring a rich kid provides different rewards for the same contribution. \(tried to place the tutoring example as a pro, to separate things\)
1.7.6.2.1. Con: Directing resources to the already well resourced can be the most efficient investment. For example, if an expensive worker is delayed by one hour in getting to work, the lost production is much greater than if an inexpensive worker is delayed by the same time. Allocating a private car to the expensive worker may be justifiable even though allocating a private car to the less expensive worker would not be.
1.7.6.2.2. Con: The above claim falsely assumes that wealth is always unearned and concludes it unfair that a rich person can outbid a poor person. However, wealth is often earned, therefore the ability to outbid more for a contested resource must sometimes be acknowledged to be a reward for earlier effort.
1.7.6.3. Con: Market forces stop making sense when people stop being able to pursue the best value deals. When people are no longer players due to lack of funds, they are unable to reward contributions made available towards them.
1.7.6.4. Con: Many market failures exist. For example, a family member caring for an elderly person is not rewarded by the market, while paying a stranger to do so is.
1.7.6.5. Pro: A UBI removes the incentive to deliver precisely those goods and services most highly valued by individuals. The value of a good or service to an individual is only measurable by what that individual is willing to forego to attain it, namely, what they are willing to pay. By flattening the price disparities between different activities, a UBI will dilute the information contained in prices and lead to inefficient allocation of resources.
1.7.6.5.1. Con: The UBI is not paid to the product, but the person.
1.7.6.6. Pro: Individuals differ in their valuation of contributions. The market economy is the only means by which we may discover those valuations.  A "contribution to society" is meaningful and valuable only insofar as the members of that society are willing to pay for it.
1.7.6.6.1. Con: Roads are pretty valuable, yet society doesn't pay for them as part of a market economy.
1.7.6.6.2. Con: Police in general are pretty valuable, yet police that individuals pay for are called protection rackets, and are a determent to society.
1.7.6.6.2.1. Con: Actually, they're called "security firms" and are widely engaged. Protection racketeering is more along the lines of blackmailing victims with property damage and extorting money out of them on these grounds.
1.7.6.6.2.1.1. Con: Security firms work within the established framework of the law, have tons of oversight, and limited power —  they are basically the deputies of the goverment-run police and can't really be evaluated as an independent agency
1.7.6.6.3. Con: By the reasoning provided, intellectual property is not valuable. It would be better not to encourage the monopoly for any length of time. While some question the extent of our monopolies, most people see a little value in them.
1.7.6.6.4. Con: This statement highly values bribable officials. [Society](https://globalcompliancenews.com/anti-corruption/anti-corruption-laws-around-the-world/) tends not to overly care for them.
1.7.6.6.5. Con: Market exchange does not account for 'positive externalities' and 'negative externalities'.
1.7.6.7. Con: Market forces value the jobs that are harder to fill, not the jobs with the biggest contribution to society.
1.7.6.8. Con: In capitalist societies there are many instances where jobs that provide the least use value are rewarded with higher wages than those that provide intrinsic value.
1.7.6.8.1. Pro: Use value is a good or service that is intrinsically valuable, or at least an instrumental good not far removed from a good in itself. Occupations that deal in exchange values are those which mediate goods and services, but themselves are not productive; advertising etc.
1.7.6.9. Con: Many jobs exist where people perform them out of a sense of duty or vocation - these jobs are frequently poorly paid, because people are willing to endure low wages in order to work in their chosen field, and because it is felt that a better wage might encourage people to seek the job for the money rather than a "purer" reason. In some cases, such as teaching or nursing, people then leave the field in order to pursue better paid jobs, because they can no longer afford to follow their vocation.
1.7.7. Con: Since UBI is by definition unconditional, it cannot reward anything other than mere existence.
1.7.7.1. Con: Since UBI is unconditional it rewards all tasks that are not generally profitable, allowing a deeper cultural and scientific enrichment.
1.7.7.1.1. Con: A UBI does not reward anything. A reward, by definition, cannot be given unconditionally — it has to be contingent on some kind of achievement. People would receive the same payments, whether they did anything worthwhile or not: the engaged parent as much as the neglectful one, the dutiful homemaker as much as the messy person, the charitable volunteer as much as the video game addict.
1.7.7.1.1.1. Con: No individual, nor entity, should have the right to determine who is worthy of the basic means to live.
1.7.7.1.1.2. Con: The point that previous claims are making is that there are activities where the achievement is not immediately obvious, but in the long run they are highly valuable to society, and a UBI would serve as a mechanism to reward them.
1.7.7.1.2. Con: No evidence has been shown to prove that people will follow transcendental endeavors such as arts and science when their material needs are covered, instead of preferring leisure.
1.7.7.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.3.1.
1.7.7.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.3.
1.7.7.2. Pro: Under a UBI, there is no guarantee that anyone would engage in meaningful tasks or even intellectual or artistic endeavors. Some might use the UBI to focus on inventions or the improvement of self or society, but others might equally use the UBI to complacently consume.
1.7.7.2.1. Con: Most intellectuals or artists aren't doing their work for a monetary reward \(as there is rarely much money to be made anyway\). Money is not necessarily a motivator for "meaningful" tasks; for example many compassionate people already use their free time to help others like the sick or the elderly.
1.7.7.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.5.3.1.
1.7.7.2.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.3.
1.7.7.2.4. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.1.1.4.
1.7.7.2.5. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.4.4.
1.7.7.3. Pro: Rewards cannot be unconditional as a UBI is. To call it so is to distort the meaning of [reward](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/reward).
1.7.7.3.1. Con: By "rewards," the previous claim effectively means "enables" or "provides compensation for." At present, a plethora of socially beneficial activities are not profitable and therefore uncompensated. In providing an unconditional fund to all persons, UBI enables all such activities by providing compensation for them.
1.7.7.3.1.1. Con: There is no reliable support or quantification of how much a UBI would actually "enable" these kinds of "socially beneficial activities," since the UBI would likely just be a very small sum of money. \(Consider that a UBI of around $10,000/year would cost 2–3 trillion dollars annually\)
1.7.7.3.1.2. Con: By definition, rewards must be granted after some event or accomplishment. Something that enables the accomplishment of a task necessarily comes before that task is started. So, you cannot use one word to mean the other. A UBI also does not provide compensation for any activity; it may enable activities but it doesn't reward them.
1.7.7.4. Con: -> See 1.7.7.3.1.
1.7.7.5. Con: Existence is a condition. And by some accounts, it is a condition that deserves to be rewarded. A person who changes that condition by committing suicide can be considered a net loss to society.
1.7.8. Con: "Meaningful contribution to society" is vague and ill define concept. To the extent economic policies incentivise certain behaviours they should do so in a targeted fashion to ensure the outcomes are positive and progress is measured relative to costs.
1.7.8.1. Con: Yes, it's a vague concept, but that's kind of the point. A number of activities that contribute to society, not only in wellbeing/community, but in solid economic gains, are undervalued. Childcare and aged care for example; or the arts. In contrast the current system, seems to incentivise only property barons, mining magnates, and telemarketers — often groups that actively detract from public good.
1.7.8.2. Con: The previous claim provided a number of examples that helped to clarify the broad category of "meaningful to society."
1.7.8.3. Con: There is much labor that is important — child care, art, charity, self-education and improvement — but is not compensated under the present form of capitalism. UBI would allow persons who raise children, create beauty, and do good works to sustain themselves without being forced to spend the majority of their time doing other jobs just to support themselves.
1.7.8.3.1. Con: The first beneficiary of self-education is oneself. If it is beneficial for also for a second party it can make a decision to sponsor the hopeful student. This ensures that the studies are progressing, as it costs funds. With unconditional UBI for all, this reality check won't work.
1.7.8.3.1.1. Con: Nobody and nothing exists in a vacuum - self-improvement and self-education will inevitably benefit one's neighbours as well, through processes as simple and basic as information and knowledge spreading through conversation, as well as whatever active uses the person puts their education, improvement and knowledge towards.
1.7.8.4. Con: A UBI will distribute the decision of what is meaningful work. Instead of having politicians decide what is "meaningful" and design policies that actually achieve the objective, you have lots of people with the ability to spend time/money on what they think is important. In that context it's not vague and ill defined, it's the average of the population's well-defined preferences.
1.7.9. Con: The State should pay people for particularly valuable contributions rather than pay everyone for \(potentially\) doing nothing.
1.7.9.1. Pro: A targeted welfare system is more able to account for this.
1.7.9.1.1. Pro: Specific benefits might be paid to single parents, or people working as caretakers for dependent family members.
1.7.9.2. Con: State actors rarely do well with picking which activities are useful or productive. Better to keep the state out of it and let the laws of basic economics and market forces guide such decisions.
1.7.9.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.12.1.3.3.1.
1.7.9.3. Con: There are many accidents that result in valuable contributions. Allowing anyone to be able to try allows more accidents to happen \(not to mention the intentional benefits\). If we don't support people trying and failing, we won't have enough trials to make any significant progress.
1.7.9.3.1. Con: There was no such support US during 19th or 20th centuries but the rate of both economic growth and innovation there was huge during most this centuries. There are plenty of other countries that enjoyed the high growth without having special support for failed entrepreneurs.
1.7.9.3.2. Pro: In many areas, "practice makes perfect" or at least has a positive relationship with quality of work produced. Permitting people to practice and indulge in areas of interest to them, even if it does not initially produce valuable goods, may permit valuable work to be produced in the future, or provide inspiration that may lead to societally beneficial innovation.
1.7.9.4. Con: There is likely to be a high amount of bureaucracy and fraud that would be involved in tracking every hour of care, cooking, helping out neighbours and then rewarding these actions.
1.7.9.5. Con: If someone would be otherwise engaged in criminal activity, doing nothing is in itself an improvement. UBI may well make those who would otherwise engage in crimes less desperate.
1.7.9.6. Con: It is impossible to track how many hours of reading and research and leisure, sometimes spread over decades, went into the making of one invention.
1.7.9.7. Pro: Even if the UBI rewards persons who currently perform unpaid \(but valuable\) contributions, such as artists or stay at home parents, it rewards everyone equally. Thus it is not a fair form of pay that recognises certain unpaid contributions are more valuable and laborious than others.
1.7.9.8. Pro: If society currently under-appreciates the contributions of important sources of unpaid labour, such as stay at home parents, a UBI may provide them some financial compensation but its universal nature denies them any form of specific recognition of their labour.
1.7.10. Con: Money is a reflection of the value of an item. By providing a basic income, we inject capital into the system without any work to support it. The only means to do this is to allow inflation by creating money out of thin air, or taking that money from those who have worked to create its value in the first place.
1.7.10.1. Con: Dollars are already created "out of thin air" by fractional reserve banking. Even if a UBI was funded by fiat funding, this could merely be replacing inflation profiting banks by inflation profiting society in general. There are several models of UBI, and at least one, proposed by Robert A Heinlein in "Beyond This Horizon" and "For Us, the Living" demonstrates this, using the replacement of fractional reserve banking with a form of [social credit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_credit).
1.7.10.1.1. Pro: Injecting money into the economy by giving it to common people may have more positive consequences overall than doing so via banks.
1.7.10.1.1.1. Con: A universal rule of giving money to people who are capable to contribute work to the economy undermines the economy, thus the capability of society to give money to those who are not capable to do so.
1.7.10.1.2. Con: Douglas' ideas on [Social Credit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_credit) do not appear to be well respected in the economics world, and are radical/questionable on their face. There is little to no support offered as to why this radical system should be accepted merely based on "someone wrote a book about it."
1.7.10.1.3. Con: Although fractional reserve banking can be misused, and the dollars *technically* are "created out of thin air," that is misleading. Fractional reserve banking is based on trust in good economic/investment decisions: banks use their expertise in business analysis to determine good investments from bad investments. Thus, service is supplied to compensate for the "new money," which limits "bad inflation" \(which occurs when the amount of money increases faster than value/welfare/wellbeing\).
1.7.10.1.4. Con: It is a myth that fractional banking "creates dollars our of thin air". Fractional banking merely prescribes how many real dollars a bank must hold to to ensure liquidity for dollar denominated deposits. Credit in a bank's book dollars is not the same as real dollars, it is still only credit exposed to \(in normal times: low\) credit risk.
1.7.10.2. Con: Money isn't a reflection of the value of an good, it is the reflection of the humam perception of the value of an good. That is why water along a river is priceless but water in the desert it is valuable. This statement was for a couple of centuries contested because it doesn't explained why the prices of goods changes, like bitcoins for example.
1.7.10.3. Pro: We cannot eat money. In hyperinflation people learn that money is but colourful paper slips of miniscule intrinsic value.
1.7.11. Con: What provides meaning to society often diverges from what provides meaning to an individual. Greed can provide intense meaning to an individual but provide no meaningful contributions to society.
1.7.11.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.7.11.2. Con: Personal motivations are not necessarily antithetical to society - surely the amount of work done by volunteers for charities and endeavours such as Wikipedia and this site show that this does not need to be the case.
1.7.12. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.7.13. Con: A UBI disincentives charitable giving and co-operating with others, because the program seeks to satiate the basic needs that charity and co-operation otherwise address. As such, a UBI will create increased selfishness and isolation in society.
1.7.13.1. Con: This would be true of any welfare system, not just UBI.
1.7.13.1.1. Con: A UBI would be far more extensive than existing welfare programs, and therefore disincentive charity and co-operation to a much greater extent.
1.7.13.1.2. Con: Yes, all welfare systems are bad. It's extra true for UBI because with most welfare systems there are gaps. So there are still some people who need charity. The point of UBI is to eliminate the need for all charity, so it will cause even more isolation.
1.7.13.2. Pro: Welfare systems have been show to disincentive charity, as comparisons between European countries and the United States [show](https://www.investors.com/politics/editorials/selfish-americans-more-charitable-than-europeans/).
1.7.13.3. Pro: There would be less incentive to co-operate with others to contribute to society if basic economic needs are automatically met.
1.7.13.4. Pro: Since people have already paid to help people they feel less of a need to help out with charity. They also have less money remaining after their money has been taken away by taxes. Giving to charity would be double.
1.7.13.5. Con: At best a UBI only sates basic needs within developed countries. In the rest of the world there would still be a clear need for charitable giving.
1.7.14. Pro: With basic livelihood guaranteed people will be more able to engage with politics and the democratic process.
1.7.14.1. Pro: A UBI ensures that no citizen is forced to choose between earning and income and exercising their democratic right to vote in an election.
1.7.14.2. Pro: Taking fewer shifts at work may allow people to vote or attend a town hall or a school board meeting. This allows lower income employees time to be more politically engaged in their communities so that they are better able to represent their own interests in government. [\(Berry 2012\)](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/)
1.7.14.2.1. Pro: Increased political engagement is good because it allows political forces to represent the people more easily. Increased political engagement will also help increase trust in government, leading to a better civil service.
1.7.14.2.2. Con: Lower income employees lack political representation due to the lobbying power that being wealthy brings, not because they lack the time to be politically engaged.
1.7.14.2.2.1. Con: Lobbying power is definitely not the only reason. [Turnout inequality](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2005/may/14/election2005.constitution) is a reality in most countries, thus regardless of the reasons \(though money and consequently time are probably among the factors\) the poor vote less and are less politically engaged.
1.7.14.2.2.2. Pro: Universal basic income will allow more people to lobby.
1.7.14.2.3. Con: Supplementing the income of higher-paid employees with a UBI \(since they would have received zero welfare beforehand\) gives them a greater ability to work less and increases their representation in government, which may work against the interests of lower income persons.
1.7.14.2.3.1. Con: High-paid employees usually have [high](https://www.forbes.com/sites/moneywisewomen/2012/03/21/average-america-vs-the-one-percent/#75150af22395) levels of [spending](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/dec/25/wealthy-americans-living-paycheck-to-paycheck-income-paying-bills), therefore a UBI that is above the poverty line is unlikely to cover their lifestyle if they work less.
1.7.14.2.3.2. Con: -> See 1.6.1.1.2.
1.7.14.2.3.3. Con: The relative value of the basic income depends on the recipient. For someone who lacks political and economic power, a supplement of $4000 might change their behaviour. Giving the same supplement to someone who earns more than $100k a year won't buy them any representation they don't already have.
1.7.14.3. Pro: A UBI would strengthen democracy. On the one hand, people would not feel as forgotten, but rather as a part of society. On the other hand since there would more free time - it would be a real oppurtunity to take part in democratic processes.
1.7.14.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.11.2.6.1.1.
1.7.14.3.2. Pro: Citizens would have more free time to become politically active/involved.
1.7.14.3.2.1. Pro: Engaged and involved citizens hold the government accountable and ensure that it is working in the people's best interests.
1.7.14.3.2.2. Con: Even if it'd be so most of them would rather be involved in quite passive ways - by reading the press, attending meetings with politicians etc. which could lead to an even further expansion of goverment control over them.
1.7.14.3.3. Pro: The disenfranchised might feel as though somebody actually cares about them, which might even encourage some of the more troubled people to seek help.
1.7.14.4. Pro: If sustainable, a UBI helps keep people invested in the society that is supporting them, leading to less likely chance of revolutionary action.
1.7.14.5. Pro: A UBI might encourage more and better civic engagement by lowering personal financial costs to core democratic activities \(not necessarily 'political' nor 'partisan'\) most notably Jury Duty, where current participation is subject to limitations by lost wages from participating at all, and speed is incentivized as well to minimize said lost wages by those who participate.
1.7.15. Pro: Many pilot UBI schemes have found that it not only improves upon income-dependent metrics \(such as health\) but that it [also increases social cohesion](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/universal-basic-income-wi_b_8354072.html) through community empowerment and mobilisation.
1.7.15.1. Pro: Alaska's long-running UBI scheme has surely contributed to it having the highest rate of well-being of any State across indicators that [include social and community well-being](http://cdn2.hubspot.net/hub/162029/file-2513997715-pdf/Well-Being_Index/2014_Data/Gallup-Healthways_State_of_American_Well-Being_2014_State_Rankings.pdf?t=1437575309454).
1.7.15.2. Pro: A UBI gives individuals the agency to invest in their own communities.
1.7.15.2.1. Con: The decision to invest in a community is a personal choice. A UBI does not guarantee the extra funds will be used towards renewing the community.
1.7.15.2.1.1. Pro: It is plausible an individual in a poorer community will use the extra funds from their UBI payments to move into a better quality community rather than to stay and make improvements.
1.7.15.2.1.2. Pro: For many individuals in poorer areas, they are surviving with minimal necessities. Funds from the UBI are more likely to go towards more urgent needs like food, shelter, and healthcare than the community at large.
1.7.15.2.1.2.1. Con: The fact that each poor individual is able to afford better food, better housing conditions or better healthcare will necessarily have a positive impact in the whole community.
1.7.15.2.2. Pro: Increased amounts of free time better allows individuals to invest their human capital in improving the community and helping other community members.
1.7.15.2.2.1. Con: It would be more effective simply to invest in those communities directly.
1.7.15.2.2.1.1. Con: Top down and hierarchical investment in communities, often driven by people that do not actually understand specific necessities, are less likely to result in valuable changes for the communities.
1.7.15.2.3. Con: It would be more effective simply to invest in those communities directly.
1.7.15.2.3.1. Pro: This way, it is easier to know the money invested is going towards directly improving the quality of life within these communities. Comparatively, with the UBI an individual can choose to use the extra income for other personal interests unrelated to improving the community.
1.7.15.2.3.1.1. Pro: This investment can go towards better housing, educational programs, and community initiatives that help to uplift the residents as a whole.
1.7.15.2.3.2. Con: Hierarchical investment in communities driven by people that don't actually live with the consequences of those investment decisions are less likely to result in community changes that the people in those communities would actually value for themselves.
1.7.15.2.4. Con: It equally gives them more time to engage in anti-social behavior, which is also supported by a UBI.
1.7.15.2.4.1. Con: This claim implies there is some inherent moral failing that applies to the economically disadvantaged and not to all other people with wealth. This is socially irresponsible.
1.7.15.2.4.2. Con: There is zero evidence that suggests people are more likely to engage in anti-social behavior than they are in positive community building efforts.
1.7.15.2.5. Pro: People who receive a UBI are more capable of contributing value than those who do not have an income above the poverty line.
1.7.15.2.5.1. Con: Giving individuals more agency to help also increases their ability to harm as well. You have given them a tool, there is no telling what they will do with it.
1.7.15.2.5.1.1. Con: This argues that the only thing that keeps people from destructive rampages is work. Work does not keep people from going on destructive rampages.
1.7.15.2.5.1.1.1. Con: An unemployed, economically strained populace can and frequently has lead to violent revolution and unrest against the Establishment. While work is not the only thing keeping people from lashing out, long-term economic hardship tends to be a major grievance that causes the populace to revolt.
1.7.15.2.5.1.1.1.1. Con: A UBI would help to reduce long-term economic hardship in many cases.
1.7.15.2.5.1.1.1.2. Con: It is difficult to see how an UBI would create an unemployed, economically strained populace.
1.7.15.2.5.1.2. Con: As presented in other claims in this debate, there are reasons to believe the net effect would be positive.
1.7.15.2.5.2. Pro: Many people would provide voluntary work if they were more financially able and/or less busy/stressed.
1.7.15.2.6. Pro: Having to rely on government investment in local communities is problematic.
1.7.15.2.6.1. Pro: People in these communities lack the resource to lobby their local officials for change.
1.7.15.2.6.1.1. Pro: They don't have the time or the money to engage in the political process.
1.7.15.2.6.1.2. Pro: Given that many of the poor are disenfranchised, the government ignores their situation and has no incentive to invest in impoverished communities.
1.7.15.2.6.2. Pro: Governments are often less capable of understanding where aid is most needed.
1.7.15.2.6.2.1. Pro: Diverting welfare funds to a UBI helps these people buy the goods that they do actually need.
1.7.15.2.6.2.2. Pro: Governments often do not include members of these communities.
1.7.15.2.6.2.2.1. Pro: The impact of this is that even if politicians then wanted to help members of these communities, their attempts to do so would be inefficient at best, and actively harmful at worst, as they do not understand the needs and concerns of the people living in these areas.
1.7.15.2.6.3. Con: [The freerider problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_rider_problem) makes individual investment in the community harder. The central, coercive, coordinating mechanism of the government is necessary to make community projects work.
1.7.15.2.6.3.1. Con: Freeriding is vastly outweighed because people feel the urgency to change the unequal systems that currently exist.
1.7.15.2.7. Pro: Under a UBI, investment within communities would better enable a process of organic change.
1.7.15.2.7.1. Pro: UBI policies shift the provision of welfare from a national level to a local level, where individuals are more capable of investing in their communities.
1.7.15.2.7.1.1. Pro: With money from a UBI, individuals within at-risk communities have the ability to invest in fixing urgent problems in areas the government has not helped \(e.g. [Flint, Michigan water crisis](http://time.com/4269300/flint-task-force-report-water-crisis/)\).
1.7.15.2.7.1.2. Pro: People are more responsible for investments they believe serve their own interests.
1.7.15.2.7.1.3. Pro: It is easier for individuals to petition local institutions and local leaders for change.
1.7.15.2.7.1.3.1. Pro: Local community leaders are closer and more reachable to people than national politicians.
1.7.15.2.7.1.3.2. Pro: Local leaders are heavily involved in the community, which makes them accountable not only to their community but also to themselves.
1.7.15.2.7.1.4. Pro: Leadership in local institutions is based on real abilities to manage money, instead of connections and nepotism \(which is usual at higher levels\). This happens because citizens have an interest in ensuring their community's success.
1.7.15.2.7.1.4.1. Con: Leadership of local government is often deeply unaccountable, due to the low stakes of the powers that they have, and the inability of media organisations to individually scrutinise each local government. As a result, corruption can flourish at a local level even when it would quickly be stamped out at a national level.
1.7.15.2.7.1.4.2. Con: Citizens have an interest in ensuring the success of their country, state, province or city, too. It is unclear why this would be stronger for very local government than for more powerful and higher-ranking government bodies.
1.7.15.2.7.2. Pro: Individuals that live and work in these communities best understand the needs of these communities. As such, they are able to prioritize which areas to invest in first and how to go about investing in the community.
1.7.15.2.8. Pro: The UBI provides individuals with more money to buy goods from local businesses. This leads to more money circulating in these communities and contributes to their economic revitalization.
1.7.15.2.8.1. Con: A UBI does not guarantee that individuals will use this additional money within their own communities. It is plausible that an individual will continue to shop online or outside of the community for certain goods and services.
1.7.15.2.8.2. Pro: Welfare policy often forces consumers to buy goods from non-local private companies that the government associates with.
1.7.15.2.9. Pro: When individuals invest more in their communities and buy goods from local businesses, they flourish and private investors are more willing to invest in them [\(Greenup 2015\)](http://www.geektime.com/2015/12/17/how-a-universal-basic-income-could-fuel-entrepreneurship/).
1.7.15.2.10. Pro: A UBI would give more people the time to focus on others; earning extra money would be optional and desirable to many but many would be happy to exist on the UBI and help others - either individuals \(eg: carers\) or the wider population.
1.7.15.2.10.1. Pro: Altruistic individuals would be more able to put forward charity or other altruistic forms of work without having to worry about their own maintenance or profitability.
1.7.15.2.11. Pro: A UBI can be a great method to facilitate full expression of individual potentials especially in communities where there is a large disparity of control and ownership of commodity resources among the members.
1.7.15.3. Con: A UBI may only exacerbate existing tendencies in social cohesion. In relatively communal countries it may build ties; [but in individualistic ones it may actually increase the level of individualism](https://www.opendemocracy.net/transformation/max-harris/will-universal-basic-income-make-us-lonely).
1.7.15.4. Con: Providing all citizens with the means to be self-sufficient decreases the need for individuals to engage with the community through work.
1.7.15.4.1. Con: Even if citizens do not engage with the community through work, there are many other ways: local associations, volunteering, leisure activities, sport, local festivities, etc.
1.7.15.5. Con: Redirecting welfare spending from community services \(such as [soup kitchens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soup_kitchen) or free healthcare clinics\) towards individual income supplements would erode much community infrastructure that builds ties between strangers.
1.7.15.5.1. Con: When every citizen has the means to pay for food, education or healthcare, some of these services are no longer necessary.
1.7.15.6. Pro: A UBI will afford those with lower incomes more flexibility, which can allow them to organize politically. You could consider it a "strike fund" that allows labor and citizens to advocate for themselves.
1.7.15.7. Con: Pilots are not universal. Basic income works well for small populations that benefit from the 'hard' economy in the rest of the world, and in developing countries where income is very low.
1.7.16. Con: A person can only perform such meaningful contributions if society provides them the chance and capability to. It could be possible that when a UBI is implemented, governments might limit certain activities.
1.7.16.1. Pro: One example is higher education. The government might not feel that it is worth maintaining universities if people do not get jobs out of them due to a UBI. This would mean that fewer schools are created and thus people have less access to higher education.
1.7.17. Pro: By de-stigmatizing being out of paid employment, a UBI would encourage a value shift towards putting less value on financial success and more on a person's contribution to society.
1.7.17.1. Con: It wouldn't de-stigmatize being out of paid employment, that person would still be considered a leech on society because society has to go to work and do something worthwhile so that person can do whatever it is they're doing that has such a low value to society that no one will pay them for it.
1.7.17.2. Con: The current stigma on "unemployment" isn't on the classical economic definition of unemployed, i.e. not having a job but being willing to work. The stigma is on people who don't have a job, don't want one, and collect unemployment payments. Stay at home parents are not stigmatized \(at least not for this reason\) even though they don't have a traditional job, and don't want one, because they don't collect payments.
1.7.17.2.1. Con: The stigma is also on people who lost their jobs, cannot find a new one, have to perform undignifying reporting to welfare stations, getting monitored, etc.
1.7.18. Con: Only a certain percentage of a given population would be interested in participating in these higher, social level activities. Some percentage of the population will seek a path of least resistance, and rely on the UBI exclusively. This group of people, who do not naturally take up hobbies or engage in the community, find themselves in a life where they're not really required for society to function, and must face the realities of a very bleak existence void of purpose and urgency.
1.7.18.1. Con: Many people who are unable to participate in the labor force effectively currently find themselves in a bleak existence, one that UBI would make less bleak.
1.7.19. Con: In this context, a UBI transfers the risk of failure of individuals engaged in these endeavors to the public. The taxpayer should not have to fund people who choose to pursue fields in which they are not qualified to make a living.
1.8. Con: -> See 1.6.11.2.
1.9. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.10. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.
1.11. Pro: A UBI will help the labor market adapt to inevitable disruptions caused by advancements in automation and artificial intelligence.
1.11.1.
