Con: A UBI is used as a way of merely making [techno-capitalism](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/may/16/universal-basic-income-equality-tech-silicon-valley) — a system that fuels inequalities \([Suarez-Villa, p. 162](https://books.google.es/books?id=UjMHDAAAQBAJ&pg=PA162&lpg=PA162&dq=technocapitalism+and+inequality&source=bl&ots=Iej_CriTK6&sig=eB_Qycqzfe2b7lS6RswdkJyr7FA&hl=it&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiL7-DeuojUAhWJjCwKHVhaAcYQ6AEIKTAA#v=onepage&q=technocapitalism%20and%20inequality&f=false)\) — more tolerable for people, instead of addressing the root causes of exploitation and economic inequality.
1.11.1.1. Pro: Silicon Valley [strongly supports](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/jun/22/silicon-valley-universal-basic-income-y-combinator) a UBI.
1.11.1.1.1. Pro: [Silicon Valley's](https://www.wired.com/2017/03/silicon-valley-lawmakers-1-trillion-plan-save-trump-country/) congressional representative Ro Khanna has proposed a 1 trillion dollar earned income tax credit expansion as a form of basic income, doubling the income going to low-income families.
1.11.1.1.2. Con: Appeal to authority is logical fallacy. Silicon Valley's support doesn't provide justification, as they are neither political, nor economic experts.
1.11.1.1.2.1. Pro: A motive for Silicon Valley to support UBI can simply be explained by the notion that Silicon Valley just wants everyone to be able to buy their products.
1.11.1.1.2.2. Pro: The motive does not gel either. By pushing for a UBI, they are also pushing for a higher general tax rate on themselves.
1.11.1.1.2.3. Con: If one takes a free market approach to equality and growth then one must accept that Silicon Valley \(representing a huge market share of automation\) must be a source of good 'knowledge' on such a topic in a free market.
1.11.1.1.3. Con: The fact that Silicon Valley supports UBI is a given since they are the main drivers behind the techo-capitalism and would thus, reasonably, support policies supporting its continued growth.
1.11.1.1.4. Con: This is conformation bias. A number of local governments and organisations strongly support UBI and have no interest in techno-capitalism.
1.11.1.2. Con: If techno-capitalism provides the impetus to implement an effective universal welfare system, that would help address economic inequality directly.
1.11.1.2.1. Con: Actually, income inequality might increase with a UBI because there would be no more societal pressure to create meaningless jobs. Which means there will be an automation boom and significantly fewer jobs; meaning income inequality would actually increase. So, while covering the basic needs of the majority of the population would certainly pacify it, the political and relative economic power of the vast majority would be greatly reduced.
1.11.1.2.1.1. Pro: This would divide society into two camps, those solely reliant on UBI and those working or owning along with a UBI. That is, the powerful and the weak whom face an uphill battle maintaining rights and power balance in the long term. Specifically, jobs become scarcer and capital is more concentrated due to growing tech monopolies and automation. On such a trajectory any safe guards fall by the wayside where convenient for elites.
1.11.1.2.1.1.1. Con: We already have those classes, and the problems that come with them, it's just right now the weaker class gets nothing.
1.11.1.3. Con: It might provide people with time to rally and create movements to solve inequality. The security provided by a basic income could allow people to become politically more vocal since they'd perhaps be less cautious about publicly holding a belief/opinion opposed to that of their bosses.
1.11.1.4. Con: Basic income would still have merits if it helps during the transition to a post-scarcity stage where we wouldn't even need money.
1.11.1.5. Con: Those root causes are much easier to address once everyone is able to stop losing sleep over not having a stable living income; which can be provided by way of a UBI.
1.11.1.6. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.4.
1.11.1.7. Con: The fact that a UBI is not a universal panacea is not an argument against a UBI.
1.11.1.7.1. Con: But it's not just that it's not a cure all, it actually creates new problems. The newest problem is that affordability will not be fixed, and the rich will still get richer by simply hiking prices of goods.
1.11.1.8. Con: While there may be structural inequalities in varying degrees \(depends on the country\), the fact is people are not equally skilled and success \(as measured in whatever way\) tends to accumulate to a small set of individuals. In this sense you can't take away all the causes of inequalities no matter what you do.
1.11.1.8.1. Con: Inequality is not the only problem a UBI is intended to address; absolute poverty and related illnesses and fatalities are also a critical issue a UBI would go a great way towards addressing.
1.11.1.9. Pro: A UBI designed by the current ruling class as a way to prolong the current socio-economic order \(infinite growth, profit-incentivised, private property, competitive based capitalism\) does not take into account the immediate problems with respect to the destruction of the environment, including global warming, pollution, biodiversity loss, etc. Any UBI implemented should be designed by the society as a whole as a means of transitioning to a post-reinforced-scarcity socio-economic system.
1.11.1.10. Pro: The root cause of inequality and affordability needs to be addressed. Otherwise, UBI is but another form of subsidy, loan and minimum wage increase, that makes no guarantee that living is affordable and allows landlords, food providers, gas providers to get rich at the expense of UBI funding sources.
1.11.1.11. Con: The emergence of the internet has turned the average citizen into a source of income for many corporations that essentially sell your identity to advertisers. A UBI would be a reasonable and necessary redistributive structure in light of our current economic framework.
1.11.1.11.1. Con: The general refusal to pay for Internet services resulting in the monetization of user data is not justification for mandating that the services make less money.
1.11.1.12. Pro: A UBI forces humanity into the model where we look to competitive markets and top-down institutions for basic human needs. With automation removing most future jobs, people need to look outside of these systems for long term solutions yet implementing a UBI obstructs this.
1.11.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.18.1.2.
1.11.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.14.1.
1.11.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.14.2.
1.11.5. Con: A UBI removes the ability of the government to fund meaningful programs to help individuals become more productive, such as technical training and higher education.
1.11.5.1. Con: There is no reason a UBI would interfere with such programs. Each can be considered seperately.
1.11.5.1.1. Con: -> See 1.6.11.2.
1.11.5.1.2. Pro: Education and training programs are rarely \(if ever\) viewed as welfare programs.
1.11.5.2. Pro: The UBI might not actually remove this ability to fund, but this may be used as a justification by politicians opposed to such a program. It serves as a way to punish the poor, rather than to meaningfully resolve any actual issue.
1.11.6. Con: This is also true of any other social support network.
1.11.6.1. Con: Other social support networks often don't cater to certain groups. For example religious groups who used to fulfill this role are less likely to support atheists.
1.11.6.1.1. Con: Government social support networks do not have this problem. That would be a breach of the 14th Amendment.
1.11.6.1.2. Con: A Christian who follows the teachings of Christ would not turn away an atheist in need. Individuals  don't have to agree with each other in order to support each other.
1.11.6.1.2.1. Con: Not all religions are Christianity, and not all Christians agree on how to follow the teachings of Christ, nor do all Christians actively and effectively follow said teachings.
1.11.6.2. Con: Other social support networks may be insufficient to support the wholesale retraining of a segment of the population whose job was obsoleted by technology because these support networks are designed to help people in between jobs, with the expectation that other similar jobs would be available in the future. Not so with technology-led obsolescence.
1.11.6.3. Pro: [Workers' Compensation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workers%27_compensation) is a social support network.
1.11.7. Pro: UBI is necessary to combat the fact that AI will be able to do any task better than humans, physical and cognitive. Unlike the previous industrial revolution, both physical and mental jobs will be replaced.
1.11.7.1. Pro: Society will reach a point where general artificial intelligences will be able to learn at a faster rate than humans, thus rendering human labor inefficient and economically impractical.
1.11.7.1.1. Pro: This can already been seen with IBM's artificial intelligence system, [Watson](http://time.com/4960778/computers-smarter-than-humans/). It has the increasing ability to learn and to solve problems that are too complex to maintain in the human mind.
1.11.7.2. Con: The technological singularity is not a 'fact' at all. It is a hypothesised possibility of a future we might live in.
1.11.7.3. Pro: As AI increases, more and more jobs will become obsolete. UBI will only make sense at this point since the majority of people will not be able to make an 'honest' living anymore.
1.11.7.4. Pro: A UBI would enable AI to do work that no longer should be done by humans - binary, repetitive, programmable. This frees humans to do work which is creative, exploratory, self fulling, voluntary, for remuneration of not is their choice as they have a basic income to fulfill basic needs.
1.11.7.4.1. Con: This is pro is contingent on having efficient and useful AI tools to achieve a rational savings enough to create a universal income. Current machine learning allows us to have high level of sales development tools and makerting web software. While the advancements of ecommerce is a popular idea to suggest the removal of work forces and thus rationalize a UBI, there could be more jobs available for people to work that as a result of AI.
1.11.7.5. Pro: This claim does not necessarily rely on the singularity but on the fact that as technology increases the productive capacity of each individual, there will come a time where two trends intersect- first a relatively small number of workers will be able to produce sufficient goods for society and second that there will be a significant number of people who are incapable of developing the skills needed to be economically viable. A UBI is needed if 20% of the population is not economically useful.
1.11.7.6. Pro: Consumers are also workers, so if there are no workers, there are no consumers. A UBI provides money to consumers to keep the economy going, when all the work is automated. The economy exists to provide goods and services to consumers, they are therefore the primary source of money in the economic cycle. [Elon Musk: Automation Will Force Universal Basic Income](https://www.geek.com/tech-science-3/elon-musk-automation-will-force-universal-basic-income-1701217/)
1.11.8. Con: Automation is not bound to a certain place. Given a UBI will require higher taxes, which in turn encourages businesses to outsource automated production, the economy and labour market of the country providing the UBI could suffer.
1.11.8.1. Con: Automation [usually decreases outsourcing](http://www.nearshoreamericas.com/blameautomation-outsourcing-contract-decreases-sharply-report/).
1.11.8.1.1. Con: This is due to the environment in which most studies were performed: developed countries who outsource to countries with cheap human labour. The new scenario talks about companies leaving highly developed country A because B offers the same level of advanced technologies without the extra taxes required for UBI.
1.11.8.2. Pro: Automation increases output. A steady production rate is easier to obtain with automation rather than manual labor. Read the story of "John Henry" to capture the concept.
1.11.8.2.1. Con: Automation does not increase output. Increasing marginal productivity increases output. Consider the marginal situation where a machine and a worker are equally efficient at a task.  Only if one is more efficient than the other is productivity increased. Steady production is irrelevant to output.
1.11.8.2.1.1. Con: Machinery has the potential to work faster and more consistently than a worker. The above point stands if the machine and worker are equally efficient and work equal hours, but it's likely that is not the situation.
1.11.8.3. Con: Not in the case of automated vehicles where taxi drivers and truck drivers would lose jobs to self driving vehicles.  This automation would not be out sourced to any other country.
1.11.8.4. Con: Automation of specific tasks \(such as menial labour - think housekeeping, shelf stacking and so on\) is very location dependent and will decimate the local labour market, providing a UBI will safeguard against the excess of unemployed as the total job pool begins to shrink faster than new jobs can be feasibly created.
1.11.8.4.1. Con: A surprising amount of human intuition and common sense goes into such "menial" tasks, so they will not be rapidly replaced by automation; there will be a transition period at the very least.
1.11.8.4.2. Pro: Automation of many tasks \(and soon professions\) that people felt "safe" is already in process. Automated haulage is less than three years out, [which is set to disrupt over 1.5m jobs](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2017/feb/16/self-driving-trucks-automation-jobs-trucking-industry) between the loss of haulage drivers and the economy that has built up around them in the US alone.
1.11.8.5. Con: This assumes that production will be taxed, which is not necessarily the case.
1.11.8.6. Con: This assumes that this is requires an increase in the tax burden. It will be most probably a shift in the tax burden that will not impact the location of production.
1.11.8.6.1. Con: This is assertive in nature. It is necessary to elaborate a bit more on why it is more probable that there will be a shift in tax burden than an increase in the burden in general.
1.11.8.7. Con: This assumes that you would tax production, when in fact you should tax income. Because of reduced costs, due to automation and outsourcing, businesses would have much higher incomes and therefore they would pay much higher taxes. This makes the location of automated production irrelevant when the businesses need to report their income regardless of the place the production is made.
1.11.8.8. Con: There is an assumption here which does not make perfect sense. There is no real argument behind "a UBI will require higher taxes": speaking only on the accounting side, it all depends on the amount of UBI.
1.11.9. Pro: The government has an obligation to ensure all citizens have the capacity to fulfill basic needs such as food, housing, education and healthcare. A UBI allows states to provide this basic level of welfare despite a fluctuating job market.
1.11.9.1. Pro: A UBI fulfills this obligation, while also maintaining individuals' ability to make choices and have control over their own lives \(unlike some forms of targeted welfare\).
1.11.9.1.1. Con: Individuals never have absolute choice in any society; our choices are constrained in the particular instance where they conflict with others' interests \(e.g. limitations on free speech\), or where they gave up their interests \(e.g. through taxation\) to enable our choices.
1.11.9.1.2. Con: As the state is the caretaker of collectivity, it ought to prioritize the efficient expenditure of taxpayers' money in the aggregate, over the marginal exercise of choice.
1.11.9.1.3. Con: Individuals are not stripped of all choice under a targeted welfare system. Individuals have a basis of good health, sufficient savings and education to make choices that significantly improve their quality of life \(e.g. taking a part-time vocational degree to upgrade skills\).
1.11.9.1.4. Con: Some individuals would [spend poorly,](https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2013-12-04/four-reasons-a-guaranteed-income-won-t-work) making it necessary to reinstate various welfare programs to help these people make better financial decisions.
1.11.9.1.4.1. Pro: Reinstating welfare programs would effectively double everyone’s tax bill and eliminate any purported cost savings that Government might claim from implementing the UBI.
1.11.9.1.4.1.1. Con: Given the sheer cost of implementing a UBI, it is unlikely that both a UBI and more traditional welfare programs will be run at the same time.
1.11.9.1.4.2. Pro: It is better for the government to provide a social safety net as individuals don't accurately consider future risks.
1.11.9.1.4.2.1. Pro: Governments have a paternalistic obligation to force individuals to devote their resources towards their safety and health. For example, that is the reason for seatbelt laws.
1.11.9.1.4.2.2. Pro: Individuals might be likely to spend a cash bonus, not anticipating a future harm \(e.g. unexpected illness\). However, if the government provides welfare or subsidized healthcare, this protects individuals from unanticipated harms.
1.11.9.1.4.2.3. Con: People should have the agency to determine their own lives, even if that means that sometimes they will not make the most optimal choice.
1.11.9.1.4.2.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.6.3.
1.11.9.1.4.3. Pro: Some individuals, especially in societies like India or China where expenditure on status symbols is accepted as a practice, would be tempted to expend on largesse e.g. feasts for distant relatives or showy cars, despite it having little utility other than showing off.
1.11.9.1.4.3.1. Con: It seems paternalistic to reject an entire policy for thinking that particular cultures will spend it poorly.
1.11.9.1.4.3.2. Pro: Even if they may not spend it on ostentatious consumption, the likelihood is there - given that individuals in such situations face societal pressures to choose poorly, the state cannot assume that choice would be all that rational or beneficial.
1.11.9.1.4.4. Pro: Some individuals, especially those in poverty who are used to purchasing cheap but comparatively less nutritious meals e.g. pre-packed or fast food, might not be incentivized to change their habits as much as they would if their food stamps were only applicable to fresh foods or “Healthy Choice” labeled meals.
1.11.9.1.4.5. Pro: At-risk families will spend on cigarettes, drugs or alcohol instead of saving up for children’s education or spending on subsidized vocational training courses. This situation is comparatively less likely with in-kind assistance than with a UBI.
1.11.9.1.4.5.1. Con: Households on welfare [statistically do not waste](http://blogs.worldbank.org/impactevaluations/do-poor-waste-transfers-booze-and-cigarettes-no) their income on alcohol and cigarettes. This perception is generally just scare-mongering from conservative groups.
1.11.9.1.4.5.1.1. Con: Even if the majority of individuals on welfare do not spend their money on alcohol and cigarettes, the prevalent perception is that there is a risk that they might do so with a UBI.
1.11.9.1.4.5.1.2. Con: Even if a tiny fraction of a UBI beneficiaries do abuse it, it only takes one sensationalist [news](http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-4076338/The-bizarre-benefit-frauds-2016-including-woman-claiming-superpowers-BLIND-driver.html) story to enable rhetoric against the policy and, more harmfully, against its most vulnerable recipients.
1.11.9.1.4.5.2. Con: Welfare programs perpetuate the stigma that sees being poor as equivalent to being unable to make good decisions or adequately look after yourself or your family.
1.11.9.1.4.5.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.11.9.1.4.5.1.
1.11.9.1.4.5.2.2. Con: The stigma against individuals in poverty exists for a multitude of other reasons, for example lack of familiarity with other cultures or lack of exposure to minority friends.
1.11.9.1.4.5.2.2.1. Con: This stigma or [otherness](https://othersociologist.com/otherness-resources/) exists even in societies that are not racially or culturally diverse, indicating that it has little to do with these things.
1.11.9.1.4.5.2.3. Con: What can eliminate stigma is a removal of the perceived costs the poor place upon the middle-class taxpayers, and welfare is more constrained such that the poor structurally cannot make bad choices.
1.11.9.1.4.5.3. Con: The use of alcohol and drugs is usually strongly linked to the[mental health impacts that result from being poor](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3449359/). A UBI will actually alleviate this phenomenon.
1.11.9.1.4.5.4. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.2.1.1.
1.11.9.1.4.6. Con: This happens in any welfare system where people are allowed to control how they allocate the resources the State gives them.
1.11.9.1.4.6.1. Con: Under a UBI, more individuals - especially those in poverty with substantial pressures affecting their decision calculus - have more income with which to potentially make poor decisions. Under means testing or in-kind assistance, fewer poor decisions will be made on aggregate.
1.11.9.1.4.7. Con: Most states will retain compulsory state-funded education systems to educate young people about sensible ways to spend their UBI payment. This kind of education allows for the provision of advice and support, while maintaining the dignity and autonomy of individuals.
1.11.9.1.4.7.1. Con: Given the high rates of absenteeism, and because the school system educates children \(not their parents who receive UBI payments\) state education is unlikely to guarantee that people will spend their UBI payments sensibly.
1.11.9.1.4.7.2. Con: Much of education-related welfare is concentrated on keeping low-income children in school \(e.g. [No Child Left Behind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No_Child_Left_Behind_Act)\). The Government would likely remove these welfare programmes to fund a UBI. Hence, on the educational advocacy level, UBI and better public education are at least partially mutually exclusive.
1.11.9.1.4.7.2.1. Con: Even if some education-welfare programs are eliminated, if a country has a strong public education that does not necessarily change with the implementation of a [UBI](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/universal-basic-income-finland-ubi-test-scheme-experiment-a7211241.html). That is the case of [Finland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_Finland), for example.
1.11.9.1.4.7.3. Con: Poor spending choices are often the result of coercion or manipulation that cannot be solved by education. Empowering those in poverty with a UBI payout is likely to embolden these oppressive actors, and put those in poverty at a greater risk of manipulation.
1.11.9.1.4.7.3.1. Pro: A stable UBI in the hands of the financially illiterate is a beacon that draws in predatory loansharks or bogus employment middlemen. The advantage of a layer of bureaucracy with means-testing or in-kind assistance is that the Government's audit processes serve as additional check and balance to protect the financially illiterate.
1.11.9.1.4.7.3.1.1. Con: Governments ensure - through various methods - that the conditions for receiving welfare are met, yet that does not prevent people who receive welfare from being the prey of loansharks, scams or any other dubious enterprises.
1.11.9.1.4.8. Con: The mentioned group is not big enought to make an argument. Even if such group people do have to spend money on survival, only a part \(probably small\) of money will be wasted.
1.11.9.1.5. Con: The government has no mandate to provide any sort of welfare at all.
1.11.9.1.5.1. Con: The government has a moral obligation to ensure a basic humane quality of life for those under its care.
1.11.9.2. Con: The obligation on government is to ensure all citizens are able to meet their basic needs. That does not automatically prescribe a UBI as the chosen mechanism to do so. Targeted welfare and in-kind subsidies can do the same - at less extravagant cost.
1.11.9.3. Con: Many of the underlying causes of poverty, such as failing schools, substandard public services or institutionalized racism, are not addressed by a UBI.
1.11.9.3.1. Pro: Funds would be better allocated towards reversing or mitigating these deeply entrenched issues.
1.11.9.3.2. Pro: Since a UBI would tie up a significant amount of government funding, it is unreasonable for the government to assume that they can fix these deeper issues while simultaneously paying for a UBI.
1.11.9.4. Con: The government is responsible to ensure every person has the capacity to fulfill basic needs. Providing those needs is not within the mandate of the government.
1.11.9.5. Con: This is not an argument for a UBI; this is merely an opinion on what is the role of the state and description of UBI. This is not an explanation of why it would be good or bad.
1.11.10. Con: A UBI will not help the market adapt. It will actually make it slower to adapt because there is a buffer/cushion to absorb the shock and lessens the incentive to adapt to new conditions. Pain, not comfort, brings about adaptation and changes in the correction amount and direction.
1.11.10.1. Con: Incentives to new conditions are irrelevant if the new conditions are impossible for human workers to adapt to, as may well be the case for AI/machine automation.
1.11.10.2. Con: Excessive pain can overwhelm individuals rather than forcing adaptation, leading to mental and physical illnesses, and potentially deaths.
1.11.10.3. Con: Pain is undesirable under many moral frameworks \(e.g. utilitarianism, humanism\) and therefore other motivators for adaptation should be used, while strategies which reduce pain \(such as a UBI\) should be implemented where their downsides are manageable.
1.11.11. Pro: In a digitalized world, the data that every individual provides is valuable but they receive little or no compensation for this data. A UBI would compensate individuals for this contribution to society.
1.11.12. Con: By removing opposition to automation, UBI could increase the rate of job loss. The faster this happens, the more chance there is of destabilising society as vast swathes of the population become unemployed and lose routine and purpose in their life.
1.11.12.1. Con: Routine and purpose can be achieved outside of an income-generating job.
1.11.12.2. Con: Many people do not feel a sense of purpose in their current jobs, and find them a bothersome rather than comforting routine.
1.11.12.3. Con: Underemployed \(especially casually employed\) people do not find either a sense of routine or purpose in their jobs, as their continuing employment is uncertain and/or does not provide enough money to reliably subsist on.
1.11.13. Pro: Significant disruptions in the labour market are inevitable. A UBI is necessary to reduce the harm of these disruptions.
1.11.13.1. Con: The presumption that technological change will inexorably create pressure on employment is not demonstrated while economic history suggests higher global productivity is not detrimental to strong employment. This tends to make UBI pointless in that respect.
1.11.13.1.1. Con: A UBI would not create redundant jobs, but rather allow people a better chance to find or create a needed job.
1.11.13.1.2. Con: A UBI could be beneficial to absorb the short term changes in the labor market dynamic, even if in the long term society would be able to adapt without UBI.
1.11.13.2. Con: There is still a scarcity of resources–technology isn't advanced enough to produce everything–making markets necessary, making human labor necessary. Removing market incentives by redistributing wealth will cause an undersupply of labor.
1.11.13.2.1. Con: This is the first time in human history where resources on a global scale are not scarce, just unequally held/distributed.
1.11.13.2.1.1. Con: Virtually no resource is infinite so they have the quality of “scarcity” even when their amount or accessibility has increased.
1.11.13.2.2. Con: If UBI will cause an undersupply of labor as claimed then wages will go up to get the needed labor. That's exactly the goal of UBI; increase wages above that needed for basics like housing, food, clothing, healthcare, etc. The amount of UBI has to be linked to a basic standard of living but not so high as to be detrimental to economic growth.
1.11.13.3. Con: Our society is not yet capable of the level of automated production required to sustain UBI. Nor do we, yet, have a level of unemployment resulting from automated production that would warrant the implementation of UBI.
1.11.13.3.1. Con: Automated production is not required for UBI. Whilst the growth of automation may lead to greater unemployment, a "sufficient" level of unemployment isn't a requirement for it to be a benefit. Nor is a certain level of automation needed to finance it: whilst automation would represent a concentration of money in the hands of capital, the existing structures could finance UBI in place of means-tested welfare.
1.11.13.3.2. Pro: While a UBI might have merit if and when when automation has eliminated most human labour, it is decades too early to implement a UBI. Government economic policies with huge effects on taxation and the incentive to work should not be implemented until there is a pressing need.
1.11.13.3.2.1. Con: We are well on our way to wide-spread job loss as a result of automation. [Some reports](https://www.usatoday.com/story/money/2017/11/29/automation-could-kill-73-million-u-s-jobs-2030/899878001/) say that the United States will face job losses of up to 73 million within the next 12 years \(by 2030\).
1.11.13.3.2.1.1. Pro: If the plans to take care of aging population are not put in place soon, the pace of disruption with AI and Automation will create havoc with their lives. UBI is a fair and predictable vehicle.
1.11.13.3.2.1.2. Con: The cited report mentions job loss, but not net job loss. In the same article, it mentions that the real challenge will be retraining workers to perform new jobs that will be created.
1.11.13.3.3. Con: -> See 1.11.13.3.2.1.
1.11.13.3.4. Con: Today is it very difficult for even highly-educated, experienced, hard-working people to get jobs. Currently we have about 22% unemployment rate in the US: [www.shadowstats.com](http://www.shadowstats.com/alternate_data/unemployment-charts) You can also visit LinkedIn to view countless stories of qualified candidates looking for 1+ years with no success.
1.11.13.3.5. Con: Given the current wealth disparities in developed countries a more proper distribution of income would indeed render a UBI affordable.
1.11.13.3.6. Con: Production does not have to be automated to sustain UBI, only efficient. Unemployment would be very high if not for existing UBI alternatives put in place without a conscious public debate. We increased red tape and regulation to slow the increases in production efficiency, dramatically increased jobs of servitude, and increased lowest level marketing jobs. A UBI addresses the pressures that created these trends, allowing waste to be rolled back and fruits of efficiency to be broadly shared.
1.11.13.3.7. Con: If we wait until there is widespread job loss, it is already too late. Preventative maintenance in the form of UBI would benefit the government in the long run, as all of those people who are unemployed due to automation will be relying on government assistance to survive. It’s far more affordable to streamline a single program than to rely on ten different ones.
1.11.13.4. Con: Existing mechanisms — such as retraining and reeducation — have been sufficient for society as a whole to adapt to previous changes in technology and work culture.
1.11.13.4.1. Con: -> See 1.4.14.1.3.1.2.
1.11.13.4.2. Con: In the past this was indeed possible, but the jobs taken by technology are many and they are usually complex. It would be like asking a shoemaker to reeducate as a mathematician. It would only be possible when provided with the financial freedom of UBI.
1.11.13.4.2.1. Con: Skill retraining requires an education system capable of understanding which skills are going to be necessary and valuable tomorrow.
1.11.13.4.2.1.1. Con: True, although there are ways to forecast what skills will have high value.
1.11.13.4.3. Con: Workers would need a UBI to support them while they go through retraining and reeducation.
1.11.13.4.4. Con: In the past, it was true to that that retraining could help, but the pace and quantity of job destruction now from automation means that at some point, the differential between job loss and creation won't be recoverable. The idea of requiring a job to pay for the right to live will become more and more antiquated.
1.11.13.4.5. Con: Relying on an already out-dated and, entirely obsolete school system to train you for the workforce 'like the good ol' days' will not do anymore. People don't care if you have a piece of paper saying you have studied something.
1.11.13.4.5.1. Pro: More and more employers are looking for experienced workers. If you want to get experience before getting a job you might start personal projects and volunteering your time — time that you would not have if you have a full time job. A UBI makes this process much easier.
1.11.13.4.5.2. Pro: There is an increased interest in a candidate's portfolio in platforms such as [GitHub](https://github.com/), complementing the background and path displayed on their CV.
1.11.13.4.5.3. Con: An outdated education is better than none at all.
1.11.13.4.6. Con: If and when a General Artificial Intelligence is developed, there will be no job that cannot be replicated by a machine.
1.11.13.4.7. Pro: Past technological advances such as the tractor or cotton gin eliminated many of jobs in agriculture, but did not result in mass unemployment in the long term, and allowed the expansion of other sectors of the economy such as healthcare or entertainment.
1.11.13.5. Pro: A UBI allows workers to maintain dignity and pay bills while training for new skills higher in demand in the market dynamically, rather than playing the "skills-based education" lottery.
1.11.14. Con: Allowing advancements in automation and artificial intelligence to substitute for human-generated productivity puts your nation at a disadvantage compared to nations who implement both.
1.11.15. Con: A UBI would be an effective solution to disruptions of labor by mechanization replacing human workers. But it does not offset that future hoped for condition here in the present.
1.11.16. Con: As old jobs are destroyed, new jobs have always been created to take their place. A UBI is not necessary for this.
1.11.16.1. Pro: The [US labour market](http://www.nationalreview.com/article/436621/universal-basic-income-ubi-terrible-idea) has added 80 million net jobs since computers were introduced \(in the 1960s\) and 25 million net jobs since the advent of the Internet \(in the 1990s\).
1.11.16.1.1. Con: Since the current technological changes that are occurring are very much different then what we have seen in the past, we cannot assume the rising of the number of jobs is going to continue as predicted by the past.
1.11.16.1.2. Con: Since the 1960s, the US population has increased by approx 132 million, so the number of jobs per capita has actually decreased. [US population by year](http://www.multpl.com/united-states-population/table)
1.11.16.2. Pro: The US labour market presently employs [around 60% of working age individuals](http://www.nationalreview.com/article/436621/universal-basic-income-ubi-terrible-idea), which is actually slightly higher than the pre-WW2 average.
1.11.16.2.1. Con: -> See 1.11.16.1.1.
1.11.16.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.1.1.1.3.1.
1.11.16.4. Con: A properly-implemented UBI acts as an economic stimulus, slowing the loss of jobs and increasing their replacement rates, thus weakening and shortening recessions.
1.11.16.5. Con: It is different this time. When people were outperformed in repetitive manual labor there was repetitive cognitive labor to harbor them \([manufacturing industry to service industry](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=WSKi8HfcxEk&t=1m23s)\). We are still adapting to having been outperformed in repetitive cognitive tasks while on the verge of being [outperformed in varying / complex cognitive tasks](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Pq-S557XQU&t=11m10s).
1.11.16.5.1. Con: Technological revolution has been faced with this argument at every era. As with previous times, the conception of current technology is hyperbolic and out of touch with the reality. To quote from Yann Lecun, a leading AI academic, "Despite these astonishing advances, we are a long way from machines that are as intelligent as humans - or even rats".
1.11.16.5.2. Con: Productivity and employment data over recent decades does not support that contemporary technology is having a net negative impact on the number of jobs. In fact, productivity has been completely stagnant or even declining in most OECD countries.
1.11.16.5.3. Pro: The [near](https://www.aarp.org/auto/trends-lifestyle/info-2018/future-driverless-cars-fd.html) [advent](https://www.fool.com/investing/2017/10/22/how-far-along-is-self-driving-car-technology-reall.aspx) of [self-driving vehicles](https://www.investors.com/news/technology/self-driving-car-waymo/) is evidence of this. Once they become mainstream, the entire transportation industry will be shaken, with millions losing their primary employment.
1.11.16.6. Pro: Though many current jobs are being replaced by automation, new job positions open up as time goes on, such as robot design and maintenance.
1.11.16.6.1. Con: The number of design, and maintenance jobs created is unlikely to be anywhere near the number of of jobs made obsolete by robots.
1.11.16.7. Con: Automation and technology is resulting in decreased demand for new labor, particularly for low income jobs. This is a disruption in the economic system that renders previous patterns less relevant as comparisons.
1.11.16.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.14.1.3.1.2.
1.11.16.8. Con: Innovation or technology does not create jobs or opportunities, it just throws all workers in the ocean and let’s them try to survive, this is historically a fact. The industry that benefits from a technological advance will normally just leave the social problems to be solved by a third party \(normally the government\).
1.11.16.9. Con: This argument is both fallacious and dangerously naive, but even if this were true on a macro scale it completely disregards localised effects, which can be disastrous.
1.11.16.10. Pro: The [absolute number](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/aug/17/technology-created-more-jobs-than-destroyed-140-years-data-census) of jobs is actually increasing because technology opens up a greater scope of possible occupations than before - especially when it comes to the digital sector, where job creation is not as constrained by physical capital inputs.
1.11.16.10.1. Con: While the impact of technology in job creation seems beneficial to developing country regions hosting offshore digital activities, in developed countries it has been viewed as a cause of job losses because of the constant search for skilled and low-cost labour. This is raising fears that all such jobs will move away and that individuals losing jobs in developed countries may require training and new skills for a more competitive environment. [\(Human Development Report, 2015, p. 85\).](http://hdr.undp.org/sites/default/files/2015_human_development_report_1.pdf)
1.11.16.10.2. Con: While new types of jobs may be created eventually, millions will suffer in the meantime because they can't catch up. We are already seeing this with coal miners as solar overtakes coal, and will see it soon with drivers as self-driving cars become ubiquitous. It is unlikely that [3-4 million displaced drivers](https://www.quora.com/How-many-American-workers-are-drivers-truck-drivers-Uber-taxi-drivers-etc) will suddenly get jobs in tech. That requires education. Luckily, UBI gives a person flexibility in order to pursue that education.
1.12. Con: A UBI enables people to work less or leave the workforce permanently.
1.12.1. Pro: As there is very little incentive to work with a UBI, people would choose not to work.
1.12.1.1. Con: [Choice](https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/ulterior-motives/201404/why-having-the-choice-do-nothing-is-so-important) has been found to be a powerful motivator, as a positive relationship has been found between one's ability to choose not to work and one's commitment to work.
1.12.1.1.1. Con: The illusion of choice can also be harmful when it comes to reinforcing harmful social norms. For example, women may be more likely to 'choose' not to work under a UBI, but this choice may be heavily influenced by social messaging and gender norms.
1.12.1.1.1.1. Con: Approximately [40%](http://www.nytimes.com/2013/05/30/business/economy/women-as-family-breadwinner-on-the-rise-study-says.html) of American mothers are the breadwinners in their household. This number increases when non-married couples are taken in consideration. Therefore, it is probable that even with a UBI, the representation of women in the workforce will continue to grow.
1.12.1.1.1.2. Con: With a UBI, women currently dependent on their partner's salary will now have their own money.
1.12.1.1.2. Con: This commitment needs to be evaluated relative to the net loss of workers who choose to opt out. Though the quality of workers may marginally increase, the quantity decreases to a more significant and harmful extent.
1.12.1.1.2.1. Con: [An experiment](https://public.econ.duke.edu/~erw/197/forget-cea%20\(2\).pdf) with a UBI resulted in households reducing their workloads by about 13%. This shows that while a UBI slightly decreases the amount individuals work, it does not disincentivize them from working all together.
1.12.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: The main reason fewer hours were worked was that male students stayed in school longer – they no longer had to help support their family – and mothers stayed at home longer with newborns. These were both responses that benefited society and ultimately the GDP.
1.12.1.1.2.1.2. Con: The experiment in question only ran for four years, and was known by participants to have a fixed term. The need for future employment is likely a major skewing factor compared to a permanent UBI program.
1.12.1.1.2.1.3. Pro: The experiment ran short term, which could have skewed the results: the rise in unemployment may have been temporary. Learning different skill sets, switching careers, starting a business, or even de-stressing \(so they can pursue a career without mental or physical health problems\) takes time. It's possible that a portion of the 13% did just that.
1.12.1.1.2.1.4. Con: A 13% reduction in work is likely to still have a significant impact on GDP and hence government funds. Taxes represent on average ~15% or less of GDP [data.worldbank.org](https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/gc.tax.totl.gd.zs)
1.12.1.2. Pro: As fewer people work, economic growth and prosperity decline. This has massive adverse consequences for society as a whole.
1.12.1.2.1. Con: A UBI reduces the inherent competitiveness of capitalism, which contributes to economic growth.
1.12.1.2.1.1. Con: Cooperation has its benefits as well, and reduced competition leads to closer bonds and easier cooperation between people.
1.12.1.2.2. Pro: People leaving the workforce results in lower production, higher costs, higher inflation, unless the job has already been fully automated.
1.12.1.2.3. Pro: As people leave the work force, tax revenue will decline, making a UBI unsustainable to fund in the long-term.
1.12.1.2.3.1. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.7.2.
1.12.1.3. Con: -> See 1.12.1.1.2.1.
1.12.1.4. Con: Germany has a very sophisticated [social state](https://www.internations.org/germany-expats/guide/15984-social-security-taxation/social-security-in-germany-15970), with a variety of public payments and benefits that enable citizens to live at least a humble life without work. Nevertheless, the [unemployment rate](http://www.tradingeconomics.com/germany/unemployment-rate) is very low.
1.12.1.5. Con: A UBI does not undermine work; it just provides payment sufficient for subsistence. Surplus and extras would derive from work, which will still be the axle of economy.
1.12.1.5.1. Pro: There are lots of ways to structure a UBI. In most of the ways it is proposed, there is still a plenty of incentive to work. Whether it's owning property or buying a car, you're not going to get all that with only a UBI payment.
1.12.1.5.2. Pro: The incentive to work is not to survive, but to obtain wealth. People don't want to be poor.
1.12.1.5.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.7.1.2.
1.12.1.6. Con: In 2011 Iran established a [program](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) that guaranteed citizens cash payments of equivalent to third of the nation's median income. A report found no evidence for the hypothesis this unconditional income reduced the willingness to work.
1.12.1.6.1. Pro: People engaged in the Iranian [service industry](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) worked more than before the implementation of the payment program.
1.12.1.6.2. Pro: Iranian business people started to expand their [businesses](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income).
1.12.1.6.3. Pro: There is [evidence](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) that citizens started to pursue more satisfying kind of work instead of leaving the labor market for good.
1.12.1.7. Pro: The free market naturally pays people for being productive. People may opt to do work that does not benefit the economy in any way and use their time for other pursuits.
1.12.1.7.1. Con: It is hard to see why people should be forced to work with absolutely no qualifiers.
1.12.1.7.2. Con: Under our current system, some people commit crimes. The general consensus is that crime — although it is work that enriches the individual — is not to the benefit of the economy. Yet still, the free market encourages it.
1.12.1.7.3. Con: In an increasingly global economy, there is very little work that can objectively be deemed 'non-beneficial to the economy.' The online entertainment, gaming, and creative market through Platforms such as patreon, youtube, and twitch allow individuals to contribute to the economy and get paid by playing video games, creating art and music, or simply being likeable on camera.
1.12.1.8. Con: Income inequality is a stronger incentive to not work.
1.12.1.9. Con: People work for reasons other than income.
1.12.1.9.1. Pro: Labor is often very meaningful to a lot of people, since it provides some sense of dignity.
1.12.1.9.1.1. Con: There are other ways people experience dignity. Being respected and acknowledged for social, familial, and political contributions also provides dignity and meaning to a person's life.
1.12.1.9.1.1.1. Pro: Contributions in the form of personal development and financially aiding the family are among the greatest of these ways.
1.12.1.9.1.2. Pro: Solely living on the UBI could make people feel useless.
1.12.1.9.1.2.1. Con: Nothing is stopping people who want to work from working.
1.12.1.9.1.2.2. Con: Or it could give them the freedom to do things they actually want to do, that would feel meaningful to them.
1.12.1.9.1.2.3. Pro: UBI will increase depression and suicide rates as people lose purpose derived from work.
1.12.1.9.1.2.3.1. Con: Many suicides are correlated with poverty, harsh living circumstances, and generally seeing no way out of a situation. Work stress and hatred of one's job is also a factor in some suicides. The "lifeline", lenience, and hope provided by UBI would likely prevent more suicides than it'd cause.
1.12.1.9.1.3. Con: Current societal and cultural shaming for "not contributing" \(despite there being no universally recognized higher cause to which someone should be contributing\) is what is depriving people of dignity in the first place, which they then attempt to regain by working.
1.12.1.9.1.4. Con: This does not represent everybody and is not an excuse to force labour onto those who would rather not engage in it, unless there is a genuine need for that labour.
1.12.1.9.2. Con: A UBI allows people to work for these reasons unrelated to income at more genuine and efficient levels by reducing financial stress and coercive incentives.
1.12.1.9.3. Con: Then their work could become an activity they choose to engage in, and UBI provides more choices and freedom of choice overall - including that one.
1.12.1.10. Con: The progress of society supports the supposition that people are happy to do more work than the bare minimum needed to survive, in order to improve their lot and save for the future. The provision of a basic income that guarantees survival is unlikely to eliminate that basic drive. Provided a job pays anough to make it worth someone's while to do it, it will find staff. Indeed, a guaranteed basic may well free people to work for the satisfaction of a job well done, without need for cash reward.
1.12.1.11. Con: People would want to work even if they do have a UBI, because they want more than just a sustenance living.
1.12.1.11.1. Pro: People want to improve their social status.
1.12.1.11.1.1. Con: There are other ways to improve social status, such as through [education](https://www.equalitytrust.org.uk/social-mobility-and-education), [marriage](https://www.demographic-research.org/volumes/vol26/8/26-8.pdf) or friendship.
1.12.1.11.1.2. Pro: Improving social status will allow people to enjoy the kind of lifestyle they envision for themselves, thus, improving their happiness.
1.12.1.11.2. Pro: The [protestant work ethic](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1501011/) is a strong element of many western societies that causes moral pressure to work and it's still a dominant spirit of our era. \([Castells, Himanen, p. 19](https://archive.org/details/TheHackerEthicAndTheSpiritOfTheInformationAge)\).
1.12.1.11.2.1. Pro: For Protestants, the ['psychic harm'](https://hbr.org/daily-stat/2013/08/there-really-is-such-a-thing-a.html) from unemployment is 40% more than for other social groups, due to hard work being viewed as a 'calling' rather than a job.
1.12.1.11.3. Pro: For many people work is an important source of [personal identity](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1501011/) and hence self-esteem.
1.12.1.11.3.1. Pro: [Fifty-five percent](http://www.gallup.com/poll/175400/workers-sense-identity-job.aspx) of US workers get their sense of identity from their job.
1.12.1.11.3.2. Con: [Work](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/joe-robinson/self-identity_b_1128731.html) as a major source of personal identity can be detrimental to a person's sense of self-worth, as it is tied to a dynamic external construct and daily performance instead of internal values and attributes. Even if people leave the workforce, it will - under this premise - lead to a more positive and sustainable sense of self-identity.
1.12.1.11.4. Con: Societies and its values are subjects of constant change and progress. Triggered by the UBI, working less or even not working at all could become the social norm and the source for individuals' identity and self-esteem becomes a different one.
1.12.1.11.4.1. Con: Just as societal change can alter the working habits toward working less, they can also form around working more efficiently or productively.
1.12.1.11.4.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1.
1.12.1.11.4.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.12.1.5.2.
1.12.1.11.4.2. Con: Whilst being productive is important, a move away from financial status as the primary source of one's pride should actually be a favourable development for societies.
1.12.1.11.5. Pro: Some people work because they are driven by a personal mission.
1.12.1.11.5.1. Pro: A UBI could restore pride in kinds of work that are culturally significant, but otherwise are not usually fiscally responsible undertakings.
1.12.1.11.5.2. Pro: Some famous and successful [CEOs](https://www.fastcompany.com/3026791/dialed/personal-mission-statements-of-5-famous-ceos-and-why-you-should-write-one-too) have a clear personal mission.
1.12.1.11.6. Pro: In many cultures, especially in East Asia \(e.g. [South Korea](http://edition.cnn.com/2015/07/23/asia/south-korea-work-culture/)\), there is huge societal pressure to work.
1.12.1.11.6.1. Pro: The societal pressure to work in South Korea is deeply rooted, as it begins in the [classroom](http://www.npr.org/sections/parallels/2015/04/15/393939759/the-all-work-no-play-culture-of-south-korean-education).
1.12.1.11.7. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.12.3.1.
1.12.1.11.8. Pro: -> See 1.12.1.9.1.
1.12.1.11.9. Con: If UBI were financed with taxation, the gains of work would mostly go towards financing UBI, and not towards improving the life of the worker.
1.12.1.11.9.1. Con: This implies taxation would be over 50%, which may not be true.
1.12.1.11.9.1.1. Con: Correct. The amount of taxation might be lower \(such as 0%\). But then UBI will be lower as well \(such as 0% of GDP\).
1.12.1.11.9.2. Con: Taxation doesn't have to be that straightforward. E.g. in Denmark, there are elaborate taxation schemes for each level of income. If you're lvl2, only what you earn upwards of lvl1 is taxed more. In the highest income tax bracket, [the tax can be \>50%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxation_in_Denmark). People still work in those jobs.
1.12.2. Con: Most pilot programs allot enough money for basics, or act as a supplementary buffer to a salary. Not many working-age people would be content living off a UBI alone for an extended period of time.
1.12.2.1. Con: If most working-age people are able to work and would not be content living off of UBI payments that is irrelevant; a UBI is targeted at those who are not.
1.12.2.1.1. Con: UBI is targeted at everyone. It works as a financial safety net that allows people to take risks such as leaving their job to start business.
1.12.3. Pro: Most people go to work today merely because they won't be able to feed themselves or their family otherwise. A UBI will remove that obligation.
1.12.3.1. Con: Existing welfare systems would already allow people to not work but also feed their families \(at least as much as a UBI would\).
1.12.3.2. Con: This statement appears to be saying that the threat of starvation is a useful spur to the workforce. This sort of enforced serfdom belongs in the previous century; an enlightened society does not seek to keep its members impoverished and subjugated. UBI will help to end this sort of thinking and release people from servitude.
1.12.3.2.1. Pro: The right to life is broadly recognized among developed countries.
1.12.3.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.4.
1.12.3.4. Pro: In wealthy countries people who work only for survival would just stop working. The ones that have passion, work capacity and potential are usually successful. What must be discuss is how to decrease the difference of income between high level positions and the others.
1.12.4. Pro: It is in the best interest for society to heavily incentivize all people to participate in the workforce.
1.12.4.1. Pro: Increased labor participation is [important for economic development.](http://www.worldwatch.org/increase-labor-force-can-be-engine-development-0)
1.12.4.2. Con: In times of increasing automation, 3D printing, and intelligent software, this assumption is outdated.
1.12.4.2.1. Con: Automation won't change the nature of employment: the amount of employment is a feature of a society trading free time for productive wealth.
1.12.4.2.1.1. Con: If a person has a mountain of gold, but can never spend it, they are not wealthy. Similarly, if a society is to busy earning money and capital to enjoy the benefits of it, there is no point to their actions.
1.12.4.2.1.1.1. Pro: Furthermore, depending on what form this capital takes and what actions are taken to increase it, their actions may in fact be actively harmful and destructive, either to other humans or to animals, land, and the environment.
1.12.4.2.1.2. Pro: High employment rates and long working hours in a highly-automated society is possible and will produce more wealth than with low employment rates.
1.12.4.2.1.2.1. Con: Total productivity of a working week [goes down](https://www.inzpire.com/2017/01/want-excuse-work-fewer-hours/) once someone starts working above 47 hours/week.
1.12.4.2.2. Pro: As our society becomes more and more automated and a society that will function with less human workers becomes possible, it may be time to reexamine our assumptions about what actually makes a society "wealthy".
1.12.4.2.3. Con: Those technologies are amazing, but we are [very far from a world without need for human labor](http://secondmachineage.com/). There are many problems that can only be addressed by humans in healthcare, childcare, eldercare, cleaning the environment, etc. Someday, we probably will not need human labor, but we do today and will for at least several decades.
1.12.4.3. Con: Societal wealth is a matter of total working hour productivity. More working-hours means more produced, and more material wealth. It is a function of society to decide how much work \(and thus wealth\) for which it is worth sacrificing free time for.
1.12.4.3.1. Con: Social wealth is not a matter of total working hours as claimed. Labour productivity depends much more on the amount of capital present in a country than on total working hours, as shown by the difference in productivity between the US and a country like India. If a UBI removes the incentive to build capital or to maintain existing capital, it will decrease social wealth, regardless of total hours worked.
1.12.4.3.1.1. Con: Given an amount of "capital"—or, more correctly, a given level of technical progress—the amount of labor exercised correlates linearly to the wealth produced until the limits of technology to scale are reached. New technology raises the carry capacity by allowing further scaling, and reduces the invested labor per unit of productive output.
1.12.4.3.1.2. Con: "Capital" is just an abstraction of labor—labor is invested to produce and maintain capital—and generally describes technology, either knowledge of processes \("human capital"\) or tools \(e.g. machines\). It is more-correct to say productivity is determined by technical progress.
1.12.4.3.1.3. Con: Humans economize: they will expend the minimum means to produce the maximum ends. If a UBI were to reduce the total labor, labor would be organized to maximize productivity—to maintain the highest level of technical progress so as to maximize the unit production per labor hour.
1.12.4.3.1.3.1. Con: The fact that society would economize is irrelevant because it does not imply society would be better off. If half of your wealth is destroyed, you economize, exactly because you are worse off than before. But the fact that you economize does not mean it is a good idea to destroy wealth. If UBI removes incentive to build capital or maintain existing capital, you surely economize but you are also worse off.
1.12.4.3.1.3.1.1. Con: The capital you would lose in this scenario would be a waste. Building a factory which cannot be staffed is a use of labor which could otherwise produce actual things and thus wealth; building said factory would be a destruction of wealth. Building or maintaining the capital you claim would go unbuilt or unmaintained would decrease wealth.
1.12.4.3.1.4. Con: This is internally-inconsistent, as it implies that the amount of capital required is fixed and does not grow or shrink with the amount of use to which that capital is put. The is implied by reasoning that an economy cannot scale down to any significant degree, in turn implied by reasoning that less labor means the capital used by that labor goes neglected and decays—rather than that some of the capital becomes excess as production scales down.
1.12.4.3.2. Con: The sentiment is true, but working more \(over employment\) has been known to cause economic crashes. Certain levels of 'productivity' just aren't sustainable due to phenomena [such as the business cycle \(see chapters 9-12\)](http://www.academia.edu/24045849/Macroeconomics_Third_Edition_by_Charles_I._Jones_W.W._Norton_2014).
1.12.4.3.2.1. Pro: Overwork also has health implications, which increases healthcare costs as well as decreasing productivity.
1.12.4.4. Pro: A UBI inflates the baseline reward for not working from $0 to an arbitrarily higher value. For society to function properly, people have to do work that is considered of value by others; an activity that is rewarded through income. But, by providing a baseline income, UBI offsets the incentives that reward value production.
1.12.4.4.1. Con: "Inflating 0 to an arbitrarily higher value" doesn't mean that people won't do work that is considered of value to others. The UBI still exists in a capitalist economy, and there will still be monetary incentive to work.
1.12.4.4.2. Con: A society exists for far greater purposes than providing meaningless work for factory fodder. People who are free from mindless tasks \(that machines can do\) are free to pursue more creative and fulfilling tasks.
1.12.4.4.3. Pro: If work were not valuable to anyone, no one would be willing to pay for it.
1.12.4.4.3.1. Con: People value work that they will not pay for.
1.12.4.4.4. Pro: A janitorial job may not provide huge amounts of value/income, but it still provides some value and thus still contributes to the functioning of society.
1.12.4.4.5. Con: Work valued by others is also an arbitrary judgement. One arbitrary judgement does not disprove another.
1.12.4.4.6. Con: If someone can get by on UBI while they chase their dreams, great things can happen. History is littered with geniuses in their fields who have died young/been forced to abandon their work through poverty.
1.12.4.5. Con: Economic measures are subjective. Contributions to society can only be measured objectively.
1.12.4.6. Con: Participation in the work force has external costs.
1.12.4.7. Con: In ancient times, this was the case, if you didn't have an income, you died.  Fortunately civilisation has moved on, and people are supported when they have a crisis in their lives, UBI is another step on that road.
1.12.4.8. Con: It is in the best interest of society to have a happy and healthy workforce. It is detrimental to society and individuals to have participation in the workforce so heavily incentivized that it leads to mental and physical illness.
1.12.4.9. Pro: Government's priority should always be to encourage employment instead of giving people the hope that they can feed on subsidies. If not the production and innovation process will stagnate.
1.12.4.9.1. Con: This would suggest that there will always be employment. There's no reason to think that all possible work that can be filled by robots \(automation\) won't be. Those wages will be saved by the former employers.
1.12.4.9.2. Pro: UBI removes the incentive to work, adding to the welfare state. We have more job openings now than total number of people seeking employment. In that case, UBI would only inflate the unemployment rate making it more difficult for companies to hire and grow.[money.cnn.com](http://money.cnn.com/2018/06/05/news/economy/job-openings-unemployed-workers/index.html)
1.12.5. Con: Currently, people work out of social and economical obligation. A UBI diminishes this obligation, enhancing people's sense of freedom and agency.
1.12.5.1. Con: People are free to choose not to work in capitalist systems. The fact that there are consequences to that choice doesn't make it economic coercion.
1.12.5.1.1. Con: By logical extension: when pointing a gun to an individual's head and asking them to do something, said individual is free not to do said thing. The fact that there are likely going to be consequences to that choice is exactly what makes it coercive.
1.12.5.2. Con: It is not the job of government to unconditionally support individual's 'freedom' if they are not willing to engage in the economic exchange of labor.
1.12.5.3. Pro: For some people, who are genuinely not fit for the workforce, this is a good thing. These people can now live much more enjoyable lives.
1.12.6. Pro: A UBI is a wealth re-distribution scheme. All wealth re-distribution inherently dis-incentivizes productive work. The wealth to pay for the UBI must come from productive people in the society. These people are net payers into the system. They are getting their wealth confiscated for the benefit of others, which discourages work. Those who are net beneficiaries of the re-distribution would also only work enough \(if at all\) to remain beneficiaries and not become net payers.
1.12.6.1. Con: Current welfare is a form of re-distribution which dis-incentivizes work more than a basic income would because of pay brackets under which there would be a [negligible increase to overall income](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welfare_trap#Examples) once starting to work. Under basic income, extra income directly contributes to increase your overall income.
1.12.6.2. Con: Most wealth in developed nations is not held by the majority of productive people, but rather historically wealthy families, corporations, and entities. Few or no individuals could work and earn a living large enough for a UBI to disincentivise hard work and general productivity.
1.12.6.3. Con: A UBI just guarantees sufficient money to pay for basic necessities. In fact, guaranteeing a home and food provides incentive for more productive work as workers will get to choose what they want to do rather than being forced to do what they have to do.
1.12.6.3.1. Pro: UBI doesn't need to provide any motivation - boredom will do that. All a UBI will do is provide enough for food / shelter / healthcare. Anything beyond basic necessities must be worked for.
1.12.6.4. Con: Many people who currently claim benefits but are capable of working do so because they are dis-incentivised to do the only jobs available to them which are highly unpleasant. Having the support structure of UBI allows them to peruse jobs they actually care about — when someone is working at a job they are doing because they want to rather than because they have to, productivity is higher.
1.12.6.4.1. Pro: Wages for unpleasant jobs will also rise, forcing such jobs to be appropriately rewarded to entice people into them rather than people being forced into them as their only real option.
1.12.6.5. Con: On the contrary, it is a payment they make to keep society relatively prosperous and crime free by not pushing people to destitution. As human labour slowly becomes inessential, this would push companies to adopt efficient production technologies by removing the incentive of cheap labour, since people are not forced to work wherever they can just to feed their family.
1.12.7. Con: This argument runs in support of UBI as it supports those who cannot find work. Systematic unemployment due to lack of available jobs is a future threat to human welfare even in the current economy, and that threat is [likely to increase in the future](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/01/what-is-the-future-of-work/).
1.12.7.1. Con: We should be trying to [create more jobs](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_n6ivGgb9RY)!  Not finding ways to get people out of the workforce!
1.12.7.1.1. Pro: Job creation increases economic well-being.  Like it did in the [Reagan era](https://www.forbes.com/sites/kylesmith/2014/09/11/sorry-obama-fans-reagan-did-better-on-jobs-and-growth/#549b9fab55e1)!
1.12.7.1.1.1. Con: It is doubtful that all relevant factors are the same as they were in the Reagan era.
1.12.7.1.2. Pro: More jobs means a better economy because more jobs means more people with money.  More people with money means more people buying things which means more profits for companies which means even more jobs!
1.12.7.1.2.1. Con: Given [that over the last few decades real wages have barely budged](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2014/10/09/for-most-workers-real-wages-have-barely-budged-for-decades/), this calculus clearly no longer holds. Any growth in jobs and the economy has not lead to increases in consumer spending power; because costs are instead rising in terms of basic expenses such as food and healthcare.
1.12.7.1.3. Con: Creating jobs is nice and even if we 'should' try, it is likely that will be [impossible in the future](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/01/what-is-the-future-of-work/), at least that 100% employment is impossible.
1.12.7.1.4. Con: This implies \(or avoids saying\) we should avoid UBI in order to drive job creation. Individuals benefit from UBI and corporations create jobs. Supporting individuals when corporations fail to create jobs makes sense. Whether or not corporations should or shouldn't create jobs is irrelevant.
1.12.7.1.5. Con: UBI is not a way of getting people out of the workforce; it is a better way of supporting those who are between jobs, those who are working in jobs that don't suit them, and those who can't find a job they are able to do. UBI could allow them the opportunity to find a better fit for their talents and abilities, or allow them the freedom to learn more in order to do so.
1.12.7.1.6. Con: Why should we be creating more jobs if we can focus on automation? Why force people to labour more when the goal should be to labour less.
1.12.7.2. Pro: The argument strengthens if humanity rejects its current apocalyptic drive for consumption. To the extent unemployment is unavoidable and not a result of personal inadequacy or market down-cycles, then UBI because a necessary protection insurance against the societal costs of widespread crime and poverty.
1.12.7.3. Pro: When employment is working at a task better done my machines and only preserved as human labor for the sake of creating jobs, 100% unemployment should be a goal not something to fear.
1.12.8. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.12.1.
1.12.9. Con: Our level of technology will not suffice to run a complete economy without human intervention. Therefore, we couldn't afford to give a full income to someone without that fully automated economy.
1.12.9.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.4.16.1.1.1.
1.12.10. Con: Leaving the workforce allows laborers the time they need to develop other skills so that they can rejoin the workforce later with value added.
1.12.11. Con: As automation and machines eventually displace more and more humans out of the workforce having people work less is actually a positive effect.
1.12.11.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.1.
1.12.11.2. Pro: -> See 1.11.
1.12.11.3. Pro: This will save companies money through lower labor costs. The money saved could be used to fund a UBI.
1.12.12. Pro: The increasing availability of cheap online entertainment has already made labour force participation [increasingly unappealing](http://marginalrevolution.com/marginalrevolution/2016/09/labor-force-participation-video-games.html); a UBI would enable and accelerate this existing trend.
1.12.12.1. Con: This problem is compared with other [scares](http://www.atlasobscura.com/articles/the-1980s-media-panic-over-dungeons-dragons) that turned out to not be of consequence.
1.12.13. Con: [A UBI experiment](http://www.developmentpathways.co.uk/resources/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/Indias-Basic-Income-Experiment-PP21.pdf) in India showed that this idea is empirically not true. Not only did overall employment increase with implementation of a UBI, but there was also a sharp increase in more independent labor - which specifically helped women. Individual debt also dropped for 73% of people, and increased for none.
1.12.13.1. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI, because benefits were temporary, which significantly alters the incentive of beneficiaries towards making something lasting out of it. If they thought this income would become the norm instead of being a financial windfall, they would not be as motivated to reduce debt, for example.
1.12.13.2. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI, because the benefits were not paid from taxes collected in the villages, but injected from outside. Instead of UBI, which is a zero-sum game within the economic zone in which it is implemented, they brought in purchasing power from outside the "UBI" zone, which of course makes the villages better off.
1.12.13.2.1. Con: While this experiment may not be an accurate representation of the macroeconomic consequences, it still shows the effects of a UBI on individual behavior: increased school attendance, reduced debts, and a shift in occupations from wage laborer to farmer.
1.12.13.2.2. Con: Even if purchasing power is redistributed rather than raised \(on net\) a zero-sum change can still improve net welfare as the benefit of the marginal dollar to the poor is much higher than the to rich.
1.12.13.2.2.1. Pro: Also in any small scale view, this is not a sum zero game. A poor area \(such as this village\) will net benefit, and a rich area \(which could have been a wealthy town nearby\) will net loose.
1.12.13.3. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI as it is usually proposed, because the benefits were designed to be just enough to make a difference, but not enough to live off. It was a supplemental income that still required the villagers to work for their livelihood, as opposed to an income that can buy at least food, clothing and shelter.
1.12.14. Con: -> See 1.12.1.11.
1.12.15. Con: This is not unambiguously bad, as many if not most people do not enjoy being obligated to work, so if work was made more voluntary and incentivized in other ways \(e.g. wages above and beyond one's UBI, contribution to causes one agrees with, jobs which are enjoyable to the worker\) this would arguably be an improvement to society and societal quality of life.
1.12.16. Con: This could be positive if it cuts bureaucracy and unecessary jobs that are only there to allow people to make a living.
1.12.17. Pro: Those who have [multiple citizenship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiple_citizenship), could receive an UBI in a wealthy nation, then spend it in a poor country with a cheap cost of life. That would be a leak of money and workforce, from the rich country to the poor one.
1.12.17.1. Pro: Somehow people already use this kind of construct when they retire abroad: they have worked all their life in a wealthy country, then they get a small pension compare to the cost of living in this country. But they chose to go to live in countries with a cheap cost of life and there they can have luxury with that same pension.
1.12.17.2. Pro: People already do that through normal activities \(jobs, traveling, purchasing stocks\), company actions \(like outsourcing and storing profits overseas\), or crime.
1.12.17.3. Con: There could be incentives to prevent that from happening, like to get a UBI, the person must not be out of the country for more than 1 month of the year \(except for vacations longer than that\). If they are, the UBI stops for them.
1.12.17.4. Con: [In 2017, the average US outbound traveler](https://travel.trade.gov/outreachpages/download_data_table/2017%20Profile%20of%20US%20Residents%20Visiting%20Overseas.xlsx) spent $1,476 outside of the US as part of a trip for business or pleasure \(cell C402\). The average length of stay abroad was over 16 days \(cell C206\). The average household income of international travelers was $127k \(cell C477\). I do not believe those that can afford to travel internationally would receive a significant portion of their income as UBI, nor that it would impact our economy any more significantly than outbound tourism already does.
1.12.18. Pro: This will lead to ex-workers losing their skills - essentially becoming vegetables.
1.12.18.1. Con: People will take up new hobbies and interests, many of which will be or will have the potential to become productive.
1.12.19. Con: People only collecting UBI would be on the bottom of society with a all the time they want and need to ascend higher and benefit the world in any way they want.
1.12.20. Con: A UBI will enable unhappy workers who detract from work productivity to leave their jobs, so happy/productive workers can take their place.
1.12.20.1. Con: This assumes there are a surplus of happy workers to take necessary jobs.
1.12.20.1.1. Con: There is an excess of all workers at the moment, and while it is possible none of them will be more agreeable with the working conditions, it is probable a good number will be.
1.12.20.2. Con: This assumes happy workers are more productive, which is [false](http://www.hiringsmart.com/articles/608/Happy_Employees_are_not_Necessarily_Productive_Employees/).
1.12.20.2.1. Con: The linked article states that disengaged workers are not productive - so enabling disengaged workers to leave a job they are not engaged with and find a job or other activities which are more engaging and satisfying to them would produce a similar benefit.
1.12.20.3. Pro: Happy workers are [more productive](http://fortune.com/2015/10/29/happy-productivity-work/).
1.12.20.3.1. Con: Workers are not happy if their work is not valued more highly than "non-work"; Ubi devalues basic work.
1.12.21. Con: This would be highly beneficial for those with moderately severe chronic illness or disability or those who otherwise cannot qualify for disability assistance programs
1.13. Con: UBIs could be used by political elites to avoid addressing structural injustices.
1.13.1. Pro: Because it is universally accessible, a UBI does nothing to address specific sociocultural issues of minorities. If anything the 'equality' it provides erases differences and unfairly redirects blame for poverty back onto minorities.
1.13.1.1. Con: Many socio-cultural issues exist because of the economic disadvantaged state of minorities. A UBI alleviates this economic discrepancy.
1.13.1.2. Pro: Poverty is an endemic issue for minority groups; but one whose root cause is systemic discrimination in the past and present. These underlying issues are overlooked, meaning a UBI is easily characterised as a 'universal handout' that will fix the issues of poverty in minority communities.
1.13.1.2.1. Con: Just because it will fix the poverty of everyone doesn't mean that it won't also fix poverty in minority groups.
1.13.1.2.2. Con: Poverty, whatever the cause, is primarily a financial condition. Any measure that eases that condition does so regardless of social divisions and other disadvantages. Systemic bigotry against minorities that may hinder further advancement of members of a given group is a separate issue that requires unrelated counter measures.
1.13.1.2.2.1. Con: Dealing with the symptoms of an ailment often helps expedite recovery.
1.13.1.3. Con: Because if people take the free living wage provided by the government and squander it on something unnecessary, it really is their fault if they are out of their living wage.
1.13.1.3.1. Con: UBI will actually help fix the spending habits of the poor. Studies show that bad spending habits[come from a mindset of the scarcity of now](http://articles.latimes.com/2012/nov/03/science/la-sci-poor-people-decisions-20121103), and when people are given enough resources to think about the future those habits disappear.
1.13.1.3.1.1. Pro: The fact is, most poor people [do not spend](http://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0083428) in the way we assume and peer reviewed studies say that policies that contain food price inflation, improve decent-paying job opportunities for the urban poor are likely to reduce the use of negative coping strategies.
