1.13.1.4. Con: A nation's choice of redistribution schemes is not the only tool by which to address specific differences in equality and so UBIs should not be dismissed as a means to provide economic relief to all individuals.
1.13.1.5. Con: The fact that while working to alleviate a particular immediate problem, a UBI doesn't fix every other issue is not an argument against a UBI.
1.13.1.6. Con: A law that ameliorates a symptom instead of a root cause can still have value. For example, distributing food or money to combat hunger does nothing to aleviate the root causes of hunger, but still adds tremendous value to humanity.
1.13.1.6.1. Pro: Many diseases are treated with "supportive care" to make things easier for the body in other ways, allowing it to focus its "efforts" and energy on the primary illness.
1.13.1.7. Con: UBI is not the solution to discrimination. UBI is the solution to automation. Discrimination must be fixed separately. If discrimination were fixed, we would still need UBI. Therefore, misuse of UBI for the sake of discrimination is not a reason to oppose UBI.
1.13.2. Pro: Passing a UBI may mean that welfare and poverty will be seen as a 'solved' issue, which requires no further help from the State.
1.13.2.1. Con: This is an argument against literally any attempt to fight poverty. This risk does not outweigh the policy's tangible benefits for the poor.
1.13.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.11.2.2.
1.13.2.3. Con: If society believes that welfare and poverty is a "solved" issue, then it is a fault of the society, not the UBI.
1.13.2.3.1. Pro: UBI can help improve faulty perceptions and beliefs around poverty.
1.13.2.4. Con: Just because an UBI exists, does not mean that people are not in poverty. This will be seen in studies performed by the government and third party organizations.
1.13.2.5. Pro: A bigger issue concerning inequality relates to distribution of wealth. There is a danger that UBI will act as a distraction, and disguise the bigger issue. This is especially the case in an era of accelerating technology, when profits to GDP have been hovering around an all-time high and we seem to be seeing a winner takes all society evolve, with the creation of natural monopolies in technology verticals, such as search, social media, room bookings, car sharing, etc.
1.13.2.6. Pro: UBI is the [New Deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Deal) of the future. In that it will appease the masses for a little bit, but then become slowly unraveled until it's meaningless. Especially since the plutocrat donors have such obscene political power.
1.13.2.7. Pro: Equality of opportunity, which can be achieved through affirmative action policies, is unlikely to be popular when huge portions of the budget are already being spent on a UBI.
1.13.2.7.1. Con: Affirmative action policies rarely cost money to implement. It is unclear why the cost of a UBI would influence whether they are implemented.
1.13.2.7.2. Con: -> See 1.6.4.1.
1.13.2.7.3. Con: Giving people a basic income would go a long way towards increasing equality of opportunity as poverty is one of the biggest factors in inequality of opportunity.
1.13.3. Pro: A UBI replacing the myriad number of welfare programs means that welfare as a political issue only has a single dimension: at what rate should the UBI be set. This can distract from other policy options that could more strategically target injustice and welfare needs.
1.13.3.1. Con: It is possible for a UBI to co-exist with a bare minimum degree of social welfare programs. These welfare programs could address other injustices.
1.13.3.2. Con: When people are free from concerns about their own destitution they will be empowered to mobilize around the plights of their fellow citizens. A UBI makes sincere prioritization of other's needs possible in a way that is currently impossible for most citizens.
1.13.3.3. Con: "Strategic" often translates to more complex, and complexity is more difficult to fight through democratic means. A UBI would simplify political discussion to a level that can be impacted by votes.
1.13.3.4. Pro: UBIs are arbitrary numbers, and once in effect, the only productive value one must provide to society is your vote, which is likely just to be for a larger UBI.
1.13.3.4.1. Con: Many activities provide value to society even if they are not remunerated activities.
1.13.3.5. Pro: UBI's will make a whole group of dependent people dependent forever on the one political party that makes UBI's law. The more people that become dependent on UBI's the stronger the one political party will become.
1.13.3.6. Pro: If a country were to hit a rough patch economically, putting UBI on a hold would throw those dependant on it back into poverty. UBI addresses the symptoms, not the problem.
1.13.3.7. Pro: A UBI is easier to quantify. Is the UBI set at the correct amount is an easier question to get meaningful political responses on than “should the poor get more?”
1.13.3.8. Pro: UBI is unsustainable in a democracy because over time because organized voting blocs will lobby and vote for increasingly larger payments out of the public treasury. Enough politicians will be incentivized to dispense them in exchange for consolidating their political power.
1.13.3.9. Pro: Ultimately the level of UBI would become distorted by politicians looking to curry favor from the population receiving them. Such biased inputs would ultimately lead to unsustainable redistribution. Who would turn down an ever increasing level of UBI?
1.13.3.10. Pro: Once the welfare system is focused into one monetary sum, it will become a political target subject to erosion at each election. Over time, politicians will drive the payments down to win votes, leading to increased poverty. The poor today have multiple options to address income, housing and healthcare. Rolling those into one payment subject to political whim is a high risk strategy for the poor.
1.13.3.10.1. Con: The poor are already under this kind of attack. A UBI might actually mitigate this as it would reduce desire for "incentive" and "work requirement" schemes. It would be simpler to implement as well.
1.13.3.10.2. Con: With it all being in one sum, it would be harder for politicians to skim some money off the top as they do now. Ie. Take 2% off of $20 in 5 different values and nobody will notice, but take 2% off of $100 once and it becomes more pronounced.
1.13.4. Pro: -> See 1.11.1.
1.13.5. Con: A universal policy like a UBI is unlikely to reduce the political will of marginalised groups as they will be just as \(relatively\) disadvantaged after the UBI. Thus political will for them to lobby to improve their position is un-diminshed, and elites will feel equally pressured.
1.13.5.1. Pro: When more people are free from concerns about their own destitution, they will be empowered to mobilize around the plights of their fellow citizens. UBI makes sincere prioritization of other's needs possible in a way that is currently impossible for most citizens.
1.13.5.2. Con: That assumes that people are motivated by altruism. If one takes the view that people are inherently selfish, providing for their basic needs may lead many toward a more apathetic view as it relates to politics, leaving them even more marginalized than they were before, when participation was necessary due to their circumstances.
1.13.6. Pro: It is difficult to create political sympathy for the poor when they are already by far one of the largest spending items on a government budget \([e.g US War on Poverty](http://budget.house.gov/waronpoverty/)\). Therefore it is difficult to politically motivate for a structural reform while implementing this policy.
1.13.6.1. Con: The [largest spending item](http://www.businessinsider.com/2012-us-defense-budget-largest-since-world-war-ii-2011-7) on the US budget in 2012 was defense, not spending on the poor, and there was very little to no loss of political support for the US military.
1.13.6.2. Con: A UBI does not just go to the poor. It goes to everyone, including the middle class. Therefore, it should have more support than most welfare programs.
1.13.6.3. Con: The lack of political sympathy assumption is false. By this reasoning, it should be difficult to create political sympathy for the elderly because the vast majority of health care money is spent on them \(and this does not happen\).
1.13.6.3.1. Con: This comparison is flawed. Contrary to poverty, aging is a process almost every member of society will experience. Therefore political sympathy for the elderly is in the self-interest of \(almost\) all citizens.
1.13.7. Pro: The effects of a UBI on local economies and politics can create harmful flow-on effects for other countries.
1.13.7.1. Pro: A UBI in wealthy nations incentivizes further automation of labor, diminishing employment opportunities everywhere.
1.13.7.2. Pro: A UBI could exacerbate global inequality if it incentivises isolationist policies, such as closed borders or an indifference to the conditions of the global labor force.
1.13.7.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.1.
1.13.7.2.2. Pro: If a UBI 'solves' many of the problems present in developed economies \(such as insufficient social safety nets or poor working conditions\) this can undermine empathy for — and solidarity with — the conditions of workers in poorer countries.
1.13.7.2.2.1. Con: The argument that doing something about why a country is horrible would make people less sympathetic to the people of that country can be applied to anything that makes that country is horrible.
1.13.7.2.2.2. Con: As it stands, there is very little international sympathy for undeveloped countries to lose.
1.13.8. Con: While possibly true, this is better addressed directly. Suggesting we should not implement UBI due to potential and indirect later harms is to put the cart before the horse.
1.13.8.1. Pro: The UBI itself would be a step towards addressing these imbalances.
1.13.8.2. Con: However we are not living in a hypothetical situation. If UBI were to be implemented, those real-life problems would occur, so they are necessary to discuss. Statements like these are what lead to issues like those that privatised prisons in the US are facing. All effects must be taken into account.
1.13.9. Pro: UBI, like the minimum wage, requires a government\(s\) to draw an arbitrary line for the "ideal" standard of living, that it aims to promote.
1.13.9.1. Con: It is widely acknowledged that there are standards of living humans should not be expected to suffer below. It is simply a matter of determining where this line is, which is not "arbitrary".
1.13.9.1.1. Pro: [Scientific studies](http://www.pnas.org/content/107/38/16489.short) have shown that 75,000 US dollars is the ideal amount of money for human happiness and prosperity.
1.13.9.2. Con: The ideal standard of living is a global, not a national concept. The UN helps reinforce this with research, and that standard is what a country deploying UBI would use.
1.13.9.3. Con: If a government would decide to give a 'too small' UBI, they wouldn't give it in the first place. The whole point of UBI is to eradicate poverty, and if it doesn't there's no reason to put in place.
1.13.10. Con: A UBI will empower the politically marginalized by freeing them from the shackles of wage slavery. When people are free to think and organize without fear of destitution they will be able to take control of their democracy and enact their political might on structural injustices.
1.13.10.1. Pro: The ability to organize one's workplace without the threat of losing access to basic needs will be a great democratizing force.
1.13.10.2. Con: If the UBI is being provided by the government, organizing against the government would create a fear of destitution.
1.13.10.3. Con: By financing UBI through this system it would gain legitimacy. The structural injustices would be seen as a necessary evil in order to provide for the basic needs of everyone.
1.13.11. Con: An UBI doesn't exclude the righting of structural injustices. It has never been claimed that the purpose of UBI would be to right structural injustices to begin with. So this argument is an irrelevant distraction.
1.13.11.1. Con: It's not irrelevant at all. The thesis of the discussion is whether rich countries should implement UBI. One does not need a claim UBI would right structural injustices in order for someone to say a UBI would entrench structural injustices.
1.13.12. Con: A UBI inherently levels the playing field to remove structural injustices.
1.13.12.1. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.
1.13.12.2. Con: -> See 1.1.2.
1.13.12.3. Pro: People with "invisible", poorly documented or otherwise hard to prove disabilities can receive a UBI without tolerating questioning or having to produce proof.
1.13.12.4. Pro: UBI is a good starting point for establishing much broader systemic economic reform. The establishment of a UBI standard that is matched with a variety of other economic factors would allow our whole society to be enriched by innovation and cultural development. It seems over simplified to suggest that it would stifle self determination; If done with, integrity it may  allow individuals to focus on their true passions and become innovators as well foster a stronger democratic-capitalist system.
1.13.13. Con: Political elites would use any available policy \(UBI or not\) to push their own agendas.
1.13.13.1. Con: Simply because there are other modes available to push agendas doesn't negate the premise that UBIs may provide another such tool and have the potential for additional harm.
1.13.14. Pro: Cultivating dependence on the state through UBI reduces the bargaining power of the governed. Only when the state depends on its population for their labour, and when the people have the ability to withhold it, do the people have any power over the state save for that of violent revolution.
1.13.14.1. Pro: For example, oil-rich rentier states of the middle east grant generous allowances to their subjects, in which demands for rights can be ignored as there is no consequence to the state of doing so.
1.13.14.1.1. Con: It is up to voters to ensure that they do not trade basic comforts for basic rights. If a goverment says 'allow torture or you can't have security' voters can \(and have\) said 'we will not allow torture, you are still being paid to provide and responsible for security'. If they do not then any basic comfort or need can hold them hostage
1.13.14.2. Con: In a democratic society the power lies in the vote. The elected officials would gain power if they could convince their constituents they would support and/or increase the UBI.
1.13.14.2.1. Con: The only power difference between a democracy and a dictatorship is the size of an incumbent's winning coalition. In a democracy, most citizens are not part of the winning coalition. Therefore their vote is not important.
1.13.14.3. Con: Most persons in mainstream western society are dependent on private employers, which could be argued to be worse and less empowering than being dependent on the state.
1.13.14.4. Pro: A UBI will be used to create deeper levels of poverty/dystopia for the former laboring class. This income will not be enough to offer much more than the poverty traps that are already offered by employers today. To maintain control they need a desperate class on the edge of poverty and insecurity.
1.13.14.4.1. Con: Even just reducing the stress of the poorest strata of society will lead to positive shifts in society and their lifestyle.
1.13.14.5. Pro: A UBI would create political inequality, as people would become afraid to bite the hand that feeds them.
1.13.14.6. Pro: Government should be dependent on people for survival, not people dependent on government. Those in power could use UBI as a tool similar to tax brackets are used today, with the difference being that depending on the change, people would be unable to feed themselves.
1.13.14.7. Pro: A UBI breaks the checks-and-balances guaranteed by laws such as those of the US Constitution. The people are the ultimate authority, and by providing unconditional sustenance and billeting allowances at a national scale, citizens no longer have authority over the state; instead the state now has direct authority over the peoples which tips the scales of checks-and-balances.
1.13.14.8. Pro: UBI would become a political tool that like welfare \(US\), is used by politicians as to gain gain/hold power. As with welfare programs, UBI recipients would be hostage of corrupt political people and processes that divide those who receive UBI from those who pay the taxes to provide UBI. This is not to mention the sad human state of dependence on government that UBI creates.
1.13.14.9. Con: People currently depend on the state for physical security \(police, military\), environmental protection \(clean water\), power, etc. The state cannot withhold universal protections to assert power, they can only withhold limited exceptions.
1.13.14.10. Pro: UBI sounds good, but someone or a small group will have to administer the program determining the benefits, collection of resources and distribution.  This puts a lot of power into a small group.  Potentially political power. History tells us political power in the hands of a small group has not always ended well.
1.13.15. Pro: A UBI together with automation will strengthen the position of high tax payers, by making the state dependent on their taxes \(to pay for UBI\).
1.13.15.1. Con: Those high tax payers already control the state in the US, and elsewhere. This is a general problem of money in politics, not of welfare policies, UBI or otherwise.
1.13.15.1.1. Pro: The wealthiest actually pay less tax than anyone else, as a percentage of income, currently, allowing them more to spend influencing politics.
1.13.15.2. Con: -> See 1.7.15.6.
1.13.15.3. Pro: Assuming a UBI is financed through high marginal tax rates, this would make democracies \(e.g. US/UK\) even more extraordinarily protective of their most profitable industries, some of which may be globally exploitative, such as investment banking and resource extraction.
1.13.15.4. Con: A UBI does not necessarily have to be funded through taxes, or can be funded through taxes which are not dependent on an individual's income.
1.13.15.5. Pro: Historically and currently, in any government the more a population pays more in taxes, the more it is likely to be listened to by representatives or the state. Those on a UBI are not taxed, and will be beholden to the increasing control of those who will be taxed and who pay for the UBI. Systemically this will concentrate into fewer hands over time, becoming serfdom.
1.13.16. Con: This could also be true of any program that promotes social equality. However, such programs in general weaken the influence of elites by empowering the disadvantaged.
1.13.16.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.
1.13.16.2. Pro: A UBI allows would-be political activists more time to engage in such activities without feeling as much financial pressure to engage in paid work instead.
1.13.17. Pro: We evolve as civilization by taming basic instincts; however we have yet to subdue the thirst for power and selfishness, which will inevitably lead any form of system to corruption.
1.13.17.1. Pro: A precursor towards a brighter future of the kind that universal welfare systems like a UBI promote is to begin taming our instincts so that a less selfish stance on each individual is rewarded, and thirst for power is tamed so it does not corrupt.
1.13.18. Con: The possibility that a system could be misused by malicious actors does not constitute evidence that the system would not be beneficial if used in good faith. If the system implemented faithfully would provide benefit, then the question of how to prevent abuse is a secondary \(if still important\) issue.
1.13.18.1. Con: Plenty of government policies if "implemented faithfully", would be beneficial, but it must be acknowledged that they aren't. A prime example includes regulatory capture, but in theory even a dictatorship, if benevolent, would likely be beneficial yet we recognize we should not support such a kind of government.
1.13.18.1.1. Con: It is possible to assess the consequences by performing experiments at a small scale. The fear itself of it failing cannot work as a deterrent, it is necessary to assess whether those fears are real or imagined. In the example of a dictatorship, it has been tried before over and over, however, most of the time it is not beneficial. It is not possible to say the same about an UBI unless experiments are completed.
1.13.18.1.1.1. Con: While it may be a good idea to test UBIs \(or not; it may not be practical to "test" UBIs on a "small scale"\), no examples of concluded, positive tests have been offered. Simply put, we cannot yet use the "possibility" of tests as support for the thesis.
1.13.18.2. Pro: Corruption is an evil in our society and is not affected by the economic policies.
1.13.18.2.1. Con: That doesn't mean that economic policies aren't affected, or even directed by such corruption, to better benefit certain groups or individuals.
1.13.19. Con: Democratic franchise is a check on all abuses of power.
1.13.20. Pro: State provision crowds out the ability for more dynamic institutions to develop. This reliance on continued redistribution makes the poor vulnerable to shifts in political sentiment and policy.
1.13.20.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.3.1.
1.13.20.2. Con: These more "dynamic" institutions have been woefully inadequate in dealing with structural poverty.
1.13.20.3. Con: A simplified system makes changes to legislation more transparent and easier to compare. The poor can vote with more confidence and keep representatives in place that meet their needs.
1.13.21. Pro: A plausible side effect of this is that a UBI could help create the conditions for widespread societal instability.
1.13.21.1. Pro: A UBI will foster social unrest. As people won't have to work they will start loitering and ultimately rioting. The \(economically passive\) population's only interest in civil society will be to demand continuous increases of the UBI.
1.13.21.1.1. Con: Nearly [40%](https://qz.com/516023/if-nearly-40-of-americans-arent-working-what-are-they-doing/) of Americans are not working but research shows their time is not spent loitering but rather on other activities such as education.
1.13.21.1.2. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.2.1.
1.13.21.2. Pro: UBI will lead to alienation, which will ultimately lead to the destruction of civilization. When the population doesn't actively participate in the economy anymore, they will disengage and lose their mental and moral markers.
1.13.21.2.1. Con: Experiments so far have not shown that UBI leads to alienation; people still participate in the economy. The majority still want to make extra money unless they are educating themselves or caring for other family members \(children, ill or disabled relatives, etc\).
1.13.21.2.2. Con: Participating in the economy is not linked to mental or moral markers.
1.13.21.3. Pro: A UBI will lead to even greater wealth disparities between countries, increasing instability and injustice.
1.13.21.3.1. Con: A UBI does not change the general wealth of a country by default, it just distributes it differently. Therefore it is no reason for instability between counties, but it could be an example that leads to a more just and peacefully social structure in neighbouring countries.
1.13.21.4. Pro: A UBI is a lot like the "[Bread and Entertainment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bread_and_circuses)" politics of Ancient Rome under the emperors. These politics accompanied the demise of democracy in Rome.
1.13.21.4.1. Con: Rome's society was stable: the Ancient Rome existed for [723 years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Rome) \(the Eastern part even for [1700 years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire)\), and was highly successful. Bread and Entertainment is therefore an effective practice of appeasement.
1.13.21.5. Pro: The people who would fund a UBI would be the ones who would need it to automate industry without creating social unrest and may not be reliable to guarantee the service in the long term.
1.13.21.6. Pro: Universal welfare will encourage more people to drop out of the workforce, which makes society much more fragile, as it means that a bankruptcy of the government will lead to many millions dying from privation, as happened when the USSR collapsed.
1.13.21.7. Pro: UBI would lead to class conflict between net payees and net recievers. The class of net receivers would enlarge till they destroy capitalism and cause collapse of economy.
1.13.21.8. Pro: Society is a group of individuals who cooperate for a common purpose, at least for survival. Not guaranteeing a job or an income means excluding some less fortunate \(or even problematic\) individuals from society. This generates new enemies of society and does not benefit anyone. Finally, ensuring a job is more expensive, ensuring the UBI is less expensive.
1.13.22. Pro: A UBI does not resolve humanity's dependence on an \(almost always\) capitalist economy to take care of our needs.
1.13.22.1. Pro: -> See discussion #8823: A monetary economy should not exist if automation takes over everyone's job.
1.13.22.2. Pro: A UBI just enables the continuation of our current economic system that requires continual production and consumption for its own sake; which we can already see is destroying our environment. Money has humanity competing with each other, sometimes to the death. We cannot allow an inanimate object \(money\) to create our reality. We need to design a better system that encourages co-operation and cares for us all and the home we share.
1.13.22.3. Pro: There is already an inherent hierarchy and wealth imbalance. In order to first create UBI the classicism that has been ingrained in us from childhood must be removed.Another flaw is limiting wealth among those without creating a Marxist government.
1.13.22.4. Pro: Focus on the monetary coverage of basic needs without a clear statement that these are the basic human rights \(housing, food, healthcare, education, electricity etc.\) allows to keep the system of control by the size of UBI determined by the elites. The whole idea behind UBI is to liberate people, not to change their chains.
1.13.23. Pro: If politicians tackle the root causes of people needing the UBI, such as a failing infrastructure that people rely on for jobs, then a UBI would not be brought up.
1.13.23.1. Pro: A UBI does not solve any systemic problems* \(only eases their effects\) and creates issues of its own. Instead we should invest in concepts that truly resolve issues without causing further damage. 
*Systemic problems could be anything from, but limited to, financial \(such needing a job to survive\), educational \(decision-making skills\), political \(infrastructure\), and environmental \(resource use from more spending\).
1.13.24. Pro: A UBI can be used to placate the general populace in order to lessen opposition to the government or calls for more fundamental reforms.
1.13.24.1. Pro: The [Saudi Arabian](https://www.forbes.com/sites/jeffreydorfman/2016/10/03/saudi-government-budget-cuts-prove-basic-income-is-no-solution-to-slow-economic-growth/#1376eff54bbe) government provides its citizens with a form of UBI in order to keep them happy and thus cement government power and the prevailing societal conditions.
1.13.24.1.1. Con: The Saudi system is not a UBI but rather large-scale public employment and spending that systematically excludes women and religious minorities.
1.13.24.1.1.1. Con: The difference between a UBI and such an employment program is negligible.
1.13.24.1.1.1.1. Con: The difference is not negligible if you are a woman or religious minority; it is the difference between receiving a healthy living wage and between receiving nothing.
1.13.24.1.1.2. Pro: It cannot even be called an UBI if it is not universal.
1.13.24.1.2. Con: There is scant evidence that the UBI prevents social critique of the regime in Saudi Arabia. Causality is hard to determine, but pressure on the Kingdom seems to have increased since transfer payments from Oil wealth were introduced.
1.13.24.1.3. Con: Saudi Arabia is also one of the states with the [worst human rights policies](https://wayback.archive-it.org/all/20110131191445/http://www.freedomhouse.org/uploads/special_report/88.pdf) of the world. That the people of Saudi Arabia don't call for a change might have little to do with a UBI.
1.13.24.2. Con: If everyone is "too happy" to care about bad social conditions, then what exactly is it that is so "bad" about them? By that logic any other kind of progress that makes people better off than before should be opposed too on the same grounds.
1.13.24.2.1. Con: While the logic itself is sound, the key element is that it cements "prevailing societal conditions", such as restricting the rights of their female citizens. The argument is akin to saying "The Colonial Slaves had food, shelter, and protection provided to them by their masters, why should they complain?"
1.13.24.2.2. Con: Privilege can blind people to the suffering and states of those not so privileged, such as first-world peoples having only a theoretical understanding of the suffering of those most disadvantaged in the third world.
1.13.24.3. Con: Saying that the UBI is used only to cement government power is misleading. You can say the same for every form of welfare. Maybe the government could act only in an egoistical way but this is still something that makes people's life better. You don't need any proof, if the government gives money to the people it can't be considered something bad.
1.13.24.4. Pro: A UBI is a way of legitimising the capitalist system, by 'paying off' the electorate, disadvantaging future generations and nature.
1.13.24.5. Pro: A UBI can be used to lure voters or group of voters. Political parties can use this as a campaign tool promising unjustified and economically non-viable income to poor voters. This could not just affect economy but also undermines principle of free and fair voting.
1.13.25. Con: Since the bread and wine dole in Rome, successful societies have recognized that their people must be economic as well as political citizens.
1.13.25.1. Con: The Roman grain dole was actually a palliative. The phrase "bread and circuses" is actually coined to describe the act of offering a diversion \(food and entertainment\) instead of good governance and solid public policies. ["Bread and Circuses"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bread_and_circuses)
1.13.26. Pro: A UBI indirectly addresses illegal immigration by providing an incentive towards citizenship.
1.14. Con: -> See 1.2.2.3.2.
1.15. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.
1.16. Pro: A UBI helps downsize government bureaucracies.
1.16.1. Pro: A UBI is simpler to administer and is therefore less prone to denying deserving people coverage because of bureaucratic inefficiencies \(delays, miscategorisation of people, and other barriers to access\).
1.16.1.1. Pro: Many people are unaware of the fact that they are eligible for government programs; for example the US [has more than $300 million in unclaimed pension benefits](http://money.cnn.com/2013/01/24/pf/unclaimed-money/index.html). This lack of information often prevents them from accessing their entitlements.
1.16.1.2. Con: That ease comes at the cost of inefficiency of expenditure. The best system would efficiently spend money \(allocate it where it is most needed\) and assume it gets there \(eliminate bureaucratic efficiencies\), but logistical errors in a system do not warrant an entire systemic overhaul.
1.16.1.2.1. Pro: Allowing individual \(distributed\) decision-making provides for the money to flow where it is most needed \(in the recipient's informed opinion\), since the recipients of the UBI stipend will decide how to spend the money.  If the money is most needed in adjacent communities/sectors from those where it was initially disbursed, market forces will eventually drive the UBI money in that direction.
1.16.1.3. Con: Assuming that even a minimal level of currently existing welfare programs need to remain, the existence of a UBI disincentivizes the state to quickly reduce bureaucratic inefficiencies.
1.16.1.3.1. Con: Having fewer welfare programs allows the state to focus on making them efficient instead of having to spread its attention across all of the currently existing programs.
1.16.1.3.2. Pro: The presence of a UBI will demand government attention directed to implementing it. This means that less attention will go towards making other social welfare programs more efficient.
1.16.2. Pro: With a one-size-fits all income transfer there is less need for governments to waste resources tracking down issues such as welfare fraud or when people cross into higher income thresholds.
1.16.3. Con: These government bureaucracies exist for a reason: they help distribute state resources in a targeted fashion. There may be savings that come from removing their jobs but doing so means the government gets less value from its spending.
1.16.3.1. Con: Targeted aid needs conditions, which creates poverty traps, encouraging people to stay at home rather than find a job \(usually insecure and low paid\) [because of the risk of losing the subsidy and passing through the welfare bureaucracy again](http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1468-0009.2006.00465.x/full).
1.16.3.1.1. Pro: Targeted welfare programmes occasionally miss their targets because bureaucracy gets in the way and makes it too hard to comply with all the required prerequisites such as attending administration offices, forms completions, record keeping, etc.
1.16.3.1.1.1. Pro: Many people give up on accessing welfare, or do not claim as much as they could, due to the stresses and complexity caused by such conditions.
1.16.3.1.1.2. Pro: Existing welfare programs are dehumanising; they require recipients to proactively prove their need through a series of hostile and complex procedures. A UBI would alleviate this source of stress for the poor.
1.16.3.1.1.2.1. Pro: People on a disability allowance in the UK are [subject to continuous testing of their capability](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/health/9590852/Botched-tests-deny-the-disabled-their-benefits.html) to work by a company incentivised to deny them further benefits.
1.16.3.1.1.2.2. Pro: 'Middle-class welfare' programs, such as home-mortgage deductions, income splitting, or tax write-offs for having children are all done automatically or through tax returns. Only for the poor does government assistance require such extensive labour and humiliation.
1.16.3.1.1.2.3. Pro: Requiring the poor to jump through government-run hoops to receive assistance is a huge barrier as they are already a group likely to feel rejected and unwanted by the state.
1.16.3.1.1.2.4. Pro: Having to jump through bureaucratic barriers is a huge waste of time for those looking for work and/or who are employed in low-paying jobs.
1.16.3.1.1.2.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.9.1.1.1.
1.16.3.1.1.2.6. Pro: The [most powerful effect of a universal dividend to Cherokee Nation members](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/wonk/wp/2015/10/08/the-remarkable-ways-a-little-money-can-change-a-childs-personality-for-life/) was in personality effects, whereby recipients \(and their children\) had much lower instances of behavioural and emotional disorders while increased amounts of conscientiousness and agreeableness.
1.16.3.1.1.2.7. Pro: Having a criminal history can discourage or prohibit those in need from applying for welfare and other programs; leaving them without help or assistance.
1.16.3.1.1.2.8. Con: The "hostile and complex" procedures are there to prevent fraud. The able-bodied among the poor could avoid these procedures by hard work, whether that means a side job or going to school \(for which they'd receive aid\).
1.16.3.1.1.2.8.1. Con: Judging purely by "able-bodiedness" ignores psychological and psychiatric considerations.
1.16.3.1.1.2.9. Con: Welfare programs may be described as complicated or inefficient, but not 'dehumanizing.' That someone poor or disabled might have to do some extra paperwork or sit through some inconvenience to receive a monetary benefit or other service offered by society doesn't mean society is treating them as 'less human' - the very fact a benefit is being offered is a sign society acknowledges their humanity and circumstance.
1.16.3.1.1.2.9.1. Con: Welfare programs are not dehumanizing when and because they're sometimes difficult to access; they're dehumanizing when and because they're inquisitorial, hostile, and often feel like they're picking on or kicking the disadvantaged while they're already down. Some even have this as an implicit or explicit policy in order to discourage people from claiming benefits and save the program money - benefits which the majority of claimants are genuinely in need of and legitimately entitled to!
1.16.3.1.1.2.10. Con: While a UBI may be more preferably and efficient in some ways to huge bureaucracies that have the same aim, it seems likely that most modern welfare states would add on a UBI to already existing bureaucratic programs, rather than replace them.
1.16.3.1.1.2.10.1. Con: Even if it would be so those who feel offended or otherwise harm by existing programs could stop using them and live off the UBI.
1.16.3.1.1.2.11. Pro: Nothing is more demeaning that having to convince a welfare worker that you are poor and need assistance. Most state's have a welfare system require beneficiaries to prove that they need help. A blanket UBI improves dignity by removing the need to beg for benefits and by giving everyone the same benefits.
1.16.3.1.1.2.11.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.19.
1.16.3.1.1.2.11.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.18.1.1.
1.16.3.1.2. Pro: Welfare programmes which have a waiting period for benefits eligibility will penalise those having short interludes between taking short-term jobs before the benefit payments kick in.
1.16.3.2. Con: With untargeted financial assistance for all of a country, people living there will be able to allocate said funds to suit their own needs. This means less government oversight is necessary. While the targeted nature of spending may be sacrificed, more people would benefit overall, meaning the government would see a different value, rather than less of one.
1.16.3.3. Con: There is an optimal point after which bureaucratic overhead exceeds the gains of their input.
1.16.3.4. Con: This wouldn't eliminate the "task" of allocating resources, it places the burden on those that are using them instead of on the government.
1.16.4. Pro: A simple process applied to many people equally is a lot simpler than many independent agencies and laws coming into play for fewer people.
1.16.4.1. Con: It may be simpler, but some people do not deserve or need a UBI \(wealthy individuals, for example\). Filtering out fewer people - who are in actual need - allows them to get more of the available resources.
1.16.4.2. Pro: A UBI eliminates bureaucracy. Social grants for a multitude of recipients and recipient classes are all encompassed into one disbursement entity. This creates vastly greater efficiency.
1.16.5. Pro: In Nijmegen, in the Netherlands, roughly  [90 million euros is given in welfare, but it costs 16 million](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/dec/26/dutch-city-utrecht-basic-income-uk-greens) euros to run the bureaucracy.
1.16.6. Con: This assumes a UBI would replace most other forms of welfare.
1.16.6.1. Con: -> See 1.6.11.2.
1.16.6.2. Con: There is no specific counter-claim in this claim. Of course UBI replaces other forms of welfare.
1.16.6.3. Con: Even if it didn't supplant other forms of welfare, the assumption offered is a relatively safe one, because of the intent of the UBI to replace welfare \(as in the original claim\).
1.16.6.4. Pro: The general idea of UBI is the replacement of all welfare programmes with unconditional income. So: yes, your assumption is correct.
1.16.7. Con: Moving to a UBI system would put thousands of government employees, currently working in the welfare system, out of work. The transition will be difficult, and needs to be handled delicately.
1.16.7.1. Con: The transition would be softened considerably by the new UBI program.
1.16.7.2. Con: At least some of them would be needed to handle the logistics of the new UBI system.
1.16.8. Con: UBI is a centralized system therefore it is subject to the [single point of failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single_point_of_failure) problem.
1.16.9. Pro: A UBI achieves both the progressive goal of providing a safety net for workers and the conservative goal of downsizing government - hence it can unify otherwise disparate political aspects of society.
1.16.9.1. Con: A UBI doesn't ultimately achieve Conservative Goals. It hands even more power to a centralized structure through the non-voluntary confiscation of resources \(higher taxes\) eliminating individual agency and freedom. The power to choose how one spends their own resources largely disappears. Conservatives aren't in favor of higher taxes for most anything — more authority over our collective resources increases the power of governments rather than decreases it.
1.16.9.1.1. Con: Conservative goals often reduce safety, stability, health, quality of life, agency, and freedom for the underprivileged and less fortunate.
1.16.10. Con: The management of the UBI will increase the size of the government to answer the question of how gets it, when and how much.
1.16.10.1. Con: Those questions are inherently easier to answer of a UBI than they are to answer for targeted welfare schemes which have many conditions to ponder and regulate. Given a UBI will displace at least some of those welfare schemes the result is likely to be a net decrease in the size of government.
1.16.11. Pro: A UBI is cheaper to administer than existing welfare systems and thus frees would-be bureaucrats to contribute to the economy and thus wastes less money.
1.16.12. Con: While downsizing government bureaucracies in general would help save taxpayers' money, replacing welfare with a UBI would ultimately cost taxpayers much more because of the immense cost of the policy.
1.16.12.1. Con: Citizens will likely spend their UBI payments on taxable goods and services and therefore mitigate some of its own cost.
1.16.12.2. Con: The costs of a UBI do not say anything about the size of the bureaucracy needed to implement it.
1.16.12.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.
1.16.12.4. Con: [Here is an example](http://www.aei.org/publication/a-budget-neutral-universal-basic-income/) of a budget neutral application of basic income, from the American Enterprise Institute.
1.16.12.5. Con: -> See 1.3.14.1.
1.16.12.6. Con: -> See 1.4.10.1.1.7.1.1.3.2.1.
1.17. Pro: A UBI enhances the freedom and dignity of individuals.
1.17.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.17.2.
1.17.2. Con: There is nothing dignified about getting money for nothing. A sense of dignity comes from experiencing the accomplishment of a job well done, and the value one's work provides to others - which is primarily expressed through payment. Thus, earning one's living is dignified, whereas living off others is not.
1.17.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.8.3.
1.17.2.2. Con: Dignity need not derive solely, or mostly, from work. This definition of dignity is just one normative standard based on rewarding notions of individual effort and responsibility with resources. Dignity could be said to derive from justice or equity, whereby resources are allocated based on an individual or societal need rather than having to be 'earned' solely through labour.
1.17.2.3. Con: Much work is hard, not properly compensated, thankless, and seems completely pointless to those who are doing the job. There is nothing dignified about such work.
1.17.2.3.1. Con: Apart from immoral and otherwise unproper jobs, work is dignifying and has real value for society: if it didn't have value it wouldn't be paid. If it is otherwise it is problem of administrative or political reason, becouse no individual will pay for work of no value.
1.17.2.3.1.1. Con: A penny has real value, but it costs more to make then it is actually worth. The jobs many people are forced to work with have the relative work to value relation of pennies.
1.17.2.3.1.1.1. Con: This is administrative problem — the penny should not be made further and prices should either adapt to a higher nominal value or the problem should be covered somehow else. If covering such problem would be more costly or improper for other reasons, then it means the penny's value is not only it's nominal but also includes the cost of this problem solving.
1.17.2.3.2. Con: That work still needs to be done regardless of UBI.
1.17.2.3.2.1. Con: If the work needs to be done, then companies can properly compensate people to the point where they want to do them.
1.17.2.3.2.2. Con: Empowering workers with a UBI encourages employers to rethink the necessity \(and methods of completing\) tasks widely disliked and considered unpleasant and/or unnecessary.
1.17.2.4. Pro: One of the [psychological benefits](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Peter_Warr3/publication/16154562_Psychological_aspects_of_employment_and_unemployment/links/564a2f8108ae295f644fbc44.pdf?inViewer=0&pdfJsDownload=0&origin=publication_detail) of employment is that it "provides a personal identity and status within social institutions. The employed person occupies a role, with associated rights and obligations which are not possessed by those out of work."
1.17.2.4.1. Con: This logic seems at face value like it would apply just the same to slavery. Slavery is obviously not desirable.
1.17.2.4.2. Con: The benefits of employment do not require that it be paid work. A UBI can enable people to do voluntary work for institutions or people who cannot afford paid staff, giving the workers the benefits of employment and providing value to society through their labour. Someone who cannot do voluntary work without risking losing their benefits is unable to provide value to society and gain the benefits of employment by volunteering.
1.17.2.5. Pro: According to [Catholic social teaching](http://www.catholicsocialteaching.org.uk/themes/dignity-in-work/), "work is dignified and an intrinsic good, and workers must always be respected and valued." As Saint John Paul II put it, "Only man is capable of work, and only man works, at the same time by work occupying his existence on earth. Thus work bears a particular mark of man and of humanity, the mark of a person operating within a community of persons. And this mark decides its interior characteristics; in a sense it constitutes its very nature."
1.17.2.5.1. Con: Not everyone lives by or follows the social teachings of Catholicism. Thus their perspective on what is considered or cultivates dignity, cannot be applied to every citizen.
1.17.2.5.1.1. Con: From whom the statement comes is less important than what it states. John Paul II was cited not because of his authority, but because he described an idea in well-fitting words.
1.17.2.5.2. Con: Many workers aren't respected or valued by their places of business.
1.17.2.5.2.1. Pro: [Some places of business deny their female employees parts of their earned medical benefit compensation as a way of cutting costs.​](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burwell_v._Hobby_Lobby_Stores,_Inc.)
1.17.2.5.2.2. Pro: If the workers were respected, they would be paid at least a living wage; instead many people need 2 or 3 jobs to stay afloat.
1.17.2.5.3. Con: With the introduction and proliferation of automation and AI into the production systems, it is now obvious that John Paul II was wrong when he asserted that "only man is capable of work, and only man works."
1.17.2.5.4. Con: There are dogs in police, border control and other places, which also do work.
1.17.2.5.5. Con: Many philosophical perspectives, such as utilitarianism, value quality and enjoyment of life over production, productivity, and labour.
1.17.2.6. Con: If dignity accrues from feeling accomplished in your work and feeling that your work has value, that diginity doesn't accrue if you actively dislike the work you do. A UBI would free poorer individuals to take risks in order to choose work that they enjoy, in contrast to the current limited work options for a person worried about securing their basic needs.
1.17.2.6.1. Con: You could dislike your work, but know it has value and is needed — therefore feeling dignified by doing it.
1.17.2.7. Con: Dignity is linked to confidence and certainty, which in turn is reduced by poverty and economic uncertainty. A UBI could help to mitigate the latter two factors, and thereby increase individuals' confidence and dignity.
1.17.2.8. Con: There are [societies which highly respect the elderly](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/2014/02/25/what-other-cultures-can-teach_n_4834228.html) even though they do not actively earn money and frequently may cause costs.
1.17.2.9. Con: While taking money for nothing is not dignified, receiving payment for the sole fact that you are a part of society provides a feeling of dignity.
1.17.2.10. Con: Provided it is true we could observe, that people desires to be accepted by community. Thus they would strive to get a job and earn funds to get rid of etiquete of lazy-bones. UBI would could provide them such possibility and by that lead them on the way of dignity.
1.17.2.10.1. Pro: People can be more proactive and productive if their daily struggles are lessened. If their quality of life goes up, so may the quality of their contributions.
1.17.2.11. Pro: The proper question should be: is it moral to ask one person to support another person of equal means with equal capacity for self-sufficiency? Ignoring any wealth disparity, it would be immoral to require a random person to financially support another random person that is capable of supporting themselves. A UBI is just applied this principle at scale and so, by extension, it is thus better to fix targeted welfare schemes than to create a UBI.
1.17.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.8.
1.17.4. Con: A UBI would further erode individual liberty and responsibility leading to more government control over citizens.

1.17.4.1.1. Con: Even when security is provided the [birthrate stabilizes below the replacement rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Income_and_fertility#Fertility_J-curve) independently of culture.
1.17.4.1.2. Pro: Since people can breed far beyond their means to acquire the resources of others, this may create resentment and a population beyond current capacities, leading to resentment and fights over resources. Since people are enabled to breed beyond their means they will then become a target for restrictions due to resource limitations or disruptions to quality of life from a surge of new children.

1.17.4.2. Con: A restructuring of social programming into simple cash transfers allow people to decide what the money is spent on, rather than bureaucrats deciding for them. This enhances individual liberty and autonomy.
1.17.4.2.1. Con: Aside from a minarchist system there is no form of governance where no negative rights are infringed. There can be no individual liberty when people are forced by the government to transfer money to others \(coercive redistribution\).
1.17.4.2.1.1. Con: Individual liberty must be protected by some organization at some expense, as there are many individuals who would otherwise be willing to infringe others' rights for their own benefit. Therefore, some form of government is necessary and essential for protecting individuals' rights.
1.17.4.3. Pro: There may be rules implemented in which an individual loses their right to their UBI based on certain circumstances or actions, making a tool to control behavior.

1.17.4.3.1.1. Con: Just because other programs do this does not mean other programs ought to do it.

1.17.4.3.2. The same draconian laws could come to apply to all under a UBI.
1.17.4.3.3. Con: That wouldn't be a Universal Basic Income, then, as it would lose the Universal aspect.
1.17.4.3.4. Con: The conditions imposed upon current welfare recipients \(drugs testing; forced budgeting\) would be unpalatable if extended outside the lower classes. Thus they are unlikely to ever be conditions of a universal benefit.
1.17.4.3.5. Con: In that case, it cannot be really called an UBI, it would just be another welfare program. UBI as a program is only relevant if it is truly universal. Even people in the prison system should be entitled to an UBI, even if they cannot use it while they are in jail. This would provide them with either a way to help their families during incarceration or small savings for when they leave the prison, reducing recidivism.

1.17.4.6.2.2. Then the countries with emigrants will need to solve their own overpopulation problems as they have less options to leave. They will also have a lower carbon footprint in their country.

1.17.11. Pro: A UBI improves the mental and physical health of a population.
1.17.11.1. Pro: A UBI would allow for short term illness, or disability to be dealt with in a non-exploitative or demeaning way. Further the lifestyle and quality of life would not have to alter completely, nor would medical bills pile up causing a continued cycle of debt and poverty.

1.17.11.3. Pro: UBI improves the health of the population, because it reduces stress.
1.17.11.4. Pro: Income transfers are some of the most effective means of [improving health outcomes](https://c.ymcdn.com/sites/alphaweb.site-ym.com/resource/collection/86D31666-E7EA-42F1-BDA1-A03ECA0B4E3D/OnPhysicians_Letter_BIG_170815.pdf) and being healthy is a fundamental precursor to almost all other rights and freedoms.

1.17.11.5. Pro: UBI will improve health which will lead to less money being spent on treatment, and higher productivity due to reduced presenteeism. This is supported by an 8.5% reduction in hospitalization observed in the Dauphin, Manitoba experiment, and the loss of an estimated $250 billion a year from people going to work sick when they should be staying home but feel they have no choice. UBI is like a vaccine. An ounce of prevention is far cheaper than a pound of cure.
1.17.11.5.1. Con: Better health while in poverty will mean more women in poverty will choose to have children out of wedlock, thus increasing the population of government dependants and the social problems associated with single parent families.

1.17.11.5.1.2. Con: Reduction of poverty means that women - in or out of wedlock - are better able to access things such as contraception, and thus better able to avoid unwanted children. This is likely to balance any increase in the number of those who choose to have children that they may not want to have if poorer - but may still have anyway.
1.17.11.5.1.3. Con: The same logic could be used to attack any initiative that would increase the health of people in poverty. This is not sufficient reason to avoid improving the health and living conditions of those living in poverty.

1.17.11.6. Con: UBI could lead to a breakdown in society. The inclination of too many in society would be toward detrimental actions to ones health if allowed more idle time.

1.17.11.7. Pro: As job security becomes less stable with AI technologies, mental health problems related to redundancies could cost a lot of money. A UBI would not only provide income security, but cost countries less in mental health related services.
1.17.11.8. Pro: A UBI can improve health, as was the case with India's experiment in 2010. See the India section in[UBI, 5 experiments from around the world](https://www.worldcrunch.com/culture-society/universal-basic-income-5-experiments-from-around-the-world-1): The trial resulted in a sharp increase in food sufficiency and a drop in illness in villages that received the funds compared to those that did not."
1.17.12. Pro: A UBI can have a positive impact on societal practices and culture by acknowledging the inherent humanity of all citizens.
1.17.12.1. Pro: A UBI provides unconditional help to all people, while existing welfare systems have limited range.
1.17.12.1.1. Pro: Placing conditions on the receipt of social benefits saps political support from the idea as it divides society into recipients and contributors. For example, Medicare in the US receives broad support because it is so broadly applied.
1.17.12.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.12.2.
1.17.12.1.3. Pro: The current economic systems is extremely unfair, often because of corrupt management and lack of workplaces.
1.17.12.2. Con: The idea that you are owed money simply for existing is toxic. Your humanity guarantees you protection from your fellow humans and the state - negative freedoms. It does not permit you to force your fellow citizen to serve you.
1.17.12.2.1. Con: There is no such thing as a pure "negative right". All rights require enforcement, and all enforcement requires labor. Even a right to be left alone is generally considered to require the labor of an enforcing police presence.
1.17.12.2.1.1. Con: A "negative right" is defined by no one having to do anything in order to respect it, as opposed to a "positive right" that requires action in order to not violate it. Enforcement is an entirely different matter, as it only becomes relevant to guard against and punish violations of rights.
1.17.12.2.2. Con: This individualism is simplistic. Actions by groups of fellow humans and the state negatively affect humans inadvertently - most humans, for instance, cannot simply leave organized society and subsist off the land, and thereby are inevitably reliant on the state/society and other humans for their subsistence, due to past and present actions of the state and humanity as a collective. This arguably creates a duty from the state to humans in general.
1.17.12.3. Pro: A UBI makes money less important and puts the value in making life wonderful. We tend to translate everything into economic terms; distracting our attention to what its important. When one feels secure with UBI its less probable that people will get distracted by concerns of financial security. One can focus on doing things, even for free, and let life's rewards come without fear.
1.17.12.4. Pro: A UBI sends a message of trust from government to citizens. Trust is usually responded to by accepting responsibility and willing to be trustworthy.

1.17.12.5. Pro: A UBI eliminates mankind's existential angst. Waking up every morning with no worries about how to maintain your life unleashes the best in people.
1.17.12.5.1. Con: One of Ghandi's 7 sins is [wealth without work](http://www.mkgandhi.org/mgmnt.htm). The reason for this is simple: that our lives are enriched by purpose and accomplishment. Helping someone over a hump is one thing but paying charity over time reduces the quality of life which is inhuman.
1.17.12.5.1.1. Con: This argument is deeply entrenched in the virtue theory of morality, to the point of ignoring any of the other ethical frameworks. 1.17.12.5.1.3.1. Con: This is not an exclusive matter; "wealth without work" can easily refer to both scenarios.
1.17.12.5.1.3.1.1. Con: It seems much more plausible that Ghandi did not think of the state providing basic resources as a 'sin' given that was one of the policies he implemented and maintained.
1.17.12.5.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.12.3.1.6.2.1.5.5.
1.17.12.5.2. Pro: A UBI will enable people to think about life's important questions more. The Earth and our human societies find themselves in the dire situations today, because we haven't given proper importance to the individual to answer these questions for themselves. 1.17.12.5.2.1. Con: Arguably, having an uninspiring and demotivating job makes us ponder these questions more than otherwise.
1.17.12.5.2.1.1. Con: Not for the majority of the population who live with minimum income to make their basic needs, they are more focused on surviving; hence not able to afford energy to improve society.

1.17.12.5.2.3. Pro: For anyone whose primary concern is their own immediate physical and financial security for themselves and family, such personal need will eclipse any beneficial concern for community and habitat. UBI enhances the likelihood that most people will consider the common good.
1.17.12.5.2.3.1. Pro: Many people who work may wish to do more meaningful or charitable labour, but are aware there is less or no money in it in most cases.
1.17.12.5.2.4. Pro: UBI allows people to make more ethical decisions based on want and fewer coerced decisions based on need. Legally and socially criminal behavior for some is a choice but for many it is the only viable choice they feel they have to survive.
1.17.12.5.3. Con: UBI reduces worries about basic needs \(i.e shelter, food etc.\), but does not by itself deliver incentives to stretch for self-actualization \(i.e. following your passion\) rather than solely giving in to short-term driven urges \(e.g. playing video games for the sake of instant gratification\).
1.17.12.5.3.1. Con: An individual whose basic needs are met doesn't have to pursue self actualization, it is a personal choice. However if a UBI cannot make that individual seek self-actualization, neither can a job. So if nothing can truly make the individual want to pursue what's good for them, we can only reduce what prevents an individual from doing so.
1.17.12.5.4. Con: Existential Angst can also be a feeling of emptiness, or lack of purpose. If we accept [Maslow's Pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs) of needs to be somewhat true, we can fathom that even if basic human needs are met, there will always be other needs. A UBI doesn't really extinguish that angst.
1.17.12.5.4.1. Con: Accepting some of Maslow's hierarchical conception of needs entails recognizing that some needs are "higher" than others. Higher needs that follow from the fulfillment of "lower" needs are not subject to feelings of emptiness or angst, as fulfillment of needs leads to climbing the pyramid toward self-actualization.
1.17.12.5.5. Pro: If the only thing ensuring initiative would be the drive to provide oneself's sustenance, then there would be no volunteer organisations.
1.17.12.5.5.1. Con: If the point implied above were valid, organizing a UBI through voluntary contributions to a non-government organization would be the superior solution.
1.17.12.5.5.1.1. Con: The point above showed a valid counterpoint, saying it's not strong enough to support a whole economic policy does not nullify it.
1.17.12.5.6. Con: No amount of money will eliminate existential angst. But, feeling worried about your economic future is an incentive to seek work and growth. In this way a UBI reduces the marginal utility of producing.
1.17.12.5.6.1. Con: Work does not necessarily lead to growth. It can simply lead to stress, exhaustion, frustration, desperation, hatred, etc.. Poor job fits — and people feeling like they have little other choice but to remain in them — can even lead to depression and suicide.
1.17.12.5.6.2. Con: If you pay attention you will see that one thing that every great past thinker had in common is that none of them was worried about surviving. That said each person has their own spectrum of what is "essential to survival."
1.17.12.6. Pro: United Nations' [Declaration of Human Rights](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/) Article 25 states that "Everyone has the right to a standard of living adequate for the health and well-being of himself and of his family ..." In a world of increasing job scarcity and rising job specialization, requiring fewer workers, UBI could be the only sustenance for hundreds of millions of people.
1.17.12.6.1. Con: Recognising the 'right' to something doesn't mean that everyone has to be provided with it.
1.17.12.6.1.1. Pro: In some situations it's impossible to answer even most crucial needs.
1.17.12.6.1.1.1. Pro: There are places where high standard medical care is unavailable.
1.17.12.6.1.1.1.1. Con: A UBI will help to fund the purchase of medical services in such areas, thus incentivizing their provision and sale.
1.17.12.6.1.1.2. Pro: There are places where access to food and water is highly limited.
1.17.12.6.1.1.2.1. Con: A UBI will help to fund the transport and sale of food and water to such locales.
1.17.12.6.1.1.3. Con: The occurrence of such situations should be reduced, and a UBI is one possible vehicle to do so.
1.17.12.7. Pro: UBI is fair because those who work also get help, not only the ones who don't contribute to society.
1.17.12.7.1. Con: A UBI would help only those, who earn less than average, because it would be financed from taxes, so those who earn more would pay more for UBI to exist, than they recieve from it.
1.17.12.7.1.1. Con: This threshold to receive a net benefit would likely be much higher than just the average income level, given progressive taxation means that the most wealthy continue a disproportionate amount of tax revenue.
1.17.12.7.1.1.1. Con: Such tax would have to be highly progressive to function properly, because rich people have lawyers, who help them to minimise the amount of tax they pay.
1.17.12.7.1.1.1.1. Pro: Some of most richest would decide to move or at least pay taxes somewhere else.
1.17.12.7.1.1.2. Con: A highly progresive tax, which would be needed to support such option, would hurt the economy significantly by reducing the motivation to earn \(and by that produce\) more of those who provide huge part of GDP.
1.17.12.7.2. Con: A UBI would hurt richest, who provide significant input into economic development.
1.17.12.7.2.1. Con: Wealthy individuals are not the only source of input for economic development; collective projects and large-scale voluntary donations can also finance development, as can governments.
1.17.12.7.2.2. Con: Many wealthy people act against the common good, creating more damage than improvement.
1.17.12.8. Pro: The economic consequences of wealth redistribution must be seen in philosophical and material terms. UBI is a humanist approach: supporting people because they exist. Against this is a materialist approach dehumanizing people to be mere earners and not able to appreciate life's demands to advance oneself.
1.17.12.9. Pro: A UBI at the least should be considered a human right, as there are not near enough job opportunities to earn more than a basic amount of income.
1.17.12.10. Pro: Current welfare systems incentivize the poor to value working over their physical and mental health. A UBI affords individuals more freedom to enjoy a greater quality of life.
1.17.12.10.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.
1.17.12.10.2. Pro: Society and government undervalue health and family life and instead [overvalue working](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/). We begin to value the quality of our own lives when we are not working solely out of necessity.
1.17.12.10.2.1. Pro: Under capitalism, the meaning of [leisure](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/) has been undermined by what is considered its opposite: work. Leisure is not merely free or unoccupied time, but rather time spent thoughtfully at your own discretion.
1.17.12.10.3. Con: If low-income people start substantially reducing the amounts they work, this is likely to trigger substantial political backlash to the UBI \(as low-income individuals are perceived to be leeching off of society\), which will result in reductions in the UBI and increased stigma against low-income workers.
1.17.12.10.3.1. Con: It requires a lot more political capital to reduce UBI payments \(because that affects everyone\) than to cut welfare entitlements.
1.17.13. Pro: A UBI empowers marginalised groups who lack influence due to economic barriers.
1.17.13.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.15.2.
1.17.13.2. Con: Economic "empowerment" to the bare minimum of basic needs does not allow marginalized voices to be heard because those individuals are still at the bottom of the heap, where there is a lot of noise. A UBI does not eliminate poverty, it simply redefines it.
1.17.13.2.1. Con: By providing money for basic needs, a UBI would allow marginalized individuals and groups to spend more time and possibly even money on making themselves heard.
1.17.13.3. Pro: A UBI will provide basic human needs and enable higher intelligence in the lower class. When people have time to think about other things than surviving the day, they can better think, evaluate choices and make long term decisions. Hunger, stress and insufficient safety result in a preoccupied  mind.
1.17.13.3.1. Pro: When given a UBI, people can use the money to fix these problems so they can focus on doing well at a job.
1.17.13.4. Pro: UBI would be a great start to eliminating homelessness, boosting the economic rims of society. Yes we are only here on earth a short time, why not give some enjoyment to those that are struggling with this winner take all game of capitalism, everyone will benefit.  The enhance flow of money can only be a good thing for all evolved. Let us not be so selfish and greedy to deny our fellow Americans. Lots of blood sweat and tears have been shed developing this great country, let's act like!
1.17.13.5. Pro: Wealth is also a significant predictor of [civic engagement](http://www.people-press.org/2015/01/08/the-politics-of-financial-insecurity-a-democratic-tilt-undercut-by-low-participation/). A UBI will provide people from less advantaged backgrounds the agency and status necessary to push for the types of political change that would not be possible otherwise due to financial discrimination.
1.17.13.5.1. Pro: A UBI changes incentives for politicians who have to more actively target or incorporate traditionally politically excluded groups.
1.17.13.5.2. Con: They still only have a finite degree of political capital when engaging with elites. If wealthy people already feel as though they are contributing towards the UBI they are less likely to support these kinds of initiatives.
1.17.13.5.2.1. Con: The majority decides what's best for the society, not just the wealthy.
1.17.13.5.3. Con: NGOs and other special interest groups are able to take on the burden of this form of engagement. E.g. the NAACP in the US.
1.17.13.5.3.1. Con: These NGOs and advocacy groups are much stronger and greater political forces in a world where their constituents have more access to capital.
1.17.13.5.3.2. Con: These groups cannot speak to all of the diverse interests and desires of their own constituency.
1.17.13.6. Con: Empowering marginalised groups is contingent on providing them meaningful amounts of wealth. An income just above the poverty line is neither meaningful wealth nor economic inclusion.
1.17.13.6.1. Con: A UBI provides as a baseline an income below the poverty line; increase your income above poverty line would then be relatively easy to achieve — so long as you work to supplement your UBI. Basic income schemes are characterized by a low clawback rate, such that it would be challenging to have even a part-time minimum-wage job and still sit below the poverty line.
1.17.13.6.2. Con: A UBI ensures one's most important needs are accounted for in a stable manner. This is still incredibly meaningful for disenfranchised groups and enables far more political engagement.
1.17.14. Con: While emotionally satisfying to the giver the payment lowers the dignity of the receiver. There is nothing more uplifting than knowing that the roof over your head and food on the table was brought about by your hard work. Not given to you by those that believe you cannot do for yourself.
1.17.14.1. Con: There is no indignity in receiving the same UBI as everyone does, even the richest. The UBI will in fact eliminate the indignity by eliminating the dependency on welfare.
1.17.15. Con: It is not the role of government to ensure the dignity of individuals.
1.17.15.1. Con: Governments such as the German one have a legal duty and task to ensure human dignity. [Art. 1 GG \(1\)](https://www.bundesregierung.de/Content/EN/StatischeSeiten/breg/basic-law-content-list.html;jsessionid=F476E9FC87CF25504294CA2DE1231A13.s2t2?nn=447370#doc94410bodyText2) "Human dignity shall be inviolable. To respect and protect it shall be the duty of all state authority."
1.17.16. Pro: A UBI provides the [real freedom](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2265291?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents) to pursue the realization of one's conception of the good life.
1.17.16.1. Con: A person who wants to laze on a beach can only be fed by taking from some other source of income. This is not enough to justify a UBI as a way to redistribute revenue.
1.17.16.2. Pro: A UBI can help individuals self-actualize and express themselves creatively. Being able to do so is a basic human right.
1.17.16.2.1. Pro: As set forth in many articles of the [Universal Declaration of Human Rights](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/), every human being has a right to certain freedoms, including leisure, expression, human dignity, and development of personality. A UBI makes this more obtainable for everyone.
1.17.16.2.2. Pro: The philosophy that guides a UBI is that a life free of poverty is a universal right, not a privilege earned through working.
1.17.16.3. Pro: The adoption of a UBI could help to change the narrative that working full-time is the only way to lead a fulfilling life, which will become important as that becomes increasingly unnecessary and/or inefficient.
1.17.16.3.1. Con: It is false to equivocate acceptance of technological advances with acceptance of a post-work world. As new industries unearth new market needs and generate new wants, new jobs in industries arise to replace those lost to obsolescence.
1.17.16.4. Pro: A UBI allows individuals to make decisions based on their free will VS whether or not they will eat/have shelter.
1.17.16.5. Pro: With a UBI, people will not be forced to settle for a job that only pays the bills, but will be free to explore career paths until they find a meaningful and fulfilling occupation.
1.17.16.5.1. Pro: A UBI gives the individual more freedom. It allows people to decide how to take part in society. There are no strings attached. People can decide whether they want to work or not and they would be urged to do the things they like and that would create happiness.
1.17.16.6. Pro: A UBI would allow people to focus on jobs that they are passionate about rather than simply monetary gain, and thus would improve mental health, job and life satisfaction, self-value and contribution to society.
1.17.16.6.1. Pro: This would also improve their effectiveness and productivity at work, due to increased effort and motivation.
1.17.16.6.2. Pro: An unconditional UBI separates work from income, so that you can do whatever you are best in - and not, what you have to do because somebody is willing to pay for it.
1.17.16.6.3. Con: If everyone does not do the work that's offered but the work they love to do, maybe the resulting work is not financially viable to support the current GDP level \(and thusly through taxes the UBI itself\), e.g., if many people do humanistic work.
1.17.16.7. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.21.1.4.
1.17.16.8. Pro: Working with an exclusive materialistic intention is sad as it makes humans subject to alienation.
1.17.16.9. Pro: A UBI would give the hardworking person a chance to work for something the person really believes in, rather than being a slave to someone else's project. This leads to a more  satisfied world.
1.17.16.10. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.
1.17.16.11. Pro: Dignity is the cornerstone of personal success and as the world is trending toward AI, a majority of citizens will find themselves without opportunity. GBI guaranteed basic income\) could be the seed of self respect and possibilities.
1.17.16.12. Pro: People who prefer to laze on beaches should not be compelled to work in order to feed themselves. The same goes for people who would opt for a part-time job, or take up volunteering, or start up a new business. A UBI [could provide everyone](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2265291?seq=1&Search=yes&searchText=fed&list=hide&searchUri=%2Fbetasearch%2F%3Facc%3Don%26Query%3Dwhy%2Bsurfers%2Bshould%2Bbe%2Bfed%26fc%3Doff%26wc%3Don&prevSearch=&resultsServiceName=null&loggedin=true#page_scan_tab_contents) with a minimal sum that would support them in living the life they want to live.
1.17.16.12.1. Con: Most people prefer engaging in recreational activities to doing work. However, if all of these people chose not to work, society would not have enough productive labor to function.
1.17.16.12.2. Con: Freedom means choosing a path without being coerced by others, and accepting the consequences of one's actions. People who laze on beaches do not produce anything, yet a UBI would allow them to avoid the natural consequences: not being able to consume.
1.17.16.12.2.1. Con: Not entirely true: they would have less resources to consume than those who choose to engage in labour in order to fund additional consumption. "Lazing on a beach" does not in fact consume resources — it is an economically neutral activity.
1.17.17. Con: -> See 1.1.5.2.11.1.25.
1.17.18. Con: Minorities will still be at a socioeconomic disadvantage.
1.17.18.1. Con: Although minorities might remain relatively disadvantaged, their socioeconomic position would definitely improve in absolute terms.
1.17.18.2. Con: If the government's actions are oppressing minorities, then those in power should not be allowed to decide how social spending dollars are allocated. A UBI can only remove decision-making power from any bigots in public office and allocate it to the downtrodden instead. If the structure is oppressive, then removing power from the structure helps the oppressed.
1.17.18.3. Con: If a UBI does not worsen the socioeconomic disadvantages of minorities, this is not a reason not to implement a UBI, as it can lead to other improvements.
1.17.18.4. Pro: -> See 1.6.16.4.3.
